EXPLANATORY

IN this book a number of dialects are used, to wit:   Missouri negro
dialect;  extremest form of  backwoods Southwestern dialect; 
ordinary “Pike County” dialect;  four modified varieties of this
last.  shadings have not been done in a haphazard fashion, or by
guesswork; but painstakingly,  with  trustworthy guidance 
support of personal familiarity with se several forms of speech.

I make this explanation for  reason that without it many readers
would suppose that all se characters were trying to talk alike 
not succeeding.

 AUTHOR.




HUCKLEBERRY FINN

Scene:   Mississippi Valley Time:  Forty to fifty years ago




CHAPTER I.

YOU don’t know about me without you have read a book by  name of 
Adventures of Tom Sawyer; but that ain’t no matter.  That book was made
by Mr. Mark Twain,  he told  truth, mainly.  re was things
which he stretched, but mainly he told  truth.  That is nothing.  I
never seen anybody but lied one time or anor, without it was Aunt
Polly, or  widow, or maybe Mary.  Aunt Polly--Tom’s Aunt Polly, she
is-- Mary,   Widow Douglas is all told about in that book, which
is mostly a true book, with some stretchers, as I said before.

Now  way that  book winds up is this:  Tom  me found  money
that  robbers hid in  cave,  it made us rich.  We got six
thous dollars apiece--all gold.  It was an awful sight of money when
it was piled up.  Well, Judge Thatcher he took it  put it out
at interest,  it fetched us a dollar a day apiece all  year
round--more than a body could tell what to do with.   Widow Douglas
she took me for her son,  allowed she would sivilize me; but it was
rough living in  house all  time, considering how dismal regular
 decent  widow was in all her ways;  so when I couldn’t st
it no longer I lit out.  I got into my old rags  my sugar-hogshead
again,  was free  satisfied.  But Tom Sawyer he hunted me up 
said he was going to start a b of robbers,  I might join if I
would go back to  widow  be respectable.  So I went back.

 widow she cried over me,  called me a poor lost lamb,  she
called me a lot of or names, too, but she never meant no harm by
it. She put me in m new clos again,  I couldn’t do nothing but
sweat  sweat,  feel all cramped up.  Well, n,  old thing
commenced again.   widow rung a bell for supper,  you had to come
to time. When you got to  table you couldn’t go right to eating, but
you had to wait for  widow to tuck down her head  grumble a little
over  victuals, though re warn’t really anything  matter with
m,--that is, nothing only everything was cooked by itself.  In a
barrel of odds  ends it is different; things get mixed up,  
juice kind of swaps around,   things go better.

After supper she got out her book  learned me about Moses  
Bulrushers,  I was in a sweat to find out all about him; but by 
by she let it out that Moses had been dead a considerable long time; so
n I didn’t care no more about him, because I don’t take no stock in
dead people.

Pretty soon I wanted to smoke,  asked  widow to let me.  But she
wouldn’t.  She said it was a mean practice  wasn’t clean,  I must
try to not do it any more.  That is just  way with some people.  y
get down on a thing when y don’t know nothing about it.  Here she was
a-boring about Moses, which was no kin to her,  no use to anybody,
being gone, you see, yet finding a power of fault with me for doing a
thing that had some good in it.   she took snuff, too; of course that
was all right, because she done it herself.

Her sister, Miss Watson, a tolerable slim old maid, with goggles on,
had just come to live with her,  took a set at me now with a
spelling-book. She worked me middling hard for about an hour,  n
 widow made her ease up.  I couldn’t stood it much longer.  n for
an hour it was deadly dull,  I was fidgety.  Miss Watson would say,
“Don’t put your feet up re, Huckleberry;”  “Don’t scrunch up
like that, Huckleberry--set up straight;”  pretty soon she would
say, “Don’t gap  stretch like that, Huckleberry--why don’t you try to
behave?”  n she told me all about  bad place,  I said I wished
I was re. She got mad n, but I didn’t mean no harm.  All I wanted
was to go somewheres; all I wanted was a change, I warn’t particular.
 She said it was wicked to say what I said; said she wouldn’t say it for
 whole world; she was going to live so as to go to  good place.
 Well, I couldn’t see no advantage in going where she was going, so I
made up my mind I wouldn’t try for it.  But I never said so, because it
would only make trouble,  wouldn’t do no good.

Now she had got a start,  she went on  told me all about  good
place.  She said all a body would have to do re was to go around all
day long with a harp  sing, forever  ever.  So I didn’t think
much of it. But I never said so.  I asked her if she reckoned Tom Sawyer
would go re,  she said not by a considerable sight.  I was glad
about that, because I wanted him  me to be toger.

Miss Watson she kept pecking at me,  it got tiresome  lonesome.
 By  by y fetched  niggers in  had prayers,  n
everybody was off to bed.  I went up to my room with a piece of cle,
 put it on  table.  n I set down in a chair by  window 
tried to think of something cheerful, but it warn’t no use.  I felt
so lonesome I most wished I was dead.   stars were shining,  
leaves rustled in  woods ever so mournful;  I heard an owl, away
off, who-whooing about somebody that was dead,  a whippowill  a
dog crying about somebody that was going to die;   wind was trying
to whisper something to me,  I couldn’t make out what it was,  so
it made  cold shivers run over me. n away out in  woods I heard
that kind of a sound that a ghost makes when it wants to tell about
something that’s on its mind  can’t make itself understood,  so
can’t rest easy in its grave,  has to go about that way every night
grieving.  I got so down-hearted  scared I did wish I had some
company.  Pretty soon a spider went crawling up my shoulder,  I
flipped it off  it lit in  cle;  before I could budge it
was all shriveled up.  I didn’t need anybody to tell me that that was
an awful bad sign  would fetch me some bad luck, so I was scared
 most shook  clos off of me. I got up  turned around in my
tracks three times  crossed my breast every time;  n I tied
up a little lock of my hair with a thread to keep witches away.  But
I hadn’t no confidence.  You do that when you’ve lost a horseshoe that
you’ve found, instead of nailing it up over  door, but I hadn’t ever
heard anybody say it was any way to keep off bad luck when you’d killed
a spider.

I set down again, a-shaking all over,  got out my pipe for a smoke;
for  house was all as still as death now,  so  widow wouldn’t
know. Well, after a long time I heard  clock away off in  town
go boom--boom--boom--twelve licks;  all still again--stiller than
ever. Pretty soon I heard a twig snap down in  dark amongst 
trees--something was a stirring.  I set still  listened.  Directly I
could just barely hear a “me-yow! me-yow!” down re.  That was good!
 Says I, “me-yow! me-yow!” as soft as I could,  n I put out 
light  scrambled out of  window on to  shed.  n I slipped
down to  ground  crawled in among  trees, , sure enough,
re was Tom Sawyer waiting for me.




CHAPTER II.

WE went tiptoeing along a path amongst  trees back towards  end of
 widow’s garden, stooping down so as  branches wouldn’t scrape our
heads. When we was passing by  kitchen I fell over a root  made
a noise.  We scrouched down  laid still.  Miss Watson’s big nigger,
named Jim, was setting in  kitchen door; we could see him pretty
clear, because re was a light behind him.  He got up  stretched
his neck out about a minute, listening.  n he says:

“Who dah?”

He listened some more; n he come tiptoeing down  stood right
between us; we could a touched him, nearly.  Well, likely it was
minutes  minutes that re warn’t a sound,  we all re so close
toger.  re was a place on my ankle that got to itching, but I
dasn’t scratch it;  n my ear begun to itch;  next my back,
right between my shoulders.  Seemed like I’d die if I couldn’t scratch.
 Well, I’ve noticed that thing plenty times since.  If you are with
 quality, or at a funeral, or trying to go to sleep when you ain’t
sleepy--if you are anywheres where it won’t do for you to scratch, why
you will itch all over in upwards of a thous places. Pretty soon Jim
says:

“Say, who is you?  Whar is you?  Dog my cats ef I didn’ hear sumf’n.
Well, I know what I’s gwyne to do:  I’s gwyne to set down here 
listen tell I hears it agin.”

So he set down on  ground betwixt me  Tom.  He leaned his back up
against a tree,  stretched his legs out till one of m most touched
one of mine.  My nose begun to itch.  It itched till  tears come into
my eyes.  But I dasn’t scratch.  n it begun to itch on  inside.
Next I got to itching underneath.  I didn’t know how I was going to set
still. This miserableness went on as much as six or seven minutes; but
it seemed a sight longer than that.  I was itching in eleven different
places now.  I reckoned I couldn’t st it more’n a minute longer,
but I set my teeth hard  got ready to try.  Just n Jim begun
to brea heavy; next he begun to snore-- n I was pretty soon
comfortable again.

Tom he made a sign to me--kind of a little noise with his mouth-- we
went creeping away on our hs  knees.  When we was ten foot off Tom
whispered to me,  wanted to tie Jim to  tree for fun.  But I said
no; he might wake  make a disturbance,  n y’d find out I
warn’t in. n Tom said he hadn’t got cles enough,  he would slip
in  kitchen  get some more.  I didn’t want him to try.  I said Jim
might wake up  come.  But Tom wanted to resk it; so we slid in re
 got three cles,  Tom laid five cents on  table for pay.
n we got out,  I was in a sweat to get away; but nothing would do
Tom but he must crawl to where Jim was, on his hs  knees,  play
something on him.  I waited,  it seemed a good while, everything was
so still  lonesome.

As soon as Tom was back we cut along  path, around  garden fence,
 by  by fetched up on  steep top of  hill  or side of
 house.  Tom said he slipped Jim’s hat off of his head  hung it
on a limb right over him,  Jim stirred a little, but he didn’t wake.
Afterwards Jim said  witches be witched him  put him in a trance,
 rode him all over  State,  n set him under  trees again,
 hung his hat on a limb to show who done it.   next time Jim told
it he said y rode him down to New Orleans; , after that, every
time he told it he spread it more  more, till by  by he said y
rode him all over  world,  tired him most to death,  his back
was all over saddle-boils.  Jim was monstrous proud about it,  he
got so he wouldn’t hardly notice  or niggers.  Niggers would come
miles to hear Jim tell about it,  he was more looked up to than any
nigger in that country.  Strange niggers would st with ir mouths
open  look him all over, same as if he was a wonder.  Niggers is
always talking about witches in  dark by  kitchen fire; but
whenever one was talking  letting on to know all about such things,
Jim would happen in  say, “Hm!  What you know ‘bout witches?” 
that nigger was corked up  had to take a back seat.  Jim always kept
that five-center piece round his neck with a string,  said it was a
charm  devil give to him with his own hs,  told him he could
cure anybody with it  fetch witches whenever he wanted to just by
saying something to it; but he never told what it was he said to it.
 Niggers would come from all around re  give Jim anything y
had, just for a sight of that five-center piece; but y wouldn’t touch
it, because  devil had had his hs on it.  Jim was most ruined for
a servant, because he got stuck up on account of having seen  devil
 been rode by witches.

Well, when Tom  me got to  edge of  hilltop we looked away down
into  village  could see three or four lights twinkling, where
re was sick folks, maybe;   stars over us was sparkling ever
so fine;  down by  village was  river, a whole mile broad, 
awful still  gr.  We went down  hill  found Jo Harper 
Ben Rogers,  two or three more of  boys, hid in  old tanyard.
 So we unhitched a skiff  pulled down  river two mile  a half,
to  big scar on  hillside,  went ashore.

We went to a clump of bushes,  Tom made everybody swear to keep 
secret,  n showed m a hole in  hill, right in  thickest
part of  bushes.  n we lit  cles,  crawled in on our
hs  knees.  We went about two hundred yards,  n  cave
opened up. Tom poked about amongst  passages,  pretty soon ducked
under a wall where you wouldn’t a noticed that re was a hole.  We
went along a narrow place  got into a kind of room, all damp 
sweaty  cold,  re we stopped.  Tom says:

“Now, we’ll start this b of robbers  call it Tom Sawyer’s Gang.
Everybody that wants to join has got to take an oath,  write his name
in blood.”

Everybody was willing.  So Tom got out a sheet of paper that he had
wrote  oath on,  read it.  It swore every boy to stick to 
b,  never tell any of  secrets;  if anybody done anything to
any boy in  b, whichever boy was ordered to kill that person 
his family must do it,  he mustn’t eat  he mustn’t sleep till he
had killed m  hacked a cross in ir breasts, which was  sign
of  b.  nobody that didn’t belong to  b could use that
mark,  if he did he must be sued;  if he done it again he must be
killed.   if anybody that belonged to  b told  secrets, he
must have his throat cut,  n have his carcass burnt up  
ashes scattered all around,  his name blotted off of  list with
blood  never mentioned again by  gang, but have a curse put on it
 be forgot forever.

Everybody said it was a real beautiful oath,  asked Tom if he got
it out of his own head.  He said, some of it, but  rest was out of
pirate-books  robber-books,  every gang that was high-toned had
it.

Some thought it would be good to kill  _families_ of boys that told
 secrets.  Tom said it was a good idea, so he took a pencil  wrote
it in. n Ben Rogers says:

“Here’s Huck Finn, he hain’t got no family; what you going to do ‘bout
him?”

“Well, hain’t he got a far?” says Tom Sawyer.

“Yes, he’s got a far, but you can’t never find him se days.  He
used to lay drunk with  hogs in  tanyard, but he hain’t been seen
in se parts for a year or more.”

y talked it over,  y was going to rule me out, because y
said every boy must have a family or somebody to kill, or else it
wouldn’t be fair  square for  ors.  Well, nobody could think of
anything to do--everybody was stumped,  set still.  I was most ready
to cry; but all at once I thought of a way,  so I offered m Miss
Watson--y could kill her.  Everybody said:

“Oh, she’ll do.  That’s all right.  Huck can come in.”

n y all stuck a pin in ir fingers to get blood to sign with,
 I made my mark on  paper.

“Now,” says Ben Rogers, “what’s  line of business of this Gang?”

“Nothing only robbery  murder,” Tom said.

“But who are we going to rob?--houses, or cattle, or--”

“Stuff! stealing cattle  such things ain’t robbery; it’s burglary,”
 says Tom Sawyer.  “We ain’t burglars.  That ain’t no sort of style.  We
are highwaymen.  We stop stages  carriages on  road, with masks
on,  kill  people  take ir watches  money.”

“Must we always kill  people?”

“Oh, certainly.  It’s best.  Some authorities think different, but
mostly it’s considered best to kill m--except some that you bring to
 cave here,  keep m till y’re ransomed.”

“Ransomed?  What’s that?”

“I don’t know.  But that’s what y do.  I’ve seen it in books;  so
of course that’s what we’ve got to do.”

“But how can we do it if we don’t know what it is?”

“Why, blame it all, we’ve _got_ to do it.  Don’t I tell you it’s in 
books?  Do you want to go to doing different from what’s in  books,
 get things all muddled up?”

“Oh, that’s all very fine to _say_, Tom Sawyer, but how in  nation
are se fellows going to be ransomed if we don’t know how to do it
to m?--that’s  thing I want to get at.  Now, what do you reckon it
is?”

“Well, I don’t know.  But per’aps if we keep m till y’re ransomed,
it means that we keep m till y’re dead.”

“Now, that’s something _like_.  That’ll answer.  Why couldn’t you said
that before?  We’ll keep m till y’re ransomed to death;  a
borsome lot y’ll be, too--eating up everything,  always trying
to get loose.”

“How you talk, Ben Rogers.  How can y get loose when re’s a guard
over m, ready to shoot m down if y move a peg?”

“A guard!  Well, that _is_ good.  So somebody’s got to set up all night
 never get any sleep, just so as to watch m.  I think that’s
foolishness. Why can’t a body take a club  ransom m as soon as
y get here?”

“Because it ain’t in  books so--that’s why.  Now, Ben Rogers, do you
want to do things regular, or don’t you?--that’s  idea.  Don’t you
reckon that  people that made  books knows what’s  correct
thing to do?  Do you reckon _you_ can learn ‘em anything?  Not by a good
deal. No, sir, we’ll just go on  ransom m in  regular way.”

“All right.  I don’t mind; but I say it’s a fool way, anyhow.  Say, do
we kill  women, too?”

“Well, Ben Rogers, if I was as ignorant as you I wouldn’t let on.  Kill
 women?  No; nobody ever saw anything in  books like that.  You
fetch m to  cave,  you’re always as polite as pie to m;
 by  by y fall in love with you,  never want to go home any
more.”

“Well, if that’s  way I’m agreed, but I don’t take no stock in it.
Mighty soon we’ll have  cave so cluttered up with women,  fellows
waiting to be ransomed, that re won’t be no place for  robbers.
But go ahead, I ain’t got nothing to say.”

Little Tommy Barnes was asleep now,  when y waked him up he was
scared,  cried,  said he wanted to go home to his ma,  didn’t
want to be a robber any more.

So y all made fun of him,  called him cry-baby,  that made him
mad,  he said he would go straight  tell all  secrets.  But
Tom give him five cents to keep quiet,  said we would all go home 
meet next week,  rob somebody  kill some people.

Ben Rogers said he couldn’t get out much, only Sundays,  so he wanted
to begin next Sunday; but all  boys said it would be wicked to do it
on Sunday,  that settled  thing.  y agreed to get toger 
fix a day as soon as y could,  n we elected Tom Sawyer first
captain  Jo Harper second captain of  Gang,  so started home.

I clumb up  shed  crept into my window just before day was
breaking. My new clos was all greased up  clayey,  I was
dog-tired.




CHAPTER III.

WELL, I got a good going-over in  morning from old Miss Watson on
account of my clos; but  widow she didn’t scold, but only cleaned
off  grease  clay,  looked so sorry that I thought I would
behave awhile if I could.  n Miss Watson she took me in  closet
 prayed, but nothing come of it.  She told me to pray every day, 
whatever I asked for I would get it.  But it warn’t so.  I tried it.
Once I got a fish-line, but no hooks.  It warn’t any good to me without
hooks.  I tried for  hooks three or four times, but somehow I
couldn’t make it work.  By  by, one day, I asked Miss Watson to
try for me, but she said I was a fool.  She never told me why,  I
couldn’t make it out no way.

I set down one time back in  woods,  had a long think about it.
 I says to myself, if a body can get anything y pray for, why don’t
Deacon Winn get back  money he lost on pork?  Why can’t  widow get
back her silver snuffbox that was stole?  Why can’t Miss Watson fat up?
No, says I to my self, re ain’t nothing in it.  I went  told 
widow about it,  she said  thing a body could get by praying for
it was “spiritual gifts.”  This was too many for me, but she told me
what she meant--I must help or people,  do everything I could for
or people,  look out for m all  time,  never think about
myself. This was including Miss Watson, as I took it.  I went out in 
woods  turned it over in my mind a long time, but I couldn’t see no
advantage about it--except for  or people; so at last I reckoned
I wouldn’t worry about it any more, but just let it go.  Sometimes 
widow would take me one side  talk about Providence in a way to make
a body’s mouth water; but maybe next day Miss Watson would take hold
 knock it all down again.  I judged I could see that re was two
Providences,  a poor chap would st considerable show with 
widow’s Providence, but if Miss Watson’s got him re warn’t no help
for him any more.  I thought it all out,  reckoned I would belong
to  widow’s if he wanted me, though I couldn’t make out how he was
a-going to be any better off n than what he was before, seeing I was
so ignorant,  so kind of low-down  ornery.

Pap he hadn’t been seen for more than a year,  that was comfortable
for me; I didn’t want to see him no more.  He used to always whale me
when he was sober  could get his hs on me; though I used to take
to  woods most of  time when he was around.  Well, about this time
he was found in  river drownded, about twelve mile above town, so
people said.  y judged it was him, anyway; said this drownded man was
just his size,  was ragged,  had uncommon long hair, which was all
like pap; but y couldn’t make nothing out of  face, because it had
been in  water so long it warn’t much like a face at all.  y said
he was floating on his back in  water.  y took him  buried him
on  bank.  But I warn’t comfortable long, because I happened to think
of something.  I knowed mighty well that a drownded man don’t float on
his back, but on his face.  So I knowed, n, that this warn’t pap, but
a woman dressed up in a man’s clos.  So I was uncomfortable again.
 I judged  old man would turn up again by  by, though I wished he
wouldn’t.

We played robber now  n about a month,  n I resigned.  All
 boys did.  We hadn’t robbed nobody, hadn’t killed any people, but
only just pretended.  We used to hop out of  woods  go charging
down on hog-drivers  women in carts taking garden stuff to market,
but we never hived any of m.  Tom Sawyer called  hogs “ingots,”
  he called  turnips  stuff “julery,”  we would go to 
cave  powwow over what we had done,  how many people we had killed
 marked.  But I couldn’t see no profit in it.  One time Tom sent a
boy to run about town with a blazing stick, which he called a slogan
(which was  sign for  Gang to get toger),  n he said he
had got secret news by his spies that next day a whole parcel of Spanish
merchants  rich A-rabs was going to camp in Cave Hollow with two
hundred elephants,  six hundred camels,  over a thous “sumter”
 mules, all loaded down with di’monds,  y didn’t have only a guard
of four hundred soldiers,  so we would lay in ambuscade, as he called
it,  kill  lot  scoop  things.  He said we must slick up
our swords  guns,  get ready.  He never could go after even a
turnip-cart but he must have  swords  guns all scoured up for it,
though y was only lath  broomsticks,  you might scour at m
till you rotted,  n y warn’t worth a mouthful of ashes more
than what y was before.  I didn’t believe we could lick such a crowd
of Spaniards  A-rabs, but I wanted to see  camels  elephants,
so I was on h next day, Saturday, in  ambuscade;  when we got
 word we rushed out of  woods  down  hill.  But re warn’t
no Spaniards  A-rabs,  re warn’t no camels nor no elephants.
 It warn’t anything but a Sunday-school picnic,  only a primer-class
at that.  We busted it up,  chased  children up  hollow; but we
never got anything but some doughnuts  jam, though Ben Rogers got
a rag doll,  Jo Harper got a hymn-book  a tract;  n 
teacher charged in,  made us drop everything  cut.

 I didn’t see no di’monds,  I told Tom Sawyer so.  He said re was
loads of m re, anyway;  he said re was A-rabs re, too,
 elephants  things.  I said, why couldn’t we see m, n?  He
said if I warn’t so ignorant, but had read a book called Don Quixote, I
would know without asking.  He said it was all done by enchantment.  He
said re was hundreds of soldiers re,  elephants  treasure,
 so on, but we had enemies which he called magicians;  y had
turned  whole thing into an infant Sunday-school, just out of spite.
 I said, all right; n  thing for us to do was to go for 
magicians.  Tom Sawyer said I was a numskull.

“Why,” said he, “a magician could call up a lot of genies,  y
would hash you up like nothing before you could say Jack Robinson.  y
are as tall as a tree  as big around as a church.”

“Well,” I says, “s’pose we got some genies to help _us_--can’t we lick
 or crowd n?”

“How you going to get m?”

“I don’t know.  How do _y_ get m?”

“Why, y rub an old tin lamp or an iron ring,  n  genies
come tearing in, with  thunder  lightning a-ripping around  
smoke a-rolling,  everything y’re told to do y up  do it.
 y don’t think nothing of pulling a shot-tower up by  roots, 
belting a Sunday-school superintendent over  head with it--or any
or man.”

“Who makes m tear around so?”

“Why, whoever rubs  lamp or  ring.  y belong to whoever rubs
 lamp or  ring,  y’ve got to do whatever he says.  If he
tells m to build a palace forty miles long out of di’monds,  fill
it full of chewing-gum, or whatever you want,  fetch an emperor’s
daughter from China for you to marry, y’ve got to do it-- y’ve
got to do it before sun-up next morning, too.   more:  y’ve got
to waltz that palace around over  country wherever you want it, you
underst.”

“Well,” says I, “I think y are a pack of flat-heads for not keeping
 palace mselves ‘stead of fooling m away like that.   what’s
more--if I was one of m I would see a man in Jericho before I would
drop my business  come to him for  rubbing of an old tin lamp.”

“How you talk, Huck Finn.  Why, you’d _have_ to come when he rubbed it,
wher you wanted to or not.”

“What!  I as high as a tree  as big as a church?  All right, n;
I _would_ come; but I lay I’d make that man climb  highest tree re
was in  country.”

“Shucks, it ain’t no use to talk to you, Huck Finn.  You don’t seem to
know anything, somehow--perfect saphead.”

I thought all this over for two or three days,  n I reckoned I
would see if re was anything in it.  I got an old tin lamp  an
iron ring,  went out in  woods  rubbed  rubbed till I sweat
like an Injun, calculating to build a palace  sell it; but it warn’t
no use, none of  genies come.  So n I judged that all that stuff
was only just one of Tom Sawyer’s lies.  I reckoned he believed in 
A-rabs   elephants, but as for me I think different.  It had all
 marks of a Sunday-school.




CHAPTER IV.

WELL, three or four months run along,  it was well into  winter
now. I had been to school most all  time  could spell  read 
write just a little,  could say  multiplication table up to six
times seven is thirty-five,  I don’t reckon I could ever get any
furr than that if I was to live forever.  I don’t take no stock in
mamatics, anyway.

At first I hated  school, but by  by I got so I could st it.
Whenever I got uncommon tired I played hookey,   hiding I got next
day done me good  cheered me up.  So  longer I went to school 
easier it got to be.  I was getting sort of used to  widow’s ways,
too,  y warn’t so raspy on me.  Living in a house  sleeping in
a bed pulled on me pretty tight mostly, but before  cold wear I
used to slide out  sleep in  woods sometimes,  so that was a
rest to me.  I liked  old ways best, but I was getting so I liked 
new ones, too, a little bit.  widow said I was coming along slow but
sure,  doing very satisfactory.  She said she warn’t ashamed of me.

One morning I happened to turn over  salt-cellar at breakfast.
 I reached for some of it as quick as I could to throw over my left
shoulder  keep off  bad luck, but Miss Watson was in ahead of me,
 crossed me off. She says, “Take your hs away, Huckleberry; what
a mess you are always making!”   widow put in a good word for me, but
that warn’t going to keep off  bad luck, I knowed that well enough.
 I started out, after breakfast, feeling worried  shaky, 
wondering where it was going to fall on me,  what it was going to be.
 re is ways to keep off some kinds of bad luck, but this wasn’t one
of m kind; so I never tried to do anything, but just poked along
low-spirited  on  watch-out.

I went down to  front garden  clumb over  stile where you go
through  high board fence.  re was an inch of new snow on 
ground,  I seen somebody’s tracks.  y had come up from  quarry
 stood around  stile a while,  n went on around  garden
fence.  It was funny y hadn’t come in, after sting around so.  I
couldn’t make it out.  It was very curious, somehow.  I was going to
follow around, but I stooped down to look at  tracks first.  I didn’t
notice anything at first, but next I did.  re was a cross in  left
boot-heel made with big nails, to keep off  devil.

I was up in a second  shinning down  hill.  I looked over my
shoulder every now  n, but I didn’t see nobody.  I was at Judge
Thatcher’s as quick as I could get re.  He said:

“Why, my boy, you are all out of breath.  Did you come for your
interest?”

“No, sir,” I says; “is re some for me?”

“Oh, yes, a half-yearly is in last night--over a hundred  fifty
dollars.  Quite a fortune for you.  You had better let me invest it
along with your six thous, because if you take it you’ll spend it.”

“No, sir,” I says, “I don’t want to spend it.  I don’t want it at
all--nor  six thous, nur.  I want you to take it; I want to give
it to you-- six thous  all.”

He looked surprised.  He couldn’t seem to make it out.  He says:

“Why, what can you mean, my boy?”

I says, “Don’t you ask me no questions about it, please.  You’ll take
it--won’t you?”

He says:

“Well, I’m puzzled.  Is something  matter?”

“Please take it,” says I, “ don’t ask me nothing--n I won’t have to
tell no lies.”

He studied a while,  n he says:

“Oho-o!  I think I see.  You want to _sell_ all your property to me--not
give it.  That’s  correct idea.”

n he wrote something on a paper  read it over,  says:

“re; you see it says ‘for a consideration.’  That means I have bought
it of you  paid you for it.  Here’s a dollar for you.  Now you sign
it.”

So I signed it,  left.

Miss Watson’s nigger, Jim, had a hair-ball as big as your fist, which
had been took out of  fourth stomach of an ox,  he used to do
magic with it.  He said re was a spirit inside of it,  it knowed
everything.  So I went to him that night  told him pap was here
again, for I found his tracks in  snow.  What I wanted to know was,
what he was going to do,  was he going to stay?  Jim got out his
hair-ball  said something over it,  n he held it up  dropped
it on  floor.  It fell pretty solid,  only rolled about an inch.
 Jim tried it again,  n anor time,  it acted just  same.
 Jim got down on his knees,  put his ear against it  listened.
 But it warn’t no use; he said it wouldn’t talk. He said sometimes it
wouldn’t talk without money.  I told him I had an old slick counterfeit
quarter that warn’t no good because  brass showed through  silver
a little,  it wouldn’t pass nohow, even if  brass didn’t show,
because it was so slick it felt greasy,  so that would tell on it
every time.  (I reckoned I wouldn’t say nothing about  dollar I got
from  judge.) I said it was pretty bad money, but maybe  hair-ball
would take it, because maybe it wouldn’t know  difference.  Jim smelt
it  bit it  rubbed it,  said he would manage so  hair-ball
would think it was good.  He said he would split open a raw Irish potato
 stick  quarter in between  keep it re all night,  next
morning you couldn’t see no brass,  it wouldn’t feel greasy no more,
 so anybody in town would take it in a minute, let alone a hair-ball.
 Well, I knowed a potato would do that before, but I had forgot it.

Jim put  quarter under  hair-ball,  got down  listened
again. This time he said  hair-ball was all right.  He said it
would tell my whole fortune if I wanted it to.  I says, go on.  So 
hair-ball talked to Jim,  Jim told it to me.  He says:

“Yo’ ole far doan’ know yit what he’s a-gwyne to do.  Sometimes he
spec he’ll go ‘way, en den agin he spec he’ll stay.  De bes’ way is to
res’ easy en let de ole man take his own way.  Dey’s two angels hoverin’
roun’ ‘bout him.  One uv ‘em is white en shiny, en t’or one is black.
De white one gits him to go right a little while, den de black one sail
in en bust it all up.  A body can’t tell yit which one gwyne to fetch
him at de las’.  But you is all right.  You gwyne to have considable
trouble in yo’ life, en considable joy.  Sometimes you gwyne to git
hurt, en sometimes you gwyne to git sick; but every time you’s gwyne
to git well agin.  Dey’s two gals flyin’ ‘bout you in yo’ life.  One
uv ‘em’s light en t’or one is dark. One is rich en t’or is po’.
 You’s gwyne to marry de po’ one fust en de rich one by en by.  You
wants to keep ‘way fum de water as much as you kin, en don’t run no
resk, ‘kase it’s down in de bills dat you’s gwyne to git hung.”

When I lit my cle  went up to my room that night re sat pap his
own self!




CHAPTER V.

I had shut  door to.  n I turned around  re he was.  I used
to be scared of him all  time, he tanned me so much.  I reckoned I
was scared now, too; but in a minute I see I was mistaken--that is, after
 first jolt, as you may say, when my breath sort of hitched, he being
so unexpected; but right away after I see I warn’t scared of him worth
bothring about.

He was most fifty,  he looked it.  His hair was long  tangled 
greasy,  hung down,  you could see his eyes shining through
like he was behind vines.  It was all black, no gray; so was his long,
mixed-up whiskers.  re warn’t no color in his face, where his face
showed; it was white; not like anor man’s white, but a white to make
a body sick, a white to make a body’s flesh crawl--a tree-toad white, a
fish-belly white.  As for his clos--just rags, that was all.  He had
one ankle resting on t’or knee;  boot on that foot was busted, 
two of his toes stuck through,  he worked m now  n.  His hat
was laying on  floor--an old black slouch with  top caved in, like
a lid.

I stood a-looking at him; he set re a-looking at me, with his chair
tilted back a little.  I set  cle down.  I noticed  window was
up; so he had clumb in by  shed.  He kept a-looking me all over.  By
 by he says:

“Starchy clos--very.  You think you’re a good deal of a big-bug,
_don’t_ you?”

“Maybe I am, maybe I ain’t,” I says.

“Don’t you give me none o’ your lip,” says he.  “You’ve put on
considerable many frills since I been away.  I’ll take you down a peg
before I get done with you.  You’re educated, too, y say--can read 
write.  You think you’re better’n your far, now, don’t you, because
he can’t?  _I’ll_ take it out of you.  Who told you you might meddle
with such hifalut’n foolishness, hey?--who told you you could?”

“ widow.  She told me.”

“ widow, hey?-- who told  widow she could put in her shovel
about a thing that ain’t none of her business?”

“Nobody never told her.”

“Well, I’ll learn her how to meddle.   looky here--you drop that
school, you hear?  I’ll learn people to bring up a boy to put on airs
over his own far  let on to be better’n what _he_ is.  You lemme
catch you fooling around that school again, you hear?  Your mor
couldn’t read,  she couldn’t write, nur, before she died.  None
of  family couldn’t before _y_ died.  I can’t;  here you’re
a-swelling yourself up like this.  I ain’t  man to st it--you hear?
Say, lemme hear you read.”

I took up a book  begun something about General Washington  
wars. When I’d read about a half a minute, he fetched  book a whack
with his h  knocked it across  house.  He says:

“It’s so.  You can do it.  I had my doubts when you told me.  Now looky
here; you stop that putting on frills.  I won’t have it.  I’ll lay for
you, my smarty;  if I catch you about that school I’ll tan you good.
First you know you’ll get religion, too.  I never see such a son.”

He took up a little blue  yaller picture of some cows  a boy, 
says:

“What’s this?”

“It’s something y give me for learning my lessons good.”

He tore it up,  says:

“I’ll give you something better--I’ll give you a cowhide.”

He set re a-mumbling  a-growling a minute,  n he says:

“_Ain’t_ you a sweet-scented dy, though?  A bed;  bedclos; 
a look’n’-glass;  a piece of carpet on  floor-- your own far
got to sleep with  hogs in  tanyard.  I never see such a son.  I
bet I’ll take some o’ se frills out o’ you before I’m done with you.
Why, re ain’t no end to your airs--y say you’re rich.  Hey?--how’s
that?”

“y lie--that’s how.”

“Looky here--mind how you talk to me; I’m a-sting about all I can
st now--so don’t gimme no sass.  I’ve been in town two days,  I
hain’t heard nothing but about you bein’ rich.  I heard about it
away down  river, too.  That’s why I come.  You git me that money
to-morrow--I want it.”

“I hain’t got no money.”

“It’s a lie.  Judge Thatcher’s got it.  You git it.  I want it.”

“I hain’t got no money, I tell you.  You ask Judge Thatcher; he’ll tell
you  same.”

“All right.  I’ll ask him;  I’ll make him pungle, too, or I’ll know
 reason why.  Say, how much you got in your pocket?  I want it.”

“I hain’t got only a dollar,  I want that to--”

“It don’t make no difference what you want it for--you just shell it
out.”

He took it  bit it to see if it was good,  n he said he was
going down town to get some whisky; said he hadn’t had a drink all day.
When he had got out on  shed he put his head in again,  cussed
me for putting on frills  trying to be better than him;  when I
reckoned he was gone he come back  put his head in again,  told me
to mind about that school, because he was going to lay for me  lick
me if I didn’t drop that.

Next day he was drunk,  he went to Judge Thatcher’s  bullyragged
him,  tried to make him give up  money; but he couldn’t,  n
he swore he’d make  law force him.

 judge   widow went to law to get  court to take me away
from him  let one of m be my guardian; but it was a new judge that
had just come,  he didn’t know  old man; so he said courts mustn’t
interfere  separate families if y could help it; said he’d drur
not take a child away from its far.  So Judge Thatcher   widow
had to quit on  business.

That pleased  old man till he couldn’t rest.  He said he’d cowhide
me till I was black  blue if I didn’t raise some money for him.  I
borrowed three dollars from Judge Thatcher,  pap took it  got
drunk,  went a-blowing around  cussing  whooping  carrying
on;  he kept it up all over town, with a tin pan, till most midnight;
n y jailed him,  next day y had him before court,  jailed
him again for a week.  But he said _he_ was satisfied; said he was boss
of his son,  he’d make it warm for _him_.

When he got out  new judge said he was a-going to make a man of him.
So he took him to his own house,  dressed him up clean  nice, 
had him to breakfast  dinner  supper with  family,  was just
old pie to him, so to speak.   after supper he talked to him about
temperance  such things till  old man cried,  said he’d been
a fool,  fooled away his life; but now he was a-going to turn over
a new leaf  be a man nobody wouldn’t be ashamed of,  he hoped 
judge would help him  not look down on him.   judge said he could
hug him for m words; so he cried,  his wife she cried again; pap
said he’d been a man that had always been misunderstood before,  
judge said he believed it.   old man said that what a man wanted
that was down was sympathy,   judge said it was so; so y cried
again.   when it was bedtime  old man rose up  held out his
h,  says:

“Look at it, gentlemen  ladies all; take a-hold of it; shake it.
re’s a h that was  h of a hog; but it ain’t so no more; it’s
 h of a man that’s started in on a new life, ’ll die before
he’ll go back.  You mark m words--don’t forget I said m.  It’s a
clean h now; shake it--don’t be afeard.”

So y shook it, one after  or, all around,  cried.  
judge’s wife she kissed it.  n  old man he signed a pledge--made
his mark.  judge said it was  holiest time on record, or something
like that. n y tucked  old man into a beautiful room, which was
 spare room,  in  night some time he got powerful thirsty 
clumb out on to  porch-roof  slid down a stanchion  traded his
new coat for a jug of forty-rod,  clumb back again  had a good old
time;  towards daylight he crawled out again, drunk as a fiddler, 
rolled off  porch  broke his left arm in two places,  was most
froze to death when somebody found him after sun-up.   when y come
to look at that spare room y had to take soundings before y could
navigate it.

 judge he felt kind of sore.  He said he reckoned a body could reform
 old man with a shotgun, maybe, but he didn’t know no or way.




CHAPTER VI.

WELL, pretty soon  old man was up  around again,  n he went
for Judge Thatcher in  courts to make him give up that money,  he
went for me, too, for not stopping school.  He catched me a couple of
times  thrashed me, but I went to school just  same,  dodged
him or outrun him most of  time.  I didn’t want to go to school much
before, but I reckoned I’d go now to spite pap.  That law trial was a
slow business--appeared like y warn’t ever going to get started on it;
so every now  n I’d borrow two or three dollars off of  judge
for him, to keep from getting a cowhiding.  Every time he got money he
got drunk;  every time he got drunk he raised Cain around town; 
every time he raised Cain he got jailed.  He was just suited--this kind
of thing was right in his line.

He got to hanging around  widow’s too much  so she told him at
last that if he didn’t quit using around re she would make trouble
for him. Well, _wasn’t_ he mad?  He said he would show who was Huck
Finn’s boss.  So he watched out for me one day in  spring, 
catched me,  took me up  river about three mile in a skiff, 
crossed over to  Illinois shore where it was woody  re warn’t
no houses but an old log hut in a place where  timber was so thick
you couldn’t find it if you didn’t know where it was.

He kept me with him all  time,  I never got a chance to run off.
We lived in that old cabin,  he always locked  door  put 
key under his head nights.  He had a gun which he had stole, I reckon,
 we fished  hunted,  that was what we lived on.  Every little
while he locked me in  went down to  store, three miles, to 
ferry,  traded fish  game for whisky,  fetched it home  got
drunk  had a good time,  licked me.   widow she found out where
I was by  by,  she sent a man over to try to get hold of me; but
pap drove him off with  gun,  it warn’t long after that till I was
used to being where I was,  liked it--all but  cowhide part.

It was kind of lazy  jolly, laying off comfortable all day, smoking
 fishing,  no books nor study.  Two months or more run along, 
my clos got to be all rags  dirt,  I didn’t see how I’d ever
got to like it so well at  widow’s, where you had to wash,  eat on
a plate,  comb up,  go to bed  get up regular,  be forever
boring over a book,  have old Miss Watson pecking at you all 
time.  I didn’t want to go back no more.  I had stopped cussing, because
 widow didn’t like it; but now I took to it again because pap hadn’t
no objections.  It was pretty good times up in  woods re, take it
all around.

But by  by pap got too hy with his hick’ry,  I couldn’t st
it. I was all over welts.  He got to going away so much, too, 
locking me in.  Once he locked me in  was gone three days.  It was
dreadful lonesome.  I judged he had got drownded,  I wasn’t ever
going to get out any more.  I was scared.  I made up my mind I would fix
up some way to leave re.  I had tried to get out of that cabin many
a time, but I couldn’t find no way.  re warn’t a window to it big
enough for a dog to get through.  I couldn’t get up  chimbly; it
was too narrow.   door was thick, solid oak slabs.  Pap was pretty
careful not to leave a knife or anything in  cabin when he was away;
I reckon I had hunted  place over as much as a hundred times; well, I
was most all  time at it, because it was about  only way to put in
 time.  But this time I found something at last; I found an old rusty
wood-saw without any hle; it was laid in between a rafter  
clapboards of  roof. I greased it up  went to work.  re was an
old horse-blanket nailed against  logs at  far end of  cabin
behind  table, to keep  wind from blowing through  chinks 
putting  cle out.  I got under  table  raised  blanket,
 went to work to saw a section of  big bottom log out--big enough
to let me through.  Well, it was a good long job, but I was getting
towards  end of it when I heard pap’s gun in  woods.  I got rid of
 signs of my work,  dropped  blanket  hid my saw,  pretty
soon pap come in.

Pap warn’t in a good humor--so he was his natural self.  He said he was
down town,  everything was going wrong.  His lawyer said he reckoned
he would win his lawsuit  get  money if y ever got started on
 trial; but n re was ways to put it off a long time,  Judge
Thatcher knowed how to do it.  he said people allowed re’d be
anor trial to get me away from him  give me to  widow for my
guardian,  y guessed it would win this time.  This shook me up
considerable, because I didn’t want to go back to  widow’s any more
 be so cramped up  sivilized, as y called it.  n  old man
got to cussing,  cussed everything  everybody he could think of,
 n cussed m all over again to make sure he hadn’t skipped any,
 after that he polished off with a kind of a general cuss all round,
including a considerable parcel of people which he didn’t know  names
of,  so called m what’s-his-name when he got to m,  went
right along with his cussing.

He said he would like to see  widow get me.  He said he would watch
out,  if y tried to come any such game on him he knowed of a place
six or seven mile off to stow me in, where y might hunt till y
dropped  y couldn’t find me.  That made me pretty uneasy again,
but only for a minute; I reckoned I wouldn’t stay on h till he got
that chance.

 old man made me go to  skiff  fetch  things he had
got. re was a fifty-pound sack of corn meal,  a side of bacon,
ammunition,  a four-gallon jug of whisky,  an old book  two
newspapers for wadding, besides some tow.  I toted up a load,  went
back  set down on  bow of  skiff to rest.  I thought it all
over,  I reckoned I would walk off with  gun  some lines, 
take to  woods when I run away.  I guessed I wouldn’t stay in one
place, but just tramp right across  country, mostly night times, 
hunt  fish to keep alive,  so get so far away that  old man nor
 widow couldn’t ever find me any more.  I judged I would saw out 
leave that night if pap got drunk enough,  I reckoned he would.  I
got so full of it I didn’t notice how long I was staying till  old
man hollered  asked me wher I was asleep or drownded.

I got  things all up to  cabin,  n it was about dark.  While
I was cooking supper  old man took a swig or two  got sort of
warmed up,  went to ripping again.  He had been drunk over in town,
 laid in  gutter all night,  he was a sight to look at.  A body
would a thought he was Adam--he was just all mud.  Whenever his liquor
begun to work he most always went for  govment, this time he says:

“Call this a govment! why, just look at it  see what it’s like.
Here’s  law a-sting ready to take a man’s son away from him--a
man’s own son, which he has had all  trouble  all  anxiety
 all  expense of raising.  Yes, just as that man has got that
son raised at last,  ready to go to work  begin to do suthin’ for
_him_  give him a rest,  law up  goes for him.   y call
_that_ govment!  That ain’t all, nur.   law backs that old Judge
Thatcher up  helps him to keep me out o’ my property.  Here’s what
 law does:   law takes a man worth six thous dollars 
up’ards,  jams him into an old trap of a cabin like this,  lets
him go round in clos that ain’t fitten for a hog. y call that
govment!  A man can’t get his rights in a govment like this. Sometimes
I’ve a mighty notion to just leave  country for good  all. Yes,
 I _told_ ‘em so; I told old Thatcher so to his face.  Lots of ‘em
heard me,  can tell what I said.  Says I, for two cents I’d leave 
blamed country  never come a-near it agin.  m’s  very words.  I
says look at my hat--if you call it a hat--but  lid raises up  
rest of it goes down till it’s below my chin,  n it ain’t rightly
a hat at all, but more like my head was shoved up through a jint o’
stove-pipe.  Look at it, says I--such a hat for me to wear--one of 
wealthiest men in this town if I could git my rights.

“Oh, yes, this is a wonderful govment, wonderful.  Why, looky here.
re was a free nigger re from Ohio--a mulatter, most as white as
a white man.  He had  whitest shirt on you ever see, too,  
shiniest hat;  re ain’t a man in that town that’s got as fine
clos as what he had;  he had a gold watch  chain,  a
silver-headed cane-- awfulest old gray-headed nabob in  State.  
what do you think?  y said he was a p’fessor in a college,  could
talk all kinds of languages,  knowed everything.   that ain’t 
wust. y said he could _vote_ when he was at home.  Well, that let me
out. Thinks I, what is  country a-coming to?  It was ‘lection day,
 I was just about to go  vote myself if I warn’t too drunk to get
re; but when y told me re was a State in this country where
y’d let that nigger vote, I drawed out.  I says I’ll never vote agin.
 m’s  very words I said; y all heard me;   country may
rot for all me--I’ll never vote agin as long as I live.   to see 
cool way of that nigger--why, he wouldn’t a give me  road if I hadn’t
shoved him out o’  way.  I says to  people, why ain’t this nigger
put up at auction  sold?--that’s what I want to know.   what do you
reckon y said? Why, y said he couldn’t be sold till he’d been in
 State six months,  he hadn’t been re that long yet.  re,
now--that’s a specimen.  y call that a govment that can’t sell a free
nigger till he’s been in  State six months.  Here’s a govment that
calls itself a govment,  lets on to be a govment,  thinks it is a
govment,  yet’s got to set stock-still for six whole months before
it can take a hold of a prowling, thieving, infernal, white-shirted free
nigger, --”

Pap was agoing on so he never noticed where his old limber legs was
taking him to, so he went head over heels over  tub of salt pork 
barked both shins,   rest of his speech was all  hottest kind
of language--mostly hove at  nigger   govment, though he give
 tub some, too, all along, here  re.  He hopped around 
cabin considerable, first on one leg  n on  or, holding
first one shin  n  or one,  at last he let out with his
left foot all of a sudden  fetched  tub a rattling kick.  But it
warn’t good judgment, because that was  boot that had a couple of his
toes leaking out of  front end of it; so now he raised a howl that
fairly made a body’s hair raise,  down he went in  dirt, 
rolled re,  held his toes;   cussing he done n laid over
anything he had ever done previous.  He said so his own self afterwards.
 He had heard old Sowberry Hagan in his best days,  he said it laid
over him, too; but I reckon that was sort of piling it on, maybe.

After supper pap took  jug,  said he had enough whisky re
for two drunks  one delirium tremens.  That was always his word.  I
judged he would be blind drunk in about an hour,  n I would steal
 key, or saw myself out, one or t’or.  He drank  drank, 
tumbled down on his blankets by  by; but luck didn’t run my way.
 He didn’t go sound asleep, but was uneasy.  He groaned  moaned 
thrashed around this way  that for a long time.  At last I got so
sleepy I couldn’t keep my eyes open all I could do,  so before I
knowed what I was about I was sound asleep,   cle burning.

I don’t know how long I was asleep, but all of a sudden re was an
awful scream  I was up.  re was pap looking wild,  skipping
around every which way  yelling about snakes.  He said y was
crawling up his legs;  n he would give a jump  scream,  say
one had bit him on  cheek--but I couldn’t see no snakes.  He started
 run round  round  cabin, hollering “Take him off! take him
off! he’s biting me on  neck!”  I never see a man look so wild in 
eyes. Pretty soon he was all fagged out,  fell down panting; n he
rolled over  over wonderful fast, kicking things every which way,
 striking  grabbing at  air with his hs,  screaming 
saying re was devils a-hold of him.  He wore out by  by,  laid
still a while, moaning.  n he laid stiller,  didn’t make a sound.
 I could hear  owls   wolves away off in  woods,  it
seemed terrible still.  He was laying over by  corner. By  by he
raised up part way  listened, with his head to one side.  He says,
very low:

“Tramp--tramp--tramp; that’s  dead; tramp--tramp--tramp; y’re coming
after me; but I won’t go.  Oh, y’re here! don’t touch me--don’t! hs
off--y’re cold; let go.  Oh, let a poor devil alone!”

n he went down on all fours  crawled off, begging m to let him
alone,  he rolled himself up in his blanket  wallowed in under 
old pine table, still a-begging;  n he went to crying.  I could
hear him through  blanket.

By  by he rolled out  jumped up on his feet looking wild,  he
see me  went for me.  He chased me round  round  place with a
clasp-knife, calling me  Angel of Death,  saying he would kill me,
 n I couldn’t come for him no more.  I begged,  told him I
was only Huck; but he laughed _such_ a screechy laugh,  roared 
cussed,  kept on chasing me up.  Once when I turned short 
dodged under his arm he made a grab  got me by  jacket between my
shoulders,  I thought I was gone; but I slid out of  jacket quick
as lightning,  saved myself. Pretty soon he was all tired out, 
dropped down with his back against  door,  said he would rest a
minute  n kill me. He put his knife under him,  said he would
sleep  get strong,  n he would see who was who.

So he dozed off pretty soon.  By  by I got  old split-bottom chair
 clumb up as easy as I could, not to make any noise,  got down 
gun.  I slipped  ramrod down it to make sure it was loaded, n I
laid it across  turnip barrel, pointing towards pap,  set down
behind it to wait for him to stir.   how slow  still  time did
drag along.




CHAPTER VII.

“GIT up!  What you ‘bout?”

I opened my eyes  looked around, trying to make out where I was.  It
was after sun-up,  I had been sound asleep.  Pap was sting over me
looking sour  sick, too.  He says:

“What you doin’ with this gun?”

I judged he didn’t know nothing about what he had been doing, so I says:

“Somebody tried to get in, so I was laying for him.”

“Why didn’t you roust me out?”

“Well, I tried to, but I couldn’t; I couldn’t budge you.”

“Well, all right.  Don’t st re palavering all day, but out with
you  see if re’s a fish on  lines for breakfast.  I’ll be along
in a minute.”

He unlocked  door,  I cleared out up  river-bank.  I noticed
some pieces of limbs  such things floating down,  a sprinkling of
bark; so I knowed  river had begun to rise.  I reckoned I would have
great times now if I was over at  town.   June rise used to be
always luck for me; because as soon as that rise begins here comes
cordwood floating down,  pieces of log rafts--sometimes a dozen logs
toger; so all you have to do is to catch m  sell m to 
wood-yards   sawmill.

I went along up  bank with one eye out for pap  t’or one out
for what  rise might fetch along.  Well, all at once here comes a
canoe; just a beauty, too, about thirteen or fourteen foot long, riding
high like a duck.  I shot head-first off of  bank like a frog,
clos  all on,  struck out for  canoe.  I just expected
re’d be somebody laying down in it, because people often done that
to fool folks,  when a chap had pulled a skiff out most to it y’d
raise up  laugh at him.  But it warn’t so this time.  It was a
drift-canoe sure enough,  I clumb in  paddled her ashore.  Thinks
I,  old man will be glad when he sees this--she’s worth ten dollars.
 But when I got to shore pap wasn’t in sight yet,  as I was running
her into a little creek like a gully, all hung over with vines 
willows, I struck anor idea:  I judged I’d hide her good,  n,
‘stead of taking to  woods when I run off, I’d go down  river
about fifty mile  camp in one place for good,  not have such a
rough time tramping on foot.

It was pretty close to  shanty,  I thought I heard  old man
coming all  time; but I got her hid;  n I out  looked around
a bunch of willows,  re was  old man down  path a piece just
drawing a bead on a bird with his gun.  So he hadn’t seen anything.

When he got along I was hard at it taking up a “trot” line.  He abused
me a little for being so slow; but I told him I fell in  river, 
that was what made me so long.  I knowed he would see I was wet, 
n he would be asking questions.  We got five catfish off  lines
 went home.

While we laid off after breakfast to sleep up, both of us being about
wore out, I got to thinking that if I could fix up some way to keep pap
  widow from trying to follow me, it would be a certainer thing
than trusting to luck to get far enough off before y missed me; you
see, all kinds of things might happen.  Well, I didn’t see no way for a
while, but by  by pap raised up a minute to drink anor barrel of
water,  he says:

“Anor time a man comes a-prowling round here you roust me out, you
hear? That man warn’t here for no good.  I’d a shot him.  Next time you
roust me out, you hear?”

n he dropped down  went to sleep again; but what he had been
saying give me  very idea I wanted.  I says to myself, I can fix it
now so nobody won’t think of following me.

About twelve o’clock we turned out  went along up  bank.  
river was coming up pretty fast,  lots of driftwood going by on 
rise. By  by along comes part of a log raft--nine logs fast toger.
 We went out with  skiff  towed it ashore.  n we had dinner.
Anybody but pap would a waited  seen  day through, so as to catch
more stuff; but that warn’t pap’s style.  Nine logs was enough for one
time; he must shove right over to town  sell.  So he locked me in 
took  skiff,  started off towing  raft about half-past three.
 I judged he wouldn’t come back that night.  I waited till I reckoned he
had got a good start; n I out with my saw,  went to work on that
log again.  Before he was t’or side of  river I was out of 
hole; him  his raft was just a speck on  water away off yonder.

I took  sack of corn meal  took it to where  canoe was hid, 
shoved  vines  branches apart  put it in; n I done  same
with  side of bacon; n  whisky-jug.  I took all  coffee 
sugar re was,  all  ammunition; I took  wadding; I took 
bucket  gourd; I took a dipper  a tin cup,  my old saw  two
blankets,   skillet   coffee-pot.  I took fish-lines 
matches  or things--everything that was worth a cent.  I cleaned
out  place.  I wanted an axe, but re wasn’t any, only  one out
at  woodpile,  I knowed why I was going to leave that.  I fetched
out  gun,  now I was done.

I had wore  ground a good deal crawling out of  hole  dragging
out so many things.  So I fixed that as good as I could from  outside
by scattering dust on  place, which covered up  smoothness  
sawdust.  n I fixed  piece of log back into its place,  put two
rocks under it  one against it to hold it re, for it was bent up
at that place  didn’t quite touch ground.  If you stood four or five
foot away  didn’t know it was sawed, you wouldn’t never notice
it;  besides, this was  back of  cabin,  it warn’t likely
anybody would go fooling around re.

It was all grass clear to  canoe, so I hadn’t left a track.  I
followed around to see.  I stood on  bank  looked out over 
river.  All safe.  So I took  gun  went up a piece into  woods,
 was hunting around for some birds when I see a wild pig; hogs soon
went wild in m bottoms after y had got away from  prairie
farms. I shot this fellow  took him into camp.

I took  axe  smashed in  door.  I beat it  hacked it
considerable a-doing it.  I fetched  pig in,  took him back nearly
to  table  hacked into his throat with  axe,  laid him down
on  ground to bleed; I say ground because it was ground--hard packed,
 no boards.  Well, next I took an old sack  put a lot of big rocks
in it--all I could drag-- I started it from  pig,  dragged it to
 door  through  woods down to  river  dumped it in, 
down it sunk, out of sight.  You could easy see that something had been
dragged over  ground.  I did wish Tom Sawyer was re; I knowed he
would take an interest in this kind of business,  throw in  fancy
touches.  Nobody could spread himself like Tom Sawyer in such a thing as
that.

Well, last I pulled out some of my hair,  blooded  axe good, 
stuck it on  back side,  slung  axe in  corner.  n I
took up  pig  held him to my breast with my jacket (so he couldn’t
drip) till I got a good piece below  house  n dumped him into
 river.  Now I thought of something else.  So I went  got  bag
of meal  my old saw out of  canoe,  fetched m to  house.
 I took  bag to where it used to st,  ripped a hole in 
bottom of it with  saw, for re warn’t no knives  forks on 
place--pap done everything with his clasp-knife about  cooking.  n
I carried  sack about a hundred yards across  grass  through
 willows east of  house, to a shallow lake that was five mile wide
 full of rushes-- ducks too, you might say, in  season.  re
was a slough or a creek leading out of it on  or side that went
miles away, I don’t know where, but it didn’t go to  river.   meal
sifted out  made a little track all  way to  lake.  I dropped
pap’s whetstone re too, so as to look like it had been done by
accident. n I tied up  rip in  meal sack with a string, so it
wouldn’t leak no more,  took it  my saw to  canoe again.

It was about dark now; so I dropped  canoe down  river under some
willows that hung over  bank,  waited for  moon to rise.  I
made fast to a willow; n I took a bite to eat,  by  by laid
down in  canoe to smoke a pipe  lay out a plan.  I says to myself,
y’ll follow  track of that sackful of rocks to  shore  n
drag  river for me.   y’ll follow that meal track to  lake
 go browsing down  creek that leads out of it to find  robbers
that killed me  took  things.  y won’t ever hunt  river for
anything but my dead carcass. y’ll soon get tired of that,  won’t
bor no more about me.  All right; I can stop anywhere I want to.
Jackson’s Isl is good enough for me; I know that isl pretty well,
 nobody ever comes re.   n I can paddle over to town nights,
 slink around  pick up things I want. Jackson’s Isl’s 
place.

I was pretty tired,   first thing I knowed I was asleep.  When
I woke up I didn’t know where I was for a minute.  I set up  looked
around, a little scared.  n I remembered.   river looked miles 
miles across.   moon was so bright I could a counted  drift logs
that went a-slipping along, black  still, hundreds of yards out from
shore. Everything was dead quiet,  it looked late,  _smelt_ late.
You know what I mean--I don’t know  words to put it in.

I took a good gap  a stretch,  was just going to unhitch  start
when I heard a sound away over  water.  I listened.  Pretty soon I
made it out.  It was that dull kind of a regular sound that comes from
oars working in rowlocks when it’s a still night.  I peeped out through
 willow branches,  re it was--a skiff, away across  water.
 I couldn’t tell how many was in it.  It kept a-coming,  when it was
abreast of me I see re warn’t but one man in it.  Think’s I, maybe
it’s pap, though I warn’t expecting him.  He dropped below me with 
current,  by  by he came a-swinging up shore in  easy water,
 he went by so close I could a reached out  gun  touched him.
 Well, it _was_ pap, sure enough-- sober, too, by  way he laid his
oars.

I didn’t lose no time.   next minute I was a-spinning down stream
soft but quick in  shade of  bank.  I made two mile  a half,
 n struck out a quarter of a mile or more towards  middle of
 river, because pretty soon I would be passing  ferry ling, 
people might see me  hail me.  I got out amongst  driftwood, 
n laid down in  bottom of  canoe  let her float.

 I laid re,  had a good rest  a smoke out of my pipe, looking
away into  sky; not a cloud in it.   sky looks ever so deep when
you lay down on your back in  moonshine; I never knowed it before.
  how far a body can hear on  water such nights!  I heard people
talking at  ferry ling. I heard what y said, too--every word
of it.  One man said it was getting towards  long days   short
nights now.  T’or one said _this_ warn’t one of  short ones, he
reckoned-- n y laughed,  he said it over again,  y
laughed again; n y waked up anor fellow  told him, 
laughed, but he didn’t laugh; he ripped out something brisk,  said
let him alone.   first fellow said he ‘lowed to tell it to his
old woman--she would think it was pretty good; but he said that warn’t
nothing to some things he had said in his time. I heard one man say it
was nearly three o’clock,  he hoped daylight wouldn’t wait more than
about a week longer.  After that  talk got furr  furr away,
 I couldn’t make out  words any more; but I could hear  mumble,
 now  n a laugh, too, but it seemed a long ways off.

I was away below  ferry now.  I rose up,  re was Jackson’s
Isl, about two mile  a half down stream, heavy timbered 
sting up out of  middle of  river, big  dark  solid, like
a steamboat without any lights.  re warn’t any signs of  bar at
 head--it was all under water now.

It didn’t take me long to get re.  I shot past  head at a ripping
rate,  current was so swift,  n I got into  dead water 
led on  side towards  Illinois shore.  I run  canoe into
a deep dent in  bank that I knowed about; I had to part  willow
branches to get in;  when I made fast nobody could a seen  canoe
from  outside.

I went up  set down on a log at  head of  isl,  looked
out on  big river   black driftwood  away over to  town,
three mile away, where re was three or four lights twinkling.  A
monstrous big lumber-raft was about a mile up stream, coming along down,
with a lantern in  middle of it.  I watched it come creeping down,
 when it was most abreast of where I stood I heard a man say, “Stern
oars, re! heave her head to stabboard!”  I heard that just as plain
as if  man was by my side.

re was a little gray in  sky now; so I stepped into  woods, 
laid down for a nap before breakfast.




CHAPTER VIII.

 sun was up so high when I waked that I judged it was after eight
o’clock.  I laid re in  grass   cool shade thinking about
things,  feeling rested  rur comfortable  satisfied.  I
could see  sun out at one or two holes, but mostly it was big trees
all about,  gloomy in re amongst m.  re was freckled places
on  ground where  light sifted down through  leaves,  
freckled places swapped about a little, showing re was a little
breeze up re.  A couple of squirrels set on a limb  jabbered at me
very friendly.

I was powerful lazy  comfortable--didn’t want to get up  cook
breakfast.  Well, I was dozing off again when I thinks I hears a deep
sound of “boom!” away up  river.  I rouses up,  rests on my elbow
 listens; pretty soon I hears it again.  I hopped up,  went 
looked out at a hole in  leaves,  I see a bunch of smoke laying
on  water a long ways up--about abreast  ferry.   re was 
ferryboat full of people floating along down.  I knowed what was 
matter now.  “Boom!” I see  white smoke squirt out of  ferryboat’s
side.  You see, y was firing cannon over  water, trying to make my
carcass come to  top.

I was pretty hungry, but it warn’t going to do for me to start a fire,
because y might see  smoke.  So I set re  watched 
cannon-smoke  listened to  boom.   river was a mile wide re,
 it always looks pretty on a summer morning--so I was having a good
enough time seeing m hunt for my remainders if I only had a bite to
eat. Well, n I happened to think how y always put quicksilver in
loaves of bread  float m off, because y always go right to 
drownded carcass  stop re.  So, says I, I’ll keep a lookout, 
if any of m’s floating around after me I’ll give m a show.  I
changed to  Illinois edge of  isl to see what luck I could
have,  I warn’t disappointed.  A big double loaf come along,  I
most got it with a long stick, but my foot slipped  she floated out
furr.  Of course I was where  current set in  closest to 
shore--I knowed enough for that.  But by  by along comes anor one,
 this time I won.  I took out  plug  shook out  little dab
of quicksilver,  set my teeth in.  It was “baker’s bread”--what 
quality eat; none of your low-down corn-pone.

I got a good place amongst  leaves,  set re on a log, munching
 bread  watching  ferry-boat,  very well satisfied.  
n something struck me.  I says, now I reckon  widow or  parson
or somebody prayed that this bread would find me,  here it has gone
 done it.  So re ain’t no doubt but re is something in that
thing--that is, re’s something in it when a body like  widow or 
parson prays, but it don’t work for me,  I reckon it don’t work for
only just  right kind.

I lit a pipe  had a good long smoke,  went on watching.  
ferryboat was floating with  current,  I allowed I’d have a chance
to see who was aboard when she come along, because she would come in
close, where  bread did.  When she’d got pretty well along down
towards me, I put out my pipe  went to where I fished out  bread,
 laid down behind a log on  bank in a little open place.  Where
 log forked I could peep through.

By  by she come along,  she drifted in so close that y could
a run out a plank  walked ashore.  Most everybody was on  boat.
 Pap,  Judge Thatcher,  Bessie Thatcher,  Jo Harper,  Tom
Sawyer,  his old Aunt Polly,  Sid  Mary,  plenty more.
 Everybody was talking about  murder, but  captain broke in 
says:

“Look sharp, now;  current sets in  closest here,  maybe he’s
washed ashore  got tangled amongst  brush at  water’s edge.  I
hope so, anyway.”

I didn’t hope so.  y all crowded up  leaned over  rails, nearly
in my face,  kept still, watching with all ir might.  I could see
m first-rate, but y couldn’t see me.  n  captain sung out:

“St away!”   cannon let off such a blast right before me that
it made me deef with  noise  pretty near blind with  smoke, 
I judged I was gone.  If y’d a had some bullets in, I reckon y’d
a got  corpse y was after.  Well, I see I warn’t hurt, thanks to
goodness.  boat floated on  went out of sight around  shoulder
of  isl.  I could hear  booming now  n, furr 
furr off,  by  by, after an hour, I didn’t hear it no more.
  isl was three mile long.  I judged y had got to  foot, 
was giving it up.  But y didn’t yet a while.  y turned around
 foot of  isl  started up  channel on  Missouri side,
under steam,  booming once in a while as y went.  I crossed over
to that side  watched m. When y got abreast  head of 
isl y quit shooting  dropped over to  Missouri shore 
went home to  town.

I knowed I was all right now.  Nobody else would come a-hunting after
me. I got my traps out of  canoe  made me a nice camp in  thick
woods.  I made a kind of a tent out of my blankets to put my things
under so  rain couldn’t get at m.  I catched a catfish  haggled
him open with my saw,  towards sundown I started my camp fire  had
supper.  n I set out a line to catch some fish for breakfast.

When it was dark I set by my camp fire smoking,  feeling pretty well
satisfied; but by  by it got sort of lonesome,  so I went  set
on  bank  listened to  current swashing along,  counted 
stars  drift logs  rafts that come down,  n went to bed;
re ain’t no better way to put in time when you are lonesome; you
can’t stay so, you soon get over it.

 so for three days  nights.  No difference--just  same thing.
But  next day I went exploring around down through  isl.  I was
boss of it; it all belonged to me, so to say,  I wanted to know
all about it; but mainly I wanted to put in  time.  I found plenty
strawberries, ripe  prime;  green summer grapes,  green
razberries;   green blackberries was just beginning to show.  y
would all come hy by  by, I judged.

Well, I went fooling along in  deep woods till I judged I warn’t
far from  foot of  isl.  I had my gun along, but I hadn’t shot
nothing; it was for protection; thought I would kill some game nigh
home. About this time I mighty near stepped on a good-sized snake,
 it went sliding off through  grass  flowers,  I after
it, trying to get a shot at it. I clipped along,  all of a sudden I
bounded right on to  ashes of a camp fire that was still smoking.

My heart jumped up amongst my lungs.  I never waited for to look
furr, but uncocked my gun  went sneaking back on my tiptoes as

thick leaves  listened, but my breath come so hard I couldn’t hear
nothing else.  I slunk along anor piece furr, n listened again;
 so on,  so on.  If I see a stump, I took it for a man; if I trod
on a stick  broke it, it made me feel like a person had cut one of my
breaths in two  I only got half,   short half, too.

When I got to camp I warn’t feeling very brash, re warn’t much s
in my craw; but I says, this ain’t no time to be fooling around.  So I
got all my traps into my canoe again so as to have m out of sight,
 I put out  fire  scattered  ashes around to look like an
old last year’s camp,  n clumb a tree.

I reckon I was up in  tree two hours; but I didn’t see nothing,
I didn’t hear nothing--I only _thought_ I heard  seen as much as a
thous things.  Well, I couldn’t stay up re forever; so at last I
got down, but I kept in  thick woods  on  lookout all 
time. All I could get to eat was berries  what was left over from
breakfast.

By  time it was night I was pretty hungry.  So when it was good
 dark I slid out from shore before moonrise  paddled over to 
Illinois bank--about a quarter of a mile.  I went out in  woods 
cooked a supper,  I had about made up my mind I would stay re
all night when I hear a _plunkety-plunk, plunkety-plunk_,  says

everything into  canoe as quick as I could,  n went creeping
through  woods to see what I could find out.  I hadn’t got far when I
hear a man say:

“We better camp here if we can find a good place;  horses is about
beat out.  Let’s look around.”

I didn’t wait, but shoved out  paddled away easy.  I tied up in 
old place,  reckoned I would sleep in  canoe.

I didn’t sleep much.  I couldn’t, somehow, for thinking.   every time
I waked up I thought somebody had me by  neck.  So  sleep didn’t
do me no good.  By  by I says to myself, I can’t live this way; I’m
a-going to find out who it is that’s here on  isl with me; I’ll
find it out or bust.  Well, I felt better right off.

So I took my paddle  slid out from shore just a step or two, 
n let  canoe drop along down amongst  shadows.   moon was
shining,  outside of  shadows it made it most as light as day.
 I poked along well on to an hour, everything still as rocks  sound
asleep. Well, by this time I was most down to  foot of  isl.  A
little ripply, cool breeze begun to blow,  that was as good as saying
 night was about done.  I give her a turn with  paddle  brung
her nose to shore; n I got my gun  slipped out  into  edge
of  woods.  I sat down re on a log,  looked out through 
leaves.  I see  moon go off watch,   darkness begin to blanket
 river. But in a little while I see a pale streak over  treetops,
 knowed  day was coming.  So I took my gun  slipped off towards
where I had run across that camp fire, stopping every minute or two
to listen.  But I hadn’t no luck somehow; I couldn’t seem to find 
place.  But by  by, sure enough, I catched a glimpse of fire away
through  trees.  I went for it, cautious  slow.  By  by I was
close enough to have a look,  re laid a man on  ground.  It
most give me  fan-tods. He had a blanket around his head,  his

about six foot of him,  kept my eyes on him steady.  It was getting
gray daylight now.  Pretty soon he gapped  stretched himself  hove
off  blanket,  it was Miss Watson’s Jim!  I bet I was glad to see
him.  I says:

“Hello, Jim!”  skipped out.

He bounced up  stared at me wild.  n he drops down on his knees,
 puts his hs toger  says:

“Doan’ hurt me--don’t!  I hain’t ever done no harm to a ghos’.  I alwuz
liked dead people, en done all I could for ‘em.  You go en git in de
river agin, whah you b’longs, en doan’ do nuffn to Ole Jim, ‘at ‘uz
awluz yo’ fren’.”

Well, I warn’t long making him underst I warn’t dead.  I was ever so
glad to see Jim.  I warn’t lonesome now.  I told him I warn’t afraid of
_him_ telling  people where I was.  I talked along, but he only set
re  looked at me; never said nothing.  n I says:

“It’s good daylight.  Le’s get breakfast.  Make up your camp fire good.”

“What’s de use er makin’ up de camp fire to cook strawbries en sich
truck? But you got a gun, hain’t you?  Den we kin git sumfn better den
strawbries.”

“Strawberries  such truck,” I says.  “Is that what you live on?”

“I couldn’ git nuffn else,” he says.

“Why, how long you been on  isl, Jim?”

“I come heah de night arter you’s killed.”

“What, all that time?”

“Yes--indeedy.”

“ ain’t you had nothing but that kind of rubbage to eat?”

“No, sah--nuffn else.”

“Well, you must be most starved, ain’t you?”

“I reck’n I could eat a hoss.  I think I could. How long you ben on de
islan’?”

“Since  night I got killed.”

“No!  W’y, what has you lived on?  But you got a gun.  Oh, yes, you got
a gun.  Dat’s good.  Now you kill sumfn en I’ll make up de fire.”

So we went over to where  canoe was,  while he built a fire in
a grassy open place amongst  trees, I fetched meal  bacon 
coffee,  coffee-pot  frying-pan,  sugar  tin cups,  
nigger was set back considerable, because he reckoned it was all done
with witchcraft. I catched a good big catfish, too,  Jim cleaned him
with his knife,  fried him.

When breakfast was ready we lolled on  grass  eat it smoking hot.
Jim laid it in with all his might, for he was most about starved.  n
when we had got pretty well stuffed, we laid off  lazied.  By  by
Jim says:

“But looky here, Huck, who wuz it dat ‘uz killed in dat shanty ef it
warn’t you?”

n I told him  whole thing,  he said it was smart.  He said Tom
Sawyer couldn’t get up no better plan than what I had.  n I says:

“How do you come to be here, Jim,  how’d you get here?”

He looked pretty uneasy,  didn’t say nothing for a minute.  n he
says:

“Maybe I better not tell.”

“Why, Jim?”

“Well, dey’s reasons.  But you wouldn’ tell on me ef I uz to tell you,
would you, Huck?”

“Blamed if I would, Jim.”

“Well, I b’lieve you, Huck.  I--_I run off_.”

“Jim!”

“But mind, you said you wouldn’ tell--you know you said you wouldn’ tell,
Huck.”

“Well, I did.  I said I wouldn’t,  I’ll stick to it.  Honest _injun_,
I will.  People would call me a low-down Abolitionist  despise me for
keeping mum--but that don’t make no difference.  I ain’t a-going to tell,
 I ain’t a-going back re, anyways.  So, now, le’s know all about
it.”

“Well, you see, it ‘uz dis way.  Ole missus--dat’s Miss Watson--she pecks
on me all de time, en treats me pooty rough, but she awluz said she
wouldn’ sell me down to Orleans.  But I noticed dey wuz a nigger trader
roun’ de place considable lately, en I begin to git oneasy.  Well, one
night I creeps to de do’ pooty late, en de do’ warn’t quite shet, en I
hear old missus tell de widder she gwyne to sell me down to Orleans, but
she didn’ want to, but she could git eight hund’d dollars for me, en it
‘uz sich a big stack o’ money she couldn’ resis’.  De widder she try to
git her to say she wouldn’ do it, but I never waited to hear de res’.  I
lit out mighty quick, I tell you.

“I tuck out en shin down de hill, en ‘spec to steal a skift ‘long de
sho’ som’ers ‘bove de town, but dey wuz people a-stirring yit, so I hid
in de ole tumble-down cooper-shop on de bank to wait for everybody to
go ‘way. Well, I wuz dah all night.  Dey wuz somebody roun’ all de time.
 ‘Long ‘bout six in de mawnin’ skifts begin to go by, en ‘bout eight er
nine every skift dat went ‘long wuz talkin’ ‘bout how yo’ pap come over
to de town en say you’s killed.  Dese las’ skifts wuz full o’ ladies en
genlmen a-goin’ over for to see de place.  Sometimes dey’d pull up at
de sho’ en take a res’ b’fo’ dey started acrost, so by de talk I got to
know all ‘bout de killin’.  I ‘uz powerful sorry you’s killed, Huck, but
I ain’t no mo’ now.

“I laid dah under de shavin’s all day.  I ‘uz hungry, but I warn’t
afeard; bekase I knowed ole missus en de widder wuz goin’ to start to
de camp-meet’n’ right arter breakfas’ en be gone all day, en dey knows
I goes off wid de cattle ‘bout daylight, so dey wouldn’ ‘spec to see me
