roun’ de place, en so dey wouldn’ miss me tell arter dark in de evenin’.
De yur servants wouldn’ miss me, kase dey’d shin out en take holiday
soon as de ole folks ‘uz out’n de way.

“Well, when it come dark I tuck out up de river road, en went ‘bout two
mile er more to whah dey warn’t no houses.  I’d made up my mine ‘bout
what I’s agwyne to do.  You see, ef I kep’ on tryin’ to git away afoot,
de dogs ‘ud track me; ef I stole a skift to cross over, dey’d miss dat
skift, you see, en dey’d know ‘bout whah I’d lan’ on de yur side, en
whah to pick up my track.  So I says, a raff is what I’s arter; it doan’
_make_ no track.

“I see a light a-comin’ roun’ de p’int bymeby, so I wade’ in en shove’
a log ahead o’ me en swum more’n half way acrost de river, en got in
‘mongst de drift-wood, en kep’ my head down low, en kinder swum agin de
current tell de raff come along.  Den I swum to de stern uv it en tuck
a-holt.  It clouded up en ‘uz pooty dark for a little while.  So I clumb
up en laid down on de planks.  De men ‘uz all ‘way yonder in de middle,
whah de lantern wuz.  De river wuz a-risin’, en dey wuz a good current;
so I reck’n’d ‘at by fo’ in de mawnin’ I’d be twenty-five mile down de
river, en den I’d slip in jis b’fo’ daylight en swim asho’, en take to
de woods on de Illinois side.

“But I didn’ have no luck.  When we ‘uz mos’ down to de head er de
islan’ a man begin to come aft wid de lantern, I see it warn’t no use
fer to wait, so I slid overboard en struck out fer de islan’.  Well, I
had a notion I could lan’ mos’ anywhers, but I couldn’t--bank too bluff.
 I ‘uz mos’ to de foot er de islan’ b’fo’ I found’ a good place.  I went
into de woods en jedged I wouldn’ fool wid raffs no mo’, long as dey
move de lantern roun’ so.  I had my pipe en a plug er dog-leg, en some
matches in my cap, en dey warn’t wet, so I ‘uz all right.”

“ so you ain’t had no meat nor bread to eat all this time?  Why
didn’t you get mud-turkles?”

“How you gwyne to git ‘m?  You can’t slip up on um en grab um; en how’s
a body gwyne to hit um wid a rock?  How could a body do it in de night?
 En I warn’t gwyne to show mysef on de bank in de daytime.”

“Well, that’s so.  You’ve had to keep in  woods all  time, of
course. Did you hear ‘em shooting  cannon?”

“Oh, yes.  I knowed dey was arter you.  I see um go by heah--watched um
thoo de bushes.”

Some young birds come along, flying a yard or two at a time 
lighting. Jim said it was a sign it was going to rain.  He said it was
a sign when young chickens flew that way,  so he reckoned it was 
same way when young birds done it.  I was going to catch some of m,
but Jim wouldn’t let me.  He said it was death.  He said his far laid
mighty sick once,  some of m catched a bird,  his old granny
said his far would die,  he did.

 Jim said you mustn’t count  things you are going to cook for
dinner, because that would bring bad luck.   same if you shook 
table-cloth after sundown.   he said if a man owned a beehive
 that man died,  bees must be told about it before sun-up next
morning, or else  bees would all weaken down  quit work  die.
 Jim said bees wouldn’t sting idiots; but I didn’t believe that, because
I had tried m lots of times myself,  y wouldn’t sting me.

I had heard about some of se things before, but not all of m.  Jim
knowed all kinds of signs.  He said he knowed most everything.  I said
it looked to me like all  signs was about bad luck,  so I asked
him if re warn’t any good-luck signs.  He says:

“Mighty few--an’ _dey_ ain’t no use to a body.  What you want to know
when good luck’s a-comin’ for?  Want to keep it off?”   he said:  “Ef
you’s got hairy arms en a hairy breas’, it’s a sign dat you’s agwyne
to be rich. Well, dey’s some use in a sign like dat, ‘kase it’s so fur
ahead. You see, maybe you’s got to be po’ a long time fust, en so you
might git discourage’ en kill yo’sef ‘f you didn’ know by de sign dat
you gwyne to be rich bymeby.”

“Have you got hairy arms  a hairy breast, Jim?”

“What’s de use to ax dat question?  Don’t you see I has?”

“Well, are you rich?”

“No, but I ben rich wunst,  gwyne to be rich agin.  Wunst I had
foteen dollars, but I tuck to specalat’n’, en got busted out.”

“What did you speculate in, Jim?”

“Well, fust I tackled stock.”

“What kind of stock?”

“Why, live stock--cattle, you know.  I put ten dollars in a cow.  But
I ain’ gwyne to resk no mo’ money in stock.  De cow up ‘n’ died on my
han’s.”

“So you lost  ten dollars.”

“No, I didn’t lose it all.  I on’y los’ ‘bout nine of it.  I sole de
hide en taller for a dollar en ten cents.”

“You had five dollars  ten cents left.  Did you speculate any more?”

“Yes.  You know that one-laigged nigger dat b’longs to old Misto
Bradish? Well, he sot up a bank, en say anybody dat put in a dollar
would git fo’ dollars mo’ at de en’ er de year.  Well, all de niggers
went in, but dey didn’t have much.  I wuz de on’y one dat had much.  So
I stuck out for mo’ dan fo’ dollars, en I said ‘f I didn’ git it I’d
start a bank mysef. Well, o’ course dat nigger want’ to keep me out er
de business, bekase he says dey warn’t business ‘nough for two banks, so
he say I could put in my five dollars en he pay me thirty-five at de en’
er de year.

“So I done it.  Den I reck’n’d I’d inves’ de thirty-five dollars right
off en keep things a-movin’.  Dey wuz a nigger name’ Bob, dat had
ketched a wood-flat, en his marster didn’ know it; en I bought it off’n
him en told him to take de thirty-five dollars when de en’ er de
year come; but somebody stole de wood-flat dat night, en nex day de
one-laigged nigger say de bank’s busted.  So dey didn’ none uv us git no
money.”

“What did you do with  ten cents, Jim?”

“Well, I ‘uz gwyne to spen’ it, but I had a dream, en de dream tole me
to give it to a nigger name’ Balum--Balum’s Ass dey call him for short;
he’s one er dem chuckleheads, you know.  But he’s lucky, dey say, en I
see I warn’t lucky.  De dream say let Balum inves’ de ten cents en he’d
make a raise for me.  Well, Balum he tuck de money, en when he wuz in
church he hear de preacher say dat whoever give to de po’ len’ to de
Lord, en boun’ to git his money back a hund’d times.  So Balum he tuck
en give de ten cents to de po’, en laid low to see what wuz gwyne to
come of it.”

“Well, what did come of it, Jim?”

“Nuffn never come of it.  I couldn’ manage to k’leck dat money no way;
en Balum he couldn’.  I ain’ gwyne to len’ no mo’ money ‘dout I see de
security.  Boun’ to git yo’ money back a hund’d times, de preacher says!
Ef I could git de ten _cents_ back, I’d call it squah, en be glad er de
chanst.”

“Well, it’s all right anyway, Jim, long as you’re going to be rich again
some time or or.”

“Yes; en I’s rich now, come to look at it.  I owns mysef, en I’s wuth
eight hund’d dollars.  I wisht I had de money, I wouldn’ want no mo’.”




CHAPTER IX.

I wanted to go  look at a place right about  middle of  isl
that I’d found when I was exploring; so we started  soon got to it,
because  isl was only three miles long  a quarter of a mile
wide.

This place was a tolerable long, steep hill or ridge about forty foot
high. We had a rough time getting to  top,  sides was so steep 
 bushes so thick.  We tramped  clumb around all over it,  by
 by found a good big cavern in  rock, most up to  top on 
side towards Illinois.   cavern was as big as two or three rooms
bunched toger,  Jim could st up straight in it.  It was cool in
re. Jim was for putting our traps in re right away, but I said we
didn’t want to be climbing up  down re all  time.

Jim said if we had  canoe hid in a good place,  had all  traps
in  cavern, we could rush re if anybody was to come to  isl,
 y would never find us without dogs.  , besides, he said m
little birds had said it was going to rain,  did I want  things to
get wet?

So we went back  got  canoe,  paddled up abreast  cavern,
 lugged all  traps up re.  n we hunted up a place close by
to hide  canoe in, amongst  thick willows.  We took some fish off
of  lines  set m again,  begun to get ready for dinner.

 door of  cavern was big enough to roll a hogshead in,  on one
side of  door  floor stuck out a little bit,  was flat  a
good place to build a fire on.  So we built it re  cooked dinner.

We spread  blankets inside for a carpet,  eat our dinner in re.
We put all  or things hy at  back of  cavern.  Pretty
soon it darkened up,  begun to thunder  lighten; so  birds was
right about it.  Directly it begun to rain,  it rained like all fury,
too,  I never see  wind blow so.  It was one of se regular
summer storms.  It would get so dark that it looked all blue-black
outside,  lovely;   rain would thrash along by so thick that
 trees off a little ways looked dim  spider-webby;  here would
come a blast of wind that would bend  trees down  turn up 
pale underside of  leaves;  n a perfect ripper of a gust would
follow along  set  branches to tossing ir arms as if y
was just wild;  next, when it was just about  bluest 
blackest--_FST_! it was as bright as glory,  you’d have a little
glimpse of tree-tops a-plunging about away off yonder in  storm,
hundreds of yards furr than you could see before; dark as sin again
in a second,  now you’d hear  thunder let go with an awful crash,
 n go rumbling, grumbling, tumbling, down  sky towards 
under side of  world, like rolling empty barrels down stairs--where
it’s long stairs  y bounce a good deal, you know.

“Jim, this is nice,” I says.  “I wouldn’t want to be nowhere else but
here. Pass me along anor hunk of fish  some hot corn-bread.”

“Well, you wouldn’t a ben here ‘f it hadn’t a ben for Jim.  You’d a ben
down dah in de woods widout any dinner, en gittn’ mos’ drownded, too;
dat you would, honey.  Chickens knows when it’s gwyne to rain, en so do
de birds, chile.”

 river went on raising  raising for ten or twelve days, till at
last it was over  banks.   water was three or four foot deep on
 isl in  low places  on  Illinois bottom.  On that side
it was a good many miles wide, but on  Missouri side it was  same
old distance across--a half a mile--because  Missouri shore was just a
wall of high bluffs.

Daytimes we paddled all over  isl in  canoe, It was mighty cool
 shady in  deep woods, even if  sun was blazing outside.  We
went winding in  out amongst  trees,  sometimes  vines hung
so thick we had to back away  go some or way.  Well, on every old
broken-down tree you could see rabbits  snakes  such things; 
when  isl had been overflowed a day or two y got so tame, on
account of being hungry, that you could paddle right up  put your
h on m if you wanted to; but not  snakes  turtles--y would
slide off in  water.   ridge our cavern was in was full of m.
We could a had pets enough if we’d wanted m.

One night we catched a little section of a lumber raft--nice pine planks.
It was twelve foot wide  about fifteen or sixteen foot long, 
 top stood above water six or seven inches--a solid, level floor.  We
could see saw-logs go by in  daylight sometimes, but we let m go;
we didn’t show ourselves in daylight.

Anor night when we was up at  head of  isl, just before
daylight, here comes a frame-house down, on  west side.  She was
a two-story,  tilted over considerable.  We paddled out  got
aboard--clumb in at an upstairs window.  But it was too dark to see yet,
so we made  canoe fast  set in her to wait for daylight.

 light begun to come before we got to  foot of  isl.  n
we looked in at  window.  We could make out a bed,  a table, 
two old chairs,  lots of things around about on  floor,  re
was clos hanging against  wall.  re was something laying on 
floor in  far corner that looked like a man.  So Jim says:

“Hello, you!”

But it didn’t budge.  So I hollered again,  n Jim says:

“De man ain’t asleep--he’s dead.  You hold still--I’ll go en see.”

He went,  bent down  looked,  says:

“It’s a dead man.  Yes, indeedy; naked, too.  He’s ben shot in de back.
I reck’n he’s ben dead two er three days.  Come in, Huck, but doan’ look
at his face--it’s too gashly.”

I didn’t look at him at all.  Jim throwed some old rags over him, but
he needn’t done it; I didn’t want to see him.  re was heaps of old
greasy cards scattered around over  floor,  old whisky bottles,
 a couple of masks made out of black cloth;  all over  walls
was  ignorantest kind of words  pictures made with charcoal.
 re was two old dirty calico dresses,  a sun-bonnet,  some
women’s underclos hanging against  wall,  some men’s clothing,
too.  We put  lot into  canoe--it might come good.  re was a
boy’s old speckled straw hat on  floor; I took that, too.   re
was a bottle that had had milk in it,  it had a rag stopper for a
baby to suck.  We would a took  bottle, but it was broke.  re was
a seedy old chest,  an old hair trunk with  hinges broke.  y
stood open, but re warn’t nothing left in m that was any account.
  way things was scattered about we reckoned  people left in a
hurry,  warn’t fixed so as to carry off most of ir stuff.

We got an old tin lantern,  a butcher-knife without any hle, 
a bran-new Barlow knife worth two bits in any store,  a lot of tallow
cles,  a tin clestick,  a gourd,  a tin cup,  a ratty
old bedquilt off  bed,  a reticule with needles  pins 
beeswax  buttons  thread  all such truck in it,  a hatchet
 some nails,  a fishline as thick as my little finger with some
monstrous hooks on it,  a roll of buckskin,  a lear dog-collar,
 a horseshoe,  some vials of medicine that didn’t have no label
on m;  just as we was leaving I found a tolerable good curry-comb,
 Jim he found a ratty old fiddle-bow,  a wooden leg.   straps
was broke off of it, but, barring that, it was a good enough leg, though
it was too long for me  not long enough for Jim,  we couldn’t find
 or one, though we hunted all around.

 so, take it all around, we made a good haul.  When we was ready to
shove off we was a quarter of a mile below  isl,  it was pretty
broad day; so I made Jim lay down in  canoe  cover up with 
quilt, because if he set up people could tell he was a nigger a good
ways off.  I paddled over to  Illinois shore,  drifted down most
a half a mile doing it.  I crept up  dead water under  bank, 
hadn’t no accidents  didn’t see nobody.  We got home all safe.




CHAPTER X.

AFTER breakfast I wanted to talk about  dead man  guess out how he
come to be killed, but Jim didn’t want to.  He said it would fetch bad
luck;  besides, he said, he might come  ha’nt us; he said a man
that warn’t buried was more likely to go a-ha’nting around than one
that was planted  comfortable.  That sounded pretty reasonable, so
I didn’t say no more; but I couldn’t keep from studying over it 
wishing I knowed who shot  man,  what y done it for.

We rummaged  clos we’d got,  found eight dollars in silver
sewed up in  lining of an old blanket overcoat.  Jim said he reckoned
 people in that house stole  coat, because if y’d a knowed 
money was re y wouldn’t a left it.  I said I reckoned y killed
him, too; but Jim didn’t want to talk about that.  I says:

“Now you think it’s bad luck; but what did you say when I fetched in 
snake-skin that I found on  top of  ridge day before yesterday?
You said it was  worst bad luck in  world to touch a snake-skin
with my hs.  Well, here’s your bad luck!  We’ve raked in all this
truck  eight dollars besides.  I wish we could have some bad luck
like this every day, Jim.”

“Never you mind, honey, never you mind.  Don’t you git too peart.  It’s
a-comin’.  Mind I tell you, it’s a-comin’.”

It did come, too.  It was a Tuesday that we had that talk.  Well, after
dinner Friday we was laying around in  grass at  upper end of 
ridge,  got out of tobacco.  I went to  cavern to get some, 
found a rattlesnake in re.  I killed him,  curled him up on 
foot of Jim’s blanket, ever so natural, thinking re’d be some fun
when Jim found him re.  Well, by night I forgot all about  snake,
 when Jim flung himself down on  blanket while I struck a light
 snake’s mate was re,  bit him.

He jumped up yelling,   first thing  light showed was 
varmint curled up  ready for anor spring.  I laid him out in a
second with a stick,  Jim grabbed pap’s whisky-jug  begun to pour
it down.

He was barefooted,   snake bit him right on  heel.  That all
comes of my being such a fool as to not remember that wherever you leave
a dead snake its mate always comes re  curls around it.  Jim told
me to chop off  snake’s head  throw it away,  n skin 
body  roast a piece of it.  I done it,  he eat it  said it
would help cure him. He made me take off  rattles  tie m around
his wrist, too.  He said that that would help.  n I slid out quiet
 throwed  snakes clear away amongst  bushes; for I warn’t going
to let Jim find out it was all my fault, not if I could help it.

Jim sucked  sucked at  jug,  now  n he got out of his
head  pitched around  yelled; but every time he come to himself he
went to sucking at  jug again.  His foot swelled up pretty big, 
so did his leg; but by  by  drunk begun to come,  so I judged
he was all right; but I’d drur been bit with a snake than pap’s
whisky.

Jim was laid up for four days  nights.  n  swelling was all
gone  he was around again.  I made up my mind I wouldn’t ever take
a-holt of a snake-skin again with my hs, now that I see what had come
of it. Jim said he reckoned I would believe him next time.   he said
that hling a snake-skin was such awful bad luck that maybe we hadn’t
got to  end of it yet.  He said he drur see  new moon over his
left shoulder as much as a thous times than take up a snake-skin
in his h.  Well, I was getting to feel that way myself, though I’ve
always reckoned that looking at  new moon over your left shoulder is
one of  carelessest  foolishest things a body can do.  Old Hank
Bunker done it once,  bragged about it;  in less than two years he
got drunk  fell off of  shot-tower,  spread himself out so
that he was just a kind of a layer, as you may say;  y slid him
edgeways between two barn doors for a coffin,  buried him so, so
y say, but I didn’t see it.  Pap told me.  But anyway it all come of
looking at  moon that way, like a fool.

Well,  days went along,   river went down between its banks
again;  about  first thing we done was to bait one of  big
hooks with a skinned rabbit  set it  catch a catfish that was
as big as a man, being six foot two inches long,  weighed over two
hundred pounds. We couldn’t hle him, of course; he would a flung us
into Illinois.  We just set re  watched him rip  tear around
till he drownded.  We found a brass button in his stomach  a round
ball,  lots of rubbage.  We split  ball open with  hatchet,
 re was a spool in it.  Jim said he’d had it re a long time, to
coat it over so  make a ball of it.  It was as big a fish as was ever
catched in  Mississippi, I reckon.  Jim said he hadn’t ever seen
a bigger one.  He would a been worth a good deal over at  village.
 y peddle out such a fish as that by  pound in  market-house
re; everybody buys some of him; his meat’s as white as snow  makes
a good fry.

Next morning I said it was getting slow  dull,  I wanted to get a
stirring up some way.  I said I reckoned I would slip over  river 
find out what was going on.  Jim liked that notion; but he said I
must go in  dark  look sharp.  n he studied it over  said,
couldn’t I put on some of m old things  dress up like a girl?
 That was a good notion, too.  So we shortened up one of  calico
gowns,  I turned up my trouser-legs to my knees  got into it.  Jim
hitched it behind with  hooks,  it was a fair fit.  I put on 
sun-bonnet  tied it under my chin,  n for a body to look in
 see my face was like looking down a joint of stove-pipe.  Jim said
nobody would know me, even in  daytime, hardly.  I practiced around
all day to get  hang of  things,  by  by I could do pretty
well in m, only Jim said I didn’t walk like a girl;  he said
I must quit pulling up my gown to get at my britches-pocket.  I took
notice,  done better.

I started up  Illinois shore in  canoe just after dark.

I started across to  town from a little below  ferry-ling, 
 drift of  current fetched me in at  bottom of  town.  I
tied up  started along  bank.  re was a light burning in a
little shanty that hadn’t been lived in for a long time,  I wondered
who had took up quarters re.  I slipped up  peeped in at 
window.  re was a woman about forty year old in re knitting by
a cle that was on a pine table.  I didn’t know her face; she was a
stranger, for you couldn’t start a face in that town that I didn’t know.
 Now this was lucky, because I was weakening; I was getting afraid I had
come; people might know my voice  find me out.  But if this woman had
been in such a little town two days she could tell me all I wanted to
know; so I knocked at  door,  made up my mind I wouldn’t forget I
was a girl.




CHAPTER XI.

“COME in,” says  woman,  I did.  She says:  “Take a cheer.”

I done it.  She looked me all over with her little shiny eyes,  says:

“What might your name be?”

“Sarah Williams.”

“Where ‘bouts do you live?  In this neighborhood?’

“No’m.  In Hookerville, seven mile below.  I’ve walked all  way 
I’m all tired out.”

“Hungry, too, I reckon.  I’ll find you something.”

“No’m, I ain’t hungry.  I was so hungry I had to stop two miles below
here at a farm; so I ain’t hungry no more.  It’s what makes me so late.
My mor’s down sick,  out of money  everything,  I come to
tell my uncle Abner Moore.  He lives at  upper end of  town, she
says.  I hain’t ever been here before.  Do you know him?”

“No; but I don’t know everybody yet.  I haven’t lived here quite two
weeks. It’s a considerable ways to  upper end of  town.  You
better stay here all night.  Take off your bonnet.”

“No,” I says; “I’ll rest a while, I reckon,  go on.  I ain’t afeared
of  dark.”

She said she wouldn’t let me go by myself, but her husb would be in
by  by, maybe in a hour  a half,  she’d send him along with me.
n she got to talking about her husb,  about her relations up
 river,  her relations down  river,  about how much better
off y used to was,  how y didn’t know but y’d made a mistake
coming to our town, instead of letting well alone-- so on  so on,
till I was afeard I had made a mistake coming to her to find out what
was going on in  town; but by  by she dropped on to pap  
murder,  n I was pretty willing to let her clatter right along.
 She told about me  Tom Sawyer finding  six thous dollars (only
she got it ten)  all about pap  what a hard lot he was,  what
a hard lot I was,  at last she got down to where I was murdered.  I
says:

“Who done it?  We’ve heard considerable about se goings on down in
Hookerville, but we don’t know who ‘twas that killed Huck Finn.”

“Well, I reckon re’s a right smart chance of people _here_ that’d
like to know who killed him.  Some think old Finn done it himself.”

“No--is that so?”

“Most everybody thought it at first.  He’ll never know how nigh he come
to getting lynched.  But before night y changed around  judged it
was done by a runaway nigger named Jim.”

“Why _he_--”

I stopped.  I reckoned I better keep still.  She run on,  never
noticed I had put in at all:

“ nigger run off  very night Huck Finn was killed.  So re’s a
reward out for him--three hundred dollars.   re’s a reward out for
old Finn, too--two hundred dollars.  You see, he come to town 
morning after  murder,  told about it,  was out with ‘em on 
ferryboat hunt,  right away after he up  left.  Before night y
wanted to lynch him, but he was gone, you see.  Well, next day y
found out  nigger was gone; y found out he hadn’t ben seen sence
ten o’clock  night  murder was done.  So n y put it on him,
you see;  while y was full of it, next day, back comes old Finn,
 went boo-hooing to Judge Thatcher to get money to hunt for 
nigger all over Illinois with.  judge gave him some,  that evening
he got drunk,  was around till after midnight with a couple of mighty
hard-looking strangers,  n went off with m.  Well, he hain’t
come back sence,  y ain’t looking for him back till this thing
blows over a little, for people thinks now that he killed his boy 
fixed things so folks would think robbers done it,  n he’d get
Huck’s money without having to bor a long time with a lawsuit.
 People do say he warn’t any too good to do it.  Oh, he’s sly, I reckon.
 If he don’t come back for a year he’ll be all right.  You can’t prove
anything on him, you know; everything will be quieted down n, 
he’ll walk in Huck’s money as easy as nothing.”

“Yes, I reckon so, ‘m.  I don’t see nothing in  way of it.  Has
everybody quit thinking  nigger done it?”

“Oh, no, not everybody.  A good many thinks he done it.  But y’ll get
 nigger pretty soon now,  maybe y can scare it out of him.”

“Why, are y after him yet?”

“Well, you’re innocent, ain’t you!  Does three hundred dollars lay
around every day for people to pick up?  Some folks think  nigger
ain’t far from here.  I’m one of m--but I hain’t talked it around.  A
few days ago I was talking with an old couple that lives next door in
 log shanty,  y happened to say hardly anybody ever goes to
that isl over yonder that y call Jackson’s Isl.  Don’t anybody
live re? says I. No, nobody, says y.  I didn’t say any more, but
I done some thinking.  I was pretty near certain I’d seen smoke over
re, about  head of  isl, a day or two before that, so I says
to myself, like as not that nigger’s hiding over re; anyway, says
I, it’s worth  trouble to give  place a hunt.  I hain’t seen any
smoke sence, so I reckon maybe he’s gone, if it was him; but husb’s
going over to see--him  anor man.  He was gone up  river; but he
got back to-day,  I told him as soon as he got here two hours ago.”

I had got so uneasy I couldn’t set still.  I had to do something with my
hs; so I took up a needle off of  table  went to threading
it. My hs shook,  I was making a bad job of it.  When  woman
stopped talking I looked up,  she was looking at me pretty curious
 smiling a little.  I put down  needle  thread,  let on to
be interested-- I was, too-- says:

“Three hundred dollars is a power of money.  I wish my mor could get
it. Is your husb going over re to-night?”

“Oh, yes.  He went up-town with  man I was telling you of, to get a
boat  see if y could borrow anor gun.  y’ll go over after
midnight.”

“Couldn’t y see better if y was to wait till daytime?”

“Yes.   couldn’t  nigger see better, too?  After midnight he’ll
likely be asleep,  y can slip around through  woods  hunt up
his camp fire all  better for  dark, if he’s got one.”

“I didn’t think of that.”

 woman kept looking at me pretty curious,  I didn’t feel a bit
comfortable.  Pretty soon she says,

“What did you say your name was, honey?”

“M--Mary Williams.”

Somehow it didn’t seem to me that I said it was Mary before, so I didn’t
look up--seemed to me I said it was Sarah; so I felt sort of cornered,
 was afeared maybe I was looking it, too.  I wished  woman would
say something more;  longer she set still  uneasier I was.  But
now she says:

“Honey, I thought you said it was Sarah when you first come in?”

“Oh, yes’m, I did.  Sarah Mary Williams.  Sarah’s my first name.  Some
calls me Sarah, some calls me Mary.”

“Oh, that’s  way of it?”

“Yes’m.”

I was feeling better n, but I wished I was out of re, anyway.  I
couldn’t look up yet.

Well,  woman fell to talking about how hard times was,  how poor
y had to live,  how  rats was as free as if y owned 
place,  so forth  so on,  n I got easy again.  She was right
about  rats. You’d see one stick his nose out of a hole in  corner
every little while.  She said she had to have things hy to throw at
m when she was alone, or y wouldn’t give her no peace.  She showed
me a bar of lead twisted up into a knot,  said she was a good shot
with it generly, but she’d wrenched her arm a day or two ago,  didn’t
know wher she could throw true now.  But she watched for a chance,
 directly banged away at a rat; but she missed him wide,  said
“Ouch!” it hurt her arm so.  n she told me to try for  next one.
 I wanted to be getting away before  old man got back, but of course
I didn’t let on.  I got  thing,   first rat that showed his
nose I let drive,  if he’d a stayed where he was he’d a been a
tolerable sick rat.  She said that was first-rate,  she reckoned I
would hive  next one.  She went  got  lump of lead  fetched
it back,  brought along a hank of yarn which she wanted me to help
her with.  I held up my two hs  she put  hank over m, 
went on talking about her  her husb’s matters.  But she broke off
to say:

“Keep your eye on  rats.  You better have  lead in your lap,
hy.”

So she dropped  lump into my lap just at that moment,  I clapped
my legs toger on it  she went on talking.  But only about a
minute. n she took off  hank  looked me straight in  face,
 very pleasant,  says:

“Come, now, what’s your real name?”

“Wh--what, mum?”

“What’s your real name?  Is it Bill, or Tom, or Bob?--or what is it?”

I reckon I shook like a leaf,  I didn’t know hardly what to do.  But
I says:

“Please to don’t poke fun at a poor girl like me, mum.  If I’m in 
way here, I’ll--”

“No, you won’t.  Set down  stay where you are.  I ain’t going to hurt
you,  I ain’t going to tell on you, nur.  You just tell me your
secret,  trust me.  I’ll keep it; , what’s more, I’ll help
you. So’ll my old man if you want him to.  You see, you’re a runaway
‘prentice, that’s all.  It ain’t anything.  re ain’t no harm in it.
You’ve been treated bad,  you made up your mind to cut.  Bless you,
child, I wouldn’t tell on you.  Tell me all about it now, that’s a good
boy.”

So I said it wouldn’t be no use to try to play it any longer,  I
would just make a clean breast  tell her everything, but she musn’t
go back on her promise.  n I told her my far  mor was dead,
  law had bound me out to a mean old farmer in  country thirty
mile back from  river,  he treated me so bad I couldn’t st it
no longer; he went away to be gone a couple of days,  so I took my
chance  stole some of his daughter’s old clos  cleared out, 
I had been three nights coming  thirty miles.  I traveled nights,
 hid daytimes  slept,   bag of bread  meat I carried from
home lasted me all  way,  I had a-plenty.  I said I believed my
uncle Abner Moore would take care of me,  so that was why I struck
out for this town of Goshen.

“Goshen, child?  This ain’t Goshen.  This is St. Petersburg.  Goshen’s
ten mile furr up  river.  Who told you this was Goshen?”

“Why, a man I met at daybreak this morning, just as I was going to turn
into  woods for my regular sleep.  He told me when  roads forked I
must take  right h,  five mile would fetch me to Goshen.”

“He was drunk, I reckon.  He told you just exactly wrong.”

“Well, he did act like he was drunk, but it ain’t no matter now.  I got
to be moving along.  I’ll fetch Goshen before daylight.”

“Hold on a minute.  I’ll put you up a snack to eat.  You might want it.”

So she put me up a snack,  says:

“Say, when a cow’s laying down, which end of her gets up first?  Answer
up prompt now--don’t stop to study over it.  Which end gets up first?”

“ hind end, mum.”

“Well, n, a horse?”

“ for’rard end, mum.”

“Which side of a tree does  moss grow on?”

“North side.”

“If fifteen cows is browsing on a hillside, how many of m eats with
ir heads pointed  same direction?”

“ whole fifteen, mum.”

“Well, I reckon you _have_ lived in  country.  I thought maybe you
was trying to hocus me again.  What’s your real name, now?”

“George Peters, mum.”

“Well, try to remember it, George.  Don’t forget  tell me it’s
Elexer before you go,  n get out by saying it’s George
Elexer when I catch you.   don’t go about women in that old
calico.  You do a girl tolerable poor, but you might fool men, maybe.
 Bless you, child, when you set out to thread a needle don’t hold 
thread still  fetch  needle up to it; hold  needle still 
poke  thread at it; that’s  way a woman most always does, but a
man always does t’or way.   when you throw at a rat or anything,
hitch yourself up a tiptoe  fetch your h up over your head as
awkward as you can,  miss your rat about six or seven foot. Throw
stiff-armed from  shoulder, like re was a pivot re for it to
turn on, like a girl; not from  wrist  elbow, with your arm out
to one side, like a boy.  , mind you, when a girl tries to catch
anything in her lap she throws her knees apart; she don’t clap m
toger,  way you did when you catched  lump of lead.  Why, I
spotted you for a boy when you was threading  needle;  I contrived
 or things just to make certain.  Now trot along to your uncle,
Sarah Mary Williams George Elexer Peters,  if you get into trouble
you send word to Mrs. Judith Loftus, which is me,  I’ll do what I can
to get you out of it.  Keep  river road all  way,  next time
you tramp take shoes  socks with you.  river road’s a rocky one,
 your feet’ll be in a condition when you get to Goshen, I reckon.”

I went up  bank about fifty yards,  n I doubled on my tracks
 slipped back to where my canoe was, a good piece below  house.  I
jumped in,  was off in a hurry.  I went up-stream far enough to
make  head of  isl,  n started across.  I took off 
sun-bonnet, for I didn’t want no blinders on n.  When I was about 
middle I heard  clock begin to strike, so I stops  listens; 
sound come faint over  water but clear--eleven.  When I struck 
head of  isl I never waited to blow, though I was most winded, but
I shoved right into  timber where my old camp used to be,  started
a good fire re on a high  dry spot.

n I jumped in  canoe  dug out for our place, a mile  a half
below, as hard as I could go.  I led,  slopped through  timber
 up  ridge  into  cavern.  re Jim laid, sound asleep on
 ground.  I roused him out  says:

“Git up  hump yourself, Jim!  re ain’t a minute to lose.  y’re
after us!”

Jim never asked no questions, he never said a word; but  way he
worked for  next half an hour showed about how he was scared.  By
that time everything we had in  world was on our raft,  she was
ready to be shoved out from  willow cove where she was hid.  We
put out  camp fire at  cavern  first thing,  didn’t show a
cle outside after that.

I took  canoe out from  shore a little piece,  took a look;
but if re was a boat around I couldn’t see it, for stars  shadows
ain’t good to see by.  n we got out  raft  slipped along down
in  shade, past  foot of  isl dead still--never saying a
word.




CHAPTER XII.

IT must a been close on to one o’clock when we got below  isl at
last,   raft did seem to go mighty slow.  If a boat was to come
along we was going to take to  canoe  break for  Illinois
shore;  it was well a boat didn’t come, for we hadn’t ever thought to
put  gun in  canoe, or a fishing-line, or anything to eat.  We
was in rur too much of a sweat to think of so many things.  It warn’t
good judgment to put _everything_ on  raft.

If  men went to  isl I just expect y found  camp fire I
built,  watched it all night for Jim to come.  Anyways, y stayed
away from us,  if my building  fire never fooled m it warn’t no
fault of mine.  I played it as low down on m as I could.

When  first streak of day began to show we tied up to a towhead in a
big bend on  Illinois side,  hacked off cottonwood branches with
 hatchet,  covered up  raft with m so she looked like re
had been a cave-in in  bank re.  A tow-head is a sbar that has
cottonwoods on it as thick as harrow-teeth.

We had mountains on  Missouri shore  heavy timber on  Illinois
side,   channel was down  Missouri shore at that place, so we
warn’t afraid of anybody running across us.  We laid re all day,
 watched  rafts  steamboats spin down  Missouri shore, 
up-bound steamboats fight  big river in  middle.  I told Jim all
about  time I had jabbering with that woman;  Jim said she was
a smart one,  if she was to start after us herself she wouldn’t set
down  watch a camp fire--no, sir, she’d fetch a dog.  Well, n, I
said, why couldn’t she tell her husb to fetch a dog?  Jim said he
bet she did think of it by  time  men was ready to start,  he
believed y must a gone up-town to get a dog  so y lost all that
time, or else we wouldn’t be here on a towhead sixteen or seventeen mile
below  village--no, indeedy, we would be in that same old town again.
 So I said I didn’t care what was  reason y didn’t get us as long
as y didn’t.

When it was beginning to come on dark we poked our heads out of 
cottonwood thicket,  looked up  down  across; nothing in sight;
so Jim took up some of  top planks of  raft  built a snug
wigwam to get under in blazing wear  rainy,  to keep  things
dry. Jim made a floor for  wigwam,  raised it a foot or more above
 level of  raft, so now  blankets  all  traps was out of
reach of steamboat waves.  Right in  middle of  wigwam we made a
layer of dirt about five or six inches deep with a frame around it for
to hold it to its place; this was to build a fire on in sloppy wear
or chilly;  wigwam would keep it from being seen.  We made an extra
steering-oar, too, because one of  ors might get broke on a snag
or something. We fixed up a short forked stick to hang  old lantern
on, because we must always light  lantern whenever we see a steamboat
coming down-stream, to keep from getting run over; but we wouldn’t have
to light it for up-stream boats unless we see we was in what y call
a “crossing”; for  river was pretty high yet, very low banks being
still a little under water; so up-bound boats didn’t always run 
channel, but hunted easy water.

This second night we run between seven  eight hours, with a current
that was making over four mile an hour.  We catched fish  talked,
 we took a swim now  n to keep off sleepiness.  It was kind of
solemn, drifting down  big, still river, laying on our backs looking
up at  stars,  we didn’t ever feel like talking loud,  it
warn’t often that we laughed--only a little kind of a low chuckle.  We
had mighty good wear as a general thing,  nothing ever happened to
us at all--that night, nor  next, nor  next.

Every night we passed towns, some of m away up on black hillsides,
nothing but just a shiny bed of lights; not a house could you see.  
fifth night we passed St. Louis,  it was like  whole world lit up.
In St. Petersburg y used to say re was twenty or thirty thous
people in St. Louis, but I never believed it till I see that wonderful
spread of lights at two o’clock that still night.  re warn’t a sound
re; everybody was asleep.

Every night now I used to slip ashore towards ten o’clock at some little
village,  buy ten or fifteen cents’ worth of meal or bacon or or
stuff to eat;  sometimes I lifted a chicken that warn’t roosting
comfortable,  took him along.  Pap always said, take a chicken when
you get a chance, because if you don’t want him yourself you can easy
find somebody that does,  a good deed ain’t ever forgot.  I never see
pap when he didn’t want  chicken himself, but that is what he used to
say, anyway.

Mornings before daylight I slipped into cornfields  borrowed a
watermelon, or a mushmelon, or a punkin, or some new corn, or things of
that kind.  Pap always said it warn’t no harm to borrow things if you
was meaning to pay m back some time; but  widow said it warn’t
anything but a soft name for stealing,  no decent body would do it.
 Jim said he reckoned  widow was partly right  pap was partly
right; so  best way would be for us to pick out two or three things
from  list  say we wouldn’t borrow m any more--n he reckoned
it wouldn’t be no harm to borrow  ors.  So we talked it over all
one night, drifting along down  river, trying to make up our minds
wher to drop  watermelons, or  cantelopes, or  mushmelons,
or what.  But towards daylight we got it all settled satisfactory, 
concluded to drop crabapples  p’simmons.  We warn’t feeling just
right before that, but it was all comfortable now.  I was glad  way
it come out, too, because crabapples ain’t ever good,   p’simmons
wouldn’t be ripe for two or three months yet.

We shot a water-fowl now  n that got up too early in  morning
or didn’t go to bed early enough in  evening.  Take it all round, we
lived pretty high.

 fifth night below St. Louis we had a big storm after midnight, with
a power of thunder  lightning,   rain poured down in a solid
sheet. We stayed in  wigwam  let  raft take care of itself.
When  lightning glared out we could see a big straight river ahead,
 high, rocky bluffs on both sides.  By  by says I, “Hel-_lo_, Jim,
looky yonder!” It was a steamboat that had killed herself on a rock.
 We was drifting straight down for her.   lightning showed her very
distinct.  She was leaning over, with part of her upper deck above
water,  you could see every little chimbly-guy clean  clear,  a
chair by  big bell, with an old slouch hat hanging on  back of it,
when  flashes come.

Well, it being away in  night  stormy,  all so mysterious-like,
I felt just  way any or boy would a felt when I see that wreck
laying re so mournful  lonesome in  middle of  river.  I
wanted to get aboard of her  slink around a little,  see what
re was re.  So I says:

“Le’s l on her, Jim.”

But Jim was dead against it at first.  He says:

“I doan’ want to go fool’n ‘long er no wrack.  We’s doin’ blame’ well,
en we better let blame’ well alone, as de good book says.  Like as not
dey’s a watchman on dat wrack.”

“Watchman your grmor,” I says; “re ain’t nothing to watch but
 texas   pilot-house;  do you reckon anybody’s going to resk
his life for a texas  a pilot-house such a night as this, when
it’s likely to break up  wash off down  river any minute?”  Jim
couldn’t say nothing to that, so he didn’t try.  “ besides,” I says,
“we might borrow something worth having out of  captain’s stateroom.
 Seegars, I bet you-- cost five cents apiece, solid cash.  Steamboat
captains is always rich,  get sixty dollars a month,  _y_ don’t
care a cent what a thing costs, you know, long as y want it.  Stick a
cle in your pocket; I can’t rest, Jim, till we give her a rummaging.
 Do you reckon Tom Sawyer would ever go by this thing?  Not for pie, he
wouldn’t. He’d call it an adventure--that’s what he’d call it;  he’d
l on that wreck if it was his last act.   wouldn’t he throw style
into it?--wouldn’t he spread himself, nor nothing?  Why, you’d think it
was Christopher C’lumbus discovering Kingdom-Come.  I wish Tom Sawyer
_was_ here.”

Jim he grumbled a little, but give in.  He said we mustn’t talk any more
than we could help,  n talk mighty low.   lightning showed us
 wreck again just in time,  we fetched  stabboard derrick, 
made fast re.

 deck was high out here.  We went sneaking down  slope of it to
labboard, in  dark, towards  texas, feeling our way slow with our
feet,  spreading our hs out to fend off  guys, for it was so
dark we couldn’t see no sign of m.  Pretty soon we struck  forward
end of  skylight,  clumb on to it;   next step fetched us in
front of  captain’s door, which was open,  by Jimminy, away down
through  texas-hall we see a light!  all in  same second we
seem to hear low voices in yonder!

Jim whispered  said he was feeling powerful sick,  told me to come
along.  I says, all right,  was going to start for  raft; but just
n I heard a voice wail out  say:

“Oh, please don’t, boys; I swear I won’t ever tell!”

Anor voice said, pretty loud:

“It’s a lie, Jim Turner.  You’ve acted this way before.  You always want
more’n your share of  truck,  you’ve always got it, too, because
you’ve swore ‘t if you didn’t you’d tell.  But this time you’ve said
it jest one time too many.  You’re  meanest, treacherousest hound in
this country.”

By this time Jim was gone for  raft.  I was just a-biling with
curiosity;  I says to myself, Tom Sawyer wouldn’t back out now,
 so I won’t eir; I’m a-going to see what’s going on here.  So I
dropped on my hs  knees in  little passage,  crept aft
in  dark till re warn’t but one stateroom betwixt me  
cross-hall of  texas.  n in re I see a man stretched on 
floor  tied h  foot,  two men sting over him,  one
of m had a dim lantern in his h,   or one had a pistol.
 This one kept pointing  pistol at  man’s head on  floor, 
saying:

“I’d _like_ to!   I orter, too--a mean skunk!”

 man on  floor would shrivel up  say, “Oh, please don’t, Bill;
I hain’t ever goin’ to tell.”

 every time he said that  man with  lantern would laugh 
say:

“‘Deed you _ain’t!_  You never said no truer thing ‘n that, you bet
you.”  once he said:  “Hear him beg!  yit if we hadn’t got 
best of him  tied him he’d a killed us both.   what _for_?  Jist
for noth’n. Jist because we stood on our _rights_--that’s what for.  But
I lay you ain’t a-goin’ to threaten nobody any more, Jim Turner.  Put
_up_ that pistol, Bill.”

Bill says:

“I don’t want to, Jake Packard.  I’m for killin’ him-- didn’t he kill
old Hatfield jist  same way-- don’t he deserve it?”

“But I don’t _want_ him killed,  I’ve got my reasons for it.”

“Bless yo’ heart for m words, Jake Packard!  I’ll never forgit you
long’s I live!” says  man on  floor, sort of blubbering.

Packard didn’t take no notice of that, but hung up his lantern on a nail
 started towards where I was re in  dark,  motioned Bill
to come.  I crawfished as fast as I could about two yards, but  boat
slanted so that I couldn’t make very good time; so to keep from getting
run over  catched I crawled into a stateroom on  upper side.
  man came a-pawing along in  dark,  when Packard got to my
stateroom, he says:

“Here--come in here.”

 in he come,  Bill after him.  But before y got in I was up
in  upper berth, cornered,  sorry I come.  n y stood re,
with ir hs on  ledge of  berth,  talked.  I couldn’t see
m, but I could tell where y was by  whisky y’d been having.
 I was glad I didn’t drink whisky; but it wouldn’t made much difference
anyway, because most of  time y couldn’t a treed me because I
didn’t brea.  I was too scared.  , besides, a body _couldn’t_
brea  hear such talk.  y talked low  earnest.  Bill wanted
to kill Turner.  He says:

“He’s said he’ll tell,  he will.  If we was to give both our shares
to him _now_ it wouldn’t make no difference after  row   way
we’ve served him.  Shore’s you’re born, he’ll turn State’s evidence; now
you hear _me_.  I’m for putting him out of his troubles.”

“So’m I,” says Packard, very quiet.

“Blame it, I’d sorter begun to think you wasn’t.  Well, n, that’s all
right.  Le’s go  do it.”

“Hold on a minute; I hain’t had my say yit.  You listen to me.
Shooting’s good, but re’s quieter ways if  thing’s _got_ to be
done. But what I say is this:  it ain’t good sense to go court’n around
after a halter if you can git at what you’re up to in some way that’s
jist as good  at  same time don’t bring you into no resks.  Ain’t
that so?”

“You bet it is.  But how you goin’ to manage it this time?”

“Well, my idea is this:  we’ll rustle around  gar up whatever
pickins we’ve overlooked in  staterooms,  shove for shore  hide
 truck. n we’ll wait.  Now I say it ain’t a-goin’ to be more’n two
hours befo’ this wrack breaks up  washes off down  river.  See?
He’ll be drownded,  won’t have nobody to blame for it but his own
self.  I reckon that’s a considerble sight better ‘n killin’ of him.
 I’m unfavorable to killin’ a man as long as you can git aroun’ it; it
ain’t good sense, it ain’t good morals.  Ain’t I right?”

“Yes, I reck’n you are.  But s’pose she _don’t_ break up  wash off?”

“Well, we can wait  two hours anyway  see, can’t we?”

“All right, n; come along.”

So y started,  I lit out, all in a cold sweat,  scrambled
forward. It was dark as pitch re; but I said, in a kind of a coarse
whisper, “Jim!”  he answered up, right at my elbow, with a sort of a
moan,  I says:

“Quick, Jim, it ain’t no time for fooling around  moaning; re’s a
gang of murderers in yonder,  if we don’t hunt up ir boat  set
her drifting down  river so se fellows can’t get away from 
wreck re’s one of ‘em going to be in a bad fix.  But if we find ir
boat we can put _all_ of ‘em in a bad fix--for  sheriff ‘ll get ‘em.
Quick--hurry!  I’ll hunt  labboard side, you hunt  stabboard. You
start at  raft, --”

“Oh, my lordy, lordy!  _raf’_?  Dey ain’ no raf’ no mo’; she done broke
loose en gone I--en here we is!”




CHAPTER XIII.

WELL, I catched my breath  most fainted.  Shut up on a wreck with
such a gang as that!  But it warn’t no time to be sentimentering.  We’d
_got_ to find that boat now--had to have it for ourselves.  So we went
a-quaking  shaking down  stabboard side,  slow work it was,
too--seemed a week before we got to  stern.  No sign of a boat.  Jim
said he didn’t believe he could go any furr--so scared he hadn’t
hardly any strength left, he said.  But I said, come on, if we get left
on this wreck we are in a fix, sure.  So on we prowled again.  We struck
for  stern of  texas,  found it,  n scrabbled along
forwards on  skylight, hanging on from shutter to shutter, for 
edge of  skylight was in  water.  When we got pretty close to 
cross-hall door re was  skiff, sure enough!  I could just barely
see her.  I felt ever so thankful.  In anor second I would a been
aboard of her, but just n  door opened.  One of  men stuck his
head out only about a couple of foot from me,  I thought I was gone;
but he jerked it in again,  says:

“Heave that blame lantern out o’ sight, Bill!”

He flung a bag of something into  boat,  n got in himself 
set down.  It was Packard.  n Bill _he_ come out  got in.  Packard
says, in a low voice:

“All ready--shove off!”

I couldn’t hardly hang on to  shutters, I was so weak.  But Bill
says:

“Hold on--‘d you go through him?”

“No.  Didn’t you?”

“No.  So he’s got his share o’  cash yet.”

“Well, n, come along; no use to take truck  leave money.”

“Say, won’t he suspicion what we’re up to?”

“Maybe he won’t.  But we got to have it anyway. Come along.”

So y got out  went in.

 door slammed to because it was on  careened side;  in a half
second I was in  boat,  Jim come tumbling after me.  I out with my
knife  cut  rope,  away we went!

We didn’t touch an oar,  we didn’t speak nor whisper, nor hardly even
brea.  We went gliding swift along, dead silent, past  tip of 
paddle-box,  past  stern; n in a second or two more we was a
hundred yards below  wreck,   darkness soaked her up, every
last sign of her,  we was safe,  knowed it.

When we was three or four hundred yards down-stream we see  lantern
show like a little spark at  texas door for a second,  we knowed
by that that  rascals had missed ir boat,  was beginning to
underst that y was in just as much trouble now as Jim Turner was.

n Jim manned  oars,  we took out after our raft.  Now was 
first time that I begun to worry about  men--I reckon I hadn’t
had time to before.  I begun to think how dreadful it was, even for
murderers, to be in such a fix.  I says to myself, re ain’t no
telling but I might come to be a murderer myself yet,  n how would
I like it?  So says I to Jim:

“ first light we see we’ll l a hundred yards below it or above
it, in a place where it’s a good hiding-place for you   skiff, 
n I’ll go  fix up some kind of a yarn,  get somebody to go for
that gang  get m out of ir scrape, so y can be hung when
ir time comes.”

But that idea was a failure; for pretty soon it begun to storm again,
 this time worse than ever.   rain poured down,  never a light
showed; everybody in bed, I reckon.  We boomed along down  river,
watching for lights  watching for our raft.  After a long time 
rain let up, but  clouds stayed,   lightning kept whimpering,
 by  by a flash showed us a black thing ahead, floating,  we
made for it.

It was  raft,  mighty glad was we to get aboard of it again.  We
seen a light now away down to  right, on shore.  So I said I would
go for it.  skiff was half full of plunder which that gang had stole
re on  wreck.  We hustled it on to  raft in a pile,  I told
Jim to float along down,  show a light when he judged he had gone
about two mile,  keep it burning till I come; n I manned my oars
 shoved for  light.  As I got down towards it three or four more
showed--up on a hillside.  It was a village.  I closed in above  shore
light,  laid on my oars  floated.  As I went by I see it was a
lantern hanging on  jackstaff of a double-hull ferryboat.  I skimmed
around for  watchman, a-wondering whereabouts he slept;  by 
by I found him roosting on  bitts forward, with his head down between
his knees.  I gave his shoulder two or three little shoves,  begun to
cry.

He stirred up in a kind of a startlish way; but when he see it was only
me he took a good gap  stretch,  n he says:

“Hello, what’s up?  Don’t cry, bub.  What’s  trouble?”

I says:

“Pap,  mam,  sis, --”

n I broke down.  He says:

“Oh, dang it now, _don’t_ take on so; we all has to have our troubles,
 this ‘n ‘ll come out all right.  What’s  matter with ‘em?”

“y’re--y’re--are you  watchman of  boat?”

“Yes,” he says, kind of pretty-well-satisfied like.  “I’m  captain
  owner   mate   pilot  watchman  head
deck-h;  sometimes I’m  freight  passengers.  I ain’t as
rich as old Jim Hornback,  I can’t be so blame’ generous  good
to Tom, Dick,  Harry as what he is,  slam around money  way he
does; but I’ve told him a many a time ‘t I wouldn’t trade places with
him; for, says I, a sailor’s life’s  life for me,  I’m derned if
_I’d_ live two mile out o’ town, where re ain’t nothing ever goin’
on, not for all his spondulicks  as much more on top of it.  Says I--”

I broke in  says:

“y’re in an awful peck of trouble, --”

“_Who_ is?”

“Why, pap  mam  sis  Miss Hooker;  if you’d take your
ferryboat  go up re--”

“Up where?  Where are y?”

“On  wreck.”

“What wreck?”

“Why, re ain’t but one.”

“What, you don’t mean  Walter Scott?”

“Yes.”

“Good l! what are y doin’ _re_, for gracious sakes?”

“Well, y didn’t go re a-purpose.”

“I bet y didn’t!  Why, great goodness, re ain’t no chance for ‘em
if y don’t git off mighty quick!  Why, how in  nation did y
ever git into such a scrape?”

“Easy enough.  Miss Hooker was a-visiting up re to  town--”

“Yes, Booth’s Ling--go on.”

“She was a-visiting re at Booth’s Ling,  just in  edge of
 evening she started over with her nigger woman in  horse-ferry
to stay all night at her friend’s house, Miss What-you-may-call-her I
disremember her name-- y lost ir steering-oar,  swung
around  went a-floating down, stern first, about two mile, 
saddle-baggsed on  wreck,   ferryman   nigger woman 
 horses was all lost, but Miss Hooker she made a grab  got aboard
 wreck.  Well, about an hour after dark we come along down in our
trading-scow,  it was so dark we didn’t notice  wreck till we was
right on it;  so _we_ saddle-baggsed; but all of us was saved but
Bill Whipple-- oh, he _was_  best cretur!--I most wish ‘t it had
been me, I do.”

“My George!  It’s  beatenest thing I ever struck.   _n_ what
did you all do?”

“Well, we hollered  took on, but it’s so wide re we couldn’t
make nobody hear.  So pap said somebody got to get ashore  get help
somehow. I was  only one that could swim, so I made a dash for it,
 Miss Hooker she said if I didn’t strike help sooner, come here 
hunt up her uncle,  he’d fix  thing.  I made  l about a mile
below,  been fooling along ever since, trying to get people to do
something, but y said, ‘What, in such a night  such a current?
re ain’t no sense in it; go for  steam ferry.’  Now if you’ll go
--”

“By Jackson, I’d _like_ to, , blame it, I don’t know but I will; but
who in  dingnation’s a-going’ to _pay_ for it?  Do you reckon your
pap--”

“Why _that’s_ all right.  Miss Hooker she tole me, _particular_, that
her uncle Hornback--”

“Great guns! is _he_ her uncle?  Looky here, you break for that light
over yonder-way,  turn out west when you git re,  about a
quarter of a mile out you’ll come to  tavern; tell ‘em to dart you
out to Jim Hornback’s,  he’ll foot  bill.   don’t you fool
around any, because he’ll want to know  news.  Tell him I’ll have
his niece all safe before he can get to town.  Hump yourself, now; I’m
a-going up around  corner here to roust out my engineer.”

I struck for  light, but as soon as he turned  corner I went back
 got into my skiff  bailed her out,  n pulled up shore in
 easy water about six hundred yards,  tucked myself in among
some woodboats; for I couldn’t rest easy till I could see  ferryboat
start. But take it all around, I was feeling rur comfortable on
accounts of taking all this trouble for that gang, for not many would
a done it.  I wished  widow knowed about it.  I judged she would be
proud of me for helping se rapscallions, because rapscallions 
dead beats is  kind  widow  good people takes  most interest
in.

Well, before long here comes  wreck, dim  dusky, sliding along
down! A kind of cold shiver went through me,  n I struck out for
her.  She was very deep,  I see in a minute re warn’t much chance
for anybody being alive in her.  I pulled all around her  hollered
a little, but re wasn’t any answer; all dead still.  I felt a little
bit heavy-hearted about  gang, but not much, for I reckoned if y
could st it I could.

n here comes  ferryboat; so I shoved for  middle of  river
on a long down-stream slant;  when I judged I was out of eye-reach
I laid on my oars,  looked back  see her go  smell around 
wreck for Miss Hooker’s remainders, because  captain would know her
uncle Hornback would want m;  n pretty soon  ferryboat give
it up  went for  shore,  I laid into my work  went a-booming
down  river.

It did seem a powerful long time before Jim’s light showed up;  when
it did show it looked like it was a thous mile off.  By  time I
got re  sky was beginning to get a little gray in  east; so we
struck for an isl,  hid  raft,  sunk  skiff,  turned
in  slept like dead people.




CHAPTER XIV.

BY  by, when we got up, we turned over  truck  gang had stole
off of  wreck,  found boots,  blankets,  clos,  all
sorts of or things,  a lot of books,  a spyglass,  three
boxes of seegars.  We hadn’t ever been this rich before in neir of
our lives.   seegars was prime.  We laid off all  afternoon in 
woods talking,  me reading  books,  having a general good
time. I told Jim all about what happened inside  wreck  at 
ferryboat,  I said se kinds of things was adventures; but he said
he didn’t want no more adventures.  He said that when I went in 
texas  he crawled back to get on  raft  found her gone he
nearly died, because he judged it was all up with _him_ anyway it could
be fixed; for if he didn’t get saved he would get drownded;  if he
did get saved, whoever saved him would send him back home so as to get
 reward,  n Miss Watson would sell him South, sure.  Well, he
was right; he was most always right; he had an uncommon level head for a
nigger.

I read considerable to Jim about kings  dukes  earls  such, 
how gaudy y dressed,  how much style y put on,  called each
or your majesty,  your grace,  your lordship,  so on, ‘stead
of mister;  Jim’s eyes bugged out,  he was interested.  He says:

“I didn’ know dey was so many un um.  I hain’t hearn ‘bout none un um,
skasely, but ole King Sollermun, onless you counts dem kings dat’s in a
pack er k’yards.  How much do a king git?”

“Get?”  I says; “why, y get a thous dollars a month if y want
it; y can have just as much as y want; everything belongs to
m.”

“_Ain’_ dat gay?  En what dey got to do, Huck?”

“_y_ don’t do nothing!  Why, how you talk! y just set around.”

“No; is dat so?”

“Of course it is.  y just set around--except, maybe, when re’s a
war; n y go to  war.  But or times y just lazy around; or
go hawking--just hawking  sp--Sh!--d’ you hear a noise?”

We skipped out  looked; but it warn’t nothing but  flutter of a
steamboat’s wheel away down, coming around  point; so we come back.

“Yes,” says I, “ or times, when things is dull, y fuss with 
parlyment;  if everybody don’t go just so he whacks ir heads off.
But mostly y hang round  harem.”

“Roun’ de which?”

“Harem.”

“What’s de harem?”

“ place where he keeps his wives.  Don’t you know about  harem?
Solomon had one; he had about a million wives.”

“Why, yes, dat’s so; I--I’d done forgot it.  A harem’s a bo’d’n-house, I
reck’n.  Mos’ likely dey has rackety times in de nussery.  En I reck’n
de wives quarrels considable; en dat ‘crease de racket.  Yit dey say
Sollermun de wises’ man dat ever live’.  I doan’ take no stock in
dat. Bekase why: would a wise man want to live in de mids’ er sich a
blim-blammin’ all de time?  No--‘deed he wouldn’t.  A wise man ‘ud take
en buil’ a biler-factry; en den he could shet _down_ de biler-factry
when he want to res’.”

“Well, but he _was_  wisest man, anyway; because  widow she told
me so, her own self.”

“I doan k’yer what de widder say, he _warn’t_ no wise man nur.  He
had some er de dad-fetchedes’ ways I ever see.  Does you know ‘bout dat
chile dat he ‘uz gwyne to chop in two?”

“Yes,  widow told me all about it.”

“_Well_, den!  Warn’ dat de beatenes’ notion in de worl’?  You jes’
take en look at it a minute.  Dah’s de stump, dah--dat’s one er de women;
heah’s you--dat’s de yur one; I’s Sollermun; en dish yer dollar bill’s
de chile.  Bofe un you claims it.  What does I do?  Does I shin aroun’
mongs’ de neighbors en fine out which un you de bill _do_ b’long to, en
han’ it over to de right one, all safe en soun’, de way dat anybody dat
had any gumption would?  No; I take en whack de bill in _two_, en give
half un it to you, en de yur half to de yur woman.  Dat’s de way
Sollermun was gwyne to do wid de chile.  Now I want to ast you:  what’s
de use er dat half a bill?--can’t buy noth’n wid it.  En what use is a
half a chile?  I wouldn’ give a dern for a million un um.”

“But hang it, Jim, you’ve clean missed  point--blame it, you’ve missed
it a thous mile.”

“Who?  Me?  Go ‘long.  Doan’ talk to me ‘bout yo’ pints.  I reck’n I
knows sense when I sees it; en dey ain’ no sense in sich doin’s as
dat. De ‘spute warn’t ‘bout a half a chile, de ‘spute was ‘bout a whole
chile; en de man dat think he kin settle a ‘spute ‘bout a whole chile
wid a half a chile doan’ know enough to come in out’n de rain.  Doan’
talk to me ‘bout Sollermun, Huck, I knows him by de back.”

“But I tell you you don’t get  point.”

“Blame de point!  I reck’n I knows what I knows.  En mine you, de _real_
pint is down furder--it’s down deeper.  It lays in de way Sollermun was
raised.  You take a man dat’s got on’y one or two chillen; is dat man
gwyne to be waseful o’ chillen?  No, he ain’t; he can’t ‘ford it.  _He_
know how to value ‘em.  But you take a man dat’s got ‘bout five million
chillen runnin’ roun’ de house, en it’s diffunt.  _He_ as soon chop a
chile in two as a cat. Dey’s plenty mo’.  A chile er two, mo’ er less,
warn’t no consekens to Sollermun, dad fatch him!”

I never see such a nigger.  If he got a notion in his head once, re
warn’t no getting it out again.  He was  most down on Solomon of
any nigger I ever see.  So I went to talking about or kings,  let
Solomon slide.  I told about Louis Sixteenth that got his head cut off
in France long time ago;  about his little boy  dolphin, that
would a been a king, but y took  shut him up in jail,  some say
he died re.

“Po’ little chap.”

“But some says he got out  got away,  come to America.”

“Dat’s good!  But he’ll be pooty lonesome--dey ain’ no kings here, is
dey, Huck?”

“No.”

“Den he cain’t git no situation.  What he gwyne to do?”

“Well, I don’t know.  Some of m gets on  police,  some of m
learns people how to talk French.”

“Why, Huck, doan’ de French people talk de same way we does?”

“_No_, Jim; you couldn’t underst a word y said--not a single word.”

“Well, now, I be ding-busted!  How do dat come?”

“I don’t know; but it’s so.  I got some of ir jabber out of a book.
S’pose a man was to come to you  say Polly-voo-franzy--what would you
think?”

“I wouldn’ think nuff’n; I’d take en bust him over de head--dat is, if he
warn’t white.  I wouldn’t ‘low no nigger to call me dat.”

“Shucks, it ain’t calling you anything.  It’s only saying, do you know
how to talk French?”

“Well, den, why couldn’t he _say_ it?”

“Why, he _is_ a-saying it.  That’s a Frenchman’s _way_ of saying it.”

“Well, it’s a blame ridicklous way, en I doan’ want to hear no mo’ ‘bout
it.  Dey ain’ no sense in it.”

“Looky here, Jim; does a cat talk like we do?”

“No, a cat don’t.”

“Well, does a cow?”

“No, a cow don’t, nur.”

“Does a cat talk like a cow, or a cow talk like a cat?”

“No, dey don’t.”

“It’s natural  right for ‘em to talk different from each or, ain’t
it?”

“Course.”

“ ain’t it natural  right for a cat  a cow to talk different
from _us_?”

“Why, mos’ sholy it is.”

“Well, n, why ain’t it natural  right for a _Frenchman_ to talk
different from us?  You answer me that.”

“Is a cat a man, Huck?”

“No.”

“Well, den, dey ain’t no sense in a cat talkin’ like a man.  Is a cow a
man?--er is a cow a cat?”

“No, she ain’t eir of m.”

“Well, den, she ain’t got no business to talk like eir one er 
yur of ‘em.  Is a Frenchman a man?”

“Yes.”

“_Well_, den!  Dad blame it, why doan’ he _talk_ like a man?  You answer
me _dat_!”

I see it warn’t no use wasting words--you can’t learn a nigger to argue.
So I quit.




CHAPTER XV.

WE judged that three nights more would fetch us to Cairo, at  bottom
of Illinois, where  Ohio River comes in,  that was what we was
after.  We would sell  raft  get on a steamboat  go way up 
Ohio amongst  free States,  n be out of trouble.

Well,  second night a fog begun to come on,  we made for a towhead
to tie to, for it wouldn’t do to try to run in a fog; but when I paddled
ahead in  canoe, with  line to make fast, re warn’t anything
but little saplings to tie to.  I passed  line around one of m
right on  edge of  cut bank, but re was a stiff current, 
 raft come booming down so lively she tore it out by  roots 
away she went.  I see  fog closing down,  it made me so sick 
scared I couldn’t budge for most a half a minute it seemed to me--
n re warn’t no raft in sight; you couldn’t see twenty yards.  I
jumped into  canoe  run back to  stern,  grabbed  paddle
 set her back a stroke.  But she didn’t come.  I was in such a hurry
I hadn’t untied her.  I got up  tried to untie her, but I was so
excited my hs shook so I couldn’t hardly do anything with m.

As soon as I got started I took out after  raft, hot  heavy, right
down  towhead.  That was all right as far as it went, but  towhead
warn’t sixty yards long,   minute I flew by  foot of it I shot
out into  solid white fog,  hadn’t no more idea which way I was
going than a dead man.

Thinks I, it won’t do to paddle; first I know I’ll run into  bank
or a towhead or something; I got to set still  float,  yet it’s
mighty fidgety business to have to hold your hs still at such a time.
 I whooped  listened.  Away down re somewheres I hears a small
whoop,  up comes my spirits.  I went tearing after it, listening
sharp to hear it again.   next time it come I see I warn’t heading
for it, but heading away to  right of it.    next time I was
heading away to  left of it-- not gaining on it much eir, for
I was flying around, this way  that  t’or, but it was going
straight ahead all  time.

I did wish  fool would think to beat a tin pan,  beat it all 
time, but he never did,  it was  still places between  whoops
that was making  trouble for me.  Well, I fought along,  directly
I hears  whoop _behind_ me.  I was tangled good now.  That was
somebody else’s whoop, or else I was turned around.

I throwed  paddle down.  I heard  whoop again; it was behind me
yet, but in a different place; it kept coming,  kept changing its
place,  I kept answering, till by  by it was in front of me again,
 I knowed  current had swung  canoe’s head down-stream,  I
was all right if that was Jim  not some or raftsman hollering.
 I couldn’t tell nothing about voices in a fog, for nothing don’t look
natural nor sound natural in a fog.

 whooping went on,  in about a minute I come a-booming down on a
cut bank with smoky ghosts of big trees on it,   current throwed
me off to  left  shot by, amongst a lot of snags that fairly
roared,  currrent was tearing by m so swift.

In anor second or two it was solid white  still again.  I set
perfectly still n, listening to my heart thump,  I reckon I didn’t
draw a breath while it thumped a hundred.

I just give up n.  I knowed what  matter was.  That cut bank
was an isl,  Jim had gone down t’or side of it.  It warn’t no
towhead that you could float by in ten minutes.  It had  big timber
of a regular isl; it might be five or six miles long  more than
half a mile wide.

I kept quiet, with my ears cocked, about fifteen minutes, I reckon.  I
was floating along, of course, four or five miles an hour; but you don’t
ever think of that.  No, you _feel_ like you are laying dead still on
 water;  if a little glimpse of a snag slips by you don’t think to
yourself how fast _you’re_ going, but you catch your breath  think,
my! how that snag’s tearing along.  If you think it ain’t dismal 
lonesome out in a fog that way by yourself in  night, you try it
once--you’ll see.

Next, for about a half an hour, I whoops now  n; at last I hears
 answer a long ways off,  tries to follow it, but I couldn’t do
it,  directly I judged I’d got into a nest of towheads, for I had
little dim glimpses of m on both sides of me--sometimes just a narrow
channel between,  some that I couldn’t see I knowed was re because
I’d hear  wash of  current against  old dead brush  trash
that hung over  banks.  Well, I warn’t long loosing  whoops down
amongst  towheads;  I only tried to chase m a little while,
anyway, because it was worse than chasing a Jack-o’-lantern.  You never
knowed a sound dodge around so,  swap places so quick  so much.

I had to claw away from  bank pretty lively four or five times, to
keep from knocking  isls out of  river;  so I judged 
raft must be butting into  bank every now  n, or else it would
get furr ahead  clear out of hearing--it was floating a little
faster than what I was.

Well, I seemed to be in  open river again by  by, but I couldn’t
hear no sign of a whoop nowheres.  I reckoned Jim had fetched up on a
snag, maybe,  it was all up with him.  I was good  tired, so I
laid down in  canoe  said I wouldn’t bor no more.  I didn’t
want to go to sleep, of course; but I was so sleepy I couldn’t help it;
so I thought I would take jest one little cat-nap.

But I reckon it was more than a cat-nap, for when I waked up  stars
was shining bright,  fog was all gone,  I was spinning down a
big bend stern first.  First I didn’t know where I was; I thought I was
dreaming;  when things began to come back to me y seemed to come
up dim out of last week.

It was a monstrous big river here, with  tallest   thickest
kind of timber on both banks; just a solid wall, as well as I could see
by  stars.  I looked away down-stream,  seen a black speck on 
water. I took after it; but when I got to it it warn’t nothing but a
couple of sawlogs made fast toger.  n I see anor speck, 
chased that; n anor,  this time I was right.  It was  raft.

When I got to it Jim was setting re with his head down between his
knees, asleep, with his right arm hanging over  steering-oar.  
or oar was smashed off,   raft was littered up with leaves 
branches  dirt.  So she’d had a rough time.

I made fast  laid down under Jim’s nose on  raft,  began to
gap,  stretch my fists out against Jim,  says:

“Hello, Jim, have I been asleep?  Why didn’t you stir me up?”

“Goodness gracious, is dat you, Huck?  En you ain’ dead--you ain’
drownded--you’s back agin?  It’s too good for true, honey, it’s too good
for true. Lemme look at you chile, lemme feel o’ you.  No, you ain’
dead! you’s back agin, ‘live en soun’, jis de same ole Huck--de same ole
Huck, thanks to goodness!”

“What’s  matter with you, Jim?  You been a-drinking?”

“Drinkin’?  Has I ben a-drinkin’?  Has I had a chance to be a-drinkin’?”

“Well, n, what makes you talk so wild?”

“How does I talk wild?”

“_How_?  Why, hain’t you been talking about my coming back,  all that
stuff, as if I’d been gone away?”

“Huck--Huck Finn, you look me in de eye; look me in de eye.  _Hain’t_ you
ben gone away?”

“Gone away?  Why, what in  nation do you mean?  I hain’t been gone
anywheres.  Where would I go to?”

“Well, looky here, boss, dey’s sumf’n wrong, dey is.  Is I _me_, or who
_is_ I? Is I heah, or whah _is_ I?  Now dat’s what I wants to know.”

“Well, I think you’re here, plain enough, but I think you’re a
tangle-headed old fool, Jim.”

“I is, is I?  Well, you answer me dis:  Didn’t you tote out de line in
de canoe fer to make fas’ to de tow-head?”

“No, I didn’t.  What tow-head?  I hain’t see no tow-head.”

“You hain’t seen no towhead?  Looky here, didn’t de line pull loose en
de raf’ go a-hummin’ down de river, en leave you en de canoe behine in
de fog?”

“What fog?”

“Why, de fog!--de fog dat’s been aroun’ all night.  En didn’t you whoop,
en didn’t I whoop, tell we got mix’ up in de isls en one un us got
los’ en t’or one was jis’ as good as los’, ‘kase he didn’ know whah
he wuz? En didn’t I bust up agin a lot er dem isls en have a turrible
time en mos’ git drownded?  Now ain’ dat so, boss--ain’t it so?  You
answer me dat.”

“Well, this is too many for me, Jim.  I hain’t seen no fog, nor no
isls, nor no troubles, nor nothing.  I been setting here talking with
you all night till you went to sleep about ten minutes ago,  I reckon
I done  same.  You couldn’t a got drunk in that time, so of course
you’ve been dreaming.”

“Dad fetch it, how is I gwyne to dream all dat in ten minutes?”

“Well, hang it all, you did dream it, because re didn’t any of it
happen.”

“But, Huck, it’s all jis’ as plain to me as--”

“It don’t make no difference how plain it is; re ain’t nothing in it.
I know, because I’ve been here all  time.”

Jim didn’t say nothing for about five minutes, but set re studying
over it.  n he says:

“Well, den, I reck’n I did dream it, Huck; but dog my cats ef it ain’t
de powerfullest dream I ever see.  En I hain’t ever had no dream b’fo’
dat’s tired me like dis one.”

“Oh, well, that’s all right, because a dream does tire a body like
everything sometimes.  But this one was a staving dream; tell me all
about it, Jim.”

So Jim went to work  told me  whole thing right through, just as
it happened, only he painted it up considerable.  n he said he must
start in  “‘terpret” it, because it was sent for a warning.  He said
 first towhead stood for a man that would try to do us some good, but
 current was anor man that would get us away from him.   whoops
was warnings that would come to us every now  n,  if we didn’t
try hard to make out to underst m y’d just take us into bad
luck, ‘stead of keeping us out of it.   lot of towheads was troubles
we was going to get into with quarrelsome people  all kinds of mean
folks, but if we minded our business  didn’t talk back  aggravate
m, we would pull through  get out of  fog  into  big
clear river, which was  free States,  wouldn’t have no more
trouble.

It had clouded up pretty dark just after I got on to  raft, but it
was clearing up again now.

“Oh, well, that’s all interpreted well enough as far as it goes, Jim,” I
says; “but what does _se_ things st for?”

It was  leaves  rubbish on  raft   smashed oar.  You
could see m first-rate now.

Jim looked at  trash,  n looked at me,  back at  trash
again.  He had got  dream fixed so strong in his head that he
couldn’t seem to shake it loose  get  facts back into its place
again right away.  But when he did get  thing straightened around he
looked at me steady without ever smiling,  says:

“What do dey stan’ for?  I’se gwyne to tell you.  When I got all wore
out wid work, en wid de callin’ for you, en went to sleep, my heart wuz
mos’ broke bekase you wuz los’, en I didn’ k’yer no’ mo’ what become
er me en de raf’.  En when I wake up en fine you back agin, all safe
en soun’, de tears come, en I could a got down on my knees en kiss yo’
foot, I’s so thankful. En all you wuz thinkin’ ‘bout wuz how you could
make a fool uv ole Jim wid a lie.  Dat truck dah is _trash_; en trash
is what people is dat puts dirt on de head er dey fren’s en makes ‘em
ashamed.”

n he got up slow  walked to  wigwam,  went in re without
saying anything but that.  But that was enough.  It made me feel so mean
I could almost kissed _his_ foot to get him to take it back.

It was fifteen minutes before I could work myself up to go  humble
myself to a nigger; but I done it,  I warn’t ever sorry for it
afterwards, neir.  I didn’t do him no more mean tricks,  I
wouldn’t done that one if I’d a knowed it would make him feel that way.




CHAPTER XVI.

WE slept most all day,  started out at night, a little ways behind a
monstrous long raft that was as long going by as a procession.  She had
four long sweeps at each end, so we judged she carried as many as thirty
men, likely.  She had five big wigwams aboard, wide apart,  an open
camp fire in  middle,  a tall flag-pole at each end.  re was a
power of style about her.  It _amounted_ to something being a raftsman
on such a craft as that.

We went drifting down into a big bend,   night clouded up  got
hot.   river was very wide,  was walled with solid timber on
both sides; you couldn’t see a break in it hardly ever, or a light.  We
talked about Cairo,  wondered wher we would know it when we got to
it.  I said likely we wouldn’t, because I had heard say re warn’t but
about a dozen houses re,  if y didn’t happen to have m lit
up, how was we going to know we was passing a town?  Jim said if  two
big rivers joined toger re, that would show.  But I said maybe
we might think we was passing  foot of an isl  coming into 
same old river again. That disturbed Jim-- me too.  So  question
was, what to do?  I said, paddle ashore  first time a light showed,
 tell m pap was behind, coming along with a trading-scow, 
was a green h at  business,  wanted to know how far it was to
Cairo.  Jim thought it was a good idea, so we took a smoke on it 
waited.

re warn’t nothing to do now but to look out sharp for  town, 
not pass it without seeing it.  He said he’d be mighty sure to see it,
because he’d be a free man  minute he seen it, but if he missed it
he’d be in a slave country again  no more show for freedom.  Every
little while he jumps up  says:

“Dah she is?”

But it warn’t.  It was Jack-o’-lanterns, or lightning bugs; so he set
down again,  went to watching, same as before.  Jim said it made him
all over trembly  feverish to be so close to freedom.  Well, I can
tell you it made me all over trembly  feverish, too, to hear him,
because I begun to get it through my head that he _was_ most free--
who was to blame for it?  Why, _me_.  I couldn’t get that out of my
conscience, no how nor no way. It got to troubling me so I couldn’t
rest; I couldn’t stay still in one place.  It hadn’t ever come home to
me before, what this thing was that I was doing.  But now it did;  it
stayed with me,  scorched me more  more.  I tried to make out to
myself that I warn’t to blame, because I didn’t run Jim off from his
rightful owner; but it warn’t no use, conscience up  says, every
time, “But you knowed he was running for his freedom,  you could a
paddled ashore  told somebody.”  That was so--I couldn’t get around
that noway.  That was where it pinched.  Conscience says to me, “What
had poor Miss Watson done to you that you could see her nigger go off
right under your eyes  never say one single word?  What did that poor
old woman do to you that you could treat her so mean?  Why, she tried to
learn you your book, she tried to learn you your manners, she tried to
be good to you every way she knowed how.  _That’s_ what she done.”

I got to feeling so mean  so miserable I most wished I was dead.  I
fidgeted up  down  raft, abusing myself to myself,  Jim was
fidgeting up  down past me.  We neir of us could keep still.
 Every time he danced around  says, “Dah’s Cairo!” it went through me
like a shot,  I thought if it _was_ Cairo I reckoned I would die of
miserableness.

Jim talked out loud all  time while I was talking to myself.  He was
saying how  first thing he would do when he got to a free State he
would go to saving up money  never spend a single cent,  when he
got enough he would buy his wife, which was owned on a farm close to
