where Miss Watson lived;  n y would both work to buy 
two children,  if ir master wouldn’t sell m, y’d get an
Ab’litionist to go  steal m.

It most froze me to hear such talk.  He wouldn’t ever dared to talk such
talk in his life before.  Just see what a difference it made in him 
minute he judged he was about free.  It was according to  old saying,
“Give a nigger an inch  he’ll take an ell.”  Thinks I, this is what
comes of my not thinking.  Here was this nigger, which I had as good
as helped to run away, coming right out flat-footed  saying he would
steal his children--children that belonged to a man I didn’t even know; a
man that hadn’t ever done me no harm.

I was sorry to hear Jim say that, it was such a lowering of him.  My
conscience got to stirring me up hotter than ever, until at last I says
to it, “Let up on me--it ain’t too late yet--I’ll paddle ashore at 
first light  tell.”  I felt easy  happy  light as a fear
right off.  All my troubles was gone.  I went to looking out sharp for a
light,  sort of singing to myself.  By  by one showed.  Jim sings
out:

“We’s safe, Huck, we’s safe!  Jump up  crack yo’ heels!  Dat’s de
good ole Cairo at las’, I jis knows it!”

I says:

“I’ll take  canoe  go  see, Jim.  It mightn’t be, you know.”

He jumped  got  canoe ready,  put his old coat in  bottom
for me to set on,  give me  paddle;  as I shoved off, he says:

“Pooty soon I’ll be a-shout’n’ for joy, en I’ll say, it’s all on
accounts o’ Huck; I’s a free man, en I couldn’t ever ben free ef it
hadn’ ben for Huck; Huck done it.  Jim won’t ever forgit you, Huck;
you’s de bes’ fren’ Jim’s ever had; en you’s de _only_ fren’ ole Jim’s
got now.”

I was paddling off, all in a sweat to tell on him; but when he says
this, it seemed to kind of take  tuck all out of me.  I went along
slow n,  I warn’t right down certain wher I was glad I started
or wher I warn’t.  When I was fifty yards off, Jim says:

“Dah you goes, de ole true Huck; de on’y white genlman dat ever kep’ his
promise to ole Jim.”

Well, I just felt sick.  But I says, I _got_ to do it--I can’t get _out_
of it.  Right n along comes a skiff with two men in it with guns, 
y stopped  I stopped.  One of m says:

“What’s that yonder?”

“A piece of a raft,” I says.

“Do you belong on it?”

“Yes, sir.”

“Any men on it?”

“Only one, sir.”

“Well, re’s five niggers run off to-night up yonder, above  head
of  bend.  Is your man white or black?”

I didn’t answer up prompt.  I tried to, but  words wouldn’t come. I
tried for a second or two to brace up  out with it, but I warn’t man
enough--hadn’t  spunk of a rabbit.  I see I was weakening; so I just
give up trying,  up  says:

“He’s white.”

“I reckon we’ll go  see for ourselves.”

“I wish you would,” says I, “because it’s pap that’s re,  maybe
you’d help me tow  raft ashore where  light is.  He’s sick-- so
is mam  Mary Ann.”

“Oh,  devil! we’re in a hurry, boy.  But I s’pose we’ve got to.
 Come, buckle to your paddle,  let’s get along.”

I buckled to my paddle  y laid to ir oars.  When we had made a
stroke or two, I says:

“Pap’ll be mighty much obleeged to you, I can tell you.  Everybody goes
away when I want m to help me tow  raft ashore,  I can’t do it
by myself.”

“Well, that’s infernal mean.  Odd, too.  Say, boy, what’s  matter
with your far?”

“It’s --a----well, it ain’t anything much.”

y stopped pulling.  It warn’t but a mighty little ways to  raft
now. One says:

“Boy, that’s a lie.  What _is_  matter with your pap?  Answer up
square now,  it’ll be  better for you.”

“I will, sir, I will, honest--but don’t leave us, please.  It’s
----Gentlemen, if you’ll only pull ahead,  let me heave you 
headline, you won’t have to come a-near  raft--please do.”

“Set her back, John, set her back!” says one.  y backed water.  “Keep
away, boy--keep to looard.  Confound it, I just expect  wind has
blowed it to us.  Your pap’s got  small-pox,  you know it precious
well.  Why didn’t you come out  say so?  Do you want to spread it all
over?”

“Well,” says I, a-blubbering, “I’ve told everybody before,  y just
went away  left us.”

“Poor devil, re’s something in that.  We are right down sorry for
you, but we--well, hang it, we don’t want  small-pox, you see.  Look
here, I’ll tell you what to do.  Don’t you try to l by yourself, or
you’ll smash everything to pieces.  You float along down about twenty
miles,  you’ll come to a town on  left-h side of  river.  It
will be long after sun-up n,  when you ask for help you tell m
your folks are all down with chills  fever.  Don’t be a fool again,
 let people guess what is  matter.  Now we’re trying to do you a
kindness; so you just put twenty miles between us, that’s a good boy.
 It wouldn’t do any good to l yonder where  light is--it’s only a
wood-yard. Say, I reckon your far’s poor,  I’m bound to say he’s
in pretty hard luck.  Here, I’ll put a twenty-dollar gold piece on this
board,  you get it when it floats by.  I feel mighty mean to leave
you; but my kingdom! it won’t do to fool with small-pox, don’t you see?”

“Hold on, Parker,” says  or man, “here’s a twenty to put on 
board for me.  Good-bye, boy; you do as Mr. Parker told you,  you’ll
be all right.”

“That’s so, my boy--good-bye, good-bye.  If you see any runaway niggers
you get help  nab m,  you can make some money by it.”

“Good-bye, sir,” says I; “I won’t let no runaway niggers get by me if I
can help it.”

y went off  I got aboard  raft, feeling bad  low, because I
knowed very well I had done wrong,  I see it warn’t no use for me
to try to learn to do right; a body that don’t get _started_ right when
he’s little ain’t got no show--when  pinch comes re ain’t nothing
to back him up  keep him to his work,  so he gets beat.  n I
thought a minute,  says to myself, hold on; s’pose you’d a done right
 give Jim up, would you felt better than what you do now?  No, says
I, I’d feel bad--I’d feel just  same way I do now.  Well, n, says
I, what’s  use you learning to do right when it’s troublesome to do
right  ain’t no trouble to do wrong,   wages is just  same?
 I was stuck.  I couldn’t answer that.  So I reckoned I wouldn’t bor
no more about it, but after this always do whichever come hiest at
 time.

I went into  wigwam; Jim warn’t re.  I looked all around; he
warn’t anywhere.  I says:

“Jim!”

“Here I is, Huck.  Is dey out o’ sight yit?  Don’t talk loud.”

He was in  river under  stern oar, with just his nose out.  I told
him y were out of sight, so he come aboard.  He says:

“I was a-listenin’ to all de talk, en I slips into de river en was gwyne
to shove for sho’ if dey come aboard.  Den I was gwyne to swim to de
raf’ agin when dey was gone.  But lawsy, how you did fool ‘em, Huck!
 Dat _wuz_ de smartes’ dodge!  I tell you, chile, I’spec it save’ ole
Jim--ole Jim ain’t going to forgit you for dat, honey.”

n we talked about  money.  It was a pretty good raise--twenty
dollars apiece.  Jim said we could take deck passage on a steamboat
now,   money would last us as far as we wanted to go in  free
States. He said twenty mile more warn’t far for  raft to go, but he
wished we was already re.

Towards daybreak we tied up,  Jim was mighty particular about hiding
 raft good.  n he worked all day fixing things in bundles, 
getting all ready to quit rafting.

That night about ten we hove in sight of  lights of a town away down
in a left-h bend.

I went off in  canoe to ask about it.  Pretty soon I found a man out
in  river with a skiff, setting a trot-line.  I ranged up  says:

“Mister, is that town Cairo?”

“Cairo? no.  You must be a blame’ fool.”

“What town is it, mister?”

“If you want to know, go  find out.  If you stay here borin’
around me for about a half a minute longer you’ll get something you
won’t want.”

I paddled to  raft.  Jim was awful disappointed, but I said never
mind, Cairo would be  next place, I reckoned.

We passed anor town before daylight,  I was going out again; but
it was high ground, so I didn’t go.  No high ground about Cairo, Jim
said. I had forgot it.  We laid up for  day on a towhead tolerable
close to  left-h bank.  I begun to suspicion something.  So did
Jim.  I says:

“Maybe we went by Cairo in  fog that night.”

He says:

“Doan’ le’s talk about it, Huck.  Po’ niggers can’t have no luck.  I
awluz ‘spected dat rattlesnake-skin warn’t done wid its work.”

“I wish I’d never seen that snake-skin, Jim--I do wish I’d never laid
eyes on it.”

“It ain’t yo’ fault, Huck; you didn’ know.  Don’t you blame yo’self
‘bout it.”

When it was daylight, here was  clear Ohio water inshore, sure
enough,  outside was  old regular Muddy!  So it was all up with
Cairo.

We talked it all over.  It wouldn’t do to take to  shore; we couldn’t
take  raft up  stream, of course.  re warn’t no way but to wait
for dark,  start back in  canoe  take  chances.  So we slept
all day amongst  cottonwood thicket, so as to be fresh for  work,
 when we went back to  raft about dark  canoe was gone!

We didn’t say a word for a good while.  re warn’t anything to
say.  We both knowed well enough it was some more work of 
rattlesnake-skin; so what was  use to talk about it?  It would only
look like we was finding fault,  that would be bound to fetch more
bad luck-- keep on fetching it, too, till we knowed enough to keep
still.

By  by we talked about what we better do,  found re warn’t no
way but just to go along down with  raft till we got a chance to buy
a canoe to go back in.  We warn’t going to borrow it when re warn’t
anybody around,  way pap would do, for that might set people after
us.

So we shoved out after dark on  raft.

Anybody that don’t believe yet that it’s foolishness to hle a
snake-skin, after all that that snake-skin done for us, will believe it
now if y read on  see what more it done for us.

 place to buy canoes is off of rafts laying up at shore.  But we
didn’t see no rafts laying up; so we went along during three hours 
more.  Well,  night got gray  rur thick, which is  next
meanest thing to fog.  You can’t tell  shape of  river,  you
can’t see no distance. It got to be very late  still,  n along
comes a steamboat up  river.  We lit  lantern,  judged she
would see it.  Up-stream boats didn’t generly come close to us; y
go out  follow  bars  hunt for easy water under  reefs; but
nights like this y bull right up  channel against  whole river.

We could hear her pounding along, but we didn’t see her good till she
was close.  She aimed right for us.  Often y do that  try to see
how close y can come without touching; sometimes  wheel bites off
a sweep,  n  pilot sticks his head out  laughs,  thinks
he’s mighty smart.  Well, here she comes,  we said she was going to
try  shave us; but she didn’t seem to be sheering off a bit.  She
was a big one,  she was coming in a hurry, too, looking like a black
cloud with rows of glow-worms around it; but all of a sudden she bulged
out, big  scary, with a long row of wide-open furnace doors shining
like red-hot teeth,  her monstrous bows  guards hanging right
over us.  re was a yell at us,  a jingling of bells to stop 
engines, a powwow of cussing,  whistling of steam-- as Jim went
overboard on one side  I on  or, she come smashing straight
through  raft.

I dived-- I aimed to find  bottom, too, for a thirty-foot wheel
had got to go over me,  I wanted it to have plenty of room.  I could
always stay under water a minute; this time I reckon I stayed under a
minute  a half.  n I bounced for  top in a hurry, for I was
nearly busting.  I popped out to my armpits  blowed  water out of
my nose,  puffed a bit.  Of course re was a booming current; 
of course that boat started her engines again ten seconds after she
stopped m, for y never cared much for raftsmen; so now she was
churning along up  river, out of sight in  thick wear, though I
could hear her.

I sung out for Jim about a dozen times, but I didn’t get any answer;
so I grabbed a plank that touched me while I was “treading water,” 
struck out for shore, shoving it ahead of me.  But I made out to see
that  drift of  current was towards  left-h shore, which
meant that I was in a crossing; so I changed off  went that way.

It was one of se long, slanting, two-mile crossings; so I was a good
long time in getting over.  I made a safe ling,  clumb up 
bank. I couldn’t see but a little ways, but I went poking along over
rough ground for a quarter of a mile or more,  n I run across a
big old-fashioned double log-house before I noticed it.  I was going to
rush by  get away, but a lot of dogs jumped out  went to howling
 barking at me,  I knowed better than to move anor peg.




CHAPTER XVII.

IN about a minute somebody spoke out of a window without putting his
head out,  says:

“Be done, boys!  Who’s re?”

I says:

“It’s me.”

“Who’s me?”

“George Jackson, sir.”

“What do you want?”

“I don’t want nothing, sir.  I only want to go along by, but  dogs
won’t let me.”

“What are you prowling around here this time of night for--hey?”

“I warn’t prowling around, sir, I fell overboard off of  steamboat.”

“Oh, you did, did you?  Strike a light re, somebody.  What did you
say your name was?”

“George Jackson, sir.  I’m only a boy.”

“Look here, if you’re telling  truth you needn’t be afraid--nobody’ll
hurt you.  But don’t try to budge; st right where you are.  Rouse out
Bob  Tom, some of you,  fetch  guns.  George Jackson, is re
anybody with you?”

“No, sir, nobody.”

I heard  people stirring around in  house now,  see a light.
 man sung out:

“Snatch that light away, Betsy, you old fool--ain’t you got any sense?
Put it on  floor behind  front door.  Bob, if you  Tom are
ready, take your places.”

“All ready.”

“Now, George Jackson, do you know  Shepherdsons?”

“No, sir; I never heard of m.”

“Well, that may be so,  it mayn’t.  Now, all ready.  Step forward,
George Jackson.   mind, don’t you hurry--come mighty slow.  If re’s
anybody with you, let him keep back--if he shows himself he’ll be shot.
Come along now.  Come slow; push  door open yourself--just enough to
squeeze in, d’ you hear?”

I didn’t hurry; I couldn’t if I’d a wanted to.  I took one slow step at
a time  re warn’t a sound, only I thought I could hear my heart.
  dogs were as still as  humans, but y followed a little behind
me. When I got to  three log doorsteps I heard m unlocking 
unbarring  unbolting.  I put my h on  door  pushed it a
little  a little more till somebody said, “re, that’s enough--put
your head in.” I done it, but I judged y would take it off.

 cle was on  floor,  re y all was, looking at me, 
me at m, for about a quarter of a minute:  Three big men with guns
pointed at me, which made me wince, I tell you;  oldest, gray
 about sixty,  or two thirty or more--all of m fine 
hsome--  sweetest old gray-headed lady,  back of her two
young women which I couldn’t see right well.   old gentleman says:

“re; I reckon it’s all right.  Come in.”

As soon as I was in  old gentleman he locked  door  barred it
 bolted it,  told  young men to come in with ir guns, 
y all went in a big parlor that had a new rag carpet on  floor,
 got toger in a corner that was out of  range of  front
windows--re warn’t none on  side.  y held  cle,  took a
good look at me,  all said, “Why, _he_ ain’t a Shepherdson--no, re
ain’t any Shepherdson about him.”  n  old man said he hoped I
wouldn’t mind being searched for arms, because he didn’t mean no harm by
it--it was only to make sure.  So he didn’t pry into my pockets, but only
felt outside with his hs,  said it was all right.  He told me to
make myself easy  at home,  tell all about myself; but  old
lady says:

“Why, bless you, Saul,  poor thing’s as wet as he can be;  don’t
you reckon it may be he’s hungry?”

“True for you, Rachel--I forgot.”

So  old lady says:

“Betsy” (this was a nigger woman), “you fly around  get him something
to eat as quick as you can, poor thing;  one of you girls go  wake
up Buck  tell him--oh, here he is himself.  Buck, take this little
stranger  get  wet clos off from him  dress him up in some
of yours that’s dry.”

Buck looked about as old as me--thirteen or fourteen or along re,
though he was a little bigger than me.  He hadn’t on anything but a
shirt,  he was very frowzy-headed.  He came in gaping  digging one
fist into his eyes,  he was dragging a gun along with  or one.
He says:

“Ain’t y no Shepherdsons around?”

y said, no, ‘twas a false alarm.

“Well,” he says, “if y’d a ben some, I reckon I’d a got one.”

y all laughed,  Bob says:

“Why, Buck, y might have scalped us all, you’ve been so slow in
coming.”

“Well, nobody come after me,  it ain’t right I’m always kept down; I
don’t get no show.”

“Never mind, Buck, my boy,” says  old man, “you’ll have show enough,
all in good time, don’t you fret about that.  Go ‘long with you now, 
do as your mor told you.”

When we got up-stairs to his room he got me a coarse shirt  a
roundabout  pants of his,  I put m on.  While I was at it he
asked me what my name was, but before I could tell him he started to
tell me about a bluejay  a young rabbit he had catched in  woods
day before yesterday,  he asked me where Moses was when  cle
went out.  I said I didn’t know; I hadn’t heard about it before, no way.

“Well, guess,” he says.

“How’m I going to guess,” says I, “when I never heard tell of it
before?”

“But you can guess, can’t you?  It’s just as easy.”

“_Which_ cle?”  I says.

“Why, any cle,” he says.

“I don’t know where he was,” says I; “where was he?”

“Why, he was in  _dark_!  That’s where he was!”

“Well, if you knowed where he was, what did you ask me for?”

“Why, blame it, it’s a riddle, don’t you see?  Say, how long are you
going to stay here?  You got to stay always.  We can just have booming
times--y don’t have no school now.  Do you own a dog?  I’ve got a
dog-- he’ll go in  river  bring out chips that you throw in.  Do
you like to comb up Sundays,  all that kind of foolishness?  You bet
I don’t, but ma she makes me.  Confound se ole britches!  I reckon
I’d better put ‘em on, but I’d rur not, it’s so warm.  Are you all
ready? All right.  Come along, old hoss.”

Cold corn-pone, cold corn-beef, butter  buttermilk--that is what y
had for me down re,  re ain’t nothing better that ever I’ve
come across yet.  Buck  his ma  all of m smoked cob pipes,
except  nigger woman, which was gone,   two young women.  y
all smoked  talked,  I eat  talked.   young women had
quilts around m,  ir hair down ir backs.  y all asked me
questions,  I told m how pap  me  all  family was living
on a little farm down at  bottom of Arkansaw,  my sister Mary Ann
run off  got married  never was heard of no more,  Bill went
to hunt m  he warn’t heard of no more,  Tom  Mort died,
 n re warn’t nobody but just me  pap left,  he was just
trimmed down to nothing, on account of his troubles; so when he died
I took what re was left, because  farm didn’t belong to us, 
started up  river, deck passage,  fell overboard;  that was how
I come to be here.  So y said I could have a home re as long as I
wanted it.  n it was most daylight  everybody went to bed,  I
went to bed with Buck,  when I waked up in  morning, drat it all,
I had forgot what my name was. So I laid re about an hour trying to
think,  when Buck waked up I says:

“Can you spell, Buck?”

“Yes,” he says.

“I bet you can’t spell my name,” says I.

“I bet you what you dare I can,” says he.

“All right,” says I, “go ahead.”

“G-e-o-r-g-e J-a-x-o-n--re now,” he says.

“Well,” says I, “you done it, but I didn’t think you could.  It ain’t no
slouch of a name to spell--right off without studying.”

I set it down, private, because somebody might want _me_ to spell it
next,  so I wanted to be hy with it  rattle it off like I was
used to it.

It was a mighty nice family,  a mighty nice house, too.  I hadn’t
seen no house out in  country before that was so nice  had so much
style.  It didn’t have an iron latch on  front door, nor a wooden one
with a buckskin string, but a brass knob to turn,  same as houses in
town. re warn’t no bed in  parlor, nor a sign of a bed; but heaps
of parlors in towns has beds in m.  re was a big fireplace that
was bricked on  bottom,   bricks was kept clean  red by
pouring water on m  scrubbing m with anor brick; sometimes
y wash m over with red water-paint that y call Spanish-brown,
same as y do in town.  y had big brass dog-irons that could hold
up a saw-log. re was a clock on  middle of  mantelpiece, with
a picture of a town painted on  bottom half of  glass front, 
a round place in  middle of it for  sun,  you could see 
pendulum swinging behind it.  It was beautiful to hear that clock tick;
 sometimes when one of se peddlers had been along  scoured her
up  got her in good shape, she would start in  strike a hundred
 fifty before she got tuckered out.  y wouldn’t took any money for
her.

Well, re was a big outlish parrot on each side of  clock,
made out of something like chalk,  painted up gaudy.  By one of 
parrots was a cat made of crockery,  a crockery dog by  or;
 when you pressed down on m y squeaked, but didn’t open
ir mouths nor look different nor interested.  y squeaked through
underneath.  re was a couple of big wild-turkey-wing fans spread out
behind those things.  On  table in  middle of  room was a kind
of a lovely crockery basket that had apples  oranges  peaches 
grapes piled up in it, which was much redder  yellower  prettier
than real ones is, but y warn’t real because you could see where
pieces had got chipped off  showed  white chalk, or whatever it
was, underneath.

This table had a cover made out of beautiful oilcloth, with a red 
blue spread-eagle painted on it,  a painted border all around.  It
come all  way from Philadelphia, y said.  re was some books,
too, piled up perfectly exact, on each corner of  table.  One was a
big family Bible full of pictures.  One was Pilgrim’s Progress, about a
man that left his family, it didn’t say why.  I read considerable in it
now  n.   statements was interesting, but tough.  Anor was
Friendship’s Offering, full of beautiful stuff  poetry; but I didn’t
read  poetry.  Anor was Henry Clay’s Speeches,  anor was Dr.
Gunn’s Family Medicine, which told you all about what to do if a body
was sick or dead.  re was a hymn book,  a lot of or books.  
re was nice split-bottom chairs,  perfectly sound, too--not bagged
down in  middle  busted, like an old basket.

y had pictures hung on  walls--mainly Washingtons  Lafayettes,
 battles,  Highl Marys,  one called “Signing 
Declaration.” re was some that y called crayons, which one of 
daughters which was dead made her own self when she was only
fifteen years old.  y was different from any pictures I ever see
before--blacker, mostly, than is common.  One was a woman in a slim black
dress, belted small under  armpits, with bulges like a cabbage in
 middle of  sleeves,  a large black scoop-shovel bonnet with
a black veil,  white slim ankles crossed about with black tape, 
very wee black slippers, like a chisel,  she was leaning pensive on a
tombstone on her right elbow, under a weeping willow,  her or h
hanging down her side holding a white hkerchief  a reticule,
 underneath  picture it said “Shall I Never See e More Alas.”
  Anor one was a young lady with her hair all combed up straight
to  top of her head,  knotted re in front of a comb like a
chair-back,  she was crying into a hkerchief  had a dead bird
laying on its back in her or h with its heels up,  underneath
 picture it said “I Shall Never Hear Thy Sweet Chirrup More Alas.”
  re was one where a young lady was at a window looking up at 
moon,  tears running down her cheeks;  she had an open letter in
one h with black sealing wax showing on one edge of it,  she was
mashing a locket with a chain to it against her mouth,  underneath
 picture it said “ Art Thou Gone Yes Thou Art Gone Alas.”  se
was all nice pictures, I reckon, but I didn’t somehow seem to take
to m, because if ever I was down a little y always give me 
fan-tods.  Everybody was sorry she died, because she had laid out a lot
more of se pictures to do,  a body could see by what she had done
what y had lost.  But I reckoned that with her disposition she was
having a better time in  graveyard.  She was at work on what y
said was her greatest picture when she took sick,  every day 
every night it was her prayer to be allowed to live till she got it
done, but she never got  chance.  It was a picture of a young woman
in a long white gown, sting on  rail of a bridge all ready to jump
off, with her hair all down her back,  looking up to  moon, with
 tears running down her face,  she had two arms folded across her
breast,  two arms stretched out in front,  two more reaching up
towards  moon--  idea was to see which pair would look best,
 n scratch out all  or arms; but, as I was saying, she died
before she got her mind made up,  now y kept this picture over 
head of  bed in her room,  every time her birthday come y hung
flowers on it.  Or times it was hid with a little curtain.   young
woman in  picture had a kind of a nice sweet face, but re was so
many arms it made her look too spidery, seemed to me.

This young girl kept a scrap-book when she was alive,  used to paste
obituaries  accidents  cases of patient suffering in it out of 
Presbyterian Observer,  write poetry after m out of her own head.
It was very good poetry. This is what she wrote about a boy by  name
of Stephen Dowling Bots that fell down a well  was drownded:

ODE TO STEPHEN DOWLING BOTS, DEC’D

 did young Stephen sicken,     did young Stephen die?  did 
sad hearts thicken,     did  mourners cry?

No; such was not  fate of    Young Stephen Dowling Bots; Though sad
hearts round him thickened,    ‘Twas not from sickness’ shots.

No whooping-cough did rack his frame,    Nor measles drear with spots;
Not se impaired  sacred name    Of Stephen Dowling Bots.

Despised love struck not with woe    That head of curly knots, Nor
stomach troubles laid him low,    Young Stephen Dowling Bots.

O no. n list with tearful eye,    Whilst I his fate do tell. His soul
did from this cold world fly    By falling down a well.

y got him out  emptied him;    Alas it was too late; His spirit
was gone for to sport aloft    In  realms of  good  great.

If Emmeline Grangerford could make poetry like that before she was
fourteen, re ain’t no telling what she could a done by  by.  Buck
said she could rattle off poetry like nothing.  She didn’t ever have to
stop to think.  He said she would slap down a line,  if she couldn’t
find anything to rhyme with it would just scratch it out  slap down
anor one,  go ahead. She warn’t particular; she could write about
anything you choose to give her to write about just so it was sadful.
Every time a man died, or a woman died, or a child died, she would be on
h with her “tribute” before he was cold.  She called m tributes.
 neighbors said it was  doctor first, n Emmeline, n 
undertaker-- undertaker never got in ahead of Emmeline but once, 
n she hung fire on a rhyme for  dead person’s name, which was
Whistler.  She warn’t ever  same after that; she never complained,
but she kinder pined away  did not live long.  Poor thing, many’s 
time I made myself go up to  little room that used to be hers  get
out her poor old scrap-book  read in it when her pictures had been
aggravating me  I had soured on her a little.  I liked all that
family, dead ones  all,  warn’t going to let anything come between
us.  Poor Emmeline made poetry about all  dead people when she was
alive,  it didn’t seem right that re warn’t nobody to make some
about her now she was gone; so I tried to sweat out a verse or two
myself, but I couldn’t seem to make it go somehow.  y kept Emmeline’s
room trim  nice,  all  things fixed in it just  way she
liked to have m when she was alive,  nobody ever slept re.
  old lady took care of  room herself, though re was plenty
of niggers,  she sewed re a good deal  read her Bible re
mostly.

Well, as I was saying about  parlor, re was beautiful curtains on
 windows:  white, with pictures painted on m of castles with vines
all down  walls,  cattle coming down to drink.  re was a little
old piano, too, that had tin pans in it, I reckon,  nothing was ever
so lovely as to hear  young ladies sing “ Last Link is Broken”
  play “ Battle of Prague” on it.   walls of all  rooms was
plastered,  most had carpets on  floors,   whole house was
whitewashed on  outside.

It was a double house,   big open place betwixt m was roofed
 floored,  sometimes  table was set re in  middle of 
day,  it was a cool, comfortable place.  Nothing couldn’t be better.
  warn’t  cooking good,  just bushels of it too!




CHAPTER XVIII.

COL.  Grangerford was a gentleman, you see.  He was a gentleman all
over;  so was his family.  He was well born, as  saying is, 
that’s worth as much in a man as it is in a horse, so  Widow Douglas
said,  nobody ever denied that she was of  first aristocracy
in our town;  pap he always said it, too, though he warn’t no more
quality than a mudcat himself.  Col.  Grangerford was very tall 
very slim,  had a darkish-paly complexion, not a sign of red in it
anywheres; he was clean shaved every morning all over his thin face, 
he had  thinnest kind of lips,   thinnest kind of nostrils, 
a high nose,  heavy eyebrows,   blackest kind of eyes, sunk so
deep back that y seemed like y was looking out of caverns at
you, as you may say.  His forehead was high,  his hair was black 
straight  hung to his shoulders. His hs was long  thin, 
every day of his life he put on a clean shirt  a full suit from head
to foot made out of linen so white it hurt your eyes to look at it;
 on Sundays he wore a blue tail-coat with brass buttons on it.  He
carried a mahogany cane with a silver head to it.  re warn’t no
frivolishness about him, not a bit,  he warn’t ever loud.  He was
as kind as he could be--you could feel that, you know,  so you had
confidence.  Sometimes he smiled,  it was good to see; but when he
straightened himself up like a liberty-pole,   lightning begun to
flicker out from under his eyebrows, you wanted to climb a tree first,
 find out what  matter was afterwards.  He didn’t ever have to
tell anybody to mind ir manners--everybody was always good-mannered
where he was.  Everybody loved to have him around, too; he was sunshine
most always--I mean he made it seem like good wear.  When he turned
into a cloudbank it was awful dark for half a minute,  that was
enough; re wouldn’t nothing go wrong again for a week.

When him   old lady come down in  morning all  family got
up out of ir chairs  give m good-day,  didn’t set down again
till y had set down.  n Tom  Bob went to  sideboard where
 decanter was,  mixed a glass of bitters  hed it to him, 
he held it in his h  waited till Tom’s  Bob’s was mixed, 
n y bowed  said, “Our duty to you, sir,  madam;”  _y_
bowed  least bit in  world  said thank you,  so y drank,
all three,  Bob  Tom poured a spoonful of water on  sugar 
 mite of whisky or apple bry in  bottom of ir tumblers, 
give it to me  Buck,  we drank to  old people too.

Bob was  oldest  Tom next--tall, beautiful men with very broad
shoulders  brown faces,  long black hair  black eyes.  y
dressed in white linen from head to foot, like  old gentleman, 
wore broad Panama hats.

n re was Miss Charlotte; she was twenty-five,  tall  proud
 gr, but as good as she could be when she warn’t stirred up; but
when she was she had a look that would make you wilt in your tracks,
like her far.  She was beautiful.

So was her sister, Miss Sophia, but it was a different kind.  She was
gentle  sweet like a dove,  she was only twenty.

Each person had ir own nigger to wait on m--Buck too.  My nigger
had a monstrous easy time, because I warn’t used to having anybody do
anything for me, but Buck’s was on  jump most of  time.

This was all re was of  family now, but re used to be
more--three sons; y got killed;  Emmeline that died.

 old gentleman owned a lot of farms  over a hundred niggers.
Sometimes a stack of people would come re, horseback, from ten or
fifteen mile around,  stay five or six days,  have such junketings
round about  on  river,  dances  picnics in  woods
daytimes,  balls at  house nights.  se people was mostly
kinfolks of  family.   men brought ir guns with m.  It was a
hsome lot of quality, I tell you.

re was anor clan of aristocracy around re--five or six
families--mostly of  name of Shepherdson.  y was as high-toned
 well born  rich  gr as  tribe of Grangerfords.  
Shepherdsons  Grangerfords used  same steamboat ling, which was
about two mile above our house; so sometimes when I went up re with a
lot of our folks I used to see a lot of  Shepherdsons re on ir
fine horses.

One day Buck  me was away out in  woods hunting,  heard a horse
coming.  We was crossing  road.  Buck says:

“Quick!  Jump for  woods!”

We done it,  n peeped down  woods through  leaves.  Pretty
soon a splendid young man come galloping down  road, setting his
horse easy  looking like a soldier.  He had his gun across his
pommel.  I had seen him before.  It was young Harney Shepherdson.  I
heard Buck’s gun go off at my ear,  Harney’s hat tumbled off from his
head.  He grabbed his gun  rode straight to  place where we was
hid.  But we didn’t wait.  We started through  woods on a run.  
woods warn’t thick, so I looked over my shoulder to dodge  bullet,
 twice I seen Harney cover Buck with his gun;  n he rode away
 way he come--to get his hat, I reckon, but I couldn’t see.  We never
stopped running till we got home.   old gentleman’s eyes blazed a
minute--‘twas pleasure, mainly, I judged--n his face sort of smood
down,  he says, kind of gentle:

“I don’t like that shooting from behind a bush.  Why didn’t you step
into  road, my boy?”

“ Shepherdsons don’t, far.  y always take advantage.”

Miss Charlotte she held her head up like a queen while Buck was telling
his tale,  her nostrils spread  her eyes snapped.   two young
men looked dark, but never said nothing.  Miss Sophia she turned pale,
but  color come back when she found  man warn’t hurt.

Soon as I could get Buck down by  corn-cribs under  trees by
ourselves, I says:

“Did you want to kill him, Buck?”

“Well, I bet I did.”

“What did he do to you?”

“Him?  He never done nothing to me.”

“Well, n, what did you want to kill him for?”

“Why, nothing--only it’s on account of  feud.”

“What’s a feud?”

“Why, where was you raised?  Don’t you know what a feud is?”

“Never heard of it before--tell me about it.”

“Well,” says Buck, “a feud is this way:  A man has a quarrel with
anor man,  kills him; n that or man’s bror kills _him_;
n  or brors, on both sides, goes for one anor; n 
_cousins_ chip in-- by  by everybody’s killed off,  re ain’t
no more feud.  But it’s kind of slow,  takes a long time.”

“Has this one been going on long, Buck?”

“Well, I should _reckon_!  It started thirty year ago, or som’ers along
re.  re was trouble ‘bout something,  n a lawsuit to settle
it;   suit went agin one of  men,  so he up  shot 
man that won  suit--which he would naturally do, of course.  Anybody
would.”

“What was  trouble about, Buck?--l?”

“I reckon maybe--I don’t know.”

“Well, who done  shooting?  Was it a Grangerford or a Shepherdson?”

“Laws, how do I know?  It was so long ago.”

“Don’t anybody know?”

“Oh, yes, pa knows, I reckon,  some of  or old people; but y
don’t know now what  row was about in  first place.”

“Has re been many killed, Buck?”

“Yes; right smart chance of funerals.  But y don’t always kill.  Pa’s
got a few buckshot in him; but he don’t mind it ‘cuz he don’t weigh
much, anyway.  Bob’s been carved up some with a bowie,  Tom’s been
hurt once or twice.”

“Has anybody been killed this year, Buck?”

“Yes; we got one  y got one.  ‘Bout three months ago my cousin
Bud, fourteen year old, was riding through  woods on t’or side
of  river,  didn’t have no weapon with him, which was blame’
foolishness,  in a lonesome place he hears a horse a-coming behind
him,  sees old Baldy Shepherdson a-linkin’ after him with his gun in
his h  his white hair a-flying in  wind;  ‘stead of jumping
off  taking to  brush, Bud ‘lowed he could out-run him; so y
had it, nip  tuck, for five mile or more,  old man a-gaining all
 time; so at last Bud seen it warn’t any use, so he stopped  faced
around so as to have  bullet holes in front, you know,   old
man he rode up  shot him down.  But he didn’t git much chance to
enjoy his luck, for inside of a week our folks laid _him_ out.”

“I reckon that old man was a coward, Buck.”

“I reckon he _warn’t_ a coward.  Not by a blame’ sight.  re ain’t a
coward amongst m Shepherdsons--not a one.   re ain’t no cowards
amongst  Grangerfords eir.  Why, that old man kep’ up his end in a
fight one day for half an hour against three Grangerfords,  come
out winner.  y was all a-horseback; he lit off of his horse  got
behind a little woodpile,  kep’ his horse before him to stop 
bullets; but  Grangerfords stayed on ir horses  capered around
 old man,  peppered away at him,  he peppered away at m.
 Him  his horse both went home pretty leaky  crippled, but 
Grangerfords had to be _fetched_ home-- one of ‘em was dead, 
anor died  next day.  No, sir; if a body’s out hunting for cowards
he don’t want to fool away any time amongst m Shepherdsons, becuz
y don’t breed any of that _kind_.”

Next Sunday we all went to church, about three mile, everybody
a-horseback.  men took ir guns along, so did Buck,  kept
m between ir knees or stood m hy against  wall.  
Shepherdsons done  same.  It was pretty ornery preaching--all about
brorly love,  such-like tiresomeness; but everybody said it was
a good sermon,  y all talked it over going home,  had such
a powerful lot to say about faith  good works  free grace 
preforeordestination,  I don’t know what all, that it did seem to me
to be one of  roughest Sundays I had run across yet.

About an hour after dinner everybody was dozing around, some in ir
chairs  some in ir rooms,  it got to be pretty dull.  Buck 
a dog was stretched out on  grass in  sun sound asleep.  I went up
to our room,  judged I would take a nap myself.  I found that sweet
Miss Sophia sting in her door, which was next to ours,  she took
me in her room  shut  door very soft,  asked me if I liked her,
 I said I did;  she asked me if I would do something for her 
not tell anybody,  I said I would.  n she said she’d forgot her
Testament,  left it in  seat at church between two or books,
 would I slip out quiet  go re  fetch it to her,  not say
nothing to nobody.  I said I would. So I slid out  slipped off up 
road,  re warn’t anybody at  church, except maybe a hog or two,
for re warn’t any lock on  door,  hogs likes a puncheon floor
in summer-time because it’s cool.  If you notice, most folks don’t go to
church only when y’ve got to; but a hog is different.

Says I to myself, something’s up; it ain’t natural for a girl to be in
such a sweat about a Testament.  So I give it a shake,  out drops a
little piece of paper with “HALF-PAST TWO” wrote on it with a pencil.  I
ransacked it, but couldn’t find anything else.  I couldn’t make anything
out of that, so I put  paper in  book again,  when I got home
 upstairs re was Miss Sophia in her door waiting for me.  She
pulled me in  shut  door; n she looked in  Testament till
she found  paper,  as soon as she read it she looked glad; 
before a body could think she grabbed me  give me a squeeze, 
said I was  best boy in  world,  not to tell anybody.  She was
mighty red in  face for a minute,  her eyes lighted up,  it
made her powerful pretty.  I was a good deal astonished, but when I got
my breath I asked her what  paper was about,  she asked me if I
had read it,  I said no,  she asked me if I could read writing,
 I told her “no, only coarse-h,”  n she said  paper
warn’t anything but a book-mark to keep her place,  I might go 
play now.

I went off down to  river, studying over this thing,  pretty soon
I noticed that my nigger was following along behind.  When we was out
of sight of  house he looked back  around a second,  n comes
a-running,  says:

“Mars Jawge, if you’ll come down into de swamp I’ll show you a whole
stack o’ water-moccasins.”

Thinks I, that’s mighty curious; he said that yesterday.  He oughter
know a body don’t love water-moccasins enough to go around hunting for
m. What is he up to, anyway?  So I says:

“All right; trot ahead.”

I followed a half a mile; n he struck out over  swamp,  waded
ankle deep as much as anor half-mile.  We come to a little flat piece
of l which was dry  very thick with trees  bushes  vines,
 he says:

“You shove right in dah jist a few steps, Mars Jawge; dah’s whah dey is.
I’s seed ‘m befo’; I don’t k’yer to see ‘em no mo’.”

n he slopped right along  went away,  pretty soon  trees hid
him.  I poked into  place a-ways  come to a little open patch
as big as a bedroom all hung around with vines,  found a man laying
re asleep--, by jings, it was my old Jim!

I waked him up,  I reckoned it was going to be a gr surprise to
him to see me again, but it warn’t.  He nearly cried he was so glad, but
he warn’t surprised.  Said he swum along behind me that night,  heard
me yell every time, but dasn’t answer, because he didn’t want nobody to
pick _him_ up  take him into slavery again.  Says he:

“I got hurt a little, en couldn’t swim fas’, so I wuz a considable ways
behine you towards de las’; when you led I reck’ned I could ketch
up wid you on de lan’ ‘dout havin’ to shout at you, but when I see dat
house I begin to go slow.  I ‘uz off too fur to hear what dey say to
you--I wuz ‘fraid o’ de dogs; but when it ‘uz all quiet agin I knowed
you’s in de house, so I struck out for de woods to wait for day.  Early
in de mawnin’ some er de niggers come along, gwyne to de fields, en dey
tuk me en showed me dis place, whah de dogs can’t track me on accounts
o’ de water, en dey brings me truck to eat every night, en tells me how
you’s a-gitt’n along.”

“Why didn’t you tell my Jack to fetch me here sooner, Jim?”

“Well, ‘twarn’t no use to ‘sturb you, Huck, tell we could do sumfn--but
we’s all right now.  I ben a-buyin’ pots en pans en vittles, as I got a
chanst, en a-patchin’ up de raf’ nights when--”

“_What_ raft, Jim?”

“Our ole raf’.”

“You mean to say our old raft warn’t smashed all to flinders?”

“No, she warn’t.  She was tore up a good deal--one en’ of her was; but
dey warn’t no great harm done, on’y our traps was mos’ all los’.  Ef we
hadn’ dive’ so deep en swum so fur under water, en de night hadn’ ben
so dark, en we warn’t so sk’yerd, en ben sich punkin-heads, as de sayin’
is, we’d a seed de raf’.  But it’s jis’ as well we didn’t, ‘kase now
she’s all fixed up agin mos’ as good as new, en we’s got a new lot o’
stuff, in de place o’ what ‘uz los’.”

“Why, how did you get hold of  raft again, Jim--did you catch her?”

“How I gwyne to ketch her en I out in de woods?  No; some er de niggers
foun’ her ketched on a snag along heah in de ben’, en dey hid her in a
crick ‘mongst de willows, en dey wuz so much jawin’ ‘bout which un ‘um
she b’long to de mos’ dat I come to heah ‘bout it pooty soon, so I ups
en settles de trouble by tellin’ ‘um she don’t b’long to none uv um, but
to you en me; en I ast ‘m if dey gwyne to grab a young white genlman’s
propaty, en git a hid’n for it?  Den I gin ‘m ten cents apiece, en dey
‘uz mighty well satisfied, en wisht some mo’ raf’s ‘ud come along en
make ‘m rich agin. Dey’s mighty good to me, dese niggers is, en whatever
I wants ‘m to do fur me I doan’ have to ast ‘m twice, honey.  Dat Jack’s
a good nigger, en pooty smart.”

“Yes, he is.  He ain’t ever told me you was here; told me to come, 
he’d show me a lot of water-moccasins.  If anything happens _he_ ain’t
mixed up in it.  He can say he never seen us toger,  it ‘ll be 
truth.”

I don’t want to talk much about  next day.  I reckon I’ll cut it
pretty short.  I waked up about dawn,  was a-going to turn over 
go to sleep again when I noticed how still it was--didn’t seem to be
anybody stirring.  That warn’t usual.  Next I noticed that Buck was
up  gone. Well, I gets up, a-wondering,  goes down stairs--nobody
around; everything as still as a mouse.  Just  same outside.  Thinks
I, what does it mean?  Down by  wood-pile I comes across my Jack, 
says:

“What’s it all about?”

Says he:

“Don’t you know, Mars Jawge?”

“No,” says I, “I don’t.”

“Well, den, Miss Sophia’s run off! ‘deed she has.  She run off in de
night some time--nobody don’t know jis’ when; run off to get married
to dat young Harney Shepherdson, you know--leastways, so dey ‘spec.  De
fambly foun’ it out ‘bout half an hour ago--maybe a little mo’--en’ I
_tell_ you dey warn’t no time los’.  Sich anor hurryin’ up guns
en hosses _you_ never see!  De women folks has gone for to stir up de
relations, en ole Mars Saul en de boys tuck dey guns en rode up de
river road for to try to ketch dat young man en kill him ‘fo’ he kin
git acrost de river wid Miss Sophia.  I reck’n dey’s gwyne to be mighty
rough times.”

“Buck went off ‘thout waking me up.”

“Well, I reck’n he _did_!  Dey warn’t gwyne to mix you up in it.
 Mars Buck he loaded up his gun en ‘lowed he’s gwyne to fetch home a
Shepherdson or bust. Well, dey’ll be plenty un ‘m dah, I reck’n, en you
bet you he’ll fetch one ef he gits a chanst.”

I took up  river road as hard as I could put.  By  by I begin to
hear guns a good ways off.  When I come in sight of  log store 
 woodpile where  steamboats ls I worked along under  trees
 brush till I got to a good place,  n I clumb up into 
forks of a cottonwood that was out of reach,  watched.  re was a
wood-rank four foot high a little ways in front of  tree,  first I
was going to hide behind that; but maybe it was luckier I didn’t.

re was four or five men cavorting around on ir horses in  open
place before  log store, cussing  yelling,  trying to get at
a couple of young chaps that was behind  wood-rank alongside of 
steamboat ling; but y couldn’t come it.  Every time one of m
showed himself on  river side of  woodpile he got shot at.  
two boys was squatting back to back behind  pile, so y could watch
both ways.

By  by  men stopped cavorting around  yelling.  y started
riding towards  store; n up gets one of  boys, draws a steady
bead over  wood-rank,  drops one of m out of his saddle.  All
 men jumped off of ir horses  grabbed  hurt one  started
to carry him to  store;  that minute  two boys started on 
run.  y got half way to  tree I was in before  men noticed.
n  men see m,  jumped on ir horses  took out after
m.  y gained on  boys, but it didn’t do no good,  boys had
too good a start; y got to  woodpile that was in front of my tree,
 slipped in behind it,  so y had  bulge on  men again.
One of  boys was Buck,   or was a slim young chap about
nineteen years old.

 men ripped around awhile,  n rode away.  As soon as y was
out of sight I sung out to Buck  told him.  He didn’t know what
to make of my voice coming out of  tree at first.  He was awful
surprised.  He told me to watch out sharp  let him know when 
men come in sight again; said y was up to some devilment or
or--wouldn’t be gone long.  I wished I was out of that tree, but I
dasn’t come down.  Buck begun to cry  rip,  ‘lowed that him 
his cousin Joe (that was  or young chap) would make up for this
day yet.  He said his far  his two brors was killed,  two
or three of  enemy.  Said  Shepherdsons laid for m in
ambush.  Buck said his far  brors ought to waited for ir
relations-- Shepherdsons was too strong for m.  I asked him what
was become of young Harney  Miss Sophia.  He said y’d got across
 river  was safe.  I was glad of that; but  way Buck did take
on because he didn’t manage to kill Harney that day he shot at him--I
hain’t ever heard anything like it.

All of a sudden, bang! bang! bang! goes three or four guns-- men had
slipped around through  woods  come in from behind without ir
horses!   boys jumped for  river--both of m hurt-- as y
swum down  current  men run along  bank shooting at m 
singing out, “Kill m, kill m!”  It made me so sick I most fell out
of  tree.  I ain’t a-going to tell _all_ that happened--it would make
me sick again if I was to do that.  I wished I hadn’t ever come ashore
that night to see such things.  I ain’t ever going to get shut of
m--lots of times I dream about m.

I stayed in  tree till it begun to get dark, afraid to come down.
Sometimes I heard guns away off in  woods;  twice I seen little
gangs of men gallop past  log store with guns; so I reckoned 
trouble was still a-going on.  I was mighty downhearted; so I made up my
mind I wouldn’t ever go anear that house again, because I reckoned I
was to blame, somehow. I judged that that piece of paper meant that Miss
Sophia was to meet Harney somewheres at half-past two  run off; 
I judged I ought to told her far about that paper   curious way
she acted,  n maybe he would a locked her up,  this awful mess
wouldn’t ever happened.

When I got down out of  tree I crept along down  river bank a
piece,  found  two bodies laying in  edge of  water, 
tugged at m till I got m ashore; n I covered up ir faces,
 got away as quick as I could.  I cried a little when I was covering
up Buck’s face, for he was mighty good to me.

It was just dark now.  I never went near  house, but struck through
 woods  made for  swamp.  Jim warn’t on his isl, so I
tramped off in a hurry for  crick,  crowded through  willows,
red-hot to jump aboard  get out of that awful country.   raft was
gone!  My souls, but I was scared!  I couldn’t get my breath for most
a minute. n I raised a yell.  A voice not twenty-five foot from me
says:

“Good lan’! is dat you, honey?  Doan’ make no noise.”

It was Jim’s voice--nothing ever sounded so good before.  I run along 
bank a piece  got aboard,  Jim he grabbed me  hugged me, he was
so glad to see me.  He says:

“Laws bless you, chile, I ‘uz right down sho’ you’s dead agin.  Jack’s
been heah; he say he reck’n you’s ben shot, kase you didn’ come home no
mo’; so I’s jes’ dis minute a startin’ de raf’ down towards de mouf er
de crick, so’s to be all ready for to shove out en leave soon as Jack
comes agin en tells me for certain you _is_ dead.  Lawsy, I’s mighty
glad to git you back again, honey.”

I says:

“All right--that’s mighty good; y won’t find me,  y’ll think
I’ve been killed,  floated down  river--re’s something up re
that ‘ll help m think so--so don’t you lose no time, Jim, but just
shove off for  big water as fast as ever you can.”

I never felt easy till  raft was two mile below re  out in
 middle of  Mississippi.  n we hung up our signal lantern, 
judged that we was free  safe once more.  I hadn’t had a bite to eat
since yesterday, so Jim he got out some corn-dodgers  buttermilk,
 pork  cabbage  greens--re ain’t nothing in  world so good
when it’s cooked right-- whilst I eat my supper we talked  had a
good time.  I was powerful glad to get away from  feuds,  so was
Jim to get away from  swamp.  We said re warn’t no home like a
raft, after all.  Or places do seem so cramped up  smory, but a
raft don’t.  You feel mighty free  easy  comfortable on a raft.




CHAPTER XIX.

TWO or three days  nights went by; I reckon I might say y swum by,
y slid along so quiet  smooth  lovely.  Here is  way we put
in  time.  It was a monstrous big river down re--sometimes a mile
 a half wide; we run nights,  laid up  hid daytimes; soon as
night was most gone we stopped navigating  tied up--nearly always
in  dead water under a towhead;  n cut young cottonwoods 
willows,  hid  raft with m.  n we set out  lines.  Next
we slid into  river  had a swim, so as to freshen up  cool
off; n we set down on  sy bottom where  water was about knee
deep,  watched  daylight come.  Not a sound anywheres--perfectly
still--just like  whole world was asleep, only sometimes  bullfrogs
a-cluttering, maybe.   first thing to see, looking away over 
water, was a kind of dull line--that was  woods on t’or side; you
couldn’t make nothing else out; n a pale place in  sky; n more
paleness spreading around; n  river softened up away off, 
warn’t black any more, but gray; you could see little dark spots
drifting along ever so far away--trading scows,  such things; 
long black streaks--rafts; sometimes you could hear a sweep screaking; or
jumbled up voices, it was so still,  sounds come so far;  by 
by you could see a streak on  water which you know by  look of 
streak that re’s a snag re in a swift current which breaks on it
 makes that streak look that way;  you see  mist curl up off
of  water,   east reddens up,   river,  you make out a
log-cabin in  edge of  woods, away on  bank on t’or side of
 river, being a woodyard, likely,  piled by m cheats so you can
throw a dog through it anywheres; n  nice breeze springs up, 
comes fanning you from over re, so cool  fresh  sweet to smell
on account of  woods   flowers; but sometimes not that way,
because y’ve left dead fish laying around, gars  such,  y
do get pretty rank;  next you’ve got  full day,  everything
smiling in  sun,   song-birds just going it!

A little smoke couldn’t be noticed now, so we would take some fish off
of  lines  cook up a hot breakfast.   afterwards we would watch
 lonesomeness of  river,  kind of lazy along,  by  by
lazy off to sleep.  Wake up by  by,  look to see what done it, 
maybe see a steamboat coughing along up-stream, so far off towards 
or side you couldn’t tell nothing about her only wher she was
a stern-wheel or side-wheel; n for about an hour re wouldn’t be
nothing to hear nor nothing to see--just solid lonesomeness.  Next
you’d see a raft sliding by, away off yonder,  maybe a galoot on it
chopping, because y’re most always doing it on a raft; you’d see 
axe flash  come down--you don’t hear nothing; you see that axe go
up again,  by  time it’s above  man’s head n you hear 
_k’chunk_!--it had took all that time to come over  water.  So we
would put in  day, lazying around, listening to  stillness.  Once
re was a thick fog,   rafts  things that went by was beating
tin pans so  steamboats wouldn’t run over m.  A scow or a
raft went by so close we could hear m talking  cussing 
laughing--heard m plain; but we couldn’t see no sign of m; it made
you feel crawly; it was like spirits carrying on that way in  air.
 Jim said he believed it was spirits; but I says:

“No; spirits wouldn’t say, ‘Dern  dern fog.’”

Soon as it was night out we shoved; when we got her out to about 
middle we let her alone,  let her float wherever  current wanted
her to; n we lit  pipes,  dangled our legs in  water, 
talked about all kinds of things--we was always naked, day  night,
whenever  mosquitoes would let us-- new clos Buck’s folks made
for me was too good to be comfortable,  besides I didn’t go much on
clos, nohow.

Sometimes we’d have that whole river all to ourselves for  longest
time. Yonder was  banks   isls, across  water;  maybe
a spark--which was a cle in a cabin window;  sometimes on  water
you could see a spark or two--on a raft or a scow, you know;  maybe
you could hear a fiddle or a song coming over from one of m crafts.
It’s lovely to live on a raft.  We had  sky up re, all speckled
with stars,  we used to lay on our backs  look up at m, 
discuss about wher y was made or only just happened.  Jim he
allowed y was made, but I allowed y happened; I judged it would
have took too long to _make_ so many.  Jim said  moon could a _laid_
m; well, that looked kind of reasonable, so I didn’t say nothing
against it, because I’ve seen a frog lay most as many, so of course it
could be done. We used to watch  stars that fell, too,  see m
streak down.  Jim allowed y’d got spoiled  was hove out of 
nest.

Once or twice of a night we would see a steamboat slipping along in 
dark,  now  n she would belch a whole world of sparks up out
of her chimbleys,  y would rain down in  river  look awful
pretty; n she would turn a corner  her lights would wink out 
her powwow shut off  leave  river still again;  by  by her
waves would get to us, a long time after she was gone,  joggle 
raft a bit,  after that you wouldn’t hear nothing for you couldn’t
tell how long, except maybe frogs or something.

After midnight  people on shore went to bed,  n for two or
three hours  shores was black--no more sparks in  cabin windows.
 se sparks was our clock-- first one that showed again meant
morning was coming, so we hunted a place to hide  tie up right away.

One morning about daybreak I found a canoe  crossed over a chute to
 main shore--it was only two hundred yards-- paddled about a mile
up a crick amongst  cypress woods, to see if I couldn’t get some
berries. Just as I was passing a place where a kind of a cowpath crossed
 crick, here comes a couple of men tearing up  path as tight as
y could foot it.  I thought I was a goner, for whenever anybody was
after anybody I judged it was _me_--or maybe Jim.  I was about to dig out
from re in a hurry, but y was pretty close to me n,  sung
out  begged me to save ir lives--said y hadn’t been doing
nothing,  was being chased for it--said re was men  dogs
a-coming.  y wanted to jump right in, but I says:

“Don’t you do it.  I don’t hear  dogs  horses yet; you’ve got time
to crowd through  brush  get up  crick a little ways; n you
take to  water  wade down to me  get in--that’ll throw  dogs
off  scent.”

y done it,  soon as y was aboard I lit out for our towhead,
 in about five or ten minutes we heard  dogs   men away off,
shouting. We heard m come along towards  crick, but couldn’t
see m; y seemed to stop  fool around a while; n, as we got
furr  furr away all  time, we couldn’t hardly hear m at
all; by  time we had left a mile of woods behind us  struck 
river, everything was quiet,  we paddled over to  towhead  hid
in  cottonwoods  was safe.

One of se fellows was about seventy or upwards,  had a bald head
 very gray whiskers.  He had an old battered-up slouch hat on, 
a greasy blue woollen shirt,  ragged old blue jeans britches stuffed
into his boot-tops,  home-knit galluses--no, he only had one.  He had
an old long-tailed blue jeans coat with slick brass buttons flung over
his arm,  both of m had big, fat, ratty-looking carpet-bags.

 or fellow was about thirty,  dressed about as ornery.  After
breakfast we all laid off  talked,   first thing that come out
was that se chaps didn’t know one anor.

“What got you into trouble?” says  baldhead to t’or chap.

“Well, I’d been selling an article to take  tartar off  teeth--
it does take it off, too,  generly  enamel along with it--but I
stayed about one night longer than I ought to,  was just in  act
of sliding out when I ran across you on  trail this side of town, 
you told me y were coming,  begged me to help you to get off.  So
I told you I was expecting trouble myself,  would scatter out _with_
you. That’s  whole yarn--what’s yourn?

“Well, I’d ben a-running’ a little temperance revival thar ‘bout a week,
 was  pet of  women folks, big  little, for I was makin’ it
mighty warm for  rummies, I _tell_ you,  takin’ as much as five
or six dollars a night--ten cents a head, children  niggers free--
business a-growin’ all  time, when somehow or anor a little report
got around last night that I had a way of puttin’ in my time with a
private jug on  sly.  A nigger rousted me out this mornin’,  told
me  people was gerin’ on  quiet with ir dogs  horses, 
y’d be along pretty soon  give me ‘bout half an hour’s start,
 n run me down if y could;  if y got me y’d tar
 fear me  ride me on a rail, sure.  I didn’t wait for no
breakfast--I warn’t hungry.”

“Old man,” said  young one, “I reckon we might double-team it
toger; what do you think?”

“I ain’t undisposed.  What’s your line--mainly?”

“Jour printer by trade; do a little in patent medicines;
ater-actor--tragedy, you know; take a turn to mesmerism  phrenology
when re’s a chance; teach singing-geography school for a change;
sling a lecture sometimes--oh, I do lots of things--most anything that
comes hy, so it ain’t work.  What’s your lay?”

“I’ve done considerble in  doctoring way in my time.  Layin’ on o’
hs is my best holt--for cancer  paralysis,  sich things;  I
k’n tell a fortune pretty good when I’ve got somebody along to find out
 facts for me.  Preachin’s my line, too,  workin’ camp-meetin’s,
 missionaryin’ around.”

Nobody never said anything for a while; n  young man hove a sigh
 says:

“Alas!”

“What ‘re you alassin’ about?” says  bald-head.

“To think I should have lived to be leading such a life,  be degraded
down into such company.”   he begun to wipe  corner of his eye
with a rag.

“Dern your skin, ain’t  company good enough for you?” says 
baldhead, pretty pert  uppish.

“Yes, it _is_ good enough for me; it’s as good as I deserve; for who
fetched me so low when I was so high?  I did myself.  I don’t blame
_you_, gentlemen--far from it; I don’t blame anybody.  I deserve it
all.  Let  cold world do its worst; one thing I know--re’s a grave
somewhere for me.  world may go on just as it’s always done,  take
everything from me--loved ones, property, everything; but it can’t take
that. Some day I’ll lie down in it  forget it all,  my poor broken
heart will be at rest.”  He went on a-wiping.

“Drot your pore broken heart,” says  baldhead; “what are you heaving
your pore broken heart at _us_ f’r?  _we_ hain’t done nothing.”

“No, I know you haven’t.  I ain’t blaming you, gentlemen.  I brought
myself down--yes, I did it myself.  It’s right I should suffer--perfectly
right--I don’t make any moan.”

“Brought you down from whar?  Whar was you brought down from?”

“Ah, you would not believe me;  world never believes--let it pass--‘tis
no matter.   secret of my birth--”

“ secret of your birth!  Do you mean to say--”

“Gentlemen,” says  young man, very solemn, “I will reveal it to you,
for I feel I may have confidence in you.  By rights I am a duke!”

Jim’s eyes bugged out when he heard that;  I reckon mine did, too.
n  baldhead says:  “No! you can’t mean it?”

“Yes.  My great-grfar, eldest son of  Duke of Bridgewater, fled
to this country about  end of  last century, to brea  pure
air of freedom; married here,  died, leaving a son, his own far
dying about  same time.   second son of  late duke seized 
titles  estates-- infant real duke was ignored.  I am  lineal
descendant of that infant--I am  rightful Duke of Bridgewater; 
here am I, forlorn, torn from my high estate, hunted of men, despised
by  cold world, ragged, worn, heart-broken,  degraded to 
companionship of felons on a raft!”

Jim pitied him ever so much,  so did I. We tried to comfort him, but
he said it warn’t much use, he couldn’t be much comforted; said if we
was a mind to acknowledge him, that would do him more good than most
anything else; so we said we would, if he would tell us how.  He said we
ought to bow when we spoke to him,  say “Your Grace,” or “My Lord,”
 or “Your Lordship”-- he wouldn’t mind it if we called him plain
“Bridgewater,” which, he said, was a title anyway,  not a name; 
one of us ought to wait on him at dinner,  do any little thing for
him he wanted done.

Well, that was all easy, so we done it.  All through dinner Jim stood
around  waited on him,  says, “Will yo’ Grace have some o’ dis or
some o’ dat?”  so on,  a body could see it was mighty pleasing to
him.

But  old man got pretty silent by  by--didn’t have much to say, 
didn’t look pretty comfortable over all that petting that was going on
around that duke.  He seemed to have something on his mind.  So, along
in  afternoon, he says:

“Looky here, Bilgewater,” he says, “I’m nation sorry for you, but you
ain’t  only person that’s had troubles like that.”

“No?”

“No you ain’t.  You ain’t  only person that’s ben snaked down
wrongfully out’n a high place.”

“Alas!”

“No, you ain’t  only person that’s had a secret of his birth.”  ,
by jings, _he_ begins to cry.

“Hold!  What do you mean?”

“Bilgewater, kin I trust you?” says  old man, still sort of sobbing.

“To  bitter death!”  He took  old man by  h  squeezed it,
 says, “That secret of your being:  speak!”

“Bilgewater, I am  late Dauphin!”

You bet you, Jim  me stared this time.  n  duke says:

“You are what?”

“Yes, my friend, it is too true--your eyes is lookin’ at this very moment
on  pore disappeared Dauphin, Looy  Seventeen, son of Looy 
Sixteen  Marry Antonette.”

“You!  At your age!  No!  You mean you’re  late Charlemagne; you must
be six or seven hundred years old, at  very least.”

“Trouble has done it, Bilgewater, trouble has done it; trouble has brung
se gray hairs  this premature balditude.  Yes, gentlemen, you
see before you, in blue jeans  misery,  werin’, exiled,
trampled-on,  sufferin’ rightful King of France.”

Well, he cried  took on so that me  Jim didn’t know hardly what to
do, we was so sorry-- so glad  proud we’d got him with us, too.
 So we set in, like we done before with  duke,  tried to comfort
_him_. But he said it warn’t no use, nothing but to be dead  done
with it all could do him any good; though he said it often made him feel
easier  better for a while if people treated him according to his
rights,  got down on one knee to speak to him,  always called him
“Your Majesty,”  waited on him first at meals,  didn’t set down
in his presence till he asked m. So Jim  me set to majestying him,
 doing this  that  t’or for him,  sting up till he
told us we might set down.  This done him heaps of good,  so he
got cheerful  comfortable.  But  duke kind of soured on him, 
didn’t look a bit satisfied with  way things was going; still,
 king acted real friendly towards him,  said  duke’s
great-grfar  all  or Dukes of Bilgewater was a good
deal thought of by _his_ far,  was allowed to come to  palace
considerable; but  duke stayed huffy a good while, till by  by 
king says:

“Like as not we got to be toger a blamed long time on this h-yer
raft, Bilgewater,  so what’s  use o’ your bein’ sour?  It ‘ll only
make things oncomfortable.  It ain’t my fault I warn’t born a duke,
it ain’t your fault you warn’t born a king--so what’s  use to worry?
 Make  best o’ things  way you find ‘em, says I--that’s my motto.
 This ain’t no bad thing that we’ve struck here--plenty grub  an easy
life--come, give us your h, duke,  le’s all be friends.”

 duke done it,  Jim  me was pretty glad to see it.  It took
away all  uncomfortableness  we felt mighty good over it, because
it would a been a miserable business to have any unfriendliness on 
raft; for what you want, above all things, on a raft, is for everybody
to be satisfied,  feel right  kind towards  ors.

It didn’t take me long to make up my mind that se liars warn’t no
kings nor dukes at all, but just low-down humbugs  frauds.  But I
never said nothing, never let on; kept it to myself; it’s  best way;
n you don’t have no quarrels,  don’t get into no trouble.  If y
wanted us to call m kings  dukes, I hadn’t no objections, ‘long as
it would keep peace in  family;  it warn’t no use to tell Jim, so
I didn’t tell him.  If I never learnt nothing else out of pap, I learnt
that  best way to get along with his kind of people is to let m
have ir own way.




CHAPTER XX.

Y asked us considerable many questions; wanted to know what we
covered up  raft that way for,  laid by in  daytime instead of
running--was Jim a runaway nigger?  Says I:

“Goodness sakes! would a runaway nigger run _south_?”

No, y allowed he wouldn’t.  I had to account for things some way, so
I says:

“My folks was living in Pike County, in Missouri, where I was born, 
y all died off but me  pa  my bror Ike.  Pa, he ‘lowed
he’d break up  go down  live with Uncle Ben, who’s got a little
one-horse place on  river, forty-four mile below Orleans.  Pa was
pretty poor,  had some debts; so when he’d squared up re warn’t
nothing left but sixteen dollars  our nigger, Jim.  That warn’t
enough to take us fourteen hundred mile, deck passage nor no or way.
 Well, when  river rose pa had a streak of luck one day; he ketched
this piece of a raft; so we reckoned we’d go down to Orleans on it.
 Pa’s luck didn’t hold out; a steamboat run over  forrard corner of
 raft one night,  we all went overboard  dove under  wheel;
Jim  me come up all right, but pa was drunk,  Ike was only four
years old, so y never come up no more.  Well, for  next day or
two we had considerable trouble, because people was always coming out in
skiffs  trying to take Jim away from me, saying y believed he was
a runaway nigger.  We don’t run daytimes no more now; nights y don’t
bor us.”

 duke says:

“Leave me alone to cipher out a way so we can run in  daytime if we
want to.  I’ll think  thing over--I’ll invent a plan that’ll fix it.
We’ll let it alone for to-day, because of course we don’t want to go by
that town yonder in daylight--it mightn’t be healthy.”

Towards night it begun to darken up  look like rain;  heat
lightning was squirting around low down in  sky,   leaves was
beginning to shiver--it was going to be pretty ugly, it was easy to see
that.  So  duke   king went to overhauling our wigwam, to see
what  beds was like.  My bed was a straw tick better than Jim’s,
which was a corn-shuck tick; re’s always cobs around about in a shuck
tick,  y poke into you  hurt;  when you roll over  dry
shucks sound like you was rolling over in a pile of dead leaves; it
makes such a rustling that you wake up.  Well,  duke allowed he would
take my bed; but  king allowed he wouldn’t.  He says:

“I should a reckoned  difference in rank would a sejested to you that
a corn-shuck bed warn’t just fitten for me to sleep on.  Your Grace ‘ll
take  shuck bed yourself.”

Jim  me was in a sweat again for a minute, being afraid re was
going to be some more trouble amongst m; so we was pretty glad when
 duke says:

“‘Tis my fate to be always ground into  mire under  iron heel of
oppression.  Misfortune has broken my once haughty spirit; I yield, I
submit; ‘tis my fate.  I am alone in  world--let me suffer; can bear
it.”

We got away as soon as it was good  dark.   king told us to st
well out towards  middle of  river,  not show a light till we
got a long ways below  town.  We come in sight of  little bunch of
lights by  by--that was  town, you know-- slid by, about a half
a mile out, all right.  When we was three-quarters of a mile below we
hoisted up our signal lantern;  about ten o’clock it come on to rain
 blow  thunder  lighten like everything; so  king told us
to both stay on watch till  wear got better; n him   duke
crawled into  wigwam  turned in for  night.  It was my watch
below till twelve, but I wouldn’t a turned in anyway if I’d had a bed,
because a body don’t see such a storm as that every day in  week, not
by a long sight.  My souls, how  wind did scream along!   every
second or two re’d come a glare that lit up  white-caps for a half
a mile around,  you’d see  isls looking dusty through  rain,
  trees thrashing around in  wind; n comes a H-WHACK!--bum!
bum! bumble-umble-um-bum-bum-bum-bum--  thunder would go rumbling
 grumbling away,  quit-- n RIP comes anor flash 
anor sockdolager.   waves most washed me off  raft sometimes,
but I hadn’t any clos on,  didn’t mind.  We didn’t have no trouble
about snags;  lightning was glaring  flittering around so constant
that we could see m plenty soon enough to throw her head this way or
that  miss m.

I had  middle watch, you know, but I was pretty sleepy by that time,
so Jim he said he would st  first half of it for me; he was always
mighty good that way, Jim was.  I crawled into  wigwam, but  king
  duke had ir legs sprawled around so re warn’t no show for
me; so I laid outside--I didn’t mind  rain, because it was warm, 
 waves warn’t running so high now.  About two y come up again,
though,  Jim was going to call me; but he changed his mind, because
he reckoned y warn’t high enough yet to do any harm; but he was
mistaken about that, for pretty soon all of a sudden along comes a
regular ripper  washed me overboard.  It most killed Jim a-laughing.
 He was  easiest nigger to laugh that ever was, anyway.

I took  watch,  Jim he laid down  snored away;  by  by
 storm let up for good  all;   first cabin-light that showed
I rousted him out,  we slid  raft into hiding quarters for 
day.

 king got out an old ratty deck of cards after breakfast,  him
  duke played seven-up a while, five cents a game.  n y got
tired of it,  allowed y would “lay out a campaign,” as y called
it.  duke went down into his carpet-bag,  fetched up a lot of
little printed bills  read m out loud.  One bill said, “
celebrated Dr. Arm de Montalban, of Paris,” would “lecture on 
Science of Phrenology” at such  such a place, on  blank day of
blank, at ten cents admission,  “furnish charts of character at
twenty-five cents apiece.”   duke said that was _him_.  In anor
bill he was  “world-renowned Shakespearian tragedian, Garrick 
Younger, of Drury Lane, London.”  In or bills he had a lot of or
names  done or wonderful things, like finding water  gold with
a “divining-rod,” “dissipating witch spells,”  so on.  By  by he
says:

“But  histrionic muse is  darling.  Have you ever trod  boards,
Royalty?”

“No,” says  king.

“You shall, n, before you’re three days older, Fallen Greur,” says
 duke.  “ first good town we come to we’ll hire a hall  do 
sword fight in Richard III.   balcony scene in Romeo  Juliet.
How does that strike you?”

“I’m in, up to  hub, for anything that will pay, Bilgewater; but, you
see, I don’t know nothing about play-actin’,  hain’t ever seen much
of it.  I was too small when pap used to have ‘em at  palace.  Do you
reckon you can learn me?”

“Easy!”

“All right.  I’m jist a-freezn’ for something fresh, anyway.  Le’s
commence right away.”

So  duke he told him all about who Romeo was  who Juliet was, 
said he was used to being Romeo, so  king could be Juliet.

“But if Juliet’s such a young gal, duke, my peeled head  my white
whiskers is goin’ to look oncommon odd on her, maybe.”

“No, don’t you worry; se country jakes won’t ever think of that.
Besides, you know, you’ll be in costume,  that makes all 
difference in  world; Juliet’s in a balcony, enjoying  moonlight
before she goes to bed,  she’s got on her night-gown  her ruffled
nightcap.  Here are  costumes for  parts.”

He got out two or three curtain-calico suits, which he said was
meedyevil armor for Richard III.  t’or chap,  a long white
cotton nightshirt  a ruffled nightcap to match.   king was
satisfied; so  duke got out his book  read  parts over in 
most splendid spread-eagle way, prancing around  acting at  same
time, to show how it had got to be done; n he give  book to 
king  told him to get his part by heart.

re was a little one-horse town about three mile down  bend, 
after dinner  duke said he had ciphered out his idea about how to run
in daylight without it being dangersome for Jim; so he allowed he would
go down to  town  fix that thing.   king allowed he would go,
too,  see if he couldn’t strike something.  We was out of coffee, so
Jim said I better go along with m in  canoe  get some.

When we got re re warn’t nobody stirring; streets empty, 
perfectly dead  still, like Sunday.  We found a sick nigger sunning
himself in a back yard,  he said everybody that warn’t too young or
too sick or too old was gone to camp-meeting, about two mile back in 
woods.   king got  directions,  allowed he’d go  work that
camp-meeting for all it was worth,  I might go, too.

 duke said what he was after was a printing-office.  We found it;
a little bit of a concern, up over a carpenter shop--carpenters 
printers all gone to  meeting,  no doors locked.  It was a dirty,
littered-up place,  had ink marks,  hbills with pictures of
horses  runaway niggers on m, all over  walls.   duke shed
his coat  said he was all right now.  So me   king lit out for
 camp-meeting.

We got re in about a half an hour fairly dripping, for it was a most
awful hot day.  re was as much as a thous people re from
twenty mile around.   woods was full of teams  wagons, hitched
everywheres, feeding out of  wagon-troughs  stomping to keep
off  flies.  re was sheds made out of poles  roofed over with
branches, where y had lemonade  gingerbread to sell,  piles of
watermelons  green corn  such-like truck.

 preaching was going on under  same kinds of sheds, only y was
bigger  held crowds of people.   benches was made out of outside
slabs of logs, with holes bored in  round side to drive sticks into
for legs. y didn’t have no backs.   preachers had high platforms
to st on at one end of  sheds.   women had on sun-bonnets;
 some had linsey-woolsey frocks, some gingham ones,  a few of 
young ones had on calico.  Some of  young men was barefooted, 
some of  children didn’t have on any clos but just a tow-linen
shirt.  Some of  old women was knitting,  some of  young folks
was courting on  sly.

 first shed we come to  preacher was lining out a hymn.  He lined
out two lines, everybody sung it,  it was kind of gr to hear it,
re was so many of m  y done it in such a rousing way; n
he lined out two more for m to sing-- so on.   people woke up
more  more,  sung louder  louder;  towards  end some
begun to groan,  some begun to shout.  n  preacher begun to
preach,  begun in earnest, too;  went weaving first to one side of
 platform  n  or,  n a-leaning down over  front
of it, with his arms  his body going all  time,  shouting his
words out with all his might;  every now  n he would hold up
his Bible  spread it open,  kind of pass it around this way 
that, shouting, “It’s  brazen serpent in  wilderness!  Look upon
it  live!”   people would shout out, “Glory!--A-a-_men_!”   so
he went on,   people groaning  crying  saying amen:

“Oh, come to  mourners’ bench! come, black with sin! (_Amen_!) come,
sick  sore! (_Amen_!) come, lame  halt  blind! (_Amen_!) come,
pore  needy, sunk in shame! (_A-A-Men_!) come, all that’s worn 
soiled  suffering!--come with a broken spirit! come with a contrite
heart! come in your rags  sin  dirt!  waters that cleanse
is free,  door of heaven sts open--oh, enter in  be at rest!”
 (_A-A-Men_!  _Glory, Glory Hallelujah!_)

 so on.  You couldn’t make out what  preacher said any more, on
account of  shouting  crying.  Folks got up everywheres in 
crowd,  worked ir way just by main strength to  mourners’
bench, with  tears running down ir faces;  when all 
mourners had got up re to  front benches in a crowd, y sung 
shouted  flung mselves down on  straw, just crazy  wild.

Well,  first I knowed  king got a-going,  you could hear him
over everybody;  next he went a-charging up on to  platform, 
 preacher he begged him to speak to  people,  he done it.  He
told m he was a pirate--been a pirate for thirty years out in 
Indian Ocean-- his crew was thinned out considerable last spring in
a fight,  he was home now to take out some fresh men,  thanks to
goodness he’d been robbed last night  put ashore off of a steamboat
without a cent,  he was glad of it; it was  blessedest thing that
ever happened to him, because he was a changed man now,  happy for
 first time in his life; , poor as he was, he was going to start
right off  work his way back to  Indian Ocean,  put in  rest
of his life trying to turn  pirates into  true path; for he could
do it better than anybody else, being acquainted with all pirate crews
in that ocean;  though it would take him a long time to get re
without money, he would get re anyway,  every time he convinced
a pirate he would say to him, “Don’t you thank me, don’t you give me no
credit; it all belongs to m dear people in Pokeville camp-meeting,
natural brors  benefactors of  race,  that dear preacher
re,  truest friend a pirate ever had!”

 n he busted into tears,  so did everybody.  n somebody
sings out, “Take up a collection for him, take up a collection!”  Well,
a half a dozen made a jump to do it, but somebody sings out, “Let _him_
pass  hat around!”  n everybody said it,  preacher too.

So  king went all through  crowd with his hat swabbing his eyes,
 blessing  people  praising m  thanking m for being
so good to  poor pirates away off re;  every little while 
prettiest kind of girls, with  tears running down ir cheeks, would
