up  ask him would he let m kiss him for to remember him by;  he
always done it;  some of m he hugged  kissed as many as five or
six times-- he was invited to stay a week;  everybody wanted him to
live in ir houses,  said y’d think it was an honor; but he said
as this was  last day of  camp-meeting he couldn’t do no good, 
besides he was in a sweat to get to  Indian Ocean right off  go to
work on  pirates.

When we got back to  raft  he come to count up he found he had
collected eighty-seven dollars  seventy-five cents.   n he had
fetched away a three-gallon jug of whisky, too, that he found under a
wagon when he was starting home through  woods.   king said,
take it all around, it laid over any day he’d ever put in in 
missionarying line.  He said it warn’t no use talking, heans don’t
amount to shucks alongside of pirates to work a camp-meeting with.

 duke was thinking _he’d_ been doing pretty well till  king come
to show up, but after that he didn’t think so so much.  He had set
up  printed off two little jobs for farmers in that
printing-office--horse bills-- took  money, four dollars.   he
had got in ten dollars’ worth of advertisements for  paper, which he
said he would put in for four dollars if y would pay in advance--so
y done it.  price of  paper was two dollars a year, but he took
in three subscriptions for half a dollar apiece on condition of m
paying him in advance; y were going to pay in cordwood  onions as
usual, but he said he had just bought  concern  knocked down 
price as low as he could afford it,  was going to run it for cash.
 He set up a little piece of poetry, which he made, himself, out of
his own head--three verses--kind of sweet  saddish-- name of it was,
“Yes, crush, cold world, this breaking heart”-- he left that all set
up  ready to print in  paper,  didn’t charge nothing for it.
 Well, he took in nine dollars  a half,  said he’d done a pretty
square day’s work for it.

n he showed us anor little job he’d printed  hadn’t charged
for, because it was for us.  It had a picture of a runaway nigger with
a bundle on a stick over his shoulder,  “$200 reward” under it.  
reading was all about Jim,  just described him to a dot.  It said
he run away from St. Jacques’ plantation, forty mile below New Orleans,
last winter,  likely went north,  whoever would catch him  send
him back he could have  reward  expenses.

“Now,” says  duke, “after to-night we can run in  daytime if we
want to.  Whenever we see anybody coming we can tie Jim h  foot
with a rope,  lay him in  wigwam  show this hbill  say we
captured him up  river,  were too poor to travel on a steamboat,
so we got this little raft on credit from our friends  are going down
to get  reward.  Hcuffs  chains would look still better on Jim,
but it wouldn’t go well with  story of us being so poor.  Too much
like jewelry.  Ropes are  correct thing--we must preserve  unities,
as we say on  boards.”

We all said  duke was pretty smart,  re couldn’t be no trouble
about running daytimes.  We judged we could make miles enough that night
to get out of  reach of  powwow we reckoned  duke’s work in
 printing office was going to make in that little town; n we could
boom right along if we wanted to.

We laid low  kept still,  never shoved out till nearly ten
o’clock; n we slid by, pretty wide away from  town,  didn’t
hoist our lantern till we was clear out of sight of it.

When Jim called me to take  watch at four in  morning, he says:

“Huck, does you reck’n we gwyne to run acrost any mo’ kings on dis
trip?”

“No,” I says, “I reckon not.”

“Well,” says he, “dat’s all right, den.  I doan’ mine one er two kings,
but dat’s enough.  Dis one’s powerful drunk, en de duke ain’ much
better.”

I found Jim had been trying to get him to talk French, so he could hear
what it was like; but he said he had been in this country so long, 
had so much trouble, he’d forgot it.




CHAPTER XXI.

IT was after sun-up now, but we went right on  didn’t tie up.  
king   duke turned out by  by looking pretty rusty; but after
y’d jumped overboard  took a swim it chippered m up a good
deal. After breakfast  king he took a seat on  corner of  raft,
 pulled off his boots  rolled up his britches,  let his legs
dangle in  water, so as to be comfortable,  lit his pipe,  went
to getting his Romeo  Juliet by heart.  When he had got it pretty
good him   duke begun to practice it toger.   duke had to
learn him over  over again how to say every speech;  he made him
sigh,  put his h on his heart,  after a while he said he done
it pretty well; “only,” he says, “you mustn’t bellow out _Romeo_!
that way, like a bull--you must say it soft  sick  languishy,
so--R-o-o-meo! that is  idea; for Juliet’s a dear sweet mere child of
a girl, you know,  she doesn’t bray like a jackass.”

Well, next y got out a couple of long swords that  duke made out
of oak laths,  begun to practice  sword fight-- duke called
himself Richard III.;   way y laid on  pranced around
 raft was gr to see.  But by  by  king tripped  fell
overboard,  after that y took a rest,  had a talk about all
kinds of adventures y’d had in or times along  river.

After dinner  duke says:

“Well, Capet, we’ll want to make this a first-class show, you know, so
I guess we’ll add a little more to it.  We want a little something to
answer encores with, anyway.”

“What’s onkores, Bilgewater?”

 duke told him,  n says:

“I’ll answer by doing  Highl fling or  sailor’s hornpipe; 
you--well, let me see--oh, I’ve got it--you can do Hamlet’s soliloquy.”

“Hamlet’s which?”

“Hamlet’s soliloquy, you know;  most celebrated thing in Shakespeare.
Ah, it’s sublime, sublime!  Always fetches  house.  I haven’t got
it in  book--I’ve only got one volume--but I reckon I can piece it out
from memory.  I’ll just walk up  down a minute,  see if I can call
it back from recollection’s vaults.”

So he went to marching up  down, thinking,  frowning horrible
every now  n; n he would hoist up his eyebrows; next he would
squeeze his h on his forehead  stagger back  kind of moan; next
he would sigh,  next he’d let on to drop a tear.  It was beautiful
to see him. By  by he got it.  He told us to give attention.  n
he strikes a most noble attitude, with one leg shoved forwards,  his
arms stretched away up,  his head tilted back, looking up at  sky;
 n he begins to rip  rave  grit his teeth;  after that,
all through his speech, he howled,  spread around,  swelled up his
chest,  just knocked  spots out of any acting ever I see before.
 This is  speech--I learned it, easy enough, while he was learning it
to  king:

To be, or not to be; that is  bare bodkin That makes calamity of
so long life; For who would fardels bear, till Birnam Wood do come
to Dunsinane, But that  fear of something after death Murders 
innocent sleep, Great nature’s second course,  makes us rar sling
 arrows of outrageous fortune Than fly to ors that we know not of.
re’s  respect must give us pause: Wake Duncan with thy knocking! I
would thou couldst; For who would bear  whips  scorns of time, 
oppressor’s wrong,  proud man’s contumely,  law’s delay,  
quietus which his pangs might take. In  dead waste  middle of 
night, when churchyards yawn In customary suits of solemn black, But
that  undiscovered country from whose bourne no traveler returns,
Breas forth contagion on  world,  thus  native hue of
resolution, like  poor cat i’  adage, Is sicklied o’er with care.
 all  clouds that lowered o’er our housetops, With this
regard ir currents turn awry,  lose  name of action. ‘Tis a
consummation devoutly to be wished. But soft you,  fair Ophelia: Ope
not thy ponderous  marble jaws. But get e to a nunnery&mdash;go!

Well,  old man he liked that speech,  he mighty soon got it so he
could do it first rate. It seemed like he was just born for it;  when
he had his h in  was excited, it was perfectly lovely  way he
would rip  tear  rair up behind when he was getting it off.

 first chance we got,  duke he had some show bills printed; 
after that, for two or three days as we floated along,  raft was a
most uncommon lively place, for re warn’t nothing but sword-fighting
 rehearsing--as  duke called it--going on all  time. One morning,
when we was pretty well down  State of Arkansaw, we come in sight
of a little one-horse town in a big bend; so we tied up about
three-quarters of a mile above it, in  mouth of a crick which was
shut in like a tunnel by  cypress trees,  all of us but Jim took
 canoe  went down re to see if re was any chance in that
place for our show.

We struck it mighty lucky; re was going to be a circus re that
afternoon,   country people was already beginning to come in, in
all kinds of old shackly wagons,  on horses.  circus would leave
before night, so our show would have a pretty good chance.  duke he
hired  court house,  we went around  stuck up our bills. y
read like this:

Shaksperean Revival!!!

Wonderful Attraction!

For One Night Only!  world renowned tragedians,

David Garrick  younger, of Drury Lane atre, London,



Edmund Kean  elder, of  Royal Haymarket atre, Whitechapel,
Pudding Lane, Piccadilly, London,   Royal Continental atres, in
ir sublime Shaksperean Spectacle entitled  Balcony Scene in

Romeo  Juliet!!!

Romeo...................................... Mr. Garrick.

Juliet..................................... Mr. Kean.

Assisted by  whole strength of  company!

New costumes, new scenery, new appointments!

Also:

 thrilling, masterly,  blood-curdling Broad-sword conflict In
Richard III.!!!

Richard III................................ Mr. Garrick.

Richmond................................... Mr. Kean.

also:

(by special request,)

Hamlet’s Immortal Soliloquy!!

By  Illustrious Kean!

Done by him 300 consecutive nights in Paris!

For One Night Only,

On account of imperative European engagements!

Admission 25 cents; children  servants, 10 cents.

n we went loafing around  town.  stores  houses was most all
old shackly dried-up frame concerns that hadn’t ever been painted; y
was set up three or four foot above ground on stilts, so as to be out of
reach of  water when  river was overflowed.  houses had little
gardens around m, but y didn’t seem to raise hardly anything in
m but jimpson weeds,  sunflowers,  ash-piles,  old curled-up
boots  shoes,  pieces of bottles,  rags,  played-out
tin-ware.  fences was made of different kinds of boards, nailed on
at different times;  y leaned every which-way,  had gates that
didn’t generly have but one hinge--a lear one. Some of  fences
had been whitewashed, some time or anor, but  duke said it was in
Clumbus’s time, like enough. re was generly hogs in  garden, 
people driving m out.

All  stores was along one street.  y had white domestic awnings in
front,   country people hitched ir horses to  awning-posts.
re was empty drygoods boxes under  awnings,  loafers roosting
on m all day long, whittling m with ir Barlow knives; 
chawing tobacco,  gaping  yawning  stretching--a mighty ornery
lot. y generly had on yellow straw hats most as wide as an umbrella,
but didn’t wear no coats nor waistcoats, y called one anor Bill,
 Buck,  Hank,  Joe,  y,  talked lazy  drawly, 
used considerable many cuss words.  re was as many as one loafer
leaning up against every awning-post,  he most always had his hs
in his britches-pockets, except when he fetched m out to lend a chaw
of tobacco or scratch.  What a body was hearing amongst m all 
time was:

“Gimme a chaw ‘v tobacker, Hank.”

“Cain’t; I hain’t got but one chaw left.  Ask Bill.”

Maybe Bill he gives him a chaw; maybe he lies  says he ain’t got
none. Some of m kinds of loafers never has a cent in  world, nor a
chaw of tobacco of ir own.  y get all ir chawing by borrowing;
y say to a fellow, “I wisht you’d len’ me a chaw, Jack, I jist this
minute give Ben Thompson  last chaw I had”--which is a lie pretty
much everytime; it don’t fool nobody but a stranger; but Jack ain’t no
stranger, so he says:

“_You_ give him a chaw, did you?  So did your sister’s cat’s
grmor. You pay me back  chaws you’ve awready borry’d off’n me,
Lafe Buckner, n I’ll loan you one or two ton of it,  won’t charge
you no back intrust, nur.”

“Well, I _did_ pay you back some of it wunst.”

“Yes, you did--‘bout six chaws.  You borry’d store tobacker  paid back
nigger-head.”

Store tobacco is flat black plug, but se fellows mostly chaws 
natural leaf twisted.  When y borrow a chaw y don’t generly cut it
off with a knife, but set  plug in between ir teeth,  gnaw with
ir teeth  tug at  plug with ir hs till y get it in
two; n sometimes  one that owns  tobacco looks mournful at it
when it’s hed back,  says, sarcastic:

“Here, gimme  _chaw_,  you take  _plug_.”

All  streets  lanes was just mud; y warn’t nothing else _but_
mud--mud as black as tar  nigh about a foot deep in some places,
 two or three inches deep in _all_  places.   hogs loafed 
grunted around everywheres.  You’d see a muddy sow  a litter of pigs
come lazying along  street  whollop herself right down in  way,
where folks had to walk around her,  she’d stretch out  shut her
eyes  wave her ears whilst  pigs was milking her,  look as
happy as if she was on salary.  pretty soon you’d hear a loafer
sing out, “Hi!  _so_ boy! sick him, Tige!”  away  sow would go,
squealing most horrible, with a dog or two swinging to each ear, 
three or four dozen more a-coming;  n you would see all 
loafers get up  watch  thing out of sight,  laugh at  fun
 look grateful for  noise.  n y’d settle back again till
re was a dog fight.  re couldn’t anything wake m up all over,
 make m happy all over, like a dog fight--unless it might be
putting turpentine on a stray dog  setting fire to him, or tying a
tin pan to his tail  see him run himself to death.

On  river front some of  houses was sticking out over  bank,
 y was bowed  bent,  about ready to tumble in.  people
had moved out of m.   bank was caved away under one corner of some
ors,  that corner was hanging over.  People lived in m yet, but
it was dangersome, because sometimes a strip of l as wide as a house
caves in at a time.  Sometimes a belt of l a quarter of a mile deep
will start in  cave along  cave along till it all caves into 
river in one summer. Such a town as that has to be always moving back,
 back,  back, because  river’s always gnawing at it.

 nearer it got to noon that day  thicker  thicker was 
wagons  horses in  streets,  more coming all  time.
 Families fetched ir dinners with m from  country,  eat m
in  wagons.  re was considerable whisky drinking going on,  I
seen three fights.  By  by somebody sings out:

“Here comes old Boggs!--in from  country for his little old monthly
drunk; here he comes, boys!”

All  loafers looked glad; I reckoned y was used to having fun out
of Boggs.  One of m says:

“Wonder who he’s a-gwyne to chaw up this time.  If he’d a-chawed up all
 men he’s ben a-gwyne to chaw up in  last twenty year he’d have
considerable ruputation now.”

Anor one says, “I wisht old Boggs ‘d threaten me, ‘cuz n I’d know
I warn’t gwyne to die for a thousan’ year.”

Boggs comes a-tearing along on his horse, whooping  yelling like an
Injun,  singing out:

“Cler  track, thar.  I’m on  waw-path,   price uv coffins is
a-gwyne to raise.”

He was drunk,  weaving about in his saddle; he was over fifty year
old,  had a very red face.  Everybody yelled at him  laughed at
him  sassed him,  he sassed back,  said he’d attend to m 
lay m out in ir regular turns, but he couldn’t wait now because
he’d come to town to kill old Colonel Sherburn,  his motto was, “Meat
first,  spoon vittles to top off on.”

He see me,  rode up  says:

“Whar’d you come f’m, boy?  You prepared to die?”

n he rode on.  I was scared, but a man says:

“He don’t mean nothing; he’s always a-carryin’ on like that when he’s
drunk.  He’s  best naturedest old fool in Arkansaw--never hurt nobody,
drunk nor sober.”

Boggs rode up before  biggest store in town,  bent his head down
so he could see under  curtain of  awning  yells:

“Come out here, Sherburn! Come out  meet  man you’ve swindled.
You’re  houn’ I’m after,  I’m a-gwyne to have you, too!”

 so he went on, calling Sherburn everything he could lay his tongue
to,   whole street packed with people listening  laughing 
going on.  By  by a proud-looking man about fifty-five-- he was a
heap  best dressed man in that town, too--steps out of  store, 
 crowd drops back on each side to let him come.  He says to Boggs,
mighty ca’m  slow--he says:

“I’m tired of this, but I’ll endure it till one o’clock.  Till one
o’clock, mind--no longer.  If you open your mouth against me only once
after that time you can’t travel so far but I will find you.”

n he turns  goes in.   crowd looked mighty sober; nobody
stirred,  re warn’t no more laughing.  Boggs rode off
blackguarding Sherburn as loud as he could yell, all down  street;
 pretty soon back he comes  stops before  store, still keeping
it up.  Some men crowded around him  tried to get him to shut up,
but he wouldn’t; y told him it would be one o’clock in about fifteen
minutes,  so he _must_ go home--he must go right away.  But it didn’t
do no good.  He cussed away with all his might,  throwed his hat down
in  mud  rode over it,  pretty soon away he went a-raging down
 street again, with his gray hair a-flying. Everybody that could get
a chance at him tried ir best to coax him off of his horse so y
could lock him up  get him sober; but it warn’t no use--up  street
he would tear again,  give Sherburn anor cussing.  By  by
somebody says:

“Go for his daughter!--quick, go for his daughter; sometimes he’ll listen
to her.  If anybody can persuade him, she can.”

So somebody started on a run.  I walked down street a ways  stopped.
In about five or ten minutes here comes Boggs again, but not on his
horse.  He was a-reeling across  street towards me, bare-headed, with
a friend on both sides of him a-holt of his arms  hurrying him along.
He was quiet,  looked uneasy;  he warn’t hanging back any, but was
doing some of  hurrying himself.  Somebody sings out:

“Boggs!”

I looked over re to see who said it,  it was that Colonel
Sherburn. He was sting perfectly still in  street,  had a
pistol raised in his right h--not aiming it, but holding it out with
 barrel tilted up towards  sky.   same second I see a young
girl coming on  run,  two men with her.  Boggs   men turned
round to see who called him,  when y see  pistol  men
jumped to one side,   pistol-barrel come down slow  steady to
a level--both barrels cocked. Boggs throws up both of his hs  says,

crowd closed up around m,  shouldered  jammed one anor, with

Colonel Sherburn he tossed his pistol on to  ground,  turned

y took Boggs to a little drug store,  crowd pressing around just

Well, pretty soon  whole town was re, squirming  scrouging

never give nobody a chance; or folks has ir rights as well as

re was considerable jawing back, so I slid out, thinking maybe

 re was a big crowd packed around each one of se fellows,

Well, by  by somebody said Sherburn ought to be lynched.  In about a

CHAPTER XXII.

Y swarmed up towards Sherburn’s house, a-whooping  raging like
Injuns,  everything had to clear  way or get run over  tromped
to mush,  it was awful to see.  Children was heeling it ahead of 
mob, screaming  trying to get out of  way;  every window along
 road was full of women’s heads,  re was nigger boys in every

y swarmed up in front of Sherburn’s palings as thick as y could
jam toger,  you couldn’t hear yourself think for  noise.  It
was a little twenty-foot yard.  Some sung out “Tear down  fence! tear
down  fence!”  n re was a racket of ripping  tearing 
smashing,  down she goes,   front wall of  crowd begins to

Just n Sherburn steps out on to  roof of his little front porch,
with a double-barrel gun in his h,  takes his st, perfectly
ca’m  deliberate, not saying a word.   racket stopped,  
wave sucked back.

Sherburn never said a word--just stood re, looking down.

that’s got s in it.

n he says, slow  scornful:

“ idea of _you_ lynching anybody!  It’s amusing.   idea of you
thinking you had pluck enough to lynch a _man_!  Because you’re brave
enough to tar  fear poor friendless cast-out women that come along
here, did that make you think you had grit enough to lay your hs on a

long as it’s daytime  you’re not behind him.

“Do I know you?  I know you clear through. I was born  raised in 
South,  I’ve lived in  North; so I know  average all around.
 average man’s a coward.  In  North he lets anybody walk over him
that wants to,  goes home  prays for a humble spirit to bear it.
In  South one man all by himself, has stopped a stage full of men
in  daytime,  robbed  lot.  Your newspapers call you a
brave people so much that you think you are braver than any or
people--whereas you’re just _as_ brave,  no braver.  Why don’t your
juries hang murderers?  Because y’re afraid  man’s friends will
shoot m in  back, in  dark-- it’s just what y _would_ do.

“So y always acquit;  n a _man_ goes in  night, with a
hundred masked cowards at his back  lynches  rascal.  Your mistake
is, that you didn’t bring a man with you; that’s one mistake,  
or is that you didn’t come in  dark  fetch your masks.  You
brought _part_ of a man--Buck Harkness, re-- if you hadn’t had him
to start you, you’d a taken it out in blowing.

“You didn’t want to come.   average man don’t like trouble 
danger. _You_ don’t like trouble  danger.  But if only _half_ a
man--like Buck Harkness, re--shouts ‘Lynch him! lynch him!’ you’re
afraid to back down--afraid you’ll be found out to be what you
are--_cowards_-- so you raise a yell,  hang yourselves on to that
half-a-man’s coat-tail,  come raging up here, swearing what big
things you’re going to do.  pitifulest thing out is a mob; that’s
what an army is--a mob; y don’t fight with courage that’s born in
m, but with courage that’s borrowed from ir mass,  from ir
officers.  But a mob without any _man_ at  head of it is _beneath_
pitifulness.  Now  thing for _you_ to do is to droop your tails 
go home  crawl in a hole.  If any real lynching’s going to be done it
will be done in  dark, Sourn fashion;  when y come y’ll
bring ir masks,  fetch a _man_ along.  Now _leave_-- take your
half-a-man with you”--tossing his gun up across his left arm  cocking
it when he says this.

 crowd washed back sudden,  n broke all apart,  went tearing

tolerable cheap.  I could a stayed if I wanted to, but I didn’t want to.

I went to  circus  loafed around  back side till  watchman
went by,  n dived in under  tent.  I had my twenty-dollar gold
piece  some or money, but I reckoned I better save it, because
re ain’t no telling how soon you are going to need it, away from
home  amongst strangers that way.  You can’t be too careful.  I ain’t
opposed to spending money on circuses when re ain’t no or way, but
re ain’t no use in _wasting_ it on m.

It was a real bully circus.  It was  splendidest sight that ever was
when y all come riding in, two  two, a gentleman  lady, side

comfortable--re must a been twenty of m-- every lady with a

I never see anything so lovely.   n one by one y got up
 stood,  went a-weaving around  ring so gentle  wavy

every lady’s rose-leafy dress flapping soft  silky around her hips,
 she looking like  most loveliest parasol.

 n faster  faster y went, all of m dancing, first one

went just about wild.

Well, all through  circus y done  most astonishing things;

ringmaster couldn’t ever say a word to him but he was back at him quick

 by  by a drunk man tried to get into  ring--said he wanted to

 So everybody laughed  said all right,   man got on.  minute

 n t’or one on t’or side,   people just crazy.  It

 But pretty soon he struggled up astraddle  grabbed  bridle,

astonishment.

n  ringmaster he see how he had been fooled,  he _was_ 
sickest ringmaster you ever see, I reckon.  Why, it was one of his own

wherever I run across it, it can have all of _my_ custom every time.

Well, that night we had _our_ show; but re warn’t only about twelve
people re--just enough to pay expenses.   y laughed all

Arkansaw lunkheads couldn’t come up to Shakespeare; what y wanted

some hbills,  stuck m up all over  village.   bills said:




CHAPTER XXIII.

WELL, all day him   king was hard at it, rigging up a stage 
a curtain  a row of cles for footlights;  that night  house
was jam full of men in no time.  When  place couldn’t hold no more,
 duke he quit tending door  went around  back way  come on
to  stage  stood up before  curtain  made a little speech,
 praised up this tragedy,  said it was  most thrillingest one
that ever was;  so he went on a-bragging about  tragedy,  about
Edmund Kean  Elder, which was to play  main principal part in it;
 at last when he’d got everybody’s expectations up high enough, he

but it was awful funny.  people most killed mselves laughing;

would make a cow laugh to see  shines that old idiot cut.

 great tragedy will be performed only two nights more, on accounts of

succeeded in pleasing m  instructing m, he will be deeply
obleeged if y will mention it to ir friends  get m to come
 see it.

Twenty people sings out:

“What, is it over?  Is that _all_?”

 duke says yes.  n re was a fine time.  Everybody sings
out, “Sold!”  rose up mad,  was a-going for that stage  m
tragedians.  But a big, fine looking man jumps up on a bench  shouts:

“Hold on!  Just a word, gentlemen.”  y stopped to listen.  “We are
sold--mighty badly sold.  But we don’t want to be  laughing stock of
this whole town, I reckon,  never hear  last of this thing as long
as we live.  _No_.  What we want is to go out of here quiet,  talk
this show up,  sell  _rest_ of  town!  n we’ll all be in 
same boat.  Ain’t that sensible?” (“You bet it is!-- jedge is right!”
 everybody sings out.) “All right, n--not a word about any sell.  Go
along home,  advise everybody to come  see  tragedy.”

Next day you couldn’t hear nothing around that town but how splendid
that show was.  House was jammed again that night,  we sold this
crowd  same way.  When me   king   duke got home to 
raft we all had a supper;  by  by, about midnight, y made Jim
 me back her out  float her down  middle of  river, 
fetch her in  hide her about two mile below town.

 third night  house was crammed again-- y warn’t new-comers
this time, but people that was at  show  or two nights.  I
stood by  duke at  door,  I see that every man that went in had
his pockets bulging, or something muffled up under his coat-- I see it
warn’t no perfumery, neir, not by a long sight.  I smelt sickly eggs
by  barrel,  rotten cabbages,  such things;  if I know 
signs of a dead cat being around,  I bet I do, re was sixty-four
of m went in.  I shoved in re for a minute, but it was too various
for me; I couldn’t st it.  Well, when  place couldn’t hold no more
people  duke he give a fellow a quarter  told him to tend door
for him a minute,  n he started around for  stage door, I after
him; but  minute we turned  corner  was in  dark he says:

“Walk fast now till you get away from  houses,  n shin for 
raft like  dickens was after you!”

I done it,  he done  same.  We struck  raft at  same time,
 in less than two seconds we was gliding down stream, all dark 
still,  edging towards  middle of  river, nobody saying a
word. I reckoned  poor king was in for a gaudy time of it with 
audience, but nothing of  sort; pretty soon he crawls out from under
 wigwam,  says:

“Well, how’d  old thing pan out this time, duke?”  He hadn’t been
up-town at all.

We never showed a light till we was about ten mile below  village.
n we lit up  had a supper,   king   duke fairly
laughed ir bones loose over  way y’d served m people.  
duke says:

“Greenhorns, flaads!  I knew  first house would keep mum  let
 rest of  town get roped in;  I knew y’d lay for us 
third night,  consider it was _ir_ turn now.  Well, it _is_ ir
turn,  I’d give something to know how much y’d take for it.  I
_would_ just like to know how y’re putting in ir opportunity.
 y can turn it into a picnic if y want to--y brought plenty
provisions.”

m rapscallions took in four hundred  sixty-five dollars in that
three nights.  I never see money hauled in by  wagon-load like that
before.  By  by, when y was asleep  snoring, Jim says:

“Don’t it s’prise you de way dem kings carries on, Huck?”

“No,” I says, “it don’t.”

“Why don’t it, Huck?”

“Well, it don’t, because it’s in  breed.  I reckon y’re all
alike.”

“But, Huck, dese kings o’ ourn is reglar rapscallions; dat’s jist what
dey is; dey’s reglar rapscallions.”

“Well, that’s what I’m a-saying; all kings is mostly rapscallions, as
fur as I can make out.”

“Is dat so?”

“You read about m once--you’ll see.  Look at Henry  Eight; this ‘n
‘s a Sunday-school Superintendent to _him_.   look at Charles Second,
 Louis Fourteen,  Louis Fifteen,  James Second,  Edward
Second,  Richard Third,  forty more; besides all m Saxon
heptarchies that used to rip around so in old times  raise Cain.  My,
you ought to seen old Henry  Eight when he was in bloom.  He _was_ a
blossom.  He used to marry a new wife every day,  chop off her head
next morning.   he would do it just as indifferent as if he was
ordering up eggs.  ‘Fetch up Nell Gwynn,’ he says.  y fetch her up.
Next morning, ‘Chop off her head!’   y chop it off.  ‘Fetch up
Jane Shore,’ he says;  up she comes, Next morning, ‘Chop off her
head’-- y chop it off.  ‘Ring up Fair Rosamun.’  Fair Rosamun
answers  bell.  Next morning, ‘Chop off her head.’   he made every
one of m tell him a tale every night;  he kept that up till he had
hogged a thous  one tales that way,  n he put m all in a
book,  called it Domesday Book--which was a good name  stated 
case.  You don’t know kings, Jim, but I know m;  this old rip
of ourn is one of  cleanest I’ve struck in history.  Well, Henry he
takes a notion he wants to get up some trouble with this country. How
does he go at it--give notice?--give  country a show?  No.  All of a
sudden he heaves all  tea in Boston Harbor overboard,  whacks
out a declaration of independence,  dares m to come on.  That was
_his_ style--he never give anybody a chance.  He had suspicions of his
far,  Duke of Wellington.  Well, what did he do?  Ask him to show
up?  No--drownded him in a butt of mamsey, like a cat.  S’pose people
left money laying around where he was--what did he do?  He collared it.
 S’pose he contracted to do a thing,  you paid him,  didn’t set
down re  see that he done it--what did he do?  He always done 
or thing. S’pose he opened his mouth--what n?  If he didn’t shut it
up powerful quick he’d lose a lie every time.  That’s  kind of a bug
Henry was;  if we’d a had him along ‘stead of our kings he’d a fooled
that town a heap worse than ourn done.  I don’t say that ourn is lambs,
because y ain’t, when you come right down to  cold facts; but y
ain’t nothing to _that_ old ram, anyway.  All I say is, kings is kings,
 you got to make allowances.  Take m all around, y’re a mighty
ornery lot. It’s  way y’re raised.”

“But dis one do _smell_ so like de nation, Huck.”

“Well, y all do, Jim.  We can’t help  way a king smells; history
don’t tell no way.”

“Now de duke, he’s a tolerble likely man in some ways.”

“Yes, a duke’s different.  But not very different.  This one’s
a middling hard lot for a duke.  When he’s drunk re ain’t no
near-sighted man could tell him from a king.”

“Well, anyways, I doan’ hanker for no mo’ un um, Huck.  Dese is all I
kin stan’.”

“It’s  way I feel, too, Jim.  But we’ve got m on our hs,  we
got to remember what y are,  make allowances.  Sometimes I wish we
could hear of a country that’s out of kings.”

What was  use to tell Jim se warn’t real kings  dukes?  It
wouldn’t a done no good; , besides, it was just as I said:  you
couldn’t tell m from  real kind.

I went to sleep,  Jim didn’t call me when it was my turn.  He often
done that.  When I waked up just at daybreak he was sitting re with
his head down betwixt his knees, moaning  mourning to himself.  I
didn’t take notice nor let on.  I knowed what it was about.  He was
thinking about his wife  his children, away up yonder,  he was low
 homesick; because he hadn’t ever been away from home before in his
life;  I do believe he cared just as much for his people as white
folks does for ir’n.  It don’t seem natural, but I reckon it’s so.
 He was often moaning  mourning that way nights, when he judged I
was asleep,  saying, “Po’ little ‘Lizabeth! po’ little Johnny! it’s
mighty hard; I spec’ I ain’t ever gwyne to see you no mo’, no mo’!”  He
was a mighty good nigger, Jim was.

But this time I somehow got to talking to him about his wife  young
ones;  by  by he says:

“What makes me feel so bad dis time ‘uz bekase I hear sumpn over yonder
on de bank like a whack, er a slam, while ago, en it mine me er de time
I treat my little ‘Lizabeth so ornery.  She warn’t on’y ‘bout fo’ year
ole, en she tuck de sk’yarlet fever, en had a powful rough spell; but
she got well, en one day she was a-stannin’ aroun’, en I says to her, I
says:

“‘Shet de do’.’

“She never done it; jis’ stood dah, kiner smilin’ up at me.  It make me
mad; en I says agin, mighty loud, I says:

“‘Doan’ you hear me?  Shet de do’!’

“She jis stood de same way, kiner smilin’ up.  I was a-bilin’!  I says:

“‘I lay I _make_ you mine!’

“En wid dat I fetch’ her a slap side de head dat sont her a-sprawlin’.
Den I went into de yur room, en ‘uz gone ‘bout ten minutes; en when
I come back dah was dat do’ a-stannin’ open _yit_, en dat chile stannin’
mos’ right in it, a-lookin’ down  mournin’, en de tears runnin’ down.
 My, but I _wuz_ mad!  I was a-gwyne for de chile, but jis’ den--it was a
do’ dat open innerds--jis’ den, ‘long come de wind en slam it to, behine
de chile, ker-BLAM!--en my lan’, de chile never move’!  My breff mos’
hop outer me; en I feel so--so--I doan’ know HOW I feel.  I crope out,
all a-tremblin’, en crope aroun’ en open de do’ easy en slow, en poke my
head in behine de chile, sof’ en still, en all uv a sudden I says POW!
jis’ as loud as I could yell.  _She never budge!_  Oh, Huck, I bust out
a-cryin’ en grab her up in my arms, en say, ‘Oh, de po’ little thing!
 De Lord God Amighty fogive po’ ole Jim, kaze he never gwyne to fogive
hisself as long’s he live!’  Oh, she was plumb deef en dumb, Huck, plumb
deef en dumb--en I’d ben a-treat’n her so!”




CHAPTER XXIV.

NEXT day, towards night, we laid up under a little willow towhead out in
 middle, where re was a village on each side of  river,  
duke   king begun to lay out a plan for working m towns.  Jim
he spoke to  duke,  said he hoped it wouldn’t take but a few
hours, because it got mighty heavy  tiresome to him when he had to
lay all day in  wigwam tied with  rope.  You see, when we left him
all alone we had to tie him, because if anybody happened on to him all
by himself  not tied it wouldn’t look much like he was a runaway
nigger, you know. So  duke said it _was_ kind of hard to have to lay
roped all day,  he’d cipher out some way to get around it.

He was uncommon bright,  duke was,  he soon struck it.  He dressed
Jim up in King Lear’s outfit--it was a long curtain-calico gown,  a
white horse-hair wig  whiskers;  n he took his ater paint
 painted Jim’s face  hs  ears  neck all over a dead,
dull, solid blue, like a man that’s been drownded nine days.  Blamed if
he warn’t  horriblest looking outrage I ever see.  n  duke took
 wrote out a sign on a shingle so:

Sick Arab--but harmless when not out of his head.

 he nailed that shingle to a lath,  stood  lath up four or five
foot in front of  wigwam.  Jim was satisfied.  He said it was a sight
better than lying tied a couple of years every day,  trembling all
over every time re was a sound.   duke told him to make himself
free  easy,  if anybody ever come meddling around, he must hop
out of  wigwam,  carry on a little,  fetch a howl or two like
a wild beast,  he reckoned y would light out  leave him alone.
 Which was sound enough judgment; but you take  average man,  he
wouldn’t wait for him to howl.  Why, he didn’t only look like he was
dead, he looked considerable more than that.

se rapscallions wanted to try  Nonesuch again, because re was
so much money in it, but y judged it wouldn’t be safe, because maybe
 news might a worked along down by this time.  y couldn’t hit no
project that suited exactly; so at last  duke said he reckoned he’d
lay off  work his brains an hour or two  see if he couldn’t put up
something on  Arkansaw village;   king he allowed he would drop
over to t’or village without any plan, but just trust in Providence
to lead him  profitable way--meaning  devil, I reckon.  We had all
bought store clos where we stopped last;  now  king put his’n
on,  he told me to put mine on.  I done it, of course.   king’s
duds was all black,  he did look real swell  starchy.  I never
knowed how clos could change a body before.  Why, before, he looked
like  orneriest old rip that ever was; but now, when he’d take off
his new white beaver  make a bow  do a smile, he looked that gr
 good  pious that you’d say he had walked right out of  ark,
 maybe was old Leviticus himself.  Jim cleaned up  canoe,  I
got my paddle ready.  re was a big steamboat laying at  shore away
up under  point, about three mile above  town--been re a couple
of hours, taking on freight.  Says  king:

“Seein’ how I’m dressed, I reckon maybe I better arrive down from St.
Louis or Cincinnati, or some or big place.  Go for  steamboat,
Huckleberry; we’ll come down to  village on her.”

I didn’t have to be ordered twice to go  take a steamboat ride.
 I fetched  shore a half a mile above  village,  n went
scooting along  bluff bank in  easy water.  Pretty soon we come to
a nice innocent-looking young country jake setting on a log swabbing 
sweat off of his face, for it was powerful warm wear;  he had a
couple of big carpet-bags by him.

“Run her nose in shore,” says  king.  I done it.  “Wher’ you bound
for, young man?”

“For  steamboat; going to Orleans.”

“Git aboard,” says  king.  “Hold on a minute, my servant ‘ll he’p you
with m bags.  Jump out  he’p  gentleman, Adolphus”--meaning me,
I see.

I done so,  n we all three started on again.   young chap was
mighty thankful; said it was tough work toting his baggage such wear.
He asked  king where he was going,   king told him he’d come
down  river  led at  or village this morning,  now he
was going up a few mile to see an old friend on a farm up re.  
young fellow says:

“When I first see you I says to myself, ‘It’s Mr. Wilks, sure,  he
come mighty near getting here in time.’  But n I says again, ‘No, I
reckon it ain’t him, or else he wouldn’t be paddling up  river.’  You
_ain’t_ him, are you?”

“No, my name’s Blodgett--Elexer Blodgett--_Reverend_ Elexer
Blodgett, I s’pose I must say, as I’m one o’  Lord’s poor servants.
 But still I’m jist as able to be sorry for Mr. Wilks for not arriving
in time, all  same, if he’s missed anything by it--which I hope he
hasn’t.”

“Well, he don’t miss any property by it, because he’ll get that all
right; but he’s missed seeing his bror Peter die--which he mayn’t
mind, nobody can tell as to that--but his bror would a give anything
in this world to see _him_ before he died; never talked about nothing
else all se three weeks; hadn’t seen him since y was boys
toger-- hadn’t ever seen his bror William at all--that’s  deef
 dumb one--William ain’t more than thirty or thirty-five.  Peter 
George were  only ones that come out here; George was  married
bror; him  his wife both died last year.  Harvey  William’s 
only ones that’s left now; , as I was saying, y haven’t got here
in time.”

“Did anybody send ‘em word?”

“Oh, yes; a month or two ago, when Peter was first took; because Peter
said n that he sorter felt like he warn’t going to get well this
time. You see, he was pretty old,  George’s g’yirls was too young to
be much company for him, except Mary Jane,  red-headed one;  so he
was kinder lonesome after George  his wife died,  didn’t seem
to care much to live.  He most desperately wanted to see Harvey--
William, too, for that matter--because he was one of m kind that can’t
bear to make a will.  He left a letter behind for Harvey,  said he’d
told in it where his money was hid,  how he wanted  rest of 
property divided up so George’s g’yirls would be all right--for George
didn’t leave nothing.   that letter was all y could get him to put
a pen to.”

“Why do you reckon Harvey don’t come?  Wher’ does he live?”

“Oh, he lives in Engl--Sheffield--preaches re--hasn’t ever been in
this country.  He hasn’t had any too much time-- besides he mightn’t a
got  letter at all, you know.”

“Too bad, too bad he couldn’t a lived to see his brors, poor soul.
You going to Orleans, you say?”

“Yes, but that ain’t only a part of it.  I’m going in a ship, next
Wednesday, for Ryo Janeero, where my uncle lives.”

“It’s a pretty long journey.  But it’ll be lovely; wisht I was a-going.
Is Mary Jane  oldest?  How old is  ors?”

“Mary Jane’s nineteen, Susan’s fifteen,  Joanna’s about
fourteen--that’s  one that gives herself to good works  has a
hare-lip.”

“Poor things! to be left alone in  cold world so.”

“Well, y could be worse off.  Old Peter had friends,  y
ain’t going to let m come to no harm.  re’s Hobson,  Babtis’
preacher;  Deacon Lot Hovey,  Ben Rucker,  Abner Shackleford,
 Levi Bell,  lawyer;  Dr. Robinson,  ir wives,  
widow Bartley, --well, re’s a lot of m; but se are  ones
that Peter was thickest with,  used to write about sometimes, when
he wrote home; so Harvey ‘ll know where to look for friends when he gets
here.”

Well,  old man went on asking questions till he just fairly emptied
that young fellow.  Blamed if he didn’t inquire about everybody 
everything in that blessed town,  all about  Wilkses;  about
Peter’s business--which was a tanner;  about George’s--which was a
carpenter;  about Harvey’s--which was a dissentering minister;  so
on,  so on.  n he says:

“What did you want to walk all  way up to  steamboat for?”

“Because she’s a big Orleans boat,  I was afeard she mightn’t stop
re.  When y’re deep y won’t stop for a hail.  A Cincinnati boat
will, but this is a St. Louis one.”

“Was Peter Wilks well off?”

“Oh, yes, pretty well off.  He had houses  l,  it’s reckoned he
left three or four thous in cash hid up som’ers.”

“When did you say he died?”

“I didn’t say, but it was last night.”

“Funeral to-morrow, likely?”

“Yes, ‘bout  middle of  day.”

“Well, it’s all terrible sad; but we’ve all got to go, one time or
anor. So what we want to do is to be prepared; n we’re all right.”

“Yes, sir, it’s  best way.  Ma used to always say that.”

When we struck  boat she was about done loading,  pretty soon she
got off.   king never said nothing about going aboard, so I lost
my ride, after all.  When  boat was gone  king made me paddle up
anor mile to a lonesome place,  n he got ashore  says:

“Now hustle back, right off,  fetch  duke up here,   new
carpet-bags.   if he’s gone over to t’or side, go over re 
git him.   tell him to git himself up regardless.  Shove along, now.”

I see what _he_ was up to; but I never said nothing, of course.  When
I got back with  duke we hid  canoe,  n y set down on a
log,   king told him everything, just like  young fellow had
said it--every last word of it.   all  time he was a-doing it he
tried to talk like an Englishman;  he done it pretty well, too, for
a slouch. I can’t imitate him,  so I ain’t a-going to try to; but he
really done it pretty good.  n he says:

“How are you on  deef  dumb, Bilgewater?”

 duke said, leave him alone for that; said he had played a deef
 dumb person on  histronic boards.  So n y waited for a
steamboat.

About  middle of  afternoon a couple of little boats come along,
but y didn’t come from high enough up  river; but at last re
was a big one,  y hailed her.  She sent out her yawl,  we went
aboard,  she was from Cincinnati;  when y found we only wanted
to go four or five mile y was booming mad,  gave us a cussing, 
said y wouldn’t l us.  But  king was ca’m.  He says:

“If gentlemen kin afford to pay a dollar a mile apiece to be took on 
put off in a yawl, a steamboat kin afford to carry ‘em, can’t it?”

So y softened down  said it was all right;  when we got to 
village y yawled us ashore.  About two dozen men flocked down when
y see  yawl a-coming,  when  king says:

“Kin any of you gentlemen tell me wher’ Mr. Peter Wilks lives?” y
give a glance at one anor,  nodded ir heads, as much as to say,
“What d’ I tell you?”  n one of m says, kind of soft  gentle:

“I’m sorry sir, but  best we can do is to tell you where he _did_
live yesterday evening.”

Sudden as winking  ornery old cretur went an to smash,  fell up
against  man,  put his chin on his shoulder,  cried down his
back,  says:

“Alas, alas, our poor bror--gone,  we never got to see him; oh,
it’s too, too hard!”

n he turns around, blubbering,  makes a lot of idiotic signs to
 duke on his hs,  blamed if he didn’t drop a carpet-bag 
bust out a-crying.  If y warn’t  beatenest lot, m two frauds,
that ever I struck.

Well,  men gared around  sympathized with m,  said all
sorts of kind things to m,  carried ir carpet-bags up  hill
for m,  let m lean on m  cry,  told  king all about
his bror’s last moments,   king he told it all over again on
his hs to  duke,  both of m took on about that dead tanner
like y’d lost  twelve disciples.  Well, if ever I struck anything
like it, I’m a nigger. It was enough to make a body ashamed of  human
race.




CHAPTER XXV.

 news was all over town in two minutes,  you could see  people
tearing down on  run from every which way, some of m putting on
ir coats as y come.  Pretty soon we was in  middle of a crowd,
  noise of  tramping was like a soldier march.   windows 
dooryards was full;  every minute somebody would say, over a fence:

“Is it _m_?”

 somebody trotting along with  gang would answer back  say:

“You bet it is.”

When we got to  house  street in front of it was packed,  
three girls was sting in  door.  Mary Jane _was_ red-headed, but
that don’t make no difference, she was most awful beautiful,  her
face  her eyes was all lit up like glory, she was so glad her uncles
was come.  king he spread his arms,  Mary Jane she jumped for
m,   hare-lip jumped for  duke,  re y had it!
 Everybody most, leastways women, cried for joy to see m meet again
at last  have such good times.

n  king he hunched  duke private--I see him do it-- n he
looked around  see  coffin, over in  corner on two chairs; so
n him   duke, with a h across each or’s shoulder, 
t’or h to ir eyes, walked slow  solemn over re, everybody

people saying “Sh!”  all  men taking ir hats off  drooping
ir heads, so you could a heard a pin fall.   when y got re

y bust out a-crying so you could a heard m to Orleans, most; 
n y put ir arms around each or’s necks,  hung ir chins
over each or’s shoulders;  n for three minutes, or maybe four,
I never see two men leak  way y done.  , mind you, everybody
was doing  same;   place was that damp I never see anything
like it. n one of m got on one side of  coffin,  t’or on
t’or side,  y kneeled down  rested ir foreheads on 
coffin,  let on to pray all to mselves.  Well, when it come

everybody broke down  went to sobbing right out loud-- poor girls,
too;  every woman, nearly, went up to  girls, without saying a

on ir head,  looked up towards  sky, with  tears running
down,  n busted out  went off sobbing  swabbing,  give
 next woman a show.  I never see anything so disgusting.

Well, by  by  king he gets up  comes forward a little, 
works himself up  slobbers out a speech, all full of tears 
flapdoodle about its being a sore trial for him  his poor bror
to lose  diseased,  to miss seeing diseased alive after  long
journey of four thous mile, but it’s a trial that’s sweetened 
sanctified to us by this dear sympathy  se holy tears,  so he
thanks m out of his heart  out of his bror’s heart, because out
of ir mouths y can’t, words being too weak  cold,  all that
kind of rot  slush, till it was just sickening;  n he blubbers
out a pious goody-goody Amen,  turns himself loose  goes to crying
fit to bust.

  minute  words were out of his mouth somebody over in 
crowd struck up  doxolojer,  everybody joined in with all ir
might,  it just warmed you up  made you feel as good as church
letting out. Music is a good thing;  after all that soul-butter 
hogwash I never see it freshen up things so,  sound so honest 
bully.

n  king begins to work his jaw again,  says how him  his
nieces would be glad if a few of  main principal friends of 
family would take supper here with m this evening,  help set up
with  ashes of  diseased;  says if his poor bror laying

was very dear to him,  mentioned often in his letters;  so he will

Lot Hovey,  Mr. Ben Rucker,  Abner Shackleford,  Levi Bell, 
Dr. Robinson,  ir wives,   widow Bartley.

Rev. Hobson  Dr. Robinson was down to  end of  town a-hunting
toger--that is, I mean  doctor was shipping a sick man to t’or
world,   preacher was pinting him right.  Lawyer Bell was away up
to Louisville on business.  But  rest was on h,  so y all
come  shook hs with  king  thanked him  talked to him;
 n y shook hs with  duke  didn’t say nothing, but just
kept a-smiling  bobbing ir heads like a passel of sapheads whilst

 all  time, like a baby that can’t talk.

So  king he blattered along,  managed to inquire about pretty
much everybody  dog in town, by his name,  mentioned all sorts

George’s family, or to Peter.   he always let on that Peter wrote him
 things; but that was a lie:  he got every blessed one of m out of
that young flaad that we canoed up to  steamboat.

n Mary Jane she fetched  letter her far left behind,  
king he read it out loud  cried over it.  It give  dwelling-house
 three thous dollars, gold, to  girls;  it give  tanyard
(which was doing a good business), along with some or houses 
l (worth about seven thous),  three thous dollars in gold
to Harvey  William,  told where  six thous cash was hid down
cellar.  So se two frauds said y’d go  fetch it up,  have

 We shut  cellar door behind us,  when y found  bag
y spilt it out on  floor,  it was a lovely sight, all m
yaller-boys.  My,  way  king’s eyes did shine!  He slaps  duke

“Oh, _this_ ain’t bully nor noth’n!  Oh, no, I reckon not!  Why,
_bully_, it beats  Nonesuch, _don’t_ it?”

 duke allowed it did.  y pawed  yaller-boys,  sifted m

“It ain’t no use talkin’; bein’ brors to a rich dead man 
representatives of furrin heirs that’s got left is  line for you

Most everybody would a been satisfied with  pile,  took it on
trust; but no, y must count it.  So y counts it,  it comes out
four hundred  fifteen dollars short.  Says  king:

“Dern him, I wonder what he done with that four hundred  fifteen

y worried over that awhile,  ransacked all around for it.  n
 duke says:

“Well, he was a pretty sick man,  likely he made a mistake--I reckon
that’s  way of it.   best way’s to let it go,  keep still about

“Oh, shucks, yes, we can _spare_ it.  I don’t k’yer noth’n ‘bout
that--it’s  _count_ I’m thinkin’ about.  We want to be awful square
 open  above-board here, you know.  We want to lug this h-yer
money up stairs  count it before everybody--n r’ ain’t noth’n
suspicious.  But when  dead man says r’s six thous’n dollars, you
know, we don’t want to--”

“Hold on,” says  duke.  “Le’s make up  deffisit,”  he begun to
haul out yaller-boys out of his pocket.

“It’s a most amaz’n’ good idea, duke--you _have_ got a rattlin’ clever
head on you,” says  king.  “Blest if  old Nonesuch ain’t a heppin’
us out agin,”  _he_ begun to haul out yaller-jackets  stack m
up.

It most busted m, but y made up  six thous clean  clear.

“Say,” says  duke, “I got anor idea.  Le’s go up stairs  count
this money,  n take  _give it to  girls_.”

“Good l, duke, lemme hug you!  It’s  most dazzling idea ‘at ever a
man struck.  You have cert’nly got  most astonishin’ head I ever see.
Oh, this is  boss dodge, r’ ain’t no mistake ‘bout it.  Let ‘em
fetch along ir suspicions now if y want to--this ‘ll lay ‘em out.”

When we got up-stairs everybody gered around  table,   king
he counted it  stacked it up, three hundred dollars in a pile--twenty
elegant little piles.  Everybody looked hungry at it,  licked ir
chops.  n y raked it into  bag again,  I see  king begin
to swell himself up for anor speech.  He says:

“Friends all, my poor bror that lays yonder has done generous by
m that’s left behind in  vale of sorrers.  He has done generous by
se yer poor little lambs that he loved  sheltered,  that’s left
farless  morless.  Yes,  we that knowed him knows that he
would a done _more_ generous by ‘em if he hadn’t ben afeard o’ woundin’
his dear William  me.  Now, _wouldn’t_ he?  r’ ain’t no question
‘bout it in _my_ mind.  Well, n, what kind o’ brors would it be
that ‘d st in his way at sech a time?   what kind o’ uncles would

so at sech a time?  If I know William-- I _think_ I do--he--well, I’ll
jest ask him.” He turns around  begins to make a lot of signs to
 duke with his hs,   duke he looks at him stupid 
lear-headed a while; n all of a sudden he seems to catch his
meaning,  jumps for  king, goo-gooing with all his might for joy,
 hugs him about fifteen times before he lets up.  n  king says,
“I knowed it; I reckon _that ‘ll_ convince anybody  way _he_ feels

Mary Jane she went for him, Susan   hare-lip went for 
duke,  n such anor hugging  kissing I never see yet.  
everybody crowded up with  tears in ir eyes,  most shook 
hs off of m frauds, saying all  time:

“You _dear_ good souls!--how _lovely_!--how _could_ you!”

Well, n, pretty soon all hs got to talking about  diseased
again,  how good he was,  what a loss he was,  all that; 
before long a big iron-jawed man worked himself in re from outside,
 stood a-listening  looking,  not saying anything;  nobody
saying anything to him eir, because  king was talking  y was
all busy listening.   king was saying--in  middle of something he’d

“--y bein’ partickler friends o’  diseased.  That’s why y’re
invited here this evenin’; but tomorrow we want _all_ to come--everybody;
for he respected everybody, he liked everybody,  so it’s fitten that
his funeral orgies sh’d be public.”

 so he went a-mooning on  on, liking to hear himself talk, 
every little while he fetched in his funeral orgies again, till  duke
he couldn’t st it no more; so he writes on a little scrap of paper,
“_Obsequies_, you old fool,”  folds it up,  goes to goo-gooing 
reaching it over people’s heads to him.   king he reads it  puts
it in his pocket,  says:

“Poor William, afflicted as he is, his _heart’s_ aluz right.  Asks me
to invite everybody to come to  funeral--wants me to make ‘em all
welcome.  But he needn’t a worried--it was jest what I was at.”

n he weaves along again, perfectly ca’m,  goes to dropping in his
funeral orgies again every now  n, just like he done before.  
when he done it  third time he says:

“I say orgies, not because it’s  common term, because it
ain’t--obsequies bein’  common term--but because orgies is  right
term. Obsequies ain’t used in Engl no more now--it’s gone out.  We
say orgies now in Engl.  Orgies is better, because it means  thing
you’re after more exact.  It’s a word that’s made up out’n  Greek
_orgo_, outside, open, abroad;   Hebrew _jeesum_, to plant, cover
up; hence in_ter._  So, you see, funeral orgies is an open er public
funeral.”

He was  _worst_ I ever struck.  Well,  iron-jawed man he laughed
right in his face.  Everybody was shocked.  Everybody says, “Why,
_doctor_!”  Abner Shackleford says:

“Why, Robinson, hain’t you heard  news?  This is Harvey Wilks.”

 king he smiled eager,  shoved out his flapper,  says:

“Is it my poor bror’s dear good friend  physician?  I--”

“Keep your hs off of me!” says  doctor.  “_You_ talk like an
Englishman, _don’t_ you?  It’s  worst imitation I ever heard.  _You_
Peter Wilks’s bror!  You’re a fraud, that’s what you are!”

Well, how y all took on!  y crowded around  doctor  tried to
quiet him down,  tried to explain to him  tell him how Harvey ‘d
showed in forty ways that he _was_ Harvey,  knowed everybody by name,
  names of  very dogs,  begged  _begged_ him not to hurt
Harvey’s feelings   poor girl’s feelings,  all that.  But it
warn’t no use; he stormed right along,  said any man that pretended
to be an Englishman  couldn’t imitate  lingo no better than what
he did was a fraud  a liar.   poor girls was hanging to  king
 crying;  all of a sudden  doctor ups  turns on _m_.  He
says:

“I was your far’s friend,  I’m your friend;  I warn you as a
friend,  an honest one that wants to protect you  keep you out of
harm  trouble, to turn your backs on that scoundrel  have nothing
to do with him,  ignorant tramp, with his idiotic Greek  Hebrew,
as he calls it.  He is  thinnest kind of an impostor--has come here
with a lot of empty names  facts which he picked up somewheres, 
you take m for _proofs_,  are helped to fool yourselves by se
foolish friends here, who ought to know better.  Mary Jane Wilks, you
know me for your friend,  for your unselfish friend, too.  Now listen
to me; turn this pitiful rascal out--I _beg_ you to do it.  Will you?”

Mary Jane straightened herself up,  my, but she was hsome!  She
says:

“_Here_ is my answer.”  She hove up  bag of money  put it in 
king’s hs,  says, “Take this six thous dollars,  invest for
me  my sisters any way you want to,  don’t give us no receipt for
it.”

n she put her arm around  king on one side,  Susan  
hare-lip done  same on  or.  Everybody clapped ir hs 
stomped on  floor like a perfect storm, whilst  king held up his
head  smiled proud.   doctor says:

“All right; I wash _my_ hs of  matter.  But I warn you all that a
time ‘s coming when you’re going to feel sick whenever you think of this
