“All right, doctor,” says  king, kinder mocking him; “we’ll try 
get ‘em to send for you;” which made m all laugh,  y said it
was a prime good hit.




CHAPTER XXVI.

WELL, when y was all gone  king he asks Mary Jane how y was off
for spare rooms,  she said she had one spare room, which would do for
Uncle William,  she’d give her own room to Uncle Harvey, which was
a little bigger,  she would turn into  room with her sisters 
sleep on a cot;  up garret was a little cubby, with a pallet in it.
 king said  cubby would do for his valley--meaning me.

So Mary Jane took us up,  she showed m ir rooms, which was
plain but nice.  She said she’d have her frocks  a lot of or traps
took out of her room if y was in Uncle Harvey’s way, but he said
y warn’t.   frocks was hung along  wall,  before m was
a curtain made out of calico that hung down to  floor.  re was an
old hair trunk in one corner,  a guitar-box in anor,  all sorts
of little knickknacks  jimcracks around, like girls brisken up a room
with.   king said it was all  more homely  more pleasanter for
se fixings,  so don’t disturb m.   duke’s room was pretty
small, but plenty good enough,  so was my cubby.

That night y had a big supper,  all m men  women was re,
 I stood behind  king   duke’s chairs  waited on m,
  niggers waited on  rest.  Mary Jane she set at  head of
 table, with Susan alongside of her,  said how bad  biscuits
was,  how mean  preserves was,  how ornery  tough  fried
chickens was-- all that kind of rot,  way women always do for to
force out compliments;   people all knowed everything was tiptop,
 said so--said “How _do_ you get biscuits to brown so nice?” 
“Where, for  l’s sake, _did_ you get se amaz’n pickles?” 
all that kind of humbug talky-talk, just  way people always does at a
supper, you know.

 when it was all done me   hare-lip had supper in  kitchen
off of  leavings, whilst  ors was helping  niggers clean up
 things.   hare-lip she got to pumping me about Engl,  blest
if I didn’t think  ice was getting mighty thin sometimes.  She says:

“Did you ever see  king?”

“Who?  William Fourth?  Well, I bet I have--he goes to our church.”  I
knowed he was dead years ago, but I never let on.  So when I says he
goes to our church, she says:

“What--regular?”

“Yes--regular.  His pew’s right over opposite ourn--on t’or side 
pulpit.”

“I thought he lived in London?”

“Well, he does.  Where _would_ he live?”

“But I thought _you_ lived in Sheffield?”

I see I was up a stump.  I had to let on to get choked with a chicken
bone, so as to get time to think how to get down again.  n I says:

“I mean he goes to our church regular when he’s in Sheffield.  That’s
only in  summer time, when he comes re to take  sea baths.”

“Why, how you talk--Sheffield ain’t on  sea.”

“Well, who said it was?”

“Why, you did.”

“I _didn’t_ nur.”

“You did!”

“I didn’t.”

“You did.”

“I never said nothing of  kind.”

“Well, what _did_ you say, n?”

“Said he come to take  sea _baths_--that’s what I said.”

“Well, n, how’s he going to take  sea baths if it ain’t on 
sea?”

“Looky here,” I says; “did you ever see any Congress-water?”

“Yes.”

“Well, did you have to go to Congress to get it?”

“Why, no.”

“Well, neir does William Fourth have to go to  sea to get a sea
bath.”

“How does he get it, n?”

“Gets it  way people down here gets Congress-water--in barrels.  re
in  palace at Sheffield y’ve got furnaces,  he wants his water
hot.  y can’t bile that amount of water away off re at  sea.
y haven’t got no conveniences for it.”

“Oh, I see, now.  You might a said that in  first place  saved
time.”

When she said that I see I was out of  woods again,  so I was
comfortable  glad.  Next, she says:

“Do you go to church, too?”

“Yes--regular.”

“Where do you set?”

“Why, in our pew.”

“_Whose_ pew?”

“Why, _ourn_--your Uncle Harvey’s.”

“His’n?  What does _he_ want with a pew?”

“Wants it to set in.  What did you _reckon_ he wanted with it?”

“Why, I thought he’d be in  pulpit.”

Rot him, I forgot he was a preacher.  I see I was up a stump again, so I
played anor chicken bone  got anor think.  n I says:

“Blame it, do you suppose re ain’t but one preacher to a church?”

“Why, what do y want with more?”

“What!--to preach before a king?  I never did see such a girl as you.
y don’t have no less than seventeen.”

“Seventeen!  My l!  Why, I wouldn’t set out such a string as that,
not if I _never_ got to glory.  It must take ‘em a week.”

“Shucks, y don’t _all_ of ‘em preach  same day--only _one_ of ‘em.”

“Well, n, what does  rest of ‘em do?”

“Oh, nothing much.  Loll around, pass  plate-- one thing or
anor.  But mainly y don’t do nothing.”

“Well, n, what are y _for_?”

“Why, y’re for _style_.  Don’t you know nothing?”

“Well, I don’t _want_ to know no such foolishness as that.  How is
servants treated in Engl?  Do y treat ‘em better ‘n we treat our
niggers?”

“_No_!  A servant ain’t nobody re.  y treat m worse than dogs.”

“Don’t y give ‘em holidays,  way we do, Christmas  New Year’s
week,  Fourth of July?”

“Oh, just listen!  A body could tell _you_ hain’t ever been to Engl
by that.  Why, Hare-l--why, Joanna, y never see a holiday from year’s
end to year’s end; never go to  circus, nor ater, nor nigger
shows, nor nowheres.”

“Nor church?”

“Nor church.”

“But _you_ always went to church.”

Well, I was gone up again.  I forgot I was  old man’s servant.  But
next minute I whirled in on a kind of an explanation how a valley was
different from a common servant  _had_ to go to church wher he
wanted to or not,  set with  family, on account of its being 
law.  But I didn’t do it pretty good,  when I got done I see she
warn’t satisfied.  She says:

“Honest injun, now, hain’t you been telling me a lot of lies?”

“Honest injun,” says I.

“None of it at all?”

“None of it at all.  Not a lie in it,” says I.

“Lay your h on this book  say it.”

I see it warn’t nothing but a dictionary, so I laid my h on it 
said it.  So n she looked a little better satisfied,  says:

“Well, n, I’ll believe some of it; but I hope to gracious if I’ll
believe  rest.”

“What is it you won’t believe, Joe?” says Mary Jane, stepping in with
Susan behind her.  “It ain’t right nor kind for you to talk so to him,
 him a stranger  so far from his people.  How would you like to be
treated so?”

“That’s always your way, Maim--always sailing in to help somebody before
y’re hurt.  I hain’t done nothing to him.  He’s told some stretchers,
I reckon,  I said I wouldn’t swallow it all;  that’s every bit
 grain I _did_ say.  I reckon he can st a little thing like that,
can’t he?”

“I don’t care wher ‘twas little or wher ‘twas big; he’s here in
our house  a stranger,  it wasn’t good of you to say it.  If you
was in his place it would make you feel ashamed;  so you oughtn’t to
say a thing to anor person that will make _m_ feel ashamed.”

“Why, Mam, he said--”

“It don’t make no difference what he _said_--that ain’t  thing.  
thing is for you to treat him _kind_,  not be saying things to make
him remember he ain’t in his own country  amongst his own folks.”

I says to myself, _this_ is a girl that I’m letting that old reptile rob
her of her money!

n Susan _she_ waltzed in;  if you’ll believe me, she did give
Hare-lip hark from  tomb!

Says I to myself,  this is _anor_ one that I’m letting him rob her
of her money!

n Mary Jane she took anor inning,  went in sweet  lovely
again--which was her way; but when she got done re warn’t hardly
anything left o’ poor Hare-lip.  So she hollered.

“All right, n,” says  or girls; “you just ask his pardon.”

She done it, too;  she done it beautiful.  She done it so beautiful
it was good to hear;  I wished I could tell her a thous lies, so
she could do it again.

I says to myself, this is _anor_ one that I’m letting him rob her of
her money.   when she got through y all jest laid irselves
out to make me feel at home  know I was amongst friends.  I felt so
ornery  low down  mean that I says to myself, my mind’s made up;
I’ll hive that money for m or bust.

So n I lit out--for bed, I said, meaning some time or anor.  When
I got by myself I went to thinking  thing over.  I says to myself,
shall I go to that doctor, private,  blow on se frauds?  No--that
won’t do. He might tell who told him; n  king   duke would
make it warm for me.  Shall I go, private,  tell Mary Jane?  No--I
dasn’t do it. Her face would give m a hint, sure; y’ve got 
money,  y’d slide right out  get away with it.  If she was to
fetch in help I’d get mixed up in  business before it was done with,
I judge.  No; re ain’t no good way but one.  I got to steal that
money, somehow;  I got to steal it some way that y won’t suspicion
that I done it. y’ve got a good thing here,  y ain’t a-going
to leave till y’ve played this family  this town for all y’re
worth, so I’ll find a chance time enough. I’ll steal it  hide it; 
by  by, when I’m away down  river, I’ll write a letter  tell
Mary Jane where it’s hid.  But I better hive it tonight if I can,
because  doctor maybe hasn’t let up as much as he lets on he has; he
might scare m out of here yet.

So, thinks I, I’ll go  search m rooms.  Upstairs  hall was
dark, but I found  duke’s room,  started to paw around it with
my hs; but I recollected it wouldn’t be much like  king to let
anybody else take care of that money but his own self; so n I went to
his room  begun to paw around re.  But I see I couldn’t do nothing
without a cle,  I dasn’t light one, of course.  So I judged I’d
got to do  or thing--lay for m  eavesdrop.  About that time
I hears ir footsteps coming,  was going to skip under  bed; I
reached for it, but it wasn’t where I thought it would be; but I touched
 curtain that hid Mary Jane’s frocks, so I jumped in behind that 
snuggled in amongst  gowns,  stood re perfectly still.

y come in  shut  door;   first thing  duke done was to
get down  look under  bed.  n I was glad I hadn’t found  bed
when I wanted it.   yet, you know, it’s kind of natural to hide under
 bed when you are up to anything private.  y sets down n, 
 king says:

“Well, what is it?   cut it middlin’ short, because it’s better for
us to be down re a-whoopin’ up  mournin’ than up here givin’ ‘em a
chance to talk us over.”

“Well, this is it, Capet.  I ain’t easy; I ain’t comfortable.  That
doctor lays on my mind.  I wanted to know your plans.  I’ve got a
notion,  I think it’s a sound one.”

“What is it, duke?”

“That we better glide out of this before three in  morning,  clip
it down  river with what we’ve got.  Specially, seeing we got it so
easy--_given_ back to us, flung at our heads, as you may say, when of
course we allowed to have to steal it back.  I’m for knocking off 
lighting out.”

That made me feel pretty bad.  About an hour or two ago it would a been
a little different, but now it made me feel bad  disappointed, 
king rips out  says:

“What!   not sell out  rest o’  property?  March off like
a passel of fools  leave eight or nine thous’n’ dollars’ worth o’
property layin’ around jest sufferin’ to be scooped in?-- all good,
salable stuff, too.”

 duke he grumbled; said  bag of gold was enough,  he didn’t
want to go no deeper--didn’t want to rob a lot of orphans of _everything_
y had.

“Why, how you talk!” says  king.  “We sha’n’t rob ‘em of nothing at
all but jest this money.   people that _buys_  property is 
suff’rers; because as soon ‘s it’s found out ‘at we didn’t own it--which
won’t be long after we’ve slid-- sale won’t be valid,  it ‘ll all
go back to  estate.  se yer orphans ‘ll git ir house back agin,
 that’s enough for _m_; y’re young  spry,  k’n easy
earn a livin’.  _y_ ain’t a-goin to suffer.  Why, jest think--re’s
thous’n’s  thous’n’s that ain’t nigh so well off.  Bless you, _y_
ain’t got noth’n’ to complain of.”

Well,  king he talked him blind; so at last he give in,  said all
right, but said he believed it was blamed foolishness to stay,  that
doctor hanging over m.  But  king says:

“Cuss  doctor!  What do we k’yer for _him_?  Hain’t we got all 
fools in town on our side?   ain’t that a big enough majority in any
town?”

So y got ready to go down stairs again.   duke says:

“I don’t think we put that money in a good place.”

That cheered me up.  I’d begun to think I warn’t going to get a hint of
no kind to help me.   king says:

“Why?”

“Because Mary Jane ‘ll be in mourning from this out;  first you know
 nigger that does up  rooms will get an order to box se duds
up  put ‘em away;  do you reckon a nigger can run across money 
not borrow some of it?”

“Your head’s level agin, duke,” says  king;  he comes a-fumbling
under  curtain two or three foot from where I was.  I stuck tight to
 wall  kept mighty still, though quivery;  I wondered what m
fellows would say to me if y catched me;  I tried to think what
I’d better do if y did catch me.  But  king he got  bag before
I could think more than about a half a thought,  he never suspicioned
I was around.  y took  shoved  bag through a rip in  straw
tick that was under  fear-bed,  crammed it in a foot or two
amongst  straw  said it was all right now, because a nigger only
makes up  fear-bed,  don’t turn over  straw tick only about
twice a year,  so it warn’t in no danger of getting stole now.

But I knowed better.  I had it out of re before y was half-way
down stairs.  I groped along up to my cubby,  hid it re till I
could get a chance to do better.  I judged I better hide it outside
of  house somewheres, because if y missed it y would give 
house a good ransacking:  I knowed that very well.  n I turned in,
with my clos all on; but I couldn’t a gone to sleep if I’d a wanted
to, I was in such a sweat to get through with  business.  By  by I
heard  king   duke come up; so I rolled off my pallet  laid
with my chin at  top of my ladder,  waited to see if anything was
going to happen.  But nothing did.

So I held on till all  late sounds had quit   early ones hadn’t
begun yet;  n I slipped down  ladder.




CHAPTER XXVII.

I crept to ir doors  listened; y was snoring.  So I tiptoed
along,  got down stairs all right.  re warn’t a sound anywheres.
 I peeped through a crack of  dining-room door,  see  men that
was watching  corpse all sound asleep on ir chairs.   door
was open into  parlor, where  corpse was laying,  re was a
cle in both rooms. I passed along,   parlor door was open; but
I see re warn’t nobody in re but  remainders of Peter; so I
shoved on by; but  front door was locked,   key wasn’t re.
 Just n I heard somebody coming down  stairs, back behind me.  I
run in  parlor  took a swift look around,   only place I
see to hide  bag was in  coffin.   lid was shoved along about
a foot, showing  dead man’s face down in re, with a wet cloth over
it,  his shroud on.  I tucked  money-bag in under  lid, just
down beyond where his hs was crossed, which made me creep, y was
so cold,  n I run back across  room  in behind  door.

 person coming was Mary Jane.  She went to  coffin, very soft, 
kneeled down  looked in; n she put up her hkerchief,  I see
she begun to cry, though I couldn’t hear her,  her back was to me.  I
slid out,  as I passed  dining-room I thought I’d make sure m
watchers hadn’t seen me; so I looked through  crack,  everything
was all right.  y hadn’t stirred.

I slipped up to bed, feeling rur blue, on accounts of  thing
playing out that way after I had took so much trouble  run so much
resk about it.  Says I, if it could stay where it is, all right; because
when we get down  river a hundred mile or two I could write back to
Mary Jane,  she could dig him up again  get it; but that ain’t 
thing that’s going to happen;  thing that’s going to happen is, 
money ‘ll be found when y come to screw on  lid.  n  king
‘ll get it again,  it ‘ll be a long day before he gives anybody
anor chance to smouch it from him. Of course I _wanted_ to slide
down  get it out of re, but I dasn’t try it.  Every minute it was
getting earlier now,  pretty soon some of m watchers would begin
to stir,  I might get catched--catched with six thous dollars in my
hs that nobody hadn’t hired me to take care of.  I don’t wish to be
mixed up in no such business as that, I says to myself.

When I got down stairs in  morning  parlor was shut up,  
watchers was gone.  re warn’t nobody around but  family  
widow Bartley  our tribe.  I watched ir faces to see if anything
had been happening, but I couldn’t tell.

Towards  middle of  day  undertaker come with his man,  y
set  coffin in  middle of  room on a couple of chairs,  n
set all our chairs in rows,  borrowed more from  neighbors till
 hall   parlor   dining-room was full.  I see  coffin
lid was  way it was before, but I dasn’t go to look in under it, with
folks around.

n  people begun to flock in,   beats   girls took
seats in  front row at  head of  coffin,  for a half an hour
 people filed around slow, in single rank,  looked down at 
dead man’s face a minute,  some dropped in a tear,  it was
all very still  solemn, only  girls   beats holding
hkerchiefs to ir eyes  keeping ir heads bent,  sobbing a
little.  re warn’t no or sound but  scraping of  feet on
 floor  blowing noses--because people always blows m more at a
funeral than y do at or places except church.

When  place was packed full  undertaker he slid around in his
black gloves with his softy sooring ways, putting on  last
touches,  getting people  things all ship-shape  comfortable,
 making no more sound than a cat.  He never spoke; he moved people
around, he squeezed in late ones, he opened up passageways,  done
it with nods,  signs with his hs.  n he took his place over
against  wall. He was  softest, glidingest, stealthiest man I ever
see;  re warn’t no more smile to him than re is to a ham.

y had borrowed a melodeum--a sick one;  when everything was ready
a young woman set down  worked it,  it was pretty skreeky 
colicky,  everybody joined in  sung,  Peter was  only one
that had a good thing, according to my notion.  n  Reverend Hobson
opened up, slow  solemn,  begun to talk;  straight off  most
outrageous row busted out in  cellar a body ever heard; it was only
one dog, but he made a most powerful racket,  he kept it up right
along;  parson he had to st re, over  coffin,  wait--you
couldn’t hear yourself think.  It was right down awkward,  nobody
didn’t seem to know what to do.  But pretty soon y see that
long-legged undertaker make a sign to  preacher as much as to say,
“Don’t you worry--just depend on me.”  n he stooped down  begun
to glide along  wall, just his shoulders showing over  people’s
heads.  So he glided along,   powwow  racket getting more 
more outrageous all  time;  at last, when he had gone around two
sides of  room, he disappears down cellar.  n in about two seconds
we heard a whack,   dog he finished up with a most amazing howl or
two,  n everything was dead still,   parson begun his solemn
talk where he left off.  In a minute or two here comes this undertaker’s
back  shoulders gliding along  wall again;  so he glided 
glided around three sides of  room,  n rose up,  shaded his
mouth with his hs,  stretched his neck out towards  preacher,
over  people’s heads,  says, in a kind of a coarse whisper, “_He
had a rat_!”  n he drooped down  glided along  wall again to
his place.  You could see it was a great satisfaction to  people,
because naturally y wanted to know.  A little thing like that don’t
cost nothing,  it’s just  little things that makes a man to be
looked up to  liked.  re warn’t no more popular man in town than
what that undertaker was.

Well,  funeral sermon was very good, but pison long  tiresome; 
n  king he shoved in  got off some of his usual rubbage, 
at last  job was through,   undertaker begun to sneak up on 
coffin with his screw-driver.  I was in a sweat n,  watched him
pretty keen. But he never meddled at all; just slid  lid along as
soft as mush,  screwed it down tight  fast.  So re I was!  I
didn’t know wher  money was in re or not.  So, says I, s’pose
somebody has hogged that bag on  sly?--now how do I know wher
to write to Mary Jane or not? S’pose she dug him up  didn’t find
nothing, what would she think of me? Blame it, I says, I might get
hunted up  jailed; I’d better lay low  keep dark,  not write at
all;  thing’s awful mixed now; trying to better it, I’ve worsened it
a hundred times,  I wish to goodness I’d just let it alone, dad fetch
 whole business!

y buried him,  we come back home,  I went to watching faces
again--I couldn’t help it,  I couldn’t rest easy.  But nothing come of
it;  faces didn’t tell me nothing.

 king he visited around in  evening,  sweetened everybody up,
 made himself ever so friendly;  he give out  idea that his
congregation over in Engl would be in a sweat about him, so he must
hurry  settle up  estate right away  leave for home.  He was
very sorry he was so pushed,  so was everybody; y wished he could
stay longer, but y said y could see it couldn’t be done.   he
said of course him  William would take  girls home with m; 
that pleased everybody too, because n  girls would be well fixed
 amongst ir own relations;  it pleased  girls, too--tickled
m so y clean forgot y ever had a trouble in  world;  told
him to sell out as quick as he wanted to, y would be ready.  m
poor things was that glad  happy it made my heart ache to see m
getting fooled  lied to so, but I didn’t see no safe way for me to
chip in  change  general tune.

Well, blamed if  king didn’t bill  house   niggers  all
 property for auction straight off--sale two days after  funeral;
but anybody could buy private beforeh if y wanted to.

So  next day after  funeral, along about noon-time,  girls’ joy
got  first jolt.  A couple of nigger traders come along,   king
sold m  niggers reasonable, for three-day drafts as y called
it,  away y went,  two sons up  river to Memphis,  ir
mor down  river to Orleans.  I thought m poor girls  m
niggers would break ir hearts for grief; y cried around each
or,  took on so it most made me down sick to see it.   girls
said y hadn’t ever dreamed of seeing  family separated or sold
away from  town.  I can’t ever get it out of my memory,  sight of
m poor miserable girls  niggers hanging around each or’s necks
 crying;  I reckon I couldn’t a stood it all, but would a had
to bust out  tell on our gang if I hadn’t knowed  sale warn’t no
account   niggers would be back home in a week or two.

 thing made a big stir in  town, too,  a good many come out
flatfooted  said it was scalous to separate  mor  
children that way.  It injured  frauds some; but  old fool he
bulled right along, spite of all  duke could say or do,  I tell
you  duke was powerful uneasy.

Next day was auction day.  About broad day in  morning  king 
 duke come up in  garret  woke me up,  I see by ir look
that re was trouble.   king says:

“Was you in my room night before last?”

“No, your majesty”--which was  way I always called him when nobody but
our gang warn’t around.

“Was you in re yisterday er last night?”

“No, your majesty.”

“Honor bright, now--no lies.”

“Honor bright, your majesty, I’m telling you  truth.  I hain’t been
a-near your room since Miss Mary Jane took you   duke  showed
it to you.”

 duke says:

“Have you seen anybody else go in re?”

“No, your grace, not as I remember, I believe.”

“Stop  think.”

I studied awhile  see my chance; n I says:

“Well, I see  niggers go in re several times.”

Both of m gave a little jump,  looked like y hadn’t ever
expected it,  n like y _had_.  n  duke says:

“What, all of m?”

“No--leastways, not all at once--that is, I don’t think I ever see m
all come _out_ at once but just one time.”

“Hello!  When was that?”

“It was  day we had  funeral.  In  morning.  It warn’t early,
because I overslept.  I was just starting down  ladder,  I see
m.”

“Well, go on, _go_ on!  What did y do?  How’d y act?”

“y didn’t do nothing.   y didn’t act anyway much, as fur as I
see. y tiptoed away; so I seen, easy enough, that y’d shoved in
re to do up your majesty’s room, or something, s’posing you was up;
 found you _warn’t_ up,  so y was hoping to slide out of 
way of trouble without waking you up, if y hadn’t already waked you
up.”

“Great guns, _this_ is a go!” says  king;  both of m looked
pretty sick  tolerable silly.  y stood re a-thinking 
scratching ir heads a minute,   duke he bust into a kind of a
little raspy chuckle,  says:

“It does beat all how neat  niggers played ir h.  y let on
to be _sorry_ y was going out of this region!   I believed y
_was_ sorry,  so did you,  so did everybody.  Don’t ever tell _me_
any more that a nigger ain’t got any histrionic talent.  Why,  way
y played that thing it would fool _anybody_.  In my opinion, re’s
a fortune in ‘em.  If I had capital  a ater, I wouldn’t want a
better lay-out than that-- here we’ve gone  sold ‘em for a song.
 Yes,  ain’t privileged to sing  song yet.  Say, where _is_ that
song--that draft?”

“In  bank for to be collected.  Where _would_ it be?”

“Well, _that’s_ all right n, thank goodness.”

Says I, kind of timid-like:

“Is something gone wrong?”

 king whirls on me  rips out:

“None o’ your business!  You keep your head shet,  mind y’r own

As y was starting down  ladder  duke he chuckles again,

“Quick sales __ small profits!  It’s a good business--yes.”

 king snarls around on him  says:

“I was trying to do for  best in sellin’ ‘em out so quick.  If

 king sassed back as much as was safe for him,  n swapped

CHAPTER XXVIII.

BY  by it was getting-up time.  So I come down  ladder  started

“ if it just happens so that I don’t get away, but get took up along

Pray for me!  I reckoned if she knowed me she’d take a job that was more

“Well, measles,  whooping-cough,  erysiplas,  consumption,

CHAPTER XXIX.

Y was fetching a very nice-looking old gentleman along,  a
nice-looking younger one, with his right arm in a sling.  , my souls,
how  people yelled  laughed,  kept it up.  But I didn’t see no
joke about it,  I judged it would strain  duke   king some

 wait.”

So him   new dummy started off;   king he laughs, 
blers out:

“Broke his arm--_very_ likely, _ain’t_ it?-- very convenient, too,
for a fraud that’s got to make signs,  ain’t learnt how.  Lost

So he laughed again;  so did everybody else, except three or four,

“ day before  funeral, friend,” says  king.

“But what time o’ day?”

“In  evenin’--‘bout an hour er two before sundown.”

“_How’d_ you come?”

“I come down on  Susan Powell from Cincinnati.”

“Well, n, how’d you come to be up at  Pint in  _mornin_’--in a

“I warn’t up at  Pint in  mornin’.”

“It’s a lie.”

Several of m jumped for him  begged him not to talk that way to an

“Preacher be hanged, he’s a fraud  a liar.  He was up at  Pint

 doctor he up  says:

“Would you know  boy again if you was to see him, Hines?”

“I reckon I would, but I don’t know.  Why, yonder he is, now.  I know

It was me he pointed at.   doctor says:

“Neighbors, I don’t know wher  new couple is frauds or not; but if

It was nuts for  crowd, though maybe not for  king’s friends; so

We all got in a big room in  hotel,  lit up some cles,

Everybody agreed to that.  So I judged y had our gang in a pretty

“Gentlemen, I wish  money was re, for I ain’t got no disposition
to throw anything in  way of a fair, open, out--out investigation
o’ this misable business; but, alas,  money ain’t re; you k’n send
 see, if you want to.”

“Where is it, n?”

“Well, when my niece give it to me to keep for her I took  hid it
inside o’  straw tick o’ my bed, not wishin’ to bank it for  few
days we’d be here,  considerin’  bed a safe place, we not bein’
used to niggers,  suppos’n’ ‘em honest, like servants in Engl.
  niggers stole it  very next mornin’ after I had went down
stairs;  when I sold ‘em I hadn’t missed  money yit, so y got
clean away with it.  My servant here k’n tell you ‘bout it, gentlemen.”

 doctor  several said “Shucks!”  I see nobody didn’t altoger
believe him.  One man asked me if I see  niggers steal it.  I said
no, but I see m sneaking out of  room  hustling away,  I
never thought nothing, only I reckoned y was afraid y had waked up
my master  was trying to get away before he made trouble with m.
 That was all y asked me.  n  doctor whirls on me  says:

“Are _you_ English, too?”

I says yes;  him  some ors laughed,  said, “Stuff!”

Well, n y sailed in on  general investigation,  re we had
it, up  down, hour in, hour out,  nobody never said a word about
supper, nor ever seemed to think about it-- so y kept it up, 
kept it up;  it _was_  worst mixed-up thing you ever see.  y
made  king tell his yarn,  y made  old gentleman tell his’n;
 anybody but a lot of prejudiced chuckleheads would a _seen_ that 
old gentleman was spinning truth  t’or one lies.   by  by
y had me up to tell what I knowed.   king he give me a left-hed
look out of  corner of his eye,  so I knowed enough to talk on 
right side.  I begun to tell about Sheffield,  how we lived re,
 all about  English Wilkses,  so on; but I didn’t get pretty
fur till  doctor begun to laugh;  Levi Bell,  lawyer, says:

“Set down, my boy; I wouldn’t strain myself if I was you.  I reckon
you ain’t used to lying, it don’t seem to come hy; what you want is
practice.  You do it pretty awkward.”

I didn’t care nothing for  compliment, but I was glad to be let off,
anyway.

 doctor he started to say something,  turns  says:

“If you’d been in town at first, Levi Bell--”  king broke in 
reached out his h,  says:

“Why, is this my poor dead bror’s old friend that he’s wrote so often
about?”

 lawyer  him shook hs,   lawyer smiled  looked
pleased,  y talked right along awhile,  n got to one side
 talked low;  at last  lawyer speaks up  says:

“That ‘ll fix it.  I’ll take  order  send it, along with your
bror’s,  n y’ll know it’s all right.”

So y got some paper  a pen,   king he set down  twisted
his head to one side,  chawed his tongue,  scrawled off something;
 n y give  pen to  duke-- n for  first time 
duke looked sick.  But he took  pen  wrote.  So n  lawyer
turns to  new old gentleman  says:

“You  your bror please write a line or two  sign your names.”

 old gentleman wrote, but nobody couldn’t read it.   lawyer looked
powerful astonished,  says:

“Well, it beats _me_”-- snaked a lot of old letters out of his pocket,
 examined m,  n examined  old man’s writing,  n
_m_ again;  n says:  “se old letters is from Harvey Wilks;
 here’s _se_ two hwritings,  anybody can see y didn’t
write m” ( king   duke looked sold  foolish, I tell
you, to see how  lawyer had took m in), “ here’s _this_ old
gentleman’s h writing,  anybody can tell, easy enough, _he_ didn’t
write m--fact is,  scratches he makes ain’t properly _writing_ at
all.  Now, here’s some letters from--”

 new old gentleman says:

“If you please, let me explain.  Nobody can read my h but my bror
re--so he copies for me.  It’s _his_ h you’ve got re, not mine.”

“_Well_!” says  lawyer, “this _is_ a state of things.  I’ve got some
of William’s letters, too; so if you’ll get him to write a line or so we
can com--”

“He _can’t_ write with his left h,” says  old gentleman.  “If he
could use his right h, you would see that he wrote his own letters
 mine too.  Look at both, please--y’re by  same h.”

 lawyer done it,  says:

“I believe it’s so-- if it ain’t so, re’s a heap stronger
resemblance than I’d noticed before, anyway.  Well, well, well!  I
thought we was right on  track of a solution, but it’s gone to grass,
partly.  But anyway, one thing is proved--_se_ two ain’t eir of ‘em
Wilkses”-- he wagged his head towards  king   duke.

Well, what do you think?  That muleheaded old fool wouldn’t give in
_n_! Indeed he wouldn’t.  Said it warn’t no fair test.  Said his
bror William was  cussedest joker in  world,  hadn’t tried
to write--_he_ see William was going to play one of his jokes  minute
he put  pen to paper.   so he warmed up  went warbling 
warbling right along till he was actuly beginning to believe what he was
saying _himself_; but pretty soon  new gentleman broke in,  says:

“I’ve thought of something.  Is re anybody here that helped to lay
out my br--helped to lay out  late Peter Wilks for burying?”

“Yes,” says somebody, “me  Ab Turner done it.  We’re both here.”

n  old man turns towards  king,  says:

“Perhaps this gentleman can tell me what was tattooed on his breast?”

Blamed if  king didn’t have to brace up mighty quick, or he’d a
squshed down like a bluff bank that  river has cut under, it took
him so sudden; , mind you, it was a thing that was calculated to make
most _anybody_ sqush to get fetched such a solid one as that without any
notice, because how was _he_ going to know what was tattooed on  man?
 He whitened a little; he couldn’t help it;  it was mighty still in
re,  everybody bending a little forwards  gazing at him.  Says
I to myself, _now_ he’ll throw up  sponge--re ain’t no more use.
 Well, did he?  A body can’t hardly believe it, but he didn’t.  I reckon
he thought he’d keep  thing up till he tired m people out, so
y’d thin out,  him   duke could break loose  get away.
 Anyway, he set re,  pretty soon he begun to smile,  says:

“Mf!  It’s a _very_ tough question, _ain’t_ it!  _yes_, sir, I k’n
tell you what’s tattooed on his breast.  It’s jest a small, thin, blue
arrow--that’s what it is;  if you don’t look clost, you can’t see it.
 _now_ what do you say--hey?”

Well, I never see anything like that old blister for clean out--out
cheek.

 new old gentleman turns brisk towards Ab Turner  his pard, 
his eye lights up like he judged he’d got  king _this_ time, 
says:

“re--you’ve heard what he said!  Was re any such mark on Peter
Wilks’ breast?”

Both of m spoke up  says:

“We didn’t see no such mark.”

“Good!” says  old gentleman.  “Now, what you _did_ see on his breast
was a small dim P,  a B (which is an initial he dropped when he was
young),  a W, with dashes between m, so:  P--B--W”-- he marked
m that way on a piece of paper.  “Come, ain’t that what you saw?”

Both of m spoke up again,  says:

“No, we _didn’t_.  We never seen any marks at all.”

Well, everybody _was_ in a state of mind now,  y sings out:

“ whole _bilin_’ of ‘m ‘s frauds!  Le’s duck ‘em! le’s drown ‘em!
le’s ride ‘em on a rail!”  everybody was whooping at once,  re
was a rattling powwow.  But  lawyer he jumps on  table  yells,
 says:

“Gentlemen--gentle_men!_  Hear me just a word--just a _single_ word--if you
_please_!  re’s one way yet--let’s go  dig up  corpse  look.”

That took m.

“Hooray!” y all shouted,  was starting right off; but  lawyer
  doctor sung out:

“Hold on, hold on!  Collar all se four men   boy,  fetch
_m_ along, too!”

“We’ll do it!” y all shouted; “ if we don’t find m marks we’ll
lynch  whole gang!”

I _was_ scared, now, I tell you.  But re warn’t no getting away, you
know. y gripped us all,  marched us right along, straight for 
graveyard, which was a mile  a half down  river,   whole
town at our heels, for we made noise enough,  it was only nine in 
evening.

As we went by our house I wished I hadn’t sent Mary Jane out of town;
because now if I could tip her  wink she’d light out  save me, 
blow on our dead-beats.

Well, we swarmed along down  river road, just carrying on like
wildcats;  to make it more scary  sky was darking up,  
lightning beginning to wink  flitter,   wind to shiver amongst
 leaves. This was  most awful trouble  most dangersome I ever
was in;  I was kinder stunned; everything was going so different from
what I had allowed for; stead of being fixed so I could take my own time
if I wanted to,  see all  fun,  have Mary Jane at my back to
save me  set me free when  close-fit come, here was nothing in 
world betwixt me  sudden death but just m tattoo-marks.  If y
didn’t find m--

I couldn’t bear to think about it;  yet, somehow, I couldn’t think
about nothing else.  It got darker  darker,  it was a beautiful
time to give  crowd  slip; but that big husky had me by 
wrist--Hines-- a body might as well try to give Goliar  slip.  He
dragged me right along, he was so excited,  I had to run to keep up.

When y got re y swarmed into  graveyard  washed over it
like an overflow.   when y got to  grave y found y had
about a hundred times as many shovels as y wanted, but nobody hadn’t
thought to fetch a lantern.  But y sailed into digging anyway by 
flicker of  lightning,  sent a man to  nearest house, a half a
mile off, to borrow one.

So y dug  dug like everything;  it got awful dark,   rain
started,   wind swished  swushed along,   lightning come
brisker  brisker,   thunder boomed; but m people never took
no notice of it, y was so full of this business;  one minute
you could see everything  every face in that big crowd,  
shovelfuls of dirt sailing up out of  grave,   next second 
dark wiped it all out,  you couldn’t see nothing at all.

At last y got out  coffin  begun to unscrew  lid,  n
such anor crowding  shouldering  shoving as re was, to
scrouge in  get a sight, you never see;  in  dark, that way, it
was awful.  Hines he hurt my wrist dreadful pulling  tugging so,
 I reckon he clean forgot I was in  world, he was so excited 
panting.

All of a sudden  lightning let go a perfect sluice of white glare,
 somebody sings out:

“By  living jingo, here’s  bag of gold on his breast!”

Hines let out a whoop, like everybody else,  dropped my wrist 
give a big surge to bust his way in  get a look,   way I lit
out  shinned for  road in  dark re ain’t nobody can tell.

I had  road all to myself,  I fairly flew--leastways, I had it all
to myself except  solid dark,   now--n glares,  
buzzing of  rain,   thrashing of  wind,   splitting of
 thunder;  sure as you are born I did clip it along!

When I struck  town I see re warn’t nobody out in  storm, so
I never hunted for no back streets, but humped it straight through 
main one;  when I begun to get towards our house I aimed my eye 
set it. No light re;  house all dark--which made me feel sorry 
disappointed, I didn’t know why.  But at last, just as I was sailing by,
_flash_ comes  light in Mary Jane’s window!  my heart swelled up
sudden, like to bust;   same second  house  all was behind
me in  dark,  wasn’t ever going to be before me no more in this
world. She _was_  best girl I ever see,  had  most s.

 minute I was far enough above  town to see I could make 
towhead, I begun to look sharp for a boat to borrow,   first
time  lightning showed me one that wasn’t chained I snatched it 
shoved. It was a canoe,  warn’t fastened with nothing but a rope.
  towhead was a rattling big distance off, away out re in 
middle of  river, but I didn’t lose no time;  when I struck 
raft at last I was so fagged I would a just laid down to blow  gasp
if I could afforded it.  But I didn’t.  As I sprung aboard I sung out:

“Out with you, Jim,  set her loose!  Glory be to goodness, we’re shut
of m!”

Jim lit out,  was a-coming for me with both arms spread, he was so
full of joy; but when I glimpsed him in  lightning my heart shot up
in my mouth  I went overboard backwards; for I forgot he was old King
Lear  a drownded A-rab all in one,  it most scared  livers 
lights out of me.  But Jim fished me out,  was going to hug me 
bless me,  so on, he was so glad I was back  we was shut of 
king   duke, but I says:

“Not now; have it for breakfast, have it for breakfast!  Cut loose 
let her slide!”

So in two seconds away we went a-sliding down  river,  it _did_
seem so good to be free again  all by ourselves on  big river, 
nobody to bor us.  I had to skip around a bit,  jump up  crack
my heels a few times--I couldn’t help it; but about  third crack
I noticed a sound that I knowed mighty well,  held my breath 
listened  waited;  sure enough, when  next flash busted out
over  water, here y come!-- just a-laying to ir oars 
making ir skiff hum!  It was  king   duke.

So I wilted right down on to  planks n,  give up;  it was
all I could do to keep from crying.




CHAPTER XXX.

WHEN y got aboard  king went for me,  shook me by  collar,
 says:

“Tryin’ to give us  slip, was ye, you pup!  Tired of our company,
hey?”

I says:

“No, your majesty, we warn’t--_please_ don’t, your majesty!”

“Quick, n,  tell us what _was_ your idea, or I’ll shake 
insides out o’ you!”

“Honest, I’ll tell you everything just as it happened, your majesty.
  man that had a-holt of me was very good to me,  kept saying he
had a boy about as big as me that died last year,  he was sorry
to see a boy in such a dangerous fix;  when y was all took by
surprise by finding  gold,  made a rush for  coffin, he lets go
of me  whispers, ‘Heel it now, or y’ll hang ye, sure!’  I lit
out.  It didn’t seem no good for _me_ to stay--I couldn’t do nothing,
 I didn’t want to be hung if I could get away.  So I never stopped
running till I found  canoe;  when I got here I told Jim to hurry,
or y’d catch me  hang me yet,  said I was afeard you  
duke wasn’t alive now,  I was awful sorry,  so was Jim,  was
awful glad when we see you coming; you may ask Jim if I didn’t.”

Jim said it was so;   king told him to shut up,  said, “Oh,
yes, it’s _mighty_ likely!”  shook me up again,  said he reckoned
he’d drownd me.  But  duke says:

“Leggo  boy, you old idiot!  Would _you_ a done any different?  Did
you inquire around for _him_ when you got loose?  I don’t remember it.”

So  king let go of me,  begun to cuss that town  everybody in
it. But  duke says:

“You better a blame’ sight give _yourself_ a good cussing, for you’re
 one that’s entitled to it most.  You hain’t done a thing from 
start that had any sense in it, except coming out so cool  cheeky
with that imaginary blue-arrow mark.  That _was_ bright--it was right
down bully;  it was  thing that saved us.  For if it hadn’t been
for that y’d a jailed us till m Englishmen’s baggage come--
n-- penitentiary, you bet! But that trick took ‘em to 
graveyard,   gold done us a still bigger kindness; for if 
excited fools hadn’t let go all holts  made that rush to get a
look we’d a slept in our cravats to-night--cravats warranted to _wear_,
too--longer than _we’d_ need ‘em.”

y was still a minute--thinking; n  king says, kind of
absent-minded like:

“Mf!   we reckoned  _niggers_ stole it!”

That made me squirm!

“Yes,” says  duke, kinder slow  deliberate  sarcastic, “_we_
did.”

After about a half a minute  king drawls out:

“Leastways, I did.”

 duke says,  same way:

“On  contrary, I did.”

 king kind of ruffles up,  says:

“Looky here, Bilgewater, what’r you referrin’ to?”

 duke says, pretty brisk:

“When it comes to that, maybe you’ll let me ask, what was _you_
referring to?”

“Shucks!” says  king, very sarcastic; “but I don’t know--maybe you was
asleep,  didn’t know what you was about.”

 duke bristles up now,  says:

“Oh, let _up_ on this cussed nonsense; do you take me for a blame’ fool?
Don’t you reckon I know who hid that money in that coffin?”

“_Yes_, sir!  I know you _do_ know, because you done it yourself!”

“It’s a lie!”--  duke went for him.   king sings out:

“Take y’r hs off!--leggo my throat!--I take it all back!”

 duke says:

“Well, you just own up, first, that you _did_ hide that money re,
intending to give me  slip one of se days,  come back  dig
it up,  have it all to yourself.”

“Wait jest a minute, duke--answer me this one question, honest  fair;
if you didn’t put  money re, say it,  I’ll b’lieve you, 
take back everything I said.”

“You old scoundrel, I didn’t,  you know I didn’t.  re, now!”

“Well, n, I b’lieve you.  But answer me only jest this one more--now
_don’t_ git mad; didn’t you have it in your mind to hook  money 
hide it?”

 duke never said nothing for a little bit; n he says:

“Well, I don’t care if I _did_, I didn’t _do_ it, anyway.  But you not
only had it in mind to do it, but you _done_ it.”

“I wisht I never die if I done it, duke,  that’s honest.  I won’t say
I warn’t goin’ to do it, because I _was_; but you--I mean somebody--got in
ahead o’ me.”

“It’s a lie!  You done it,  you got to _say_ you done it, or--”

 king began to gurgle,  n he gasps out:

“‘Nough!--I _own up!_”

I was very glad to hear him say that; it made me feel much more easier
than what I was feeling before.  So  duke took his hs off 
says:

“If you ever deny it again I’ll drown you.  It’s _well_ for you to set
re  blubber like a baby--it’s fitten for you, after  way
you’ve acted. I never see such an old ostrich for wanting to gobble
everything-- I a-trusting you all  time, like you was my own
far.  You ought to been ashamed of yourself to st by  hear it
saddled on to a lot of poor niggers,  you never say a word for ‘em.
 It makes me feel ridiculous to think I was soft enough to _believe_
that rubbage.  Cuss you, I can see now why you was so anxious to make
up  deffisit--you wanted to get what money I’d got out of  Nonesuch
 one thing or anor,  scoop it _all_!”

 king says, timid,  still a-snuffling:

“Why, duke, it was you that said make up  deffisit; it warn’t me.”

“Dry up!  I don’t want to hear no more out of you!” says  duke.  “
_now_ you see what you GOT by it.  y’ve got all ir own money back,
 all of _ourn_ but a shekel or two _besides_.  G’long to bed, 
don’t you deffersit _me_ no more deffersits, long ‘s _you_ live!”

So  king sneaked into  wigwam  took to his bottle for comfort,
 before long  duke tackled HIS bottle;  so in about a half an
hour y was as thick as thieves again,   tighter y got 
lovinger y got,  went off a-snoring in each or’s arms.  y
both got powerful mellow, but I noticed  king didn’t get mellow
enough to forget to remember to not deny about hiding  money-bag
again.  That made me feel easy  satisfied.  Of course when y got
to snoring we had a long gabble,  I told Jim everything.




CHAPTER XXXI.

WE dasn’t stop again at any town for days  days; kept right along
down  river.  We was down south in  warm wear now,  a mighty
long ways from home.  We begun to come to trees with Spanish moss on
m, hanging down from  limbs like long, gray beards.  It was 
first I ever see it growing,  it made  woods look solemn 
dismal.  So now  frauds reckoned y was out of danger,  y
begun to work  villages again.

First y done a lecture on temperance; but y didn’t make enough
for m both to get drunk on.  n in anor village y started
a dancing-school; but y didn’t know no more how to dance than a
kangaroo does; so  first prance y made  general public jumped
in  pranced m out of town.  Anor time y tried to go at
yellocution; but y didn’t yellocute long till  audience got up 
give m a solid good cussing,  made m skip out.  y tackled
missionarying,  mesmerizing,  doctoring,  telling fortunes, 
a little of everything; but y couldn’t seem to have no luck.  So at
last y got just about dead broke,  laid around  raft as she
floated along, thinking  thinking,  never saying nothing, by 
half a day at a time,  dreadful blue  desperate.

 at last y took a change  begun to lay ir heads toger in
 wigwam  talk low  confidential two or three hours at a time.
Jim  me got uneasy.  We didn’t like  look of it.  We judged y
was studying up some kind of worse deviltry than ever.  We turned it
over  over,  at last we made up our minds y was going to break
into somebody’s house or store, or was going into  counterfeit-money
business, or something. So n we was pretty scared,  made up an
agreement that we wouldn’t have nothing in  world to do with such
actions,  if we ever got  least show we would give m  cold
shake  clear out  leave m behind. Well, early one morning we
hid  raft in a good, safe place about two mile below a little bit of
a shabby village named Pikesville,   king he went ashore  told
us all to stay hid whilst he went up to town  smelt around to see
if anybody had got any wind of  Royal Nonesuch re yet. (“House to
rob, you _mean_,” says I to myself; “ when you get through robbing it
you’ll come back here  wonder what has become of me  Jim  
raft-- you’ll have to take it out in wondering.”)  he said if he
warn’t back by midday  duke  me would know it was all right, 
we was to come along.

So we stayed where we was.   duke he fretted  sweated around, 
was in a mighty sour way.  He scolded us for everything,  we couldn’t
seem to do nothing right; he found fault with every little thing.
Something was a-brewing, sure.  I was good  glad when midday come
 no king; we could have a change, anyway-- maybe a chance for __
change on top of it.  So me   duke went up to  village, 
hunted around re for  king,  by  by we found him in 
back room of a little low doggery, very tight,  a lot of loafers
bullyragging him for sport,  he a-cussing  a-threatening with all
his might,  so tight he couldn’t walk,  couldn’t do nothing to
m.   duke he begun to abuse him for an old fool,   king
begun to sass back,   minute y was fairly at it I lit out 
shook  reefs out of my hind legs,  spun down  river road like
a deer, for I see our chance;  I made up my mind that it would be a
long day before y ever see me  Jim again.  I got down re all
out of breath but loaded up with joy,  sung out:

“Set her loose, Jim! we’re all right now!”

But re warn’t no answer,  nobody come out of  wigwam.  Jim was
gone!  I set up a shout-- n anor-- n anor one;  run
this way  that in  woods, whooping  screeching; but it warn’t
no use--old Jim was gone.  n I set down  cried; I couldn’t help
it. But I couldn’t set still long.  Pretty soon I went out on  road,
trying to think what I better do,  I run across a boy walking, 
asked him if he’d seen a strange nigger dressed so  so,  he says:

“Yes.”

“Whereabouts?” says I.

“Down to Silas Phelps’ place, two mile below here.  He’s a runaway
nigger,  y’ve got him.  Was you looking for him?”

“You bet I ain’t!  I run across him in  woods about an hour or two
ago,  he said if I hollered he’d cut my livers out-- told me to lay
down  stay where I was;  I done it.  Been re ever since; afeard
to come out.”

“Well,” he says, “you needn’t be afeard no more, becuz y’ve got him.
He run off f’m down South, som’ers.”

“It’s a good job y got him.”

“Well, I _reckon_!  re’s two hunderd dollars reward on him.  It’s
like picking up money out’n  road.”

“Yes, it is-- I could a had it if I’d been big enough; I see him
_first_. Who nailed him?”

“It was an old fellow--a stranger-- he sold out his chance in him for
forty dollars, becuz he’s got to go up  river  can’t wait.  Think
o’ that, now!  You bet _I’d_ wait, if it was seven year.”

“That’s me, every time,” says I.  “But maybe his chance ain’t worth
no more than that, if he’ll sell it so cheap.  Maybe re’s something
ain’t straight about it.”

“But it _is_, though--straight as a string.  I see  hbill myself.
 It tells all about him, to a dot--paints him like a picture,  tells
 plantation he’s frum, below Newr_leans_.  No-sirree-_bob_, y
ain’t no trouble ‘bout _that_ speculation, you bet you.  Say, gimme a
chaw tobacker, won’t ye?”

I didn’t have none, so he left.  I went to  raft,  set down in 
wigwam to think.  But I couldn’t come to nothing.  I thought till I wore
my head sore, but I couldn’t see no way out of  trouble.  After all
this long journey,  after all we’d done for m scoundrels, here it
was all come to nothing, everything all busted up  ruined, because
y could have  heart to serve Jim such a trick as that,  make
him a slave again all his life,  amongst strangers, too, for forty
dirty dollars.

Once I said to myself it would be a thous times better for Jim to
be a slave at home where his family was, as long as he’d _got_ to be a
slave,  so I’d better write a letter to Tom Sawyer  tell him to
tell Miss Watson where he was.  But I soon give up that notion for two
things: she’d be mad  disgusted at his rascality  ungratefulness
for leaving her,  so she’d sell him straight down  river again;
 if she didn’t, everybody naturally despises an ungrateful nigger,
 y’d make Jim feel it all  time,  so he’d feel ornery 
disgraced.  n think of _me_!  It would get all around that Huck
Finn helped a nigger to get his freedom;  if I was ever to see
anybody from that town again I’d be ready to get down  lick his boots
for shame.  That’s just  way:  a person does a low-down thing, 
n he don’t want to take no consequences of it. Thinks as long as he
can hide it, it ain’t no disgrace.  That was my fix exactly.  more I
studied about this  more my conscience went to grinding me,  
more wicked  low-down  ornery I got to feeling.  at last, when

slapping me in  face  letting me know my wickedness was being
watched all  time from up re in heaven, whilst I was stealing a

I most dropped in my tracks I was so scared.  Well, I tried  best I
could to kinder soften it up somehow for myself by saying I was brung

kept saying, “re was  Sunday-school, you could a gone to it; 
if you’d a done it y’d a learnt you re that people that acts as
I’d been acting about that nigger goes to everlasting fire.”

It made me shiver.   I about made up my mind to pray,  see if I
couldn’t try to quit being  kind of a boy I was  be better.  So
I kneeled down.  But  words wouldn’t come.  Why wouldn’t y?  It
warn’t no use to try  hide it from Him.  Nor from _me_, neir.  I
knowed very well why y wouldn’t come.  It was because my heart warn’t
right; it was because I warn’t square; it was because I was playing
double.  I was letting _on_ to give up sin, but away inside of me I was
holding on to  biggest one of all.  I was trying to make my mouth
_say_ I would do  right thing   clean thing,  go  write
to that nigger’s owner  tell where he was; but deep down in me I
knowed it was a lie,  He knowed it.  You can’t pray a lie--I found
that out.

So I was full of trouble, full as I could be;  didn’t know what to
do. At last I had an idea;  I says, I’ll go  write  letter--
n see if I can pray.  Why, it was astonishing,  way I felt as
light as a fear right straight off,  my troubles all gone.  So I
got a piece of paper  a pencil, all glad  excited,  set down
 wrote:

Miss Watson, your runaway nigger Jim is down here two mile below
Pikesville,  Mr. Phelps has got him  he will give him up for 
reward if you send.

_Huck Finn._

I felt good  all washed clean of sin for  first time I had ever
felt so in my life,  I knowed I could pray now.  But I didn’t do it

how good it was all this happened so,  how near I come to being lost
 going to hell.   went on thinking.   got to thinking over our
trip down  river;  I see Jim before me all  time:  in  day
 in  night-time, sometimes moonlight, sometimes storms,  we
a-floating along, talking  singing  laughing.  But somehow I
couldn’t seem to strike no places to harden me against him, but only 
or kind.  I’d see him sting my watch on top of his’n, ‘stead of

up re where  feud was;  such-like times;  would always call
me honey,  pet me  do everything he could think of for me,  how
good he always was;  at last I struck  time I saved him by telling
 men we had small-pox aboard,  he was so grateful,  said I was
 best friend old Jim ever had in  world,   _only_ one he’s

It was a close place.  I took it up,  held it in my h.  I was

I knowed it.  I studied a minute, sort of holding my breath,  n

“All right, n, I’ll _go_ to hell”-- tore it up.

It was awful thoughts  awful words, but y was said.   I let
m stay said;  never thought no more about reforming.  I shoved 
whole thing out of my head,  said I would take up wickedness again,

n I set to thinking over how to get at it,  turned over some

sunk her where I could find her again when I wanted her, about a quarter

n I struck up  road,  when I passed  mill I see a sign on

 duke.  He was sticking up a bill for  Royal Nonesuch--three-night

eager, “Where’s  raft?--got her in a good place?”

I says:

“Why, that’s just what I was going to ask your grace.”

n he didn’t look so joyful,  says:

“What was your idea for asking _me_?” he says.

“Well,” I says, “when I see  king in that doggery yesterday I says

“Blamed if I know--that is, what’s become of  raft.  That old fool had
made a trade  got forty dollars,  when we found him in  doggery
 loafers had matched half-dollars with him  got every cent but

“I wouldn’t shake my _nigger_, would I?-- only nigger I had in

“We never thought of that.  Fact is, I reckon we’d come to consider him

I had considerable money, so I give him ten cents, but begged him to

“Do you reckon that nigger would blow on us?  We’d skin him if he done

“How can he blow?  Hain’t he run off?”

“No!  That old fool sold him,  never divided with me,   money’s
gone.”

“_Sold_ him?”  I says,  begun to cry; “why, he was _my_ nigger, 
that was my money.  Where is he?--I want my nigger.”

“Well, you can’t _get_ your nigger, that’s all--so dry up your

 Blamed if I think I’d trust you.  Why, if you _was_ to blow on us--”

He stopped, but I never see  duke look so ugly out of his eyes
before. I went on a-whimpering,  says:

“I don’t want to blow on nobody;  I ain’t got no time to blow, nohow.
I got to turn out  find my nigger.”

He looked kinder bored,  stood re with his bills fluttering on

“I’ll tell you something.  We got to be here three days.  If you’ll

So I promised,  he says:

“A farmer by  name of Silas Ph--”  n he stopped.  You see, he

“ man that bought him is named Abram Foster--Abram G. Foster-- he

“All right,” I says, “I can walk it in three days.   I’ll start this

“No you wont, you’ll start _now_;  don’t you lose any time about it,
neir, nor do any gabbling by  way.  Just keep a tight tongue in

That was  order I wanted,  that was  one I played for.  I
wanted to be left free to work my plans.

“So clear out,” he says; “ you can tell Mr. Foster whatever you want

mind you don’t work your jaw any _between_ here  re.”

So I left,  struck for  back country.  I didn’t look around, but I
kinder felt like he was watching me.  But I knowed I could tire him out

wanted to of m,  wanted to get entirely shut of m.




CHAPTER XXXII.

WHEN I got re it was all still  Sunday-like,  hot  sunshiny;
 hs was gone to  fields;  re was m kind of faint
dronings of bugs  flies in  air that makes it seem so lonesome 
like everybody’s dead  gone;  if a breeze fans along  quivers
 leaves it makes you feel mournful, because you feel like it’s
spirits whispering--spirits that’s been dead ever so many years-- you
always think y’re talking about _you_.  As a general thing it makes a
body wish _he_ was dead, too,  done with it all.

Phelps’ was one of se little one-horse cotton plantations,  y
all look alike.  A rail fence round a two-acre yard; a stile made out

“No,” says her husb.

He sprung to  window at  head of  bed,  that give Mrs.

By jings, I most slumped through  floor!  But re warn’t no time to

Well, I couldn’t _have_ it that way; it wouldn’t do at all.  I must go

CHAPTER XXXIII.

SO I started for town in  wagon,  when I was half-way I see a
wagon coming,  sure enough it was Tom Sawyer,  I stopped 
waited till he come along.  I says “Hold on!”  it stopped alongside,
 his mouth opened up like a trunk,  stayed so;  he swallowed
two or three times like a person that’s got a dry throat,  n says:

“I hain’t ever done you no harm.  You know that.  So, n, what you
want to come back  ha’nt _me_ for?”

I says:

“I hain’t come back--I hain’t been _gone_.”

When he heard my voice it righted him up some, but he warn’t quite
satisfied yet.  He says:

“Don’t you play nothing on me, because I wouldn’t on you.  Honest injun
now, you ain’t a ghost?”

“Well--I--I--well, that ought to settle it, of course; but I can’t somehow
seem to underst it no way.  Looky here, warn’t you ever murdered _at

“No.  I warn’t ever murdered at all--I played it on m.  You come in
here  feel of me if you don’t believe me.”

So he done it;  it satisfied him;  he was that glad to see me

thought,  pretty soon he says:

“It’s all right; I’ve got it.  Take my trunk in your wagon,  let on
it’s your’n;  you turn back  fool along slow, so as to get to 
house about  time you ought to;  I’ll go towards town a piece, 
take a fresh start,  get re a quarter or a half an hour after you;
 you needn’t let on to know me at first.”

I says:

“All right; but wait a minute.  re’s one more thing--a thing that
_nobody_ don’t know but me.   that is, re’s a nigger here that
I’m a-trying to steal out of slavery,  his name is _Jim_--old Miss
Watson’s Jim.”

He says:

“What!  Why, Jim is--”

He stopped  went to studying.  I says:

“I know what you’ll say.  You’ll say it’s dirty, low-down business; but
what if it is?  I’m low down;  I’m a-going to steal him,  I want
you keep mum  not let on.  Will you?”

His eye lit up,  he says:

“I’ll _help_ you steal him!”

Well, I let go all holts n, like I was shot.  It was  most
astonishing speech I ever heard-- I’m bound to say Tom Sawyer fell
considerable in my estimation.  Only I couldn’t believe it.  Tom Sawyer
a _nigger-stealer!_

“Oh, shucks!”  I says; “you’re joking.”

“I ain’t joking, eir.”

“Well, n,” I says, “joking or no joking, if you hear anything said
about a runaway nigger, don’t forget to remember that _you_ don’t know
nothing about him,  I don’t know nothing about him.”

n we took  trunk  put it in my wagon,  he drove off his
way  I drove mine.  But of course I forgot all about driving slow on
accounts of being glad  full of thinking; so I got home a heap too
quick for that length of a trip.   old gentleman was at  door, 
he says:

“Why, this is wonderful!  Whoever would a thought it was in that mare
to do it?  I wish we’d a timed her.   she hain’t sweated a hair--not
a hair. It’s wonderful.  Why, I wouldn’t take a hundred dollars for that
horse now--I wouldn’t, honest;  yet I’d a sold her for fifteen before,
 thought ‘twas all she was worth.”

That’s all he said.  He was  innocentest, best old soul I ever see.
But it warn’t surprising; because he warn’t only just a farmer, he was
a preacher, too,  had a little one-horse log church down back of 
plantation, which he built it himself at his own expense, for a church
 schoolhouse,  never charged nothing for his preaching,  it was
worth it, too.  re was plenty or farmer-preachers like that, 
done  same way, down South.

In about half an hour Tom’s wagon drove up to  front stile,  Aunt
Sally she see it through  window, because it was only about fifty
yards,  says:

“Why, re’s somebody come!  I wonder who ‘tis?  Why, I do believe it’s
a stranger.  Jimmy” (that’s one of  children) “run  tell Lize to
put on anor plate for dinner.”

Everybody made a rush for  front door, because, of course, a stranger
don’t come _every_ year,  so he lays over  yaller-fever, for
interest, when he does come.  Tom was over  stile  starting for
 house;  wagon was spinning up  road for  village,  we
was all bunched in  front door.  Tom had his store clos on,  an
audience-- that was always nuts for Tom Sawyer.  In m circumstances
it warn’t no trouble to him to throw in an amount of style that was
suitable.  He warn’t a boy to meeky along up that yard like a sheep; no,
he come ca’m  important, like  ram.  When he got a-front of us he
lifts his hat ever so gracious  dainty, like it was  lid of a box
that had butterflies asleep in it  he didn’t want to disturb m,
 says:

“Mr. Archibald Nichols, I presume?”

“No, my boy,” says  old gentleman, “I’m sorry to say ‘t your driver
has deceived you; Nichols’s place is down a matter of three mile more.
Come in, come in.”

Tom he took a look back over his shoulder,  says, “Too late--he’s out
of sight.”

“Yes, he’s gone, my son,  you must come in  eat your dinner with
us;  n we’ll hitch up  take you down to Nichols’s.”

“Oh, I _can’t_ make you so much trouble; I couldn’t think of it.  I’ll
walk--I don’t mind  distance.”

“But we won’t _let_ you walk--it wouldn’t be Sourn hospitality to do
it. Come right in.”

“Oh, _do_,” says Aunt Sally; “it ain’t a bit of trouble to us, not a
bit in  world.  You must stay.  It’s a long, dusty three mile, 
we can’t let you walk.  , besides, I’ve already told ‘em to put on
anor plate when I see you coming; so you mustn’t disappoint us.  Come
right in  make yourself at home.”

So Tom he thanked m very hearty  hsome,  let himself be
persuaded,  come in;  when he was in he said he was a stranger
from Hicksville, Ohio,  his name was William Thompson-- he made
anor bow.

Well, he run on,  on,  on, making up stuff about Hicksville 
everybody in it he could invent,  I getting a little nervious, 
wondering how this was going to help me out of my scrape;  at last,
still talking along, he reached over  kissed Aunt Sally right on 
mouth,  n settled back again in his chair comfortable,  was
going on talking; but she jumped up  wiped it off with  back of
her h,  says:

“You owdacious puppy!”

He looked kind of hurt,  says:

“I’m surprised at you, m’am.”

“You’re s’rp--Why, what do you reckon I am?  I’ve a good notion to take
--Say, what do you mean by kissing me?”

He looked kind of humble,  says:

“I didn’t mean nothing, m’am.  I didn’t mean no harm.  I--I--thought you’d
like it.”

“Why, you born fool!”  She took up  spinning stick,  it looked
like it was all she could do to keep from giving him a crack with it.
 “What made you think I’d like it?”

“Well, I don’t know.  Only, y--y--told me you would.”

“_y_ told you I would.  Whoever told you’s _anor_ lunatic.  I
never heard  beat of it.  Who’s _y_?”

“Why, everybody.  y all said so, m’am.”

It was all she could do to hold in;  her eyes snapped,  her
fingers worked like she wanted to scratch him;  she says:

“Who’s ‘everybody’?  Out with ir names, or r’ll be an idiot
short.”

He got up  looked distressed,  fumbled his hat,  says:

“I’m sorry,  I warn’t expecting it.  y told me to.  y all told
me to.  y all said, kiss her;  said she’d like it.  y all said
it--every one of m.  But I’m sorry, m’am,  I won’t do it no more--I
won’t, honest.”

“You won’t, won’t you?  Well, I sh’d _reckon_ you won’t!”

“No’m, I’m honest about it; I won’t ever do it again--till you ask me.”

“Till I _ask_ you!  Well, I never see  beat of it in my born days!
 I lay you’ll be  Methusalem-numskull of creation before ever I ask
you--or  likes of you.”

“Well,” he says, “it does surprise me so.  I can’t make it out, somehow.
y said you would,  I thought you would.  But--” He stopped 
looked around slow, like he wished he could run across a friendly eye
somewheres,  fetched up on  old gentleman’s,  says, “Didn’t
_you_ think she’d like me to kiss her, sir?”

“Why, no; I--I--well, no, I b’lieve I didn’t.”

n he looks on around  same way to me,  says:

“Tom, didn’t _you_ think Aunt Sally ‘d open out her arms  say, ‘Sid
Sawyer--’”

“My l!” she says, breaking in  jumping for him, “you impudent
young rascal, to fool a body so--”  was going to hug him, but he
fended her off,  says:

“No, not till you’ve asked me first.”

So she didn’t lose no time, but asked him;  hugged him  kissed
him over  over again,  n turned him over to  old man,  he
took what was left.   after y got a little quiet again she says:

“Why, dear me, I never see such a surprise.  We warn’t looking for _you_
at all, but only Tom.  Sis never wrote to me about anybody coming but
him.”

“It’s because it warn’t _intended_ for any of us to come but Tom,” he
says; “but I begged  begged,  at  last minute she let me
come, too; so, coming down  river, me  Tom thought it would be a
first-rate surprise for him to come here to  house first,  for me
to by  by tag along  drop in,  let on to be a stranger.  But it
was a mistake, Aunt Sally.  This ain’t no healthy place for a stranger
to come.”

“No--not impudent whelps, Sid.  You ought to had your jaws boxed; I
hain’t been so put out since I don’t know when.  But I don’t care, I
don’t mind  terms--I’d be willing to st a thous such jokes to
have you here. Well, to think of that performance!  I don’t deny it, I
was most putrified with astonishment when you give me that smack.”

We had dinner out in that broad open passage betwixt  house 
 kitchen;  re was things enough on that table for seven
families-- all hot, too; none of your flabby, tough meat that’s laid
in a cupboard in a damp cellar all night  tastes like a hunk of
old cold cannibal in  morning.  Uncle Silas he asked a pretty long
blessing over it, but it was worth it;  it didn’t cool it a bit,
neir,  way I’ve seen m kind of interruptions do lots of times.
 re was a considerable good deal of talk all  afternoon,  me
 Tom was on  lookout all  time; but it warn’t no use, y
didn’t happen to say nothing about any runaway nigger,  we was afraid
to try to work up to it.  But at supper, at night, one of  little
boys says:

“Pa, mayn’t Tom  Sid  me go to  show?”

“No,” says  old man, “I reckon re ain’t going to be any;  you
couldn’t go if re was; because  runaway nigger told Burton 
me all about that scalous show,  Burton said he would tell 
people; so I reckon y’ve drove  owdacious loafers out of town
before this time.”

So re it was!--but I couldn’t help it.  Tom  me was to sleep in 
same room  bed; so, being tired, we bid good-night  went up to
bed right after supper,  clumb out of  window  down 
lightning-rod,  shoved for  town; for I didn’t believe anybody was
going to give  king   duke a hint,  so if I didn’t hurry up
 give m one y’d get into trouble sure.

On  road Tom he told me all about how it was reckoned I was murdered,
 how pap disappeared pretty soon,  didn’t come back no more, 
what a stir re was when Jim run away;  I told Tom all about our
Royal Nonesuch rapscallions,  as much of  raft voyage as I had
time to;  as we struck into  town  up through   middle of
it--it was as much as half-after eight, n--here comes a raging rush of
people with torches,  an awful whooping  yelling,  banging tin
pans  blowing horns;  we jumped to one side to let m go by;
 as y went by I see y had  king   duke astraddle of a
rail--that is, I knowed it _was_  king   duke, though y was
all over tar  fears,  didn’t look like nothing in 
world that was human--just looked like a couple of monstrous big
soldier-plumes.  Well, it made me sick to see it;  I was sorry for
m poor pitiful rascals, it seemed like I couldn’t ever feel any
hardness against m any more in  world.  It was a dreadful thing to
see.  Human beings _can_ be awful cruel to one anor.

We see we was too late--couldn’t do no good.  We asked some stragglers
about it,  y said everybody went to  show looking very
innocent;  laid low  kept dark till  poor old king was in 
middle of his cavortings on  stage; n somebody give a signal, 
 house rose up  went for m.

So we poked along back home,  I warn’t feeling so brash as I was
before, but kind of ornery,  humble,  to blame, somehow--though
I hadn’t done nothing.  But that’s always  way; it don’t make no
difference wher you do right or wrong, a person’s conscience ain’t
got no sense,  just goes for him anyway.  If I had a yaller dog that
didn’t know no more than a person’s conscience does I would pison him.
It takes up more room than all  rest of a person’s insides,  yet
ain’t no good, nohow.  Tom Sawyer he says  same.




CHAPTER XXXIV.

WE stopped talking,  got to thinking.  By  by Tom says:

“Looky here, Huck, what fools we are to not think of it before!  I bet I
know where Jim is.”

“No!  Where?”

“In that hut down by  ash-hopper.  Why, looky here.  When we was at
dinner, didn’t you see a nigger man go in re with some vittles?”

“Yes.”

“What did you think  vittles was for?”

“For a dog.”

“So ‘d I. Well, it wasn’t for a dog.”

“Why?”

“Because part of it was watermelon.”

“So it was--I noticed it.  Well, it does beat all that I never thought
about a dog not eating watermelon.  It shows how a body can see 
don’t see at  same time.”

“Well,  nigger unlocked  padlock when he went in,  he locked it
again when he came out.  He fetched uncle a key about  time we got up
from table--same key, I bet.  Watermelon shows man, lock shows prisoner;
 it ain’t likely re’s two prisoners on such a little plantation,
 where  people’s all so kind  good.  Jim’s  prisoner.  All
right--I’m glad we found it out detective fashion; I wouldn’t give shucks
for any or way.  Now you work your mind,  study out a plan to
steal Jim,  I will study out one, too;  we’ll take  one we like
 best.”

What a head for just a boy to have!  If I had Tom Sawyer’s head I
wouldn’t trade it off to be a duke, nor mate of a steamboat, nor clown
in a circus, nor nothing I can think of.  I went to thinking out a plan,
but only just to be doing something; I knowed very well where  right
plan was going to come from.  Pretty soon Tom says:

“Ready?”

“Yes,” I says.

“All right--bring it out.”

“My plan is this,” I says.  “We can easy find out if it’s Jim in re.
n get up my canoe to-morrow night,  fetch my raft over from 
isl.  n  first dark night that comes steal  key out of 
old man’s britches after he goes to bed,  shove off down  river
on  raft with Jim, hiding daytimes  running nights,  way me 
Jim used to do before.  Wouldn’t that plan work?”

“_Work_?  Why, cert’nly it would work, like rats a-fighting.  But it’s
too blame’ simple; re ain’t nothing _to_ it.  What’s  good of a
plan that ain’t no more trouble than that?  It’s as mild as goose-milk.
 Why, Huck, it wouldn’t make no more talk than breaking into a soap
factory.”

I never said nothing, because I warn’t expecting nothing different; but
I knowed mighty well that whenever he got _his_ plan ready it wouldn’t
have none of m objections to it.

 it didn’t.  He told me what it was,  I see in a minute it was
worth fifteen of mine for style,  would make Jim just as free a man
as mine would,  maybe get us all killed besides.  So I was satisfied,
 said we would waltz in on it.  I needn’t tell what it was here,
because I knowed it wouldn’t stay  way, it was.  I knowed he would be
changing it around every which way as we went along,  heaving in new
bullinesses wherever he got a chance.   that is what he done.

Well, one thing was dead sure,  that was that Tom Sawyer was in
earnest,  was actuly going to help steal that nigger out of slavery.
That was  thing that was too many for me.  Here was a boy that was
respectable  well brung up;  had a character to lose;  folks at
home that had characters;  he was bright  not lear-headed; 
knowing  not ignorant;  not mean, but kind;  yet here he was,
without any more pride, or rightness, or feeling, than to stoop to
this business,  make himself a shame,  his family a shame,
before everybody.  I _couldn’t_ underst it no way at all.  It was
outrageous,  I knowed I ought to just up  tell him so;  so be
his true friend,  let him quit  thing right where he was  save
himself.  I _did_ start to tell him; but he shut me up,  says:

“Don’t you reckon I know what I’m about?  Don’t I generly know what I’m
about?”

“Yes.”

“Didn’t I _say_ I was going to help steal  nigger?”

“Yes.”

“_Well_, n.”

That’s all he said,  that’s all I said.  It warn’t no use to say any
more; because when he said he’d do a thing, he always done it.  But I
couldn’t make out how he was willing to go into this thing; so I just
let it go,  never bored no more about it.  If he was bound to have
it so, I couldn’t help it.

When we got home  house was all dark  still; so we went on down to
 hut by  ash-hopper for to examine it.  We went through  yard
so as to see what  hounds would do.  y knowed us,  didn’t make
no more noise than country dogs is always doing when anything comes by
in  night.  When we got to  cabin we took a look at  front 
 two sides;  on  side I warn’t acquainted with--which was 
north side--we found a square window-hole, up tolerable high, with just
one stout board nailed across it.  I says:

“Here’s  ticket.  This hole’s big enough for Jim to get through if we
wrench off  board.”

Tom says:

“It’s as simple as tit-tat-toe, three-in-a-row,  as easy as
playing hooky.  I should _hope_ we can find a way that’s a little more
complicated than _that_, Huck Finn.”

“Well, n,” I says, “how ‘ll it do to saw him out,  way I done
before I was murdered that time?”

“That’s more _like_,” he says.  “It’s real mysterious,  troublesome,
 good,” he says; “but I bet we can find a way that’s twice as long.
 re ain’t no hurry; le’s keep on looking around.”

Betwixt  hut   fence, on  back side, was a lean-to that
joined  hut at  eaves,  was made out of plank.  It was as long
as  hut, but narrow--only about six foot wide.   door to it was at
 south end,  was padlocked.  Tom he went to  soap-kettle 
searched around,  fetched back  iron thing y lift  lid with;
so he took it  prized out one of  staples.   chain fell down,
 we opened  door  went in,  shut it,  struck a match,
 see  shed was only built against a cabin  hadn’t no connection
with it;  re warn’t no floor to  shed, nor nothing in it but
some old rusty played-out hoes  spades  picks  a crippled plow.
  match went out,  so did we,  shoved in  staple again, 
 door was locked as good as ever. Tom was joyful.  He says;

“Now we’re all right.  We’ll _dig_ him out.  It ‘ll take about a week!”

n we started for  house,  I went in  back door--you only have
to pull a buckskin latch-string, y don’t fasten  doors--but that
warn’t romantical enough for Tom Sawyer; no way would do him but he must
climb up  lightning-rod.  But after he got up half way about three
times,  missed fire  fell every time,   last time most
busted his brains out, he thought he’d got to give it up; but after he
was rested he allowed he would give her one more turn for luck,  this
time he made  trip.

In  morning we was up at break of day,  down to  nigger cabins
to pet  dogs  make friends with  nigger that fed Jim--if it
_was_ Jim that was being fed.   niggers was just getting through
breakfast  starting for  fields;  Jim’s nigger was piling up
a tin pan with bread  meat  things;  whilst  ors was
leaving,  key come from  house.

This nigger had a good-natured, chuckle-headed face,  his wool was
all tied up in little bunches with thread.  That was to keep witches
off.  He said  witches was pestering him awful se nights, 
making him see all kinds of strange things,  hear all kinds of
strange words  noises,  he didn’t believe he was ever witched so
long before in his life.  He got so worked up,  got to running on so
about his troubles, he forgot all about what he’d been a-going to do.
 So Tom says:

“What’s  vittles for?  Going to feed  dogs?”

 nigger kind of smiled around gradually over his face, like when you
heave a brickbat in a mud-puddle,  he says:

“Yes, Mars Sid, A dog.  Cur’us dog, too.  Does you want to go en look at
‘im?”

“Yes.”

I hunched Tom,  whispers:

“You going, right here in  daybreak?  _that_ warn’t  plan.”

“No, it warn’t; but it’s  plan _now_.”

So, drat him, we went along, but I didn’t like it much.  When we got in
we couldn’t hardly see anything, it was so dark; but Jim was re, sure
enough,  could see us;  he sings out:

“Why, _Huck_!  En good _lan_’! ain’ dat Misto Tom?”

I just knowed how it would be; I just expected it.  I didn’t know
nothing to do;  if I had I couldn’t a done it, because that nigger
busted in  says:

“Why, de gracious sakes! do he know you genlmen?”

We could see pretty well now.  Tom he looked at  nigger, steady 
kind of wondering,  says:

“Does _who_ know us?”

“Why, dis-yer runaway nigger.”

“I don’t reckon he does; but what put that into your head?”

“What _put_ it dar?  Didn’ he jis’ dis minute sing out like he knowed
you?”

Tom says, in a puzzled-up kind of way:

“Well, that’s mighty curious.  _Who_ sung out? _when_ did he sing out?
 _what_ did he sing out?”  turns to me, perfectly ca’m,  says,
“Did _you_ hear anybody sing out?”

Of course re warn’t nothing to be said but  one thing; so I says:

“No; I ain’t heard nobody say nothing.”

n he turns to Jim,  looks him over like he never see him before,
 says:

“Did you sing out?”

“No, sah,” says Jim; “I hain’t said nothing, sah.”

“Not a word?”

“No, sah, I hain’t said a word.”

“Did you ever see us before?”

“No, sah; not as I knows on.”

So Tom turns to  nigger, which was looking wild  distressed, 
says, kind of severe:

“What do you reckon’s  matter with you, anyway?  What made you think
somebody sung out?”

“Oh, it’s de dad-blame’ witches, sah, en I wisht I was dead, I do.
 Dey’s awluz at it, sah, en dey do mos’ kill me, dey sk’yers me so.
 Please to don’t tell nobody ‘bout it sah, er ole Mars Silas he’ll scole
