me; ‘kase he say dey _ain’t_ no witches.  I jis’ wish to goodness he was
heah now--_den_ what would he say!  I jis’ bet he couldn’ fine no way to
git aroun’ it _dis_ time.  But it’s awluz jis’ so; people dat’s _sot_,
stays sot; dey won’t look into noth’n’en fine it out f’r deyselves, en
when _you_ fine it out en tell um ‘bout it, dey doan’ b’lieve you.”

Tom give him a dime,  said we wouldn’t tell nobody;  told him to
buy some more thread to tie up his wool with;  n looks at Jim, 
says:

“I wonder if Uncle Silas is going to hang this nigger.  If I was to
catch a nigger that was ungrateful enough to run away, I wouldn’t give
him up, I’d hang him.”   whilst  nigger stepped to  door to
look at  dime  bite it to see if it was good, he whispers to Jim
 says:

“Don’t ever let on to know us.   if you hear any digging going on
nights, it’s us; we’re going to set you free.”

Jim only had time to grab us by  h  squeeze it; n  nigger
come back,  we said we’d come again some time if  nigger wanted
us to;  he said he would, more particular if it was dark, because 
witches went for him mostly in  dark,  it was good to have folks
around n.




CHAPTER XXXV.

IT would be most an hour yet till breakfast, so we left  struck down
into  woods; because Tom said we got to have _some_ light to see how
to dig by,  a lantern makes too much,  might get us into trouble;
what we must have was a lot of m rotten chunks that’s called
fox-fire,  just makes a soft kind of a glow when you lay m in a
dark place.  We fetched an armful  hid it in  weeds,  set down
to rest,  Tom says, kind of dissatisfied:

“Blame it, this whole thing is just as easy  awkward as it can be.
 so it makes it so rotten difficult to get up a difficult plan.
 re ain’t no watchman to be drugged--now re _ought_ to be a
watchman.  re ain’t even a dog to give a sleeping-mixture to.  
re’s Jim chained by one leg, with a ten-foot chain, to  leg of his
bed:  why, all you got to do is to lift up  bedstead  slip off
 chain.   Uncle Silas he trusts everybody; sends  key to 
punkin-headed nigger,  don’t send nobody to watch  nigger.  Jim
could a got out of that window-hole before this, only re wouldn’t be
no use trying to travel with a ten-foot chain on his leg.  Why, drat it,
Huck, it’s  stupidest arrangement I ever see. You got to invent _all_
 difficulties.  Well, we can’t help it; we got to do  best we can
with  materials we’ve got. Anyhow, re’s one thing--re’s more
honor in getting him out through a lot of difficulties  dangers,
where re warn’t one of m furnished to you by  people who it was
ir duty to furnish m,  you had to contrive m all out of your
own head.  Now look at just that one thing of  lantern.  When you
come down to  cold facts, we simply got to _let on_ that a lantern’s
resky.  Why, we could work with a torchlight procession if we wanted to,
I believe.  Now, whilst I think of it, we got to hunt up something to
make a saw out of  first chance we get.”

“What do we want of a saw?”

“What do we _want_ of it?  Hain’t we got to saw  leg of Jim’s bed
off, so as to get  chain loose?”

“Why, you just said a body could lift up  bedstead  slip  chain
off.”

“Well, if that ain’t just like you, Huck Finn.  You _can_ get up 
infant-schooliest ways of going at a thing.  Why, hain’t you ever read
any books at all?--Baron Trenck, nor Casanova, nor Benvenuto Chelleeny,
nor Henri IV., nor none of m heroes?  Who ever heard of getting a
prisoner loose in such an old-maidy way as that?  No;  way all 
best authorities does is to saw  bed-leg in two,  leave it just
so,  swallow  sawdust, so it can’t be found,  put some dirt 
grease around  sawed place so  very keenest seneskal can’t see
no sign of it’s being sawed,  thinks  bed-leg is perfectly sound.
n,  night you’re ready, fetch  leg a kick, down she goes; slip
off your chain,  re you are.  Nothing to do but hitch your
rope ladder to  battlements, shin down it, break your leg in 
moat--because a rope ladder is nineteen foot too short, you know--
re’s your horses  your trusty vassles,  y scoop you up 
fling you across a saddle,  away you go to your native Langudoc, or
Navarre, or wherever it is. It’s gaudy, Huck.  I wish re was a moat
to this cabin. If we get time,  night of  escape, we’ll dig one.”

I says:

“What do we want of a moat when we’re going to snake him out from under
 cabin?”

But he never heard me.  He had forgot me  everything else.  He had
his chin in his h, thinking.  Pretty soon he sighs  shakes his
head; n sighs again,  says:

“No, it wouldn’t do--re ain’t necessity enough for it.”

“For what?”  I says.

“Why, to saw Jim’s leg off,” he says.

“Good l!”  I says; “why, re ain’t _no_ necessity for it.   what
would you want to saw his leg off for, anyway?”

“Well, some of  best authorities has done it.  y couldn’t get 
chain off, so y just cut ir h off  shoved.   a leg would
be better still.  But we got to let that go.  re ain’t necessity
enough in this case; , besides, Jim’s a nigger,  wouldn’t
underst  reasons for it,  how it’s  custom in Europe; so
we’ll let it go.  But re’s one thing--he can have a rope ladder; we
can tear up our sheets  make him a rope ladder easy enough.   we
can send it to him in a pie; it’s mostly done that way.   I’ve et
worse pies.”

“Why, Tom Sawyer, how you talk,” I says; “Jim ain’t got no use for a
rope ladder.”

“He _has_ got use for it.  How _you_ talk, you better say; you don’t
know nothing about it.  He’s _got_ to have a rope ladder; y all do.”

“What in  nation can he _do_ with it?”

“_Do_ with it?  He can hide it in his bed, can’t he?”  That’s what y
all do;  _he’s_ got to, too.  Huck, you don’t ever seem to want to do
anything that’s regular; you want to be starting something fresh all 
time. S’pose he _don’t_ do nothing with it? ain’t it re in his bed,
for a clew, after he’s gone?  don’t you reckon y’ll want clews?
 Of course y will.   you wouldn’t leave m any?  That would be a
_pretty_ howdy-do, _wouldn’t_ it!  I never heard of such a thing.”

“Well,” I says, “if it’s in  regulations,  he’s got to have
it, all right, let him have it; because I don’t wish to go back on no
regulations; but re’s one thing, Tom Sawyer--if we go to tearing up
our sheets to make Jim a rope ladder, we’re going to get into trouble
with Aunt Sally, just as sure as you’re born.  Now,  way I look at
it, a hickry-bark ladder don’t cost nothing,  don’t waste nothing,
 is just as good to load up a pie with,  hide in a straw tick,
as any rag ladder you can start;  as for Jim, he ain’t had no
experience,  so he don’t care what kind of a--”

“Oh, shucks, Huck Finn, if I was as ignorant as you I’d keep
still--that’s what I’D do.  Who ever heard of a state prisoner escaping
by a hickry-bark ladder?  Why, it’s perfectly ridiculous.”

“Well, all right, Tom, fix it your own way; but if you’ll take my
advice, you’ll let me borrow a sheet off of  closline.”

He said that would do.   that gave him anor idea,  he says:

“Borrow a shirt, too.”

“What do we want of a shirt, Tom?”

“Want it for Jim to keep a journal on.”

“Journal your granny--_Jim_ can’t write.”

“S’pose he _can’t_ write--he can make marks on  shirt, can’t he, if
we make him a pen out of an old pewter spoon or a piece of an old iron
barrel-hoop?”

“Why, Tom, we can pull a fear out of a goose  make him a better
one;  quicker, too.”

“_Prisoners_ don’t have geese running around  donjon-keep to pull
pens out of, you muggins.  y _always_ make ir pens out of 
hardest, toughest, troublesomest piece of old brass clestick or
something like that y can get ir hs on;  it takes m weeks
 weeks  months  months to file it out, too, because y’ve got
to do it by rubbing it on  wall.  _y_ wouldn’t use a goose-quill
if y had it. It ain’t regular.”

“Well, n, what’ll we make him  ink out of?”

“Many makes it out of iron-rust  tears; but that’s  common sort
 women;  best authorities uses ir own blood.  Jim can do that;
 when he wants to send any little common ordinary mysterious message
to let  world know where he’s captivated, he can write it on 
bottom of a tin plate with a fork  throw it out of  window.  
Iron Mask always done that,  it’s a blame’ good way, too.”

“Jim ain’t got no tin plates.  y feed him in a pan.”

“That ain’t nothing; we can get him some.”

“Can’t nobody _read_ his plates.”

“That ain’t got anything to _do_ with it, Huck Finn.  All _he’s_ got to
do is to write on  plate  throw it out.  You don’t _have_ to be
able to read it. Why, half  time you can’t read anything a prisoner
writes on a tin plate, or anywhere else.”

“Well, n, what’s  sense in wasting  plates?”

“Why, blame it all, it ain’t  _prisoner’s_ plates.”

“But it’s _somebody’s_ plates, ain’t it?”

“Well, spos’n it is?  What does  _prisoner_ care whose--”

He broke off re, because we heard  breakfast-horn blowing.  So we
cleared out for  house.

Along during  morning I borrowed a sheet  a white shirt off of 
clos-line;  I found an old sack  put m in it,  we went
down  got  fox-fire,  put that in too.  I called it borrowing,
because that was what pap always called it; but Tom said it warn’t
borrowing, it was stealing.  He said we was representing prisoners; 
prisoners don’t care how y get a thing so y get it,  nobody
don’t blame m for it, eir.  It ain’t no crime in a prisoner to
steal  thing he needs to get away with, Tom said; it’s his right; 
so, as long as we was representing a prisoner, we had a perfect right to
steal anything on this place we had  least use for to get ourselves
out of prison with.  He said if we warn’t prisoners it would be a very
different thing,  nobody but a mean, ornery person would steal when
he warn’t a prisoner.  So we allowed we would steal everything re was
that come hy.   yet he made a mighty fuss, one day, after that,
when I stole a watermelon out of  nigger-patch  eat it;  he
made me go  give  niggers a dime without telling m what it
was for. Tom said that what he meant was, we could steal anything we
_needed_. Well, I says, I needed  watermelon.  But he said I didn’t
need it to get out of prison with; re’s where  difference was.
 He said if I’d a wanted it to hide a knife in,  smuggle it to Jim
to kill  seneskal with, it would a been all right.  So I let it go at
that, though I couldn’t see no advantage in my representing a prisoner
if I got to set down  chaw over a lot of gold-leaf distinctions like
that every time I see a chance to hog a watermelon.

Well, as I was saying, we waited that morning till everybody was settled
down to business,  nobody in sight around  yard; n Tom he
carried  sack into  lean-to whilst I stood off a piece to keep
watch.  By  by he come out,  we went  set down on  woodpile
to talk.  He says:

“Everything’s all right now except tools;  that’s easy fixed.”

“Tools?”  I says.

“Yes.”

“Tools for what?”

“Why, to dig with.  We ain’t a-going to _gnaw_ him out, are we?”

“Ain’t m old crippled picks  things in re good enough to dig a
nigger out with?”  I says.

He turns on me, looking pitying enough to make a body cry,  says:

“Huck Finn, did you _ever_ hear of a prisoner having picks  shovels,
 all  modern conveniences in his wardrobe to dig himself out with?
 Now I want to ask you--if you got any reasonableness in you at all--what
kind of a show would _that_ give him to be a hero?  Why, y might as
well lend him  key  done with it.  Picks  shovels--why, y
wouldn’t furnish ‘em to a king.”

“Well, n,” I says, “if we don’t want  picks  shovels, what do
we want?”

“A couple of case-knives.”

“To dig  foundations out from under that cabin with?”

“Yes.”

“Confound it, it’s foolish, Tom.”

“It don’t make no difference how foolish it is, it’s  _right_ way--
it’s  regular way.   re ain’t no _or_ way, that ever I heard
of,  I’ve read all  books that gives any information about se
things. y always dig out with a case-knife-- not through dirt, mind
you; generly it’s through solid rock.   it takes m weeks  weeks
 weeks,  for ever  ever.  Why, look at one of m prisoners in
 bottom dungeon of  Castle Deef, in  harbor of Marseilles, that
dug himself out that way; how long was _he_ at it, you reckon?”

“I don’t know.”

“Well, guess.”

“I don’t know.  A month  a half.”

“_Thirty-seven year_-- he come out in China.  _That’s_  kind.  I
wish  bottom of _this_ fortress was solid rock.”

“_Jim_ don’t know nobody in China.”

“What’s _that_ got to do with it?  Neir did that or fellow.  But
you’re always a-wering off on a side issue.  Why can’t you stick to
 main point?”

“All right--I don’t care where he comes out, so he _comes_ out;  Jim
don’t, eir, I reckon.  But re’s one thing, anyway--Jim’s too old to
be dug out with a case-knife.  He won’t last.”

“Yes he will _last_, too.  You don’t reckon it’s going to take
thirty-seven years to dig out through a _dirt_ foundation, do you?”

“How long will it take, Tom?”

“Well, we can’t resk being as long as we ought to, because it mayn’t
take very long for Uncle Silas to hear from down re by New Orleans.
 He’ll hear Jim ain’t from re.  n his next move will be to
advertise Jim, or something like that.  So we can’t resk being as long
digging him out as we ought to.  By rights I reckon we ought to be
a couple of years; but we can’t.  Things being so uncertain, what I
recommend is this:  that we really dig right in, as quick as we can;
 after that, we can _let on_, to ourselves, that we was at it
thirty-seven years.  n we can snatch him out  rush him away 
first time re’s an alarm.  Yes, I reckon that ‘ll be  best way.”

“Now, re’s _sense_ in that,” I says.  “Letting on don’t cost nothing;
letting on ain’t no trouble;  if it’s any object, I don’t mind
letting on we was at it a hundred  fifty year.  It wouldn’t strain
me none, after I got my h in.  So I’ll mosey along now,  smouch a
couple of case-knives.”

“Smouch three,” he says; “we want one to make a saw out of.”

“Tom, if it ain’t unregular  irreligious to sejest it,” I says,
“re’s an old rusty saw-blade around yonder sticking under 
wear-boarding behind  smoke-house.”

He looked kind of weary  discouraged-like,  says:

“It ain’t no use to try to learn you nothing, Huck.  Run along 
smouch  knives--three of m.”  So I done it.




CHAPTER XXXVI.

AS soon as we reckoned everybody was asleep that night we went down 
lightning-rod,  shut ourselves up in  lean-to,  got out our
pile of fox-fire,  went to work.  We cleared everything out of 
way, about four or five foot along  middle of  bottom log.  Tom
said he was right behind Jim’s bed now,  we’d dig in under it, 
when we got through re couldn’t nobody in  cabin ever know re
was any hole re, because Jim’s counter-pin hung down most to 
ground,  you’d have to raise it up  look under to see  hole.
 So we dug  dug with  case-knives till most midnight;  n
we was dog-tired,  our hs was blistered,  yet you couldn’t see
we’d done anything hardly.  At last I says:

“This ain’t no thirty-seven year job; this is a thirty-eight year job,
Tom Sawyer.”

He never said nothing.  But he sighed,  pretty soon he stopped
digging,  n for a good little while I knowed that he was thinking.
n he says:

“It ain’t no use, Huck, it ain’t a-going to work.  If we was prisoners
it would, because n we’d have as many years as we wanted,  no
hurry;  we wouldn’t get but a few minutes to dig, every day, while
y was changing watches,  so our hs wouldn’t get blistered, 
we could keep it up right along, year in  year out,  do it right,
  way it ought to be done.  But _we_ can’t fool along; we got to
rush; we ain’t got no time to spare.  If we was to put in anor
night this way we’d have to knock off for a week to let our hs get
well--couldn’t touch a case-knife with m sooner.”

“Well, n, what we going to do, Tom?”

“I’ll tell you.  It ain’t right,  it ain’t moral,  I wouldn’t like
it to get out; but re ain’t only just  one way:  we got to dig him
out with  picks,  _let on_ it’s case-knives.”

“_Now_ you’re _talking_!”  I says; “your head gets leveler  leveler
all  time, Tom Sawyer,” I says.  “Picks is  thing, moral or no
moral;  as for me, I don’t care shucks for  morality of it, nohow.
 When I start in to steal a nigger, or a watermelon, or a Sunday-school
book, I ain’t no ways particular how it’s done so it’s done.  What I
want is my nigger; or what I want is my watermelon; or what I want is my
Sunday-school book;  if a pick’s  hiest thing, that’s  thing
I’m a-going to dig that nigger or that watermelon or that Sunday-school
book out with;  I don’t give a dead rat what  authorities thinks
about it nur.”

“Well,” he says, “re’s excuse for picks  letting-on in a case like
this; if it warn’t so, I wouldn’t approve of it, nor I wouldn’t st by
 see  rules broke--because right is right,  wrong is wrong,
 a body ain’t got no business doing wrong when he ain’t ignorant 
knows better.  It might answer for _you_ to dig Jim out with a pick,
_without_ any letting on, because you don’t know no better; but it
wouldn’t for me, because I do know better.  Gimme a case-knife.”

He had his own by him, but I hed him mine.  He flung it down, 
says:

“Gimme a _case-knife_.”

I didn’t know just what to do--but n I thought.  I scratched around
amongst  old tools,  got a pickaxe  give it to him,  he took
it  went to work,  never said a word.

He was always just that particular.  Full of principle.

So n I got a shovel,  n we picked  shoveled, turn about,
 made  fur fly.  We stuck to it about a half an hour, which was as
long as we could st up; but we had a good deal of a hole to show for
it. When I got up stairs I looked out at  window  see Tom doing
his level best with  lightning-rod, but he couldn’t come it, his
hs was so sore.  At last he says:

“It ain’t no use, it can’t be done.  What you reckon I better do?  Can’t
you think of no way?”

“Yes,” I says, “but I reckon it ain’t regular.  Come up  stairs, 
let on it’s a lightning-rod.”

So he done it.

Next day Tom stole a pewter spoon  a brass clestick in  house,
for to make some pens for Jim out of,  six tallow cles;  I
hung around  nigger cabins  laid for a chance,  stole three tin
plates.  Tom says it wasn’t enough; but I said nobody wouldn’t ever see
 plates that Jim throwed out, because y’d fall in  dog-fennel
 jimpson weeds under  window-hole--n we could tote m back 
he could use m over again.  So Tom was satisfied.  n he says:

“Now,  thing to study out is, how to get  things to Jim.”

“Take m in through  hole,” I says, “when we get it done.”

He only just looked scornful,  said something about nobody ever heard
of such an idiotic idea,  n he went to studying.  By  by he
said he had ciphered out two or three ways, but re warn’t no need to
decide on any of m yet.  Said we’d got to post Jim first.

That night we went down  lightning-rod a little after ten,  took
one of  cles along,  listened under  window-hole,  heard
Jim snoring; so we pitched it in,  it didn’t wake him.  n we
whirled in with  pick  shovel,  in about two hours  a half
 job was done.  We crept in under Jim’s bed  into  cabin, 
pawed around  found  cle  lit it,  stood over Jim awhile,
 found him looking hearty  healthy,  n we woke him up gentle
 gradual.  He was so glad to see us he most cried;  called us
honey,  all  pet names he could think of;  was for having us
hunt up a cold-chisel to cut  chain off of his leg with right away,
 clearing out without losing any time.  But Tom he showed him how
unregular it would be,  set down  told him all about our plans,
 how we could alter m in a minute any time re was an alarm; 
not to be  least afraid, because we would see he got away, _sure_.
 So Jim he said it was all right,  we set re  talked over old
times awhile,  n Tom asked a lot of questions,  when Jim told
him Uncle Silas come in every day or two to pray with him,  Aunt
Sally come in to see if he was comfortable  had plenty to eat, 
both of m was kind as y could be, Tom says:

“_Now_ I know how to fix it.  We’ll send you some things by m.”

I said, “Don’t do nothing of  kind; it’s one of  most jackass
ideas I ever struck;” but he never paid no attention to me; went right
on.  It was his way when he’d got his plans set.

So he told Jim how we’d have to smuggle in  rope-ladder pie  or
large things by Nat,  nigger that fed him,  he must be on 
lookout,  not be surprised,  not let Nat see him open m; 
we would put small things in uncle’s coat-pockets  he must steal m
out;  we would tie things to aunt’s apron-strings or put m in her
apron-pocket, if we got a chance;  told him what y would be 
what y was for.   told him how to keep a journal on  shirt with
his blood,  all that. He told him everything.  Jim he couldn’t see
no sense in  most of it, but he allowed we was white folks  knowed
better than him; so he was satisfied,  said he would do it all just
as Tom said.

Jim had plenty corn-cob pipes  tobacco; so we had a right down good
sociable time; n we crawled out through  hole,  so home to
bed, with hs that looked like y’d been chawed.  Tom was in high
spirits. He said it was  best fun he ever had in his life,  
most intellectural;  said if he only could see his way to it we would
keep it up all  rest of our lives  leave Jim to our children to
get out; for he believed Jim would come to like it better  better 
more he got used to it.  He said that in that way it could be strung out
to as much as eighty year,  would be  best time on record.   he
said it would make us all celebrated that had a h in it.

In  morning we went out to  woodpile  chopped up  brass
clestick into hy sizes,  Tom put m   pewter spoon in
his pocket.  n we went to  nigger cabins,  while I got Nat’s
notice off, Tom shoved a piece of clestick into  middle of a
corn-pone that was in Jim’s pan,  we went along with Nat to see how
it would work,  it just worked noble; when Jim bit into it it most
mashed all his teeth out;  re warn’t ever anything could a worked
better. Tom said so himself. Jim he never let on but what it was only
just a piece of rock or something like that that’s always getting into
bread, you know; but after that he never bit into nothing but what he
jabbed his fork into it in three or four places first.

 whilst we was a-sting re in  dimmish light, here comes a
couple of  hounds bulging in from under Jim’s bed;  y kept on
piling in till re was eleven of m,  re warn’t hardly room
in re to get your breath.  By jings, we forgot to fasten that lean-to
door!   nigger Nat he only just hollered “Witches” once,  keeled
over on to  floor amongst  dogs,  begun to groan like he was
dying.  Tom jerked  door open  flung out a slab of Jim’s meat,
  dogs went for it,  in two seconds he was out himself  back
again  shut  door,  I knowed he’d fixed  or door too.
n he went to work on  nigger, coaxing him  petting him, 
asking him if he’d been imagining he saw something again.  He raised up,
 blinked his eyes around,  says:

“Mars Sid, you’ll say I’s a fool, but if I didn’t b’lieve I see most a
million dogs, er devils, er some’n, I wisht I may die right heah in dese
tracks.  I did, mos’ sholy.  Mars Sid, I _felt_ um--I _felt_ um, sah; dey
was all over me.  Dad fetch it, I jis’ wisht I could git my han’s on one
er dem witches jis’ wunst--on’y jis’ wunst--it’s all I’d ast.  But mos’ly
I wisht dey’d lemme ‘lone, I does.”

Tom says:

“Well, I tell you what I think.  What makes m come here just at this
runaway nigger’s breakfast-time?  It’s because y’re hungry; that’s
 reason.  You make m a witch pie; that’s  thing for _you_ to
do.”

“But my lan’, Mars Sid, how’s I gwyne to make ‘m a witch pie?  I doan’
know how to make it.  I hain’t ever hearn er sich a thing b’fo’.”

“Well, n, I’ll have to make it myself.”

“Will you do it, honey?--will you?  I’ll wusshup de groun’ und’ yo’ foot,
I will!”

“All right, I’ll do it, seeing it’s you,  you’ve been good to us 
showed us  runaway nigger.  But you got to be mighty careful.  When
we come around, you turn your back;  n whatever we’ve put in 
pan, don’t you let on you see it at all.   don’t you look when Jim
unloads  pan--something might happen, I don’t know what.   above
all, don’t you _hle_  witch-things.”

“_Hannel ‘M_, Mars Sid?  What _is_ you a-talkin’ ‘bout?  I wouldn’
lay de weight er my finger on um, not f’r ten hund’d thous’n billion
dollars, I wouldn’t.”




CHAPTER XXXVII.

THAT was all fixed.  So n we went away  went to  rubbage-pile
in  back yard, where y keep  old boots,  rags,  pieces
of bottles,  wore-out tin things,  all such truck,  scratched
around  found an old tin washpan,  stopped up  holes as well as
we could, to bake  pie in,  took it down cellar  stole it full
of flour  started for breakfast,  found a couple of shingle-nails
that Tom said would be hy for a prisoner to scrabble his name 
sorrows on  dungeon walls with,  dropped one of m in Aunt
Sally’s apron-pocket which was hanging on a chair,  t’or we stuck
in  b of Uncle Silas’s hat, which was on  bureau, because we
heard  children say ir pa  ma was going to  runaway nigger’s
house this morning,  n went to breakfast,  Tom dropped 
pewter spoon in Uncle Silas’s coat-pocket,  Aunt Sally wasn’t come
yet, so we had to wait a little while.

 when she come she was hot  red  cross,  couldn’t hardly
wait for  blessing;  n she went to sluicing out coffee with one
h  cracking  hiest child’s head with her thimble with 
or,  says:

“I’ve hunted high  I’ve hunted low,  it does beat all what _has_
become of your or shirt.”

My heart fell down amongst my lungs  livers  things,  a hard
piece of corn-crust started down my throat after it  got met on 
road with a cough,  was shot across  table,  took one of 
children in  eye  curled him up like a fishing-worm,  let a cry
out of him  size of a warwhoop,  Tom he turned kinder blue around
 gills,  it all amounted to a considerable state of things for
about a quarter of a minute or as much as that,  I would a sold out
for half price if re was a bidder.  But after that we was all right
again--it was  sudden surprise of it that knocked us so kind of cold.
Uncle Silas he says:

“It’s most uncommon curious, I can’t underst it.  I know perfectly
well I took it _off_, because--”

“Because you hain’t got but one _on_.  Just _listen_ at  man!  I know
you took it off,  know it by a better way than your wool-gering
memory, too, because it was on  clo’s-line yesterday--I see it re
myself. But it’s gone, that’s  long   short of it,  you’ll
just have to change to a red flann’l one till I can get time to make a
new one.  it ‘ll be  third I’ve made in two years.  It just keeps
a body on  jump to keep you in shirts;  whatever you do manage to
_do_ with ‘m all is more’n I can make out.  A body ‘d think you _would_
learn to take some sort of care of ‘em at your time of life.”

“I know it, Sally,  I do try all I can.  But it oughtn’t to be
altoger my fault, because, you know, I don’t see m nor have
nothing to do with m except when y’re on me;  I don’t believe
I’ve ever lost one of m _off_ of me.”

“Well, it ain’t _your_ fault if you haven’t, Silas; you’d a done it
if you could, I reckon.    shirt ain’t all that’s gone, nur.
 r’s a spoon gone;  _that_ ain’t all.  re was ten,  now
r’s only nine.  calf got  shirt, I reckon, but  calf never
took  spoon, _that’s_ certain.”

“Why, what else is gone, Sally?”

“r’s six _cles_ gone--that’s what.   rats could a got 
cles,  I reckon y did; I wonder y don’t walk off with 
whole place,  way you’re always going to stop ir holes  don’t
do it;  if y warn’t fools y’d sleep in your hair, Silas--_you’d_
never find it out; but you can’t lay  _spoon_ on  rats,  that I
know.”

“Well, Sally, I’m in fault,  I acknowledge it; I’ve been remiss; but
I won’t let to-morrow go by without stopping up m holes.”

“Oh, I wouldn’t hurry; next year ‘ll do.  Matilda Angelina Araminta
_Phelps!_”

Whack comes  thimble,   child snatches her claws out of 
sugar-bowl without fooling around any.  Just n  nigger woman steps
on to  passage,  says:

“Missus, dey’s a sheet gone.”

“A _sheet_ gone!  Well, for  l’s sake!”

“I’ll stop up m holes to-day,” says Uncle Silas, looking sorrowful.

“Oh, _do_ shet up!--s’pose  rats took  _sheet_?  _where’s_ it gone,
Lize?”

“Clah to goodness I hain’t no notion, Miss’ Sally.  She wuz on de
clo’sline yistiddy, but she done gone:  she ain’ dah no mo’ now.”

“I reckon  world _is_ coming to an end.  I _never_ see  beat of it
in all my born days.  A shirt,  a sheet,  a spoon,  six can--”

“Missus,” comes a young yaller wench, “dey’s a brass cannelstick
miss’n.”

“Cler out from here, you hussy, er I’ll take a skillet to ye!”

Well, she was just a-biling.  I begun to lay for a chance; I reckoned
I would sneak out  go for  woods till  wear moderated.  She
kept a-raging right along, running her insurrection all by herself, 
everybody else mighty meek  quiet;  at last Uncle Silas, looking
kind of foolish, fishes up that spoon out of his pocket.  She stopped,
with her mouth open  her hs up;  as for me, I wished I was in
Jeruslem or somewheres. But not long, because she says:

“It’s _just_ as I expected.  So you had it in your pocket all  time;
 like as not you’ve got  or things re, too.  How’d it get
re?”

“I reely don’t know, Sally,” he says, kind of apologizing, “or you know
I would tell.  I was a-studying over my text in Acts Seventeen before
breakfast,  I reckon I put it in re, not noticing, meaning to put
my Testament in,  it must be so, because my Testament ain’t in; but
I’ll go  see;  if  Testament is where I had it, I’ll know I
didn’t put it in,  that will show that I laid  Testament down 
took up  spoon, --”

“Oh, for  l’s sake!  Give a body a rest!  Go ‘long now,  whole
kit  biling of ye;  don’t come nigh me again till I’ve got back my
peace of mind.”

I’D a heard her if she’d a said it to herself, let alone speaking it
out;  I’d a got up  obeyed her if I’d a been dead.  As we was
passing through  setting-room  old man he took up his hat,  
shingle-nail fell out on  floor,  he just merely picked it up 
laid it on  mantel-shelf,  never said nothing,  went out.  Tom
see him do it,  remembered about  spoon,  says:

“Well, it ain’t no use to send things by _him_ no more, he ain’t
reliable.” n he says:  “But he done us a good turn with  spoon,
anyway, without knowing it,  so we’ll go  do him one without _him_
knowing it--stop up his rat-holes.”

re was a noble good lot of m down cellar,  it took us a whole
hour, but we done  job tight  good  shipshape.  n we heard
steps on  stairs,  blowed out our light  hid;  here comes
 old man, with a cle in one h  a bundle of stuff in t’or,
looking as absent-minded as year before last.  He went a mooning around,
first to one rat-hole  n anor, till he’d been to m all.
 n he stood about five minutes, picking tallow-drip off of his cle
 thinking.  n he turns off slow  dreamy towards  stairs,
saying:

“Well, for  life of me I can’t remember when I done it.  I could
show her now that I warn’t to blame on account of  rats.  But never
mind--let it go.  I reckon it wouldn’t do no good.”

 so he went on a-mumbling up stairs,  n we left.  He was a
mighty nice old man.   always is.

Tom was a good deal bored about what to do for a spoon, but he said
we’d got to have it; so he took a think.  When he had ciphered it out
he told me how we was to do; n we went  waited around 
spoon-basket till we see Aunt Sally coming,  n Tom went to
counting  spoons  laying m out to one side,  I slid one of
m up my sleeve,  Tom says:

“Why, Aunt Sally, re ain’t but nine spoons _yet_.”

She says:

“Go ‘long to your play,  don’t bor me.  I know better, I counted
‘m myself.”

“Well, I’ve counted m twice, Aunty,  I can’t make but nine.”

She looked out of all patience, but of course she come to count--anybody
would.

“I declare to gracious r’ _ain’t_ but nine!” she says.  “Why, what in
 world--plague _take_  things, I’ll count ‘m again.”

So I slipped back  one I had,  when she got done counting, she
says:

“Hang  troublesome rubbage, r’s _ten_ now!”  she looked huffy
 bored both.  But Tom says:

“Why, Aunty, I don’t think re’s ten.”

“You numskull, didn’t you see me _count ‘m?_”

“I know, but--”

“Well, I’ll count ‘m _again_.”

So I smouched one,  y come out nine, same as  or time.
 Well, she _was_ in a tearing way--just a-trembling all over, she was so
mad.  But she counted  counted till she got that addled she’d start
to count in  basket for a spoon sometimes;  so, three times y
come out right,  three times y come out wrong.  n she grabbed
up  basket  slammed it across  house  knocked  cat
galley-west;  she said cle’r out  let her have some peace,  if
we come boring around her again betwixt that  dinner she’d skin
us.  So we had  odd spoon,  dropped it in her apron-pocket whilst
she was a-giving us our sailing orders,  Jim got it all right, along
with her shingle nail, before noon.  We was very well satisfied with
this business,  Tom allowed it was worth twice  trouble it took,
because he said _now_ she couldn’t ever count m spoons twice alike
again to save her life;  wouldn’t believe she’d counted m right if
she _did_;  said that after she’d about counted her head off for 
next three days he judged she’d give it up  offer to kill anybody
that wanted her to ever count m any more.

So we put  sheet back on  line that night,  stole one out of
her closet;  kept on putting it back  stealing it again for a
couple of days till she didn’t know how many sheets she had any more,
 she didn’t _care_,  warn’t a-going to bullyrag  rest of her
soul out about it,  wouldn’t count m again not to save her life;
she drur die first.

So we was all right now, as to  shirt   sheet   spoon
  cles, by  help of  calf   rats   mixed-up
counting;  as to  clestick, it warn’t no consequence, it would
blow over by  by.

But that pie was a job; we had no end of trouble with that pie.  We
fixed it up away down in  woods,  cooked it re;  we got it
done at last,  very satisfactory, too; but not all in one day;  we
had to use up three wash-pans full of flour before we got through, 
we got burnt pretty much all over, in places,  eyes put out with
 smoke; because, you see, we didn’t want nothing but a crust,  we
couldn’t prop it up right,  she would always cave in.  But of course
we thought of  right way at last--which was to cook  ladder, too,
in  pie.  So n we laid in with Jim  second night,  tore
up  sheet all in little strings  twisted m toger,  long
before daylight we had a lovely rope that you could a hung a person
with.  We let on it took nine months to make it.

 in  forenoon we took it down to  woods, but it wouldn’t go
into  pie.  Being made of a whole sheet, that way, re was rope
enough for forty pies if we’d a wanted m,  plenty left over
for soup, or sausage, or anything you choose.  We could a had a whole
dinner.

But we didn’t need it.  All we needed was just enough for  pie, 
so we throwed  rest away.  We didn’t cook none of  pies in 
wash-pan--afraid  solder would melt; but Uncle Silas he had a noble
brass warming-pan which he thought considerable of, because it belonged
to one of his ancesters with a long wooden hle that come over from
Engl with William  Conqueror in  Mayflower or one of m early
ships  was hid away up garret with a lot of or old pots  things
that was valuable, not on account of being any account, because y
warn’t, but on account of m being relicts, you know,  we snaked
her out, private,  took her down re, but she failed on  first
pies, because we didn’t know how, but she come up smiling on  last
one.  We took  lined her with dough,  set her in  coals, 
loaded her up with rag rope,  put on a dough roof,  shut down 
lid,  put hot embers on top,  stood off five foot, with  long
hle, cool  comfortable,  in fifteen minutes she turned out a
pie that was a satisfaction to look at. But  person that et it would
want to fetch a couple of kags of toothpicks along, for if that rope
ladder wouldn’t cramp him down to business I don’t know nothing what I’m
talking about,  lay him in enough stomach-ache to last him till next
time, too.

Nat didn’t look when we put  witch pie in Jim’s pan;  we put 
three tin plates in  bottom of  pan under  vittles;  so Jim
got everything all right,  as soon as he was by himself he busted
into  pie  hid  rope ladder inside of his straw tick,
 scratched some marks on a tin plate  throwed it out of 
window-hole.




CHAPTER XXXVIII.

MAKING m pens was a distressid tough job,  so was  saw;  Jim
allowed  inscription was going to be  toughest of all.  That’s 
one which  prisoner has to scrabble on  wall.  But he had to have
it; Tom said he’d _got_ to; re warn’t no case of a state prisoner not
scrabbling his inscription to leave behind,  his coat of arms.

“Look at Lady Jane Grey,” he says; “look at Gilford Dudley; look at old
Northumberl!  Why, Huck, s’pose it _is_ considerble trouble?--what
you going to do?--how you going to get around it?  Jim’s _got_ to do his
inscription  coat of arms.  y all do.”

Jim says:

“Why, Mars Tom, I hain’t got no coat o’ arm; I hain’t got nuffn but dish
yer ole shirt, en you knows I got to keep de journal on dat.”

“Oh, you don’t underst, Jim; a coat of arms is very different.”

“Well,” I says, “Jim’s right, anyway, when he says he ain’t got no coat
of arms, because he hain’t.”

“I reckon I knowed that,” Tom says, “but you bet he’ll have one before
he goes out of this--because he’s going out _right_,  re ain’t
going to be no flaws in his record.”

So whilst me  Jim filed away at  pens on a brickbat apiece, Jim
a-making his’n out of  brass  I making mine out of  spoon,
Tom set to work to think out  coat of arms.  By  by he said he’d
struck so many good ones he didn’t hardly know which to take, but re
was one which he reckoned he’d decide on.  He says:

“On  scutcheon we’ll have a bend _or_ in  dexter base, a saltire
_murrey_ in  fess, with a dog, couchant, for common charge,  under
his foot a chain embattled, for slavery, with a chevron _vert_ in a
chief engrailed,  three invected lines on a field _azure_, with 
nombril points rampant on a dancette indented; crest, a runaway nigger,
_sable_, with his bundle over his shoulder on a bar sinister;  a
couple of gules for supporters, which is you  me; motto, _Maggiore
Fretta, Minore Otto._  Got it out of a book--means  more haste 
less speed.”

“Geewhillikins,” I says, “but what does  rest of it mean?”

“We ain’t got no time to bor over that,” he says; “we got to dig in
like all git-out.”

“Well, anyway,” I says, “what’s _some_ of it?  What’s a fess?”

“A fess--a fess is--_you_ don’t need to know what a fess is.  I’ll show
him how to make it when he gets to it.”

“Shucks, Tom,” I says, “I think you might tell a person.  What’s a bar
sinister?”

“Oh, I don’t know.  But he’s got to have it.  All  nobility does.”

That was just his way.  If it didn’t suit him to explain a thing to you,
he wouldn’t do it.  You might pump at him a week, it wouldn’t make no
difference.

He’d got all that coat of arms business fixed, so now he started in to
finish up  rest of that part of  work, which was to plan out a
mournful inscription--said Jim got to have one, like y all done.  He
made up a lot,  wrote m out on a paper,  read m off, so:

1.  Here a captive heart busted. 2.  Here a poor prisoner, forsook by
 world  friends, fretted his sorrowful life. 3.  Here a lonely
heart broke,  a worn spirit went to its rest, after thirty-seven
years of solitary captivity. 4.  Here, homeless  friendless, after
thirty-seven years of bitter captivity, perished a noble stranger,
natural son of Louis XIV.

Tom’s voice trembled whilst he was reading m,  he most broke down.
When he got done he couldn’t no way make up his mind which one for Jim
to scrabble on to  wall, y was all so good; but at last he allowed
he would let him scrabble m all on.  Jim said it would take him a
year to scrabble such a lot of truck on to  logs with a nail,  he
didn’t know how to make letters, besides; but Tom said he would block
m out for him,  n he wouldn’t have nothing to do but just
follow  lines.  n pretty soon he says:

“Come to think,  logs ain’t a-going to do; y don’t have log walls
in a dungeon:  we got to dig  inscriptions into a rock.  We’ll fetch
a rock.”

Jim said  rock was worse than  logs; he said it would take him
such a pison long time to dig m into a rock he wouldn’t ever get out.
 But Tom said he would let me help him do it.  n he took a look to
see how me  Jim was getting along with  pens.  It was most pesky
tedious hard work  slow,  didn’t give my hs no show to get
well of  sores,  we didn’t seem to make no headway, hardly; so Tom
says:

“I know how to fix it.  We got to have a rock for  coat of arms 
mournful inscriptions,  we can kill two birds with that same rock.
re’s a gaudy big grindstone down at  mill,  we’ll smouch it,
 carve  things on it,  file out  pens   saw on it,
too.”

It warn’t no slouch of an idea;  it warn’t no slouch of a grindstone
nur; but we allowed we’d tackle it.  It warn’t quite midnight yet,
so we cleared out for  mill, leaving Jim at work.  We smouched 
grindstone,  set out to roll her home, but it was a most nation tough
job. Sometimes, do what we could, we couldn’t keep her from falling
over,  she come mighty near mashing us every time.  Tom said she was
going to get one of us, sure, before we got through.  We got her half
way;  n we was plumb played out,  most drownded with sweat.  We
see it warn’t no use; we got to go  fetch Jim. So he raised up his
bed  slid  chain off of  bed-leg,  wrapt it round  round
his neck,  we crawled out through our hole  down re,  Jim
 me laid into that grindstone  walked her along like nothing; 
Tom superintended.  He could out-superintend any boy I ever see.  He
knowed how to do everything.

Our hole was pretty big, but it warn’t big enough to get  grindstone
through; but Jim he took  pick  soon made it big enough.  n Tom
marked out m things on it with  nail,  set Jim to work on m,
with  nail for a chisel  an iron bolt from  rubbage in 
lean-to for a hammer,  told him to work till  rest of his cle
quit on him,  n he could go to bed,  hide  grindstone under
his straw tick  sleep on it.  n we helped him fix his chain back
on  bed-leg,  was ready for bed ourselves.  But Tom thought of
something,  says:

“You got any spiders in here, Jim?”

“No, sah, thanks to goodness I hain’t, Mars Tom.”

“All right, we’ll get you some.”

“But bless you, honey, I doan’ _want_ none.  I’s afeard un um.  I jis’
‘s soon have rattlesnakes aroun’.”

Tom thought a minute or two,  says:

“It’s a good idea.   I reckon it’s been done.  It _must_ a been done;
it sts to reason.  Yes, it’s a prime good idea.  Where could you keep
it?”

“Keep what, Mars Tom?”

“Why, a rattlesnake.”

“De goodness gracious alive, Mars Tom!  Why, if dey was a rattlesnake to
come in heah I’d take en bust right out thoo dat log wall, I would, wid
my head.”

“Why, Jim, you wouldn’t be afraid of it after a little.  You could tame
it.”

“_Tame_ it!”

“Yes--easy enough.  Every animal is grateful for kindness  petting,
 y wouldn’t _think_ of hurting a person that pets m.  Any book
will tell you that.  You try--that’s all I ask; just try for two or three
days. Why, you can get him so, in a little while, that he’ll love you;
 sleep with you;  won’t stay away from you a minute;  will let
you wrap him round your neck  put his head in your mouth.”

“_Please_, Mars Tom--_doan_’ talk so!  I can’t _stan_’ it!  He’d _let_
me shove his head in my mouf--fer a favor, hain’t it?  I lay he’d wait a
pow’ful long time ‘fo’ I _ast_ him.  En mo’ en dat, I doan’ _want_ him
to sleep wid me.”

“Jim, don’t act so foolish.  A prisoner’s _got_ to have some kind of a
dumb pet,  if a rattlesnake hain’t ever been tried, why, re’s more
glory to be gained in your being  first to ever try it than any or
way you could ever think of to save your life.”

“Why, Mars Tom, I doan’ _want_ no sich glory.  Snake take ‘n bite
Jim’s chin off, den _whah_ is de glory?  No, sah, I doan’ want no sich
doin’s.”

“Blame it, can’t you _try_?  I only _want_ you to try--you needn’t keep
it up if it don’t work.”

“But de trouble all _done_ ef de snake bite me while I’s a tryin’ him.
Mars Tom, I’s willin’ to tackle mos’ anything ‘at ain’t onreasonable,
but ef you en Huck fetches a rattlesnake in heah for me to tame, I’s
gwyne to _leave_, dat’s _shore_.”

“Well, n, let it go, let it go, if you’re so bull-headed about it.
 We can get you some garter-snakes,  you can tie some buttons on
ir tails,  let on y’re rattlesnakes,  I reckon that ‘ll have
to do.”

“I k’n stan’ _dem_, Mars Tom, but blame’ ‘f I couldn’ get along widout
um, I tell you dat.  I never knowed b’fo’ ‘t was so much bor 
trouble to be a prisoner.”

“Well, it _always_ is when it’s done right.  You got any rats around
here?”

“No, sah, I hain’t seed none.”

“Well, we’ll get you some rats.”

“Why, Mars Tom, I doan’ _want_ no rats.  Dey’s de dadblamedest creturs
to ‘sturb a body, en rustle roun’ over ‘im, en bite his feet, when he’s
tryin’ to sleep, I ever see.  No, sah, gimme g’yarter-snakes, ‘f I’s
got to have ‘m, but doan’ gimme no rats; I hain’ got no use f’r um,
skasely.”

“But, Jim, you _got_ to have ‘em--y all do.  So don’t make no more
fuss about it.  Prisoners ain’t ever without rats.  re ain’t no
instance of it.   y train m,  pet m,  learn m
tricks,  y get to be as sociable as flies.  But you got to play
music to m.  You got anything to play music on?”

“I ain’ got nuffn but a coase comb en a piece o’ paper, en a juice-harp;
but I reck’n dey wouldn’ take no stock in a juice-harp.”

“Yes y would _y_ don’t care what kind of music ‘tis.  A
jews-harp’s plenty good enough for a rat.  All animals like music--in a
prison y dote on it.  Specially, painful music;  you can’t get no
or kind out of a jews-harp.  It always interests m; y come out
to see what’s  matter with you.  Yes, you’re all right; you’re fixed
very well.  You want to set on your bed nights before you go to sleep,
 early in  mornings,  play your jews-harp; play ‘ Last Link
is Broken’--that’s  thing that ‘ll scoop a rat quicker ‘n anything
else;  when you’ve played about two minutes you’ll see all  rats,
  snakes,  spiders,  things begin to feel worried about you,
 come.   y’ll just fairly swarm over you,  have a noble good
time.”

“Yes, _dey_ will, I reck’n, Mars Tom, but what kine er time is _Jim_
havin’? Blest if I kin see de pint.  But I’ll do it ef I got to.  I
reck’n I better keep de animals satisfied, en not have no trouble in de
house.”

Tom waited to think it over,  see if re wasn’t nothing else; 
pretty soon he says:

“Oh, re’s one thing I forgot.  Could you raise a flower here, do you
reckon?”

“I doan know but maybe I could, Mars Tom; but it’s tolable dark in heah,
en I ain’ got no use f’r no flower, nohow, en she’d be a pow’ful sight
o’ trouble.”

“Well, you try it, anyway.  Some or prisoners has done it.”

“One er dem big cat-tail-lookin’ mullen-stalks would grow in heah, Mars
Tom, I reck’n, but she wouldn’t be wuth half de trouble she’d coss.”

“Don’t you believe it.  We’ll fetch you a little one  you plant it in
 corner over re,  raise it.   don’t call it mullen, call it
Pitchiola--that’s its right name when it’s in a prison.   you want to
water it with your tears.”

“Why, I got plenty spring water, Mars Tom.”

“You don’t _want_ spring water; you want to water it with your tears.
 It’s  way y always do.”

“Why, Mars Tom, I lay I kin raise one er dem mullen-stalks twyste wid
spring water whiles anor man’s a _start’n_ one wid tears.”

“That ain’t  idea.  You _got_ to do it with tears.”

“She’ll die on my han’s, Mars Tom, she sholy will; kase I doan’ skasely
ever cry.”

So Tom was stumped.  But he studied it over,  n said Jim would
have to worry along  best he could with an onion.  He promised
he would go to  nigger cabins  drop one, private, in Jim’s
coffee-pot, in  morning. Jim said he would “jis’ ‘s soon have
tobacker in his coffee;”  found so much fault with it,  with 
work  bor of raising  mullen,  jews-harping  rats, 
petting  flattering up  snakes  spiders  things, on top of
all  or work he had to do on pens,  inscriptions,  journals,
 things, which made it more trouble  worry  responsibility to
be a prisoner than anything he ever undertook, that Tom most lost all
patience with him;  said he was just loadened down with more gaudier
chances than a prisoner ever had in  world to make a name for
himself,  yet he didn’t know enough to appreciate m,  y was
just about wasted on him.  So Jim he was sorry,  said he wouldn’t
behave so no more,  n me  Tom shoved for bed.




CHAPTER XXXIX.

IN  morning we went up to  village  bought a wire rat-trap 
fetched it down,  unstopped  best rat-hole,  in about an hour
we had fifteen of  bulliest kind of ones;  n we took it  put
it in a safe place under Aunt Sally’s bed.  But while we was gone for
spiders little Thomas Franklin Benjamin Jefferson Elexer Phelps found
it re,  opened  door of it to see if  rats would come out,
 y did;  Aunt Sally she come in,  when we got back she was
a-sting on top of  bed raising Cain,   rats was doing what
y could to keep off  dull times for her.  So she took  dusted
us both with  hickry,  we was as much as two hours catching
anor fifteen or sixteen, drat that meddlesome cub,  y warn’t
 likeliest, nur, because  first haul was  pick of  flock.
 I never see a likelier lot of rats than what that first haul was.

We got a splendid stock of sorted spiders,  bugs,  frogs, 
caterpillars,  one thing or anor;  we like to got a hornet’s
nest, but we didn’t.   family was at home.  We didn’t give it right
up, but stayed with m as long as we could; because we allowed we’d
tire m out or y’d got to tire us out,  y done it.  n we
got allycumpain  rubbed on  places,  was pretty near all right
again, but couldn’t set down convenient.   so we went for  snakes,
 grabbed a couple of dozen garters  house-snakes,  put m in
a bag,  put it in our room,  by that time it was supper-time, 
a rattling good honest day’s work:   hungry?--oh, no, I reckon not!
  re warn’t a blessed snake up re when we went back--we didn’t
half tie  sack,  y worked out somehow,  left.  But it didn’t
matter much, because y was still on  premises somewheres.  So
we judged we could get some of m again.  No, re warn’t no real
scarcity of snakes about  house for a considerable spell.  You’d see
m dripping from  rafters  places every now  n;  y
generly led in your plate, or down  back of your neck,  most
of  time where you didn’t want m.  Well, y was hsome 
striped,  re warn’t no harm in a million of m; but that never
made no difference to Aunt Sally; she despised snakes, be  breed what
y might,  she couldn’t st m no way you could fix it; 
every time one of m flopped down on her, it didn’t make no difference
what she was doing, she would just lay that work down  light out.  I
never see such a woman.   you could hear her whoop to Jericho.  You
couldn’t get her to take a-holt of one of m with  tongs.   if
she turned over  found one in bed she would scramble out  lift a
howl that you would think  house was afire.  She disturbed  old
man so that he said he could most wish re hadn’t ever been no snakes
created.  Why, after every last snake had been gone clear out of 
house for as much as a week Aunt Sally warn’t over it yet; she warn’t
near over it; when she was setting thinking about something you could
touch her on  back of her neck with a fear  she would jump
right out of her stockings.  It was very curious.  But Tom said all
women was just so.  He said y was made that way for some reason or
or.

We got a licking every time one of our snakes come in her way,  she
allowed se lickings warn’t nothing to what she would do if we ever
loaded up  place again with m.  I didn’t mind  lickings,
because y didn’t amount to nothing; but I minded  trouble we
had to lay in anor lot.  But we got m laid in,  all  or
things;  you never see a cabin as blisome as Jim’s was when y’d
all swarm out for music  go for him.  Jim didn’t like  spiders,
  spiders didn’t like Jim;  so y’d lay for him,  make it
mighty warm for him.   he said that between  rats   snakes
  grindstone re warn’t no room in bed for him, skasely; 
when re was, a body couldn’t sleep, it was so lively,  it was
always lively, he said, because _y_ never all slept at one time, but
took turn about, so when  snakes was asleep  rats was on deck, 
when  rats turned in  snakes come on watch, so he always had one
gang under him, in his way,  t’or gang having a circus over him,
 if he got up to hunt a new place  spiders would take a chance at
him as he crossed over. He said if he ever got out this time he wouldn’t
ever be a prisoner again, not for a salary.

Well, by  end of three weeks everything was in pretty good shape.
  shirt was sent in early, in a pie,  every time a rat bit Jim he
would get up  write a little in his journal whilst  ink was fresh;
 pens was made,  inscriptions  so on was all carved on 
grindstone;  bed-leg was sawed in two,  we had et up  sawdust,
 it give us a most amazing stomach-ache.  We reckoned we was all
going to die, but didn’t.  It was  most undigestible sawdust I ever
see;  Tom said  same.

But as I was saying, we’d got all  work done now, at last;  we was
all pretty much fagged out, too, but mainly Jim.   old man had wrote
a couple of times to  plantation below Orleans to come  get ir
runaway nigger, but hadn’t got no answer, because re warn’t no such
plantation; so he allowed he would advertise Jim in  St. Louis 
New Orleans papers;  when he mentioned  St. Louis ones it give me
 cold shivers,  I see we hadn’t no time to lose. So Tom said, now
for  nonnamous letters.

“What’s m?”  I says.

“Warnings to  people that something is up.  Sometimes it’s done one
way, sometimes anor.  But re’s always somebody spying around that
gives notice to  governor of  castle.  When Louis XVI. was going
to light out of  Tooleries, a servant-girl done it.  It’s a very good
way,  so is  nonnamous letters.  We’ll use m both.   it’s
usual for  prisoner’s mor to change clos with him,  she
stays in,  he slides out in her clos.  We’ll do that, too.”

“But looky here, Tom, what do we want to _warn_ anybody for that
something’s up?  Let m find it out for mselves--it’s ir
lookout.”

“Yes, I know; but you can’t depend on m.  It’s  way y’ve acted
from  very start--left us to do _everything_.  y’re so confiding
 mullet-headed y don’t take notice of nothing at all.  So if we
don’t _give_ m notice re won’t be nobody nor nothing to interfere
with us,  so after all our hard work  trouble this escape ‘ll go
off perfectly flat; won’t amount to nothing--won’t be nothing _to_ it.”

“Well, as for me, Tom, that’s  way I’d like.”

“Shucks!” he says,  looked disgusted.  So I says:

“But I ain’t going to make no complaint.  Any way that suits you suits
me. What you going to do about  servant-girl?”

“You’ll be her.  You slide in, in  middle of  night,  hook that
yaller girl’s frock.”

“Why, Tom, that ‘ll make trouble next morning; because, of course, she
prob’bly hain’t got any but that one.”

“I know; but you don’t want it but fifteen minutes, to carry 
nonnamous letter  shove it under  front door.”

“All right, n, I’ll do it; but I could carry it just as hy in my
own togs.”

“You wouldn’t look like a servant-girl _n_, would you?”

“No, but re won’t be nobody to see what I look like, _anyway_.”

“That ain’t got nothing to do with it.   thing for us to do is just
to do our _duty_,  not worry about wher anybody _sees_ us do it or
not. Hain’t you got no principle at all?”

“All right, I ain’t saying nothing; I’m  servant-girl.  Who’s Jim’s
mor?”

“I’m his mor.  I’ll hook a gown from Aunt Sally.”

“Well, n, you’ll have to stay in  cabin when me  Jim leaves.”

“Not much.  I’ll stuff Jim’s clos full of straw  lay it on his bed
to represent his mor in disguise,  Jim ‘ll take  nigger woman’s
gown off of me  wear it,  we’ll all evade toger.  When a
prisoner of style escapes it’s called an evasion.  It’s always called
so when a king escapes, f’rinstance.    same with a king’s son;
it don’t make no difference wher he’s a natural one or an unnatural
one.”

So Tom he wrote  nonnamous letter,  I smouched  yaller wench’s
frock that night,  put it on,  shoved it under  front door, 
way Tom told me to.  It said:

Beware.  Trouble is brewing.  Keep a sharp lookout. _Unknown_ _Friend_.

Next night we stuck a picture, which Tom drawed in blood, of a skull 
crossbones on  front door;  next night anor one of a coffin on
 back door.  I never see a family in such a sweat.  y couldn’t a
been worse scared if  place had a been full of ghosts laying for m
behind everything  under  beds  shivering through  air.  If
a door banged, Aunt Sally she jumped  said “ouch!” if anything fell,
she jumped  said “ouch!” if you happened to touch her, when she
warn’t noticing, she done  same; she couldn’t face noway  be
satisfied, because she allowed re was something behind her every
time--so she was always a-whirling around sudden,  saying “ouch,” 
before she’d got two-thirds around she’d whirl back again,  say it
again;  she was afraid to go to bed, but she dasn’t set up.  So 
thing was working very well, Tom said; he said he never see a thing work
more satisfactory. He said it showed it was done right.

So he said, now for  gr bulge!  So  very next morning at 
streak of dawn we got anor letter ready,  was wondering what we
better do with it, because we heard m say at supper y was going
to have a nigger on watch at both doors all night.  Tom he went down 
lightning-rod to spy around;   nigger at  back door was asleep,
 he stuck it in  back of his neck  come back.  This letter
said:

Don’t betray me, I wish to be your friend.  re is a desprate gang of
cutthroats from over in  Indian Territory going to steal your runaway
nigger to-night,  y have been trying to scare you so as you will
stay in  house  not bor m.  I am one of  gang, but have
got religgion  wish to quit it  lead an honest life again, 
will betray  helish design. y will sneak down from northards,
along  fence, at midnight exact, with a false key,  go in 
nigger’s cabin to get him. I am to be off a piece  blow a tin horn
if I see any danger; but stead of that I will _baa_ like a sheep soon as
y get in  not blow at all; n whilst y are getting his
chains loose, you slip re  lock m in,  can kill m at your
leasure.  Don’t do anything but just  way I am telling you, if you do
y will suspicion something  raise whoop-jamboreehoo. I do not wish
any reward but to know I have done  right thing. _Unknown Friend._




CHAPTER XL.

WE was feeling pretty good after breakfast,  took my canoe  went
over  river a-fishing, with a lunch,  had a good time,  took a
look at  raft  found her all right,  got home late to supper,
 found m in such a sweat  worry y didn’t know which end y
was sting on,  made us go right off to bed  minute we was done
supper,  wouldn’t tell us what  trouble was,  never let on a
word about  new letter, but didn’t need to, because we knowed as much
about it as anybody did,  as soon as we was half up stairs  her
back was turned we slid for  cellar cupboard  loaded up a good
lunch  took it up to our room  went to bed,  got up about
half-past eleven,  Tom put on Aunt Sally’s dress that he stole 
was going to start with  lunch, but says:

“Where’s  butter?”

“I laid out a hunk of it,” I says, “on a piece of a corn-pone.”

“Well, you _left_ it laid out, n--it ain’t here.”

“We can get along without it,” I says.

“We can get along _with_ it, too,” he says; “just you slide down cellar
 fetch it.   n mosey right down  lightning-rod  come
along. I’ll go  stuff  straw into Jim’s clos to represent his
mor in disguise,  be ready to _baa_ like a sheep  shove soon as
you get re.”

So out he went,  down cellar went I.  hunk of butter, big as
a person’s fist, was where I had left it, so I took up  slab of
corn-pone with it on,  blowed out my light,  started up stairs
very stealthy,  got up to  main floor all right, but here comes
Aunt Sally with a cle,  I clapped  truck in my hat,  clapped
my hat on my head,   next second she see me;  she says:

“You been down cellar?”

“Yes’m.”

“What you been doing down re?”

“Noth’n.”

“_Noth’n!_”

“No’m.”

“Well, n, what possessed you to go down re this time of night?”

“I don’t know ‘m.”

“You don’t _know_?  Don’t answer me that way. Tom, I want to know what
you been _doing_ down re.”

“I hain’t been doing a single thing, Aunt Sally, I hope to gracious if I
have.”

I reckoned she’d let me go now,  as a generl thing she would; but I
s’pose re was so many strange things going on she was just in a sweat
about every little thing that warn’t yard-stick straight; so she says,
very decided:

“You just march into that setting-room  stay re till I come.  You
been up to something you no business to,  I lay I’ll find out what it
is before I’M done with you.”

So she went away as I opened  door  walked into  setting-room.
My, but re was a crowd re!  Fifteen farmers,  every one of m
had a gun.  I was most powerful sick,  slunk to a chair  set down.
y was setting around, some of m talking a little, in a low voice,
 all of m fidgety  uneasy, but trying to look like y warn’t;
but I knowed y was, because y was always taking off ir hats,
 putting m on,  scratching ir heads,  changing ir
seats,  fumbling with ir buttons.  I warn’t easy myself, but I
didn’t take my hat off, all  same.

I did wish Aunt Sally would come,  get done with me,  lick me, if
she wanted to,  let me get away  tell Tom how we’d overdone this
thing,  what a thundering hornet’s-nest we’d got ourselves into, so
we could stop fooling around straight off,  clear out with Jim before
se rips got out of patience  come for us.

At last she come  begun to ask me questions, but I _couldn’t_ answer
m straight, I didn’t know which end of me was up; because se men
was in such a fidget now that some was wanting to start right NOW 
lay for m desperadoes,  saying it warn’t but a few minutes to
midnight;  ors was trying to get m to hold on  wait for 
sheep-signal;  here was Aunty pegging away at  questions, 
me a-shaking all over  ready to sink down in my tracks I was
that scared;   place getting hotter  hotter,   butter
beginning to melt  run down my neck  behind my ears;  pretty
soon, when one of m says, “I’M for going  getting in  cabin
_first_  right _now_,  catching m when y come,” I most
dropped;  a streak of butter come a-trickling down my forehead, 
Aunt Sally she see it,  turns white as a sheet,  says:

“For  l’s sake, what _is_  matter with  child?  He’s got 
brain-fever as shore as you’re born,  y’re oozing out!”

 everybody runs to see,  she snatches off my hat,  out comes
 bread  what was left of  butter,  she grabbed me, 
hugged me,  says:

“Oh, what a turn you did give me!  how glad  grateful I am it
ain’t no worse; for luck’s against us,  it never rains but it pours,
 when I see that truck I thought we’d lost you, for I knowed by
 color  all it was just like your brains would be if--Dear,
dear, whyd’nt you _tell_ me that was what you’d been down re for, I
wouldn’t a cared.  Now cler out to bed,  don’t lemme see no more of
you till morning!”

I was up stairs in a second,  down  lightning-rod in anor one,
 shinning through  dark for  lean-to.  I couldn’t hardly get my
words out, I was so anxious; but I told Tom as quick as I could we must
jump for it now,  not a minute to lose-- house full of men, yonder,
with guns!

His eyes just blazed;  he says:

“No!--is that so?  _ain’t_ it bully!  Why, Huck, if it was to do over
again, I bet I could fetch two hundred!  If we could put it off till--”

“Hurry!  _Hurry_!”  I says.  “Where’s Jim?”

“Right at your elbow; if you reach out your arm you can touch him.
 He’s dressed,  everything’s ready.  Now we’ll slide out  give 
sheep-signal.”

But n we heard  tramp of men coming to  door,  heard m
begin to fumble with  pad-lock,  heard a man say:

“I _told_ you we’d be too soon; y haven’t come-- door is locked.
Here, I’ll lock some of you into  cabin,  you lay for ‘em in 
dark  kill ‘em when y come;   rest scatter around a piece,
 listen if you can hear ‘em coming.”

So in y come, but couldn’t see us in  dark,  most trod on
us whilst we was hustling to get under  bed.  But we got under all
right,  out through  hole, swift but soft--Jim first, me next,
 Tom last, which was according to Tom’s orders.  Now we was in 
lean-to,  heard trampings close by outside.  So we crept to  door,
 Tom stopped us re  put his eye to  crack, but couldn’t make
out nothing, it was so dark;  whispered  said he would listen
for  steps to get furr,  when he nudged us Jim must glide out
first,  him last.  So he set his ear to  crack  listened, 
listened,  listened,   steps a-scraping around out re all
 time;  at last he nudged us,  we slid out,  stooped down,
not breathing,  not making  least noise,  slipped stealthy
towards  fence in Injun file,  got to it all right,  me  Jim
over it; but Tom’s britches catched fast on a splinter on  top
rail,  n he hear  steps coming, so he had to pull loose, which
snapped  splinter  made a noise;  as he dropped in our tracks
 started somebody sings out:

“Who’s that?  Answer, or I’ll shoot!”

But we didn’t answer; we just unfurled our heels  shoved.  n re
was a rush,  a _Bang, Bang, Bang!_   bullets fairly whizzed
around us! We heard m sing out:

“Here y are!  y’ve broke for  river!  After ‘em, boys,  turn
loose  dogs!”

So here y come, full tilt.  We could hear m because y wore
boots  yelled, but we didn’t wear no boots  didn’t yell.  We was
in  path to  mill;  when y got pretty close on to us we
dodged into  bush  let m go by,  n dropped in behind
m.  y’d had all  dogs shut up, so y wouldn’t scare off 
robbers; but by this time somebody had let m loose,  here y
come, making powwow enough for a million; but y was our dogs; so we
stopped in our tracks till y catched up;  when y see it warn’t
nobody but us,  no excitement to offer m, y only just said
howdy,  tore right ahead towards  shouting  clattering; 
n we up-steam again,  whizzed along after m till we was nearly
to  mill,  n struck up through  bush to where my canoe was
tied,  hopped in  pulled for dear life towards  middle of 
river, but didn’t make no more noise than we was obleeged to. n we
struck out, easy  comfortable, for  isl where my raft was; 
we could hear m yelling  barking at each or all up  down 
bank, till we was so far away  sounds got dim  died out.   when
we stepped on to  raft I says:

“_Now_, old Jim, you’re a free man again,  I bet you won’t ever be a
slave no more.”

“En a mighty good job it wuz, too, Huck.  It ‘uz planned beautiful, en
it ‘uz done beautiful; en dey ain’t _nobody_ kin git up a plan dat’s mo’
mixed-up en splendid den what dat one wuz.”

We was all glad as we could be, but Tom was  gladdest of all because
he had a bullet in  calf of his leg.

When me  Jim heard that we didn’t feel so brash as what we did
before. It was hurting him considerable,  bleeding; so we laid him in
 wigwam  tore up one of  duke’s shirts for to bage him, but
he says:

“Gimme  rags; I can do it myself.  Don’t stop now; don’t fool around
here,   evasion booming along so hsome; man  sweeps,  set
her loose!  Boys, we done it elegant!--‘deed we did.  I wish _we’d_ a
had  hling of Louis XVI., re wouldn’t a been no ‘Son of Saint
Louis, ascend to heaven!’ wrote down in _his_ biography; no, sir, we’d
a whooped him over  _border_--that’s what we’d a done with _him_--
done it just as slick as nothing at all, too.  Man  sweeps--man 
sweeps!”

But me  Jim was consulting-- thinking.   after we’d thought a
minute, I says:

“Say it, Jim.”

So he says:

“Well, den, dis is de way it look to me, Huck.  Ef it wuz _him_ dat ‘uz
bein’ sot free, en one er de boys wuz to git shot, would he say, ‘Go on
en save me, nemmine ‘bout a doctor f’r to save dis one?’  Is dat like
Mars Tom Sawyer?  Would he say dat?  You _bet_ he wouldn’t!  _well_,
den, is _Jim_ gywne to say it?  No, sah--I doan’ budge a step out’n dis
place ‘dout a _doctor_, not if it’s forty year!”

I knowed he was white inside,  I reckoned he’d say what he did say--so
it was all right now,  I told Tom I was a-going for a doctor.
 He raised considerable row about it, but me  Jim stuck to it 
wouldn’t budge; so he was for crawling out  setting  raft loose
himself; but we wouldn’t let him.  n he give us a piece of his mind,
but it didn’t do no good.

So when he sees me getting  canoe ready, he says:

“Well, n, if you’re bound to go, I’ll tell you  way to do when you
get to  village.  Shut  door  blindfold  doctor tight 
fast,  make him swear to be silent as  grave,  put a purse
full of gold in his h,  n take  lead him all around 
back alleys  everywheres in  dark,  n fetch him here in 
canoe, in a roundabout way amongst  isls,  search him  take
his chalk away from him,  don’t give it back to him till you get him
back to  village, or else he will chalk this raft so he can find it
again. It’s  way y all do.”

So I said I would,  left,  Jim was to hide in  woods when he
see  doctor coming till he was gone again.




CHAPTER XLI.

 doctor was an old man; a very nice, kind-looking old man when I got
him up.  I told him me  my bror was over on Spanish Isl hunting
yesterday afternoon,  camped on a piece of a raft we found,  about
midnight he must a kicked his gun in his dreams, for it went off 
shot him in  leg,  we wanted him to go over re  fix it 
not say nothing about it, nor let anybody know, because we wanted to
come home this evening  surprise  folks.

“Who is your folks?” he says.

“ Phelpses, down yonder.”

“Oh,” he says.   after a minute, he says:

“How’d you say he got shot?”

“He had a dream,” I says, “ it shot him.”

“Singular dream,” he says.

So he lit up his lantern,  got his saddle-bags,  we started.  But
when he sees  canoe he didn’t like  look of her--said she was big
enough for one, but didn’t look pretty safe for two.  I says:

“Oh, you needn’t be afeard, sir, she carried  three of us easy
enough.”

“What three?”

“Why, me  Sid, ---- _ guns_; that’s what I mean.”

“Oh,” he says.

But he put his foot on  gunnel  rocked her,  shook his head,
 said he reckoned he’d look around for a bigger one.  But y was
all locked  chained; so he took my canoe,  said for me to wait
till he come back, or I could hunt around furr, or maybe I better
go down home  get m ready for  surprise if I wanted to.  But
I said I didn’t; so I told him just how to find  raft,  n he
started.

I struck an idea pretty soon.  I says to myself, spos’n he can’t fix
that leg just in three shakes of a sheep’s tail, as  saying is?
spos’n it takes him three or four days?  What are we going to do?--lay
around re till he lets  cat out of  bag?  No, sir; I know what
_I’ll_ do.  I’ll wait,  when he comes back if he says he’s got to
go any more I’ll get down re, too, if I swim;  we’ll take  tie
him,  keep him,  shove out down  river;  when Tom’s done
with him we’ll give him what it’s worth, or all we got,  n let him
get ashore.

So n I crept into a lumber-pile to get some sleep;  next time I
waked up  sun was away up over my head!  I shot out  went for 
doctor’s house, but y told me he’d gone away in  night some time
or or,  warn’t back yet.  Well, thinks I, that looks powerful bad
for Tom,  I’ll dig out for  isl right off.  So away I shoved,
 turned  corner,  nearly rammed my head into Uncle Silas’s
stomach! He says:

“Why, _Tom!_  Where you been all this time, you rascal?”

“I hain’t been nowheres,” I says, “only just hunting for  runaway
nigger--me  Sid.”

“Why, where ever did you go?” he says.  “Your aunt’s been mighty
uneasy.”

“She needn’t,” I says, “because we was all right.  We followed  men
  dogs, but y outrun us,  we lost m; but we thought we
heard m on  water, so we got a canoe  took out after m 
crossed over, but couldn’t find nothing of m; so we cruised along
up-shore till we got kind of tired  beat out;  tied up  canoe
 went to sleep,  never waked up till about an hour ago; n we
paddled over here to hear  news,  Sid’s at  post-office to see
what he can hear,  I’m a-branching out to get something to eat for
us,  n we’re going home.”

So n we went to  post-office to get “Sid”; but just as I
suspicioned, he warn’t re; so  old man he got a letter out of 
office,  we waited awhile longer, but Sid didn’t come; so  old man
said, come along, let Sid foot it home, or canoe it, when he got done
fooling around--but we would ride.  I couldn’t get him to let me stay
 wait for Sid;  he said re warn’t no use in it,  I must come
along,  let Aunt Sally see we was all right.

When we got home Aunt Sally was that glad to see me she laughed 
cried both,  hugged me,  give me one of m lickings of hern that
don’t amount to shucks,  said she’d serve Sid  same when he come.

  place was plum full of farmers  farmers’ wives, to dinner;
 such anor clack a body never heard.  Old Mrs. Hotchkiss was 
worst; her tongue was a-going all  time.  She says:

“Well, Sister Phelps, I’ve ransacked that-air cabin over, an’ I b’lieve
 nigger was crazy.  I says to Sister Damrell--didn’t I, Sister
Damrell?--s’I, he’s crazy, s’I--m’s  very words I said.  You all
hearn me: he’s crazy, s’I; everything shows it, s’I.  Look at that-air
grindstone, s’I; want to tell _me_’t any cretur ‘t’s in his right mind
‘s a goin’ to scrabble all m crazy things onto a grindstone, s’I?
 Here sich ‘n’ sich a person busted his heart; ‘n’ here so ‘n’ so
pegged along for thirty-seven year, ‘n’ all that--natcherl son o’ Louis
somebody, ‘n’ sich everlast’n rubbage.  He’s plumb crazy, s’I; it’s what
I says in  fust place, it’s what I says in  middle, ‘n’ it’s what
I says last ‘n’ all  time-- nigger’s crazy--crazy ‘s Nebokoodneezer,
s’I.”

“An’ look at that-air ladder made out’n rags, Sister Hotchkiss,” says
old Mrs. Damrell; “what in  name o’ goodness _could_ he ever want
of--”

“ very words I was a-sayin’ no longer ago th’n this minute to Sister
Utterback, ‘n’ she’ll tell you so herself.  Sh-she, look at that-air rag
ladder, sh-she; ‘n’ s’I, yes, _look_ at it, s’I--what _could_ he a-wanted
of it, s’I.  Sh-she, Sister Hotchkiss, sh-she--”

“But how in  nation’d y ever _git_ that grindstone _in_ re,
_anyway_? ‘n’ who dug that-air _hole_? ‘n’ who--”

“My very _words_, Brer Penrod!  I was a-sayin’--pass that-air sasser o’
m’lasses, won’t ye?--I was a-sayin’ to Sister Dunlap, jist this minute,
how _did_ y git that grindstone in re, s’I.  Without _help_, mind
you--‘thout _help_!  _that’s_ wher ‘tis.  Don’t tell _me_, s’I; re
_wuz_ help, s’I; ‘n’ r’ wuz a _plenty_ help, too, s’I; r’s ben a
_dozen_ a-helpin’ that nigger, ‘n’ I lay I’d skin every last nigger on
this place but _I’d_ find out who done it, s’I; ‘n’ moreover, s’I--”

“A _dozen_ says you!--_forty_ couldn’t a done every thing that’s been
done. Look at m case-knife saws  things, how tedious y’ve been
made; look at that bed-leg sawed off with ‘m, a week’s work for six men;
look at that nigger made out’n straw on  bed;  look at--”

“You may _well_ say it, Brer Hightower!  It’s jist as I was a-sayin’
to Brer Phelps, his own self.  S’e, what do _you_ think of it, Sister
Hotchkiss, s’e? Think o’ what, Brer Phelps, s’I?  Think o’ that bed-leg
sawed off that a way, s’e?  _think_ of it, s’I?  I lay it never sawed
_itself_ off, s’I--somebody _sawed_ it, s’I; that’s my opinion, take it
or leave it, it mayn’t be no ‘count, s’I, but sich as ‘t is, it’s my
opinion, s’I, ‘n’ if any body k’n start a better one, s’I, let him _do_
it, s’I, that’s all.  I says to Sister Dunlap, s’I--”

“Why, dog my cats, y must a ben a house-full o’ niggers in re
every night for four weeks to a done all that work, Sister Phelps.  Look
at that shirt--every last inch of it kivered over with secret African
writ’n done with blood!  Must a ben a raft uv ‘m at it right along, all
 time, amost.  Why, I’d give two dollars to have it read to me; ‘n’
as for  niggers that wrote it, I ‘low I’d take ‘n’ lash ‘m t’ll--”

“People to _help_ him, Bror Marples!  Well, I reckon you’d _think_
so if you’d a been in this house for a while back.  Why, y’ve stole
everything y could lay ir hs on-- we a-watching all  time,
mind you. y stole that shirt right off o’  line!  as for that
sheet y made  rag ladder out of, r’ ain’t no telling how
many times y _didn’t_ steal that;  flour,  cles, 
clesticks,  spoons,   old warming-pan,  most a thous
things that I disremember now,  my new calico dress;  me 
Silas  my Sid  Tom on  constant watch day __ night, as I was
a-telling you,  not a one of us could catch hide nor hair nor sight
nor sound of m;  here at  last minute, lo  behold you, y
slides right in under our noses  fools us,  not only fools _us_
but  Injun Territory robbers too,  actuly gets _away_ with that
nigger safe  sound,  that with sixteen men  twenty-two dogs
right on ir very heels at that very time!  I tell you, it just bangs
anything I ever _heard_ of. Why, _sperits_ couldn’t a done better 
been no smarter.  I reckon y must a _been_ sperits--because, _you_
know our dogs,  r’ ain’t no better; well, m dogs never even got
on  _track_ of ‘m once!  You explain _that_ to me if you can!--_any_
of you!”

“Well, it does beat--”

“Laws alive, I never--”

“So help me, I wouldn’t a be--”

“_House_-thieves as well as--”

“Goodnessgracioussakes, I’d a ben afeard to live in sich a--”

“‘Fraid to _live_!--why, I was that scared I dasn’t hardly go to bed, or
get up, or lay down, or _set_ down, Sister Ridgeway.  Why, y’d steal
 very--why, goodness sakes, you can guess what kind of a fluster I was
in by  time midnight come last night.  I hope to gracious if I warn’t
afraid y’d steal some o’  family!  I was just to that pass I
didn’t have no reasoning faculties no more.  It looks foolish enough
_now_, in  daytime; but I says to myself, re’s my two poor boys
asleep, ‘way up stairs in that lonesome room,  I declare to goodness
I was that uneasy ‘t I crep’ up re  locked ‘em in!  I _did_.  
anybody would. Because, you know, when you get scared that way,  it
keeps running on,  getting worse  worse all  time,  your
wits gets to addling,  you get to doing all sorts o’ wild things,
 by  by you think to yourself, spos’n I was a boy,  was away up
re,   door ain’t locked,  you--” She stopped, looking kind
of wondering,  n she turned her head around slow,  when her eye
lit on me--I got up  took a walk.

Says I to myself, I can explain better how we come to not be in that
room this morning if I go out to one side  study over it a little.
 So I done it.  But I dasn’t go fur, or she’d a sent for me.   when
it was late in  day  people all went,  n I come in 
told her  noise  shooting waked up me  “Sid,”   door was
locked,  we wanted to see  fun, so we went down  lightning-rod,
 both of us got hurt a little,  we didn’t never want to try _that_
no more.   n I went on  told her all what I told Uncle Silas
before;  n she said she’d forgive us,  maybe it was all right
enough anyway,  about what a body might expect of boys, for all boys
was a pretty harum-scarum lot as fur as she could see;  so, as long
as no harm hadn’t come of it, she judged she better put in her time
being grateful we was alive  well  she had us still, stead of
fretting over what was past  done.  So n she kissed me,  patted
me on  head,  dropped into a kind of a brown study;  pretty
soon jumps up,  says:

“Why, lawsamercy, it’s most night,  Sid not come yet!  What _has_
become of that boy?”

I see my chance; so I skips up  says:

“I’ll run right up to town  get him,” I says.

“No you won’t,” she says.  “You’ll stay right wher’ you are; _one’s_
enough to be lost at a time.  If he ain’t here to supper, your uncle ‘ll
go.”

Well, he warn’t re to supper; so right after supper uncle went.

He come back about ten a little bit uneasy; hadn’t run across Tom’s
track. Aunt Sally was a good _deal_ uneasy; but Uncle Silas he said
re warn’t no occasion to be--boys will be boys, he said,  you’ll
see this one turn up in  morning all sound  right.  So she had
to be satisfied.  But she said she’d set up for him a while anyway, 
keep a light burning so he could see it.

 n when I went up to bed she come up with me  fetched her
