cle,  tucked me in,  mored me so good I felt mean,  like
I couldn’t look her in  face;  she set down on  bed  talked
with me a long time,  said what a splendid boy Sid was,  didn’t
seem to want to ever stop talking about him;  kept asking me every
now  n if I reckoned he could a got lost, or hurt, or maybe
drownded,  might be laying at this minute somewheres suffering or
dead,  she not by him to help him,  so  tears would drip down
silent,  I would tell her that Sid was all right,  would be home
in  morning, sure;  she would squeeze my h, or maybe kiss me,
 tell me to say it again,  keep on saying it, because it done her
good,  she was in so much trouble.   when she was going away she
looked down in my eyes so steady  gentle,  says:

“ door ain’t going to be locked, Tom,  re’s  window 
 rod; but you’ll be good, _won’t_ you?   you won’t go?  For _my_
sake.”

Laws knows I _wanted_ to go bad enough to see about Tom,  was all
intending to go; but after that I wouldn’t a went, not for kingdoms.

But she was on my mind  Tom was on my mind, so I slept very restless.
 twice I went down  rod away in  night,  slipped around
front,  see her setting re by her cle in  window with her
eyes towards  road   tears in m;  I wished I could do
something for her, but I couldn’t, only to swear that I wouldn’t never
do nothing to grieve her any more.    third time I waked up at
dawn,  slid down,  she was re yet,  her cle was most out,
 her old gray head was resting on her h,  she was asleep.




CHAPTER XLII.

 old man was uptown again before breakfast, but couldn’t get no
track of Tom;  both of m set at  table thinking,  not saying
nothing,  looking mournful,  ir coffee getting cold,  not
eating anything.  by  by  old man says:

“Did I give you  letter?”

“What letter?”

“ one I got yesterday out of  post-office.”

“No, you didn’t give me no letter.”

“Well, I must a forgot it.”

So he rummaged his pockets,  n went off somewheres where he had
laid it down,  fetched it,  give it to her.  She says:

“Why, it’s from St. Petersburg--it’s from Sis.”

I allowed anor walk would do me good; but I couldn’t stir.  But
before she could break it open she dropped it  run--for she see
something.  so did I. It was Tom Sawyer on a mattress;  that old
doctor;  Jim, in _her_ calico dress, with his hs tied behind him;
 a lot of people.  I hid  letter behind  first thing that come
hy,  rushed.  She flung herself at Tom, crying,  says:

“Oh, he’s dead, he’s dead, I know he’s dead!”

 Tom he turned his head a little,  muttered something or or,
which showed he warn’t in his right mind; n she flung up her hs,
 says:

“He’s alive, thank God!   that’s enough!”  she snatched a kiss of
him,  flew for  house to get  bed ready,  scattering orders
right  left at  niggers  everybody else, as fast as her tongue
could go, every jump of  way.

I followed  men to see what y was going to do with Jim;  
old doctor  Uncle Silas followed after Tom into  house.   men
was very huffy,  some of m wanted to hang Jim for an example to
all  or niggers around re, so y wouldn’t be trying to run
away like Jim done,  making such a raft of trouble,  keeping a
whole family scared most to death for days  nights.  But  ors
said, don’t do it, it wouldn’t answer at all; he ain’t our nigger, 
his owner would turn up  make us pay for him, sure.  So that cooled
m down a little, because  people that’s always  most anxious
for to hang a nigger that hain’t done just right is always  very
ones that ain’t  most anxious to pay for him when y’ve got ir
satisfaction out of him.

y cussed Jim considerble, though,  give him a cuff or two side 
head once in a while, but Jim never said nothing,  he never let on to
know me,  y took him to  same cabin,  put his own clos
on him,  chained him again,  not to no bed-leg this time, but to
a big staple drove into  bottom log,  chained his hs, too, 
both legs,  said he warn’t to have nothing but bread  water to
eat after this till his owner come, or he was sold at auction because
he didn’t come in a certain length of time,  filled up our hole, 
said a couple of farmers with guns must st watch around about 
cabin every night,  a bulldog tied to  door in  daytime; 
about this time y was through with  job  was tapering off with
a kind of generl good-bye cussing,  n  old doctor comes 
takes a look,  says:

“Don’t be no rougher on him than you’re obleeged to, because he ain’t
a bad nigger.  When I got to where I found  boy I see I couldn’t cut
 bullet out without some help,  he warn’t in no condition for
me to leave to go  get help;  he got a little worse  a little
worse,  after a long time he went out of his head,  wouldn’t let
me come a-nigh him any more,  said if I chalked his raft he’d kill
me,  no end of wild foolishness like that,  I see I couldn’t do
anything at all with him; so I says, I got to have _help_ somehow; 
 minute I says it out crawls this nigger from somewheres  says
he’ll help,  he done it, too,  done it very well.  Of course I
judged he must be a runaway nigger,  re I _was_!  re I had
to stick right straight along all  rest of  day  all night.  It
was a fix, I tell you! I had a couple of patients with  chills, 
of course I’d of liked to run up to town  see m, but I dasn’t,
because  nigger might get away,  n I’d be to blame;  yet
never a skiff come close enough for me to hail.  So re I had to stick
plumb until daylight this morning;  I never see a nigger that was a
better nuss or faithfuller,  yet he was risking his freedom to do it,
 was all tired out, too,  I see plain enough he’d been worked
main hard lately.  I liked  nigger for that; I tell you, gentlemen, a
nigger like that is worth a thous dollars-- kind treatment, too.  I
had everything I needed,   boy was doing as well re as he
would a done at home--better, maybe, because it was so quiet; but re I
_was_, with both of ‘m on my hs,  re I had to stick till about
dawn this morning; n some men in a skiff come by,  as good luck
would have it  nigger was setting by  pallet with his head propped
on his knees sound asleep; so I motioned m in quiet,  y slipped
up on him  grabbed him  tied him before he knowed what he was
about,  we never had no trouble.   boy being in a kind of a
flighty sleep, too, we muffled  oars  hitched  raft on, 
towed her over very nice  quiet,   nigger never made  least
row nor said a word from  start.  He ain’t no bad nigger, gentlemen;
that’s what I think about him.”

Somebody says:

“Well, it sounds very good, doctor, I’m obleeged to say.”

n  ors softened up a little, too,  I was mighty thankful
to that old doctor for doing Jim that good turn;  I was glad it was
according to my judgment of him, too; because I thought he had a good
heart in him  was a good man  first time I see him.  n y
all agreed that Jim had acted very well,  was deserving to have some
notice took of it,  reward.  So every one of m promised, right out
 hearty, that y wouldn’t cuss him no more.

n y come out  locked him up.  I hoped y was going to say he
could have one or two of  chains took off, because y was rotten
heavy, or could have meat  greens with his bread  water; but y
didn’t think of it,  I reckoned it warn’t best for me to mix in, but
I judged I’d get  doctor’s yarn to Aunt Sally somehow or or as
soon as I’d got through  breakers that was laying just ahead of
me--explanations, I mean, of how I forgot to mention about Sid being shot
when I was telling how him  me put in that dratted night paddling
around hunting  runaway nigger.

But I had plenty time.  Aunt Sally she stuck to  sick-room all day
 all night,  every time I see Uncle Silas mooning around I dodged
him.

Next morning I heard Tom was a good deal better,  y said Aunt
Sally was gone to get a nap.  So I slips to  sick-room,  if I
found him awake I reckoned we could put up a yarn for  family that
would wash. But he was sleeping,  sleeping very peaceful, too; 
pale, not fire-faced  way he was when he come.  So I set down 
laid for him to wake.  In about half an hour Aunt Sally comes gliding
in,  re I was, up a stump again!  She motioned me to be still, 
set down by me,  begun to whisper,  said we could all be joyful
now, because all  symptoms was first-rate,  he’d been sleeping
like that for ever so long,  looking better  peacefuller all 
time,  ten to one he’d wake up in his right mind.

So we set re watching,  by  by he stirs a bit,  opened his
eyes very natural,  takes a look,  says:

“Hello!--why, I’m at _home_!  How’s that?  Where’s  raft?”

“It’s all right,” I says.

“ _Jim_?”

“ same,” I says, but couldn’t say it pretty brash.  But he never
noticed, but says:

“Good!  Splendid!  _Now_ we’re all right  safe! Did you tell Aunty?”

I was going to say yes; but she chipped in  says:  “About what, Sid?”

“Why, about  way  whole thing was done.”

“What whole thing?”

“Why, __ whole thing.  re ain’t but one; how we set  runaway
nigger free--me  Tom.”

“Good l!  Set  run--What _is_  child talking about!  Dear, dear,
out of his head again!”

“_No_, I ain’t out of my _head_; I know all what I’m talking about.  We
_did_ set him free--me  Tom.  We laid out to do it,  we _done_ it.
  we done it elegant, too.”  He’d got a start,  she never checked
him up, just set  stared  stared,  let him clip along, 
I see it warn’t no use for _me_ to put in.  “Why, Aunty, it cost us a
power of work--weeks of it--hours  hours, every night, whilst you was
all asleep.  we had to steal cles,   sheet,   shirt,
 your dress,  spoons,  tin plates,  case-knives,  
warming-pan,   grindstone,  flour,  just no end of things,
 you can’t think what work it was to make  saws,  pens, 
inscriptions,  one thing or anor,  you can’t think _half_ 
fun it was.   we had to make up  pictures of coffins  things,
 nonnamous letters from  robbers,  get up  down 
lightning-rod,  dig  hole into  cabin,  made  rope ladder
 send it in cooked up in a pie,  send in spoons  things to work
with in your apron pocket--”

“Mercy sakes!”

“-- load up  cabin with rats  snakes  so on, for company for
Jim;  n you kept Tom here so long with  butter in his hat that
you come near spiling  whole business, because  men come before
we was out of  cabin,  we had to rush,  y heard us  let
drive at us,  I got my share,  we dodged out of  path  let
m go by,  when  dogs come y warn’t interested in us, but
went for  most noise,  we got our canoe,  made for 
raft,  was all safe,  Jim was a free man,  we done it all by
ourselves,  _wasn’t_ it bully, Aunty!”

“Well, I never heard  likes of it in all my born days!  So it was
_you_, you little rapscallions, that’s been making all this trouble,
 turned everybody’s wits clean inside out  scared us all most to
death.  I’ve as good a notion as ever I had in my life to take it out
o’ you this very minute.  To think, here I’ve been, night after night,
a--_you_ just get well once, you young scamp,  I lay I’ll tan  Old
Harry out o’ both o’ ye!”

But Tom, he _was_ so proud  joyful, he just _couldn’t_ hold in,
 his tongue just _went_ it--she a-chipping in,  spitting fire all
along,  both of m going it at once, like a cat convention;  she
says:

“_Well_, you get all  enjoyment you can out of it _now_, for mind I
tell you if I catch you meddling with him again--”

“Meddling with _who_?”  Tom says, dropping his smile  looking
surprised.

“With _who_?  Why,  runaway nigger, of course.  Who’d you reckon?”

Tom looks at me very grave,  says:

“Tom, didn’t you just tell me he was all right?  Hasn’t he got away?”

“_Him_?” says Aunt Sally; “ runaway nigger?  ‘Deed he hasn’t.
 y’ve got him back, safe  sound,  he’s in that cabin again,
on bread  water,  loaded down with chains, till he’s claimed or
sold!”

Tom rose square up in bed, with his eye hot,  his nostrils opening
 shutting like gills,  sings out to me:

“y hain’t no _right_ to shut him up!  SHOVE!-- don’t you lose a
minute.  Turn him loose! he ain’t no slave; he’s as free as any cretur
that walks this earth!”

“What _does_  child mean?”

“I mean every word I _say_, Aunt Sally,  if somebody don’t go, _I’ll_
go. I’ve knowed him all his life,  so has Tom, re.  Old Miss
Watson died two months ago,  she was ashamed she ever was going to
sell him down  river,  _said_ so;  she set him free in her
will.”

“n what on earth did _you_ want to set him free for, seeing he was
already free?”

“Well, that _is_ a question, I must say;  just like women!  Why,
I wanted  _adventure_ of it;  I’d a waded neck-deep in blood
to--goodness alive, _Aunt Polly!_”

If she warn’t sting right re, just inside  door, looking as
sweet  contented as an angel half full of pie, I wish I may never!

Aunt Sally jumped for her,  most hugged  head off of her, 
cried over her,  I found a good enough place for me under  bed,
for it was getting pretty sultry for us, seemed to me.   I peeped
out,  in a little while Tom’s Aunt Polly shook herself loose 
stood re looking across at Tom over her spectacles--kind of grinding
him into  earth, you know.   n she says:

“Yes, you _better_ turn y’r head away--I would if I was you, Tom.”

“Oh, deary me!” says Aunt Sally; “_Is_ he changed so?  Why, that ain’t
_Tom_, it’s Sid; Tom’s--Tom’s--why, where is Tom?  He was here a minute
ago.”

“You mean where’s Huck _Finn_--that’s what you mean!  I reckon I hain’t
raised such a scamp as my Tom all se years not to know him when I
_see_ him.  That _would_ be a pretty howdy-do. Come out from under that
bed, Huck Finn.”

So I done it.  But not feeling brash.

Aunt Sally she was one of  mixed-upest-looking persons I ever
see--except one,  that was Uncle Silas, when he come in  y told
it all to him.  It kind of made him drunk, as you may say,  he didn’t
know nothing at all  rest of  day,  preached a prayer-meeting
sermon that night that gave him a rattling ruputation, because 
oldest man in  world couldn’t a understood it.  So Tom’s Aunt Polly,
she told all about who I was,  what;  I had to up  tell how
I was in such a tight place that when Mrs. Phelps took me for Tom
Sawyer--she chipped in  says, “Oh, go on  call me Aunt Sally, I’m
used to it now,  ‘tain’t no need to change”--that when Aunt Sally took
me for Tom Sawyer I had to st it--re warn’t no or way, 
I knowed he wouldn’t mind, because it would be nuts for him, being
a mystery,  he’d make an adventure out of it,  be perfectly
satisfied.   so it turned out,  he let on to be Sid,  made
things as soft as he could for me.

 his Aunt Polly she said Tom was right about old Miss Watson setting
Jim free in her will;  so, sure enough, Tom Sawyer had gone  took
all that trouble  bor to set a free nigger free!  I couldn’t
ever underst before, until that minute  that talk, how he _could_
help a body set a nigger free with his bringing-up.

Well, Aunt Polly she said that when Aunt Sally wrote to her that Tom 
_Sid_ had come all right  safe, she says to herself:

“Look at that, now!  I might have expected it, letting him go off that
way without anybody to watch him.  So now I got to go  trapse all
 way down  river, eleven hundred mile,  find out what that
creetur’s up to _this_ time, as long as I couldn’t seem to get any
answer out of you about it.”

“Why, I never heard nothing from you,” says Aunt Sally.

“Well, I wonder!  Why, I wrote you twice to ask you what you could mean
by Sid being here.”

“Well, I never got ‘em, Sis.”

Aunt Polly she turns around slow  severe,  says:

“You, Tom!”

“Well--_what_?” he says, kind of pettish.

“Don’t you what _me_, you impudent thing--h out m letters.”

“What letters?”

“_m_ letters.  I be bound, if I have to take a-holt of you I’ll--”

“y’re in  trunk.  re, now.   y’re just  same as y
was when I got m out of  office.  I hain’t looked into m, I
hain’t touched m.  But I knowed y’d make trouble,  I thought if
you warn’t in no hurry, I’d--”

“Well, you _do_ need skinning, re ain’t no mistake about it.   I
wrote anor one to tell you I was coming;  I s’pose he--”

“No, it come yesterday; I hain’t read it yet, but _it’s_ all right, I’ve
got that one.”

I wanted to offer to bet two dollars she hadn’t, but I reckoned maybe it
was just as safe to not to.  So I never said nothing.




CHAPTER  LAST

 first time I catched Tom private I asked him what was his idea, time
of  evasion?--what it was he’d planned to do if  evasion worked all
right  he managed to set a nigger free that was already free before?
 he said, what he had planned in his head from  start, if we got
Jim out all safe, was for us to run him down  river on  raft, 
have adventures plumb to  mouth of  river,  n tell him about
his being free,  take him back up home on a steamboat, in style,
 pay him for his lost time,  write word ahead  get out all
 niggers around,  have m waltz him into town with a torchlight
procession  a brass-b,  n he would be a hero,  so would
we.  But I reckoned it was about as well  way it was.

We had Jim out of  chains in no time,  when Aunt Polly  Uncle
Silas  Aunt Sally found out how good he helped  doctor nurse Tom,
y made a heap of fuss over him,  fixed him up prime,  give him
all he wanted to eat,  a good time,  nothing to do.   we had
him up to  sick-room,  had a high talk;  Tom give Jim forty
dollars for being prisoner for us so patient,  doing it up so good,
 Jim was pleased most to death,  busted out,  says:

“Dah, now, Huck, what I tell you?--what I tell you up dah on Jackson
islan’?  I _tole_ you I got a hairy breas’, en what’s de sign un it; en
I _tole_ you I ben rich wunst, en gwineter to be rich _agin_; en it’s
come true; en heah she is!  _dah_, now! doan’ talk to _me_--signs is
_signs_, mine I tell you; en I knowed jis’ ‘s well ‘at I ‘uz gwineter be
rich agin as I’s a-stannin’ heah dis minute!”

 n Tom he talked along  talked along,  says, le’s all three
slide out of here one of se nights  get an outfit,  go for
howling adventures amongst  Injuns, over in  Territory, for a
couple of weeks or two;  I says, all right, that suits me, but I
ain’t got no money for to buy  outfit,  I reckon I couldn’t get
none from home, because it’s likely pap’s been back before now,  got
it all away from Judge Thatcher  drunk it up.

“No, he hain’t,” Tom says; “it’s all re yet--six thous dollars
 more;  your pap hain’t ever been back since.  Hadn’t when I come
away, anyhow.”

Jim says, kind of solemn:

“He ain’t a-comin’ back no mo’, Huck.”

I says:

“Why, Jim?”

“Nemmine why, Huck--but he ain’t comin’ back no mo.”

But I kept at him; so at last he says:

“Doan’ you ‘member de house dat was float’n down de river, en dey wuz a
man in dah, kivered up, en I went in en unkivered him  didn’ let you
come in?  Well, den, you kin git yo’ money when you wants it, kase dat
wuz him.”

Tom’s most well now,  got his bullet around his neck on a watch-guard
for a watch,  is always seeing what time it is,  so re ain’t
nothing more to write about,  I am rotten glad of it, because if I’d
a knowed what a trouble it was to make a book I wouldn’t a tackled it,
 ain’t a-going to no more.  But I reckon I got to light out for 
Territory ahead of  rest, because Aunt Sally she’s going to adopt me
 sivilize me,  I can’t st it.  I been re before.

 END. YOURS TRULY, _HUCK FINN_.





End of  Project Gutenberg EBook of Adventures of Huckleberry Finn,
Complete, by Mark Twain (Samuel Clemens)
